# 伊川击壤集

**Yichuan’s Clod-Pounding Collection**

**Canonical Title:** *Yichuan Jirang Ji* (*Yichuan’s Clod-Pounding Collection*)
**Author:** Shao Yong of the Song Dynasty
**Extent:** Twenty *juan*
**Base Text:** *Zhengtong Daozang* (*Daoist Canon of the Zhengtong Era*), *Taixuan* Section
**Collated Edition:** *Siku Quanshu* (*Complete Library of the Four Treasuries*), Wenyuan Pavilion edition (hereafter *Wenyuan Pavilion edition*)

---

### Preface to *Yichuan’s Clod-Pounding Collection*

The *Clod-Pounding Collection* is a compilation of poems in which Master Yichuan found his own joy. Yet it is not merely self-joy—it also delights in the times and in the self-contentment of the myriad things.

Master Yichuan said: Zixia once declared, “Poetry is the expression of one’s aspirations. What resides in the heart is *zhi* (aspiration); when spoken, it becomes poetry. When emotions stir within and take form in words, and sounds coalesce into patterns, we call this *yin* (music). Thus, to harbor the times is to call it *zhi*; to be moved by things is to call it *qing* (emotion). To give voice to *zhi* is to call it *yan* (speech); to elevate *qing* is to call it *sheng* (sound). When speech forms a composition, it is called poetry; when sound forms patterns, it is called music. Thereafter, by hearing one’s poetry and listening to one’s music, the *zhi* and *qing* of a person may be known.

Now, emotions are seven in number, but their essence lies in two: the self and the times. As for the self, it concerns the fortunes and misfortunes of the individual; as for the times, it concerns the prosperity or decline of the age. The fortunes and misfortunes of the self extend no further than poverty or wealth, nobility or lowliness. The prosperity or decline of the times rests with the rise and fall, order and chaos of the realm.

Thus, when Confucius edited the *Book of Poetry*, he discarded nine-tenths of it. Of the over a thousand states of the feudal lords, only fifteen were selected for the *Airs*; of the twelve kings of the Western Zhou, only six were chosen for the *Odes*. This was because the Way of transmitting instruction lies in making clear what is good and what is evil, and only the most salient examples were preserved.

In recent times, poets, when afflicted by hardship, devote themselves to resentment and indignation; when favored with prosperity, they indulge in excess and dissipation. The fortunes and misfortunes of the self manifest in joy and anger; the prosperity or decline of the times arise from love and aversion. Rarely do they speak with the great righteousness of the world in mind. Thus, their poetry is largely submerged in personal predilections.

Alas! The drowning power of emotion is greater than that of water. The ancients said that water can bear a boat, but it can also capsize it. Whether it bears or capsizes depends on the water, not on the person. When it bears, it brings benefit; when it capsizes, it brings harm. Thus, benefit and harm lie with the person, not with the water. But does the bearing or capsizing determine a person’s benefit or harm, or do benefit and harm determine the bearing or capsizing? Between the two, there must be a proper course.

It is like a person treading water: it is the person who treads the water, not the water that treads the person. Yet even those praised for their skill in treading water have never escaped being harmed by it. If one treads water for external gain, then the nature of water follows the nature of the person. If one treads water for internal gain, then the peril of ruin stands immediately before them. In such cases, what need is there to distinguish between person and water? The injury to one’s nature and the harm to one’s life are one and the same.

Nature is the embodiment of the Dao. If nature is injured, the Dao follows. The heart is the outer enclosure of nature. If the heart is injured, nature follows. The body is the dwelling of the heart. If the body is injured, the heart follows. Things are the vessels of the body. If things are injured, the body follows.

Thus, to observe nature through the Dao, the heart through nature, the body through the heart, and things through the body—this is governance. Yet even so, one has not escaped harm. It is better to observe the Dao through the Dao, nature through nature, the heart through the heart, the body through the body, and things through things. Then, even if one wished to inflict harm, how could it be achieved?

If this is so, then to observe the family through the family, the state through the state, and the world through the world—this principle may likewise be understood.

From my youth, I devoted myself to the teachings of the Confucians, believing that the joys of the human world scarcely amounted to one ten-thousandth of what they could be, while the joys of the *mingjiao* (teachings of names and rituals) were boundless. Moreover, the joys of observing things were also boundless. Though life and death, honor and disgrace battled before me, they never entered my heart—how were they different from the four seasons, wind and flowers, snow and moon, passing before one’s eyes?

Truly, this is the ability to observe things as things, so that neither harms the other. For in this state, the entanglements of emotion are entirely forgotten; the only thing not forgotten is poetry. Yet though it is said not to be forgotten, in truth, it is as if forgotten. Why? Because what I compose differs from what others compose.

My compositions know no bounds; their rhythms and meters are not [restricted]; love and aversion are not established. Thus, they do not seek fame or reputation, but respond like a mirror to a reflection, like a bell to a sound. Occasionally, in the leisure after cultivating the Dao, I observe the times in tranquility, reflect upon things in stillness, rouse my aspirations in accord with the times, lodge words in things, give voice to aspirations in song, form poetry from words, give sound to song, and create music from poetry.

Thus, though there is sorrow, it never wounds; though there is joy, it never indulges. Though it is said to chant and sing of emotions and nature, how could it ever burden nature and emotion?

Bells and drums are music; jade and silk are ritual. To be addicted to bells, drums, jade, and silk—such words cannot avoid being vulgar. Yet to abolish bells, drums, jade, and silk—what then becomes of ritual and music?

Some say that the Way of the *Airs* and *Odes* flourished in antiquity but not in the present—this is hardly a comprehensive view. It is the speech of those constrained by their own circumstances.

Alas! Do they not consider that those who do good in the world are few, while those who harm the good are many; that those who create peril are numerous, while those who uphold peril are few? When a man of aspiration dwells in the fields, he speaks of the fields. Thus, his poetry is named the *Yichuan Clod-Pounding Collection*.

Written on the Mid-Autumn Festival of the *bingwu* year of the *Zhiping* era of the Song Dynasty.

---

**Volume One of *Yichuan’s Clod-Pounding Collection***
**Shao Yong (Yaofu) of Yichuan**

**The Great Chant on Watching Chess**

Some men excel in refined arts;
I once observed them play at chess.
Beyond the moves, I saw creation’s scheme,
Within each piece, the subtle shifts confess.

An endless thirst for triumph burns,
A will to lead that never turns.
Though seated face to face as host and guest,
They meet like strangers, savage and unblessed.

Wealth stirs the heart, its fire untamed,
Joy, wrath—each passion plainly named.
Life and death lie in their hands,
Gain and loss upon their lips command.

Harmony shatters like ice and flame,
No flute and pipe their discord tame.
No bond of friendship here is known,
No love between a wife and husband shown.

Pearls and jade from sleeves they draw,
Dragons, serpents writhe and gnaw.
Walls of bronze rise from the board,
Swords and halberds clash, the air is scored.

By day they summon ghosts and gods,
On level ground, the dragons plod.
Empty rivers roar with hail and thunder,
Land and sea see leviathans sunder.

Cold and heat in equal sway,
Light and dark alternate day.
Mountains, rivers gleam like jewels bright,
Stars and spheres align in cosmic sight.

Watching fortunes ebb and flow,
I startle at the paths of weal and woe.
High and low shift with ease,
Yet reversal’s grip none can appease.

Heart and deed stand sundered wide,
Gain and harm cannot abide.
Fold or spread—know the hour,
Take or leave—seize the power.

The cunning drown in their own deceit,
The trusting fall to blunt deceit.
Truth and falsehood twist and blend,
Name and substance meet their end.

Victory is loss’s root,
Fortune climbs on misfortune’s boot.
Heaven and Earth in strife engage,
Fire and Water turn the page.

Bows and arrows clash in might,
Words and taunts ignite the fight.
What game lacks win or loss?
What age escapes rise and toss?

What once was right may now be wrong,
What seems so strong may soon be gone.
Viewing past through present’s eye,
Not just Fu Xi’s age meets the sky.

Yao and Shun bowed in graceful yield,
Yet Four Fiends lurked in the field.
Tang and Wu took up the blade,
Two Elders’ reproach did not fade.

Though Gao Yao spoke with wisdom’s grace,
Yi Yin and Zhou’s counsel held their place.
Yet even then, the cure brought pain,
Mending bone while flesh was slain.

Benevolence fell to fame’s deceit,
Righteousness was crushed by gain’s deceit.
Order, chaos—none stand still,
Change and reform bend to time’s will.

Favor the worthy, not the son,
Yet flaw and folly still are spun.
Favor the son, not the wise,
And scions bear the wounds’ device.

Some, like Miao, defied command,
Some, like Youhu, barred the land.
Yi stirred the strife’s first spark,
Guan and Cai spread doubt’s dark mark.

The Shang marched on Ge’s domain,
The Zhou struck Li’s domain.
At Mingtiao, banners flew,
At Muye, standards fell anew.

The sun that lit Xia’s prison bright
Once shone on Sheng’s flight.
Who knows if moonlight’s gentle ray,
On Mengshi’s path did not hold sway?

King Li fled to Zhi,
King You died at Li.
King Ping moved to Luo,
King Nan fell at Yi.

Some pledged at Shaoling’s plain,
Some met at Huangchi’s main.
Some fought at Chang’an’s shore,
Some slew at Ganxi’s door.

Some entered Yan and Ying,
Some fled to Kuaiji’s ring.
Some razed Daliang’s pride,
Some stormed Linzi’s side.

Five Hegemons devoured all,
Seven States lashed with scornful maul.
Cruel Qin crushed the Six Lands,
Chu and Han clashed for command.

Heaven ends where the sun still gleams,
Earth fades where no bound remains.
Far and near, all is embraced,
Length and breadth, all is traced.

Fields in grids once stretched wide,
Armor piled in endless tide.
Then came roads to cleave the land,
Borders drawn with ruthless hand.

Weapons melted, sharp and keen,
Books and poems burned unseen.
Lords dismissed, prefects installed,
Trunk strengthened, branches maimed and galled.

Heavy troops garrisoned Shang,
The Great Wall choked the frontier’s song.
They boasted of their rock-like might,
A reign to last through endless night.

Yet with a flick of fate’s own hand,
They ceded land where they did stand.
Viewed men as ants in scornful gaze,
Spent wealth like sand in reckless haze.

E’pang Palace stood unfinished still,
The First Emperor’s cart reached Xi.
Before the tomb’s last stone was laid,
Chen She’s troops rose from Qi’s glade.

At Ba Shang, hearts ran deep,
At Hong Gate, tempers seethe.
Xianyang’s smoke rose to the sky,
Nanzheng’s flames burned on high.

Men and ghosts in chaos twined,
Wind and clouds in sorrow joined.
Xiang’s might, Liu’s strength yet vied,
Who would claim the deer denied?

Three Chapters’ law stood fast,
Five States’ troops followed at last.
Temple schemes weighed deep and wise,
Tent-bound plans won swift surprise.

At Guangwu, rage burned bright,
At Honggou, beasts fought in fright.
Xingyang mourned Ji Xin’s ruse,
Gaixia bid Yu Ji adieu.

Three Heroes’ talents then took flight,
A thousand years shone with new light.
Old rivers kept their storied grace,
New sun and moon lit heaven’s face.

Three Systems’ calendar aligned,
Chariots and script in one design.
No lands were sundered, far or wide,
The distant held in bonds of pride.

Yet royal kin vied for the throne,
Armies split to North and South alone.
Had no great ministers stood tall,
Who then could save the empire’s fall?

A century passed in fleeting sun,
Long wars shook the vaulted one.
Who knew the witchcraft’s dark design,
Would rise from Liu Qu’s tangled line?

The steward held the scales with care,
Bright and clear, he met despair.
Seeing peril, he gave his life,
No shame to the orphan’s strife.

Bandits mocked the orphan’s plight,
Acting like fox and raccoon in night.
Palace halls saw widows weep,
Throne rooms chased the infant deep.

Dragon wars—where did they lead?
Ice stood firm in time of need.
Though the dike may break and flood,
Who dares forget the dike’s true good?

Eastern Han saw dawn’s last glow,
At Kunyang, tiles flew in the blow.
Wang Mang’s ghost in sorrow sighed,
On Jiantai’s steps, corpses piled.

At Hao, they chased the high-nosed king,
At Xin’an, Red Eyebrows’ sting.
Fire’s virtue ruled once more,
Han’s court restored its ancient lore.

Dou and Deng rose from the hearth,
Yan and Liang grasped the hen’s worth.
What use were Jing’s labors vast,
When Dong’s deeds left all aghast?

Yellow River ran with scarce a flame,
The capital choked with thistle and bramble.
Long skies saw crows take flight,
White bones drew no pity’s light.

Moats cried out to be refilled,
No blood left for the sheep to yield.
A great house nears its fall,
No single beam can prop it all.

Mengde struck first with daring hand,
Zhongmou rode his father’s land.
Xuande’s ambition met no end,
Yet tears were all he could expend.

Western Jin prized empty talk,
Great plans hung on rank and mark.
Women held the reins of state,
Kin turned to plague and hate.

Two Lords bore frost and dew,
Five Hu defiled the sacred vessel true.
No Guan Yiwu stood in sight,
Men longed for Xian Huan’s might.

On broad roads, Qiang dust swarms,
In the Central Plains, Hu horses scream.
Dragon’s light pierces Ox and Dipper,
Sun’s shadow sheds like a rainbow’s molt.

Clearing grass, they came to Luo’s bend,
Reclaiming fields, they rushed to Jiang’s shore.
Two hundred and four years passed,
Then chariots raced abreast in stride.

Eastern Jin split its southern tail,
At times yielding sacred lingzhi.
Five times the mandate changed hands,
Till Chen at last flourished under Sui.

Nations shattered in autumn’s dusk,
Cities lay waste with spring’s lush weeds.
The Yangtze stretched empty to the eye,
Travelers’ tears soaked their robes.

Later Wei opened its northern gate,
Xiaowen nearly stitched peace’s seams.
Yet Heyin soon saw upheaval,
The realm split east and west.

Erzhu rose with Gao’s might,
Yuwen wiped out Northern Qi.
When Sui at last merged with Chen,
The four seas became mere hedges.

The Bian Canal drained public and private stores,
Liao campaigns left soldiers weary.
Every body knew weariness,
Every mouth sighed in lament.

Everywhere, eras bore new names,
All longed for chaos to depart.
The Central Plains lacked a lord,
Who could discern deer from elk?

In times hard as "one in a thousand,"
Heaven and man set their tryst.
When dragons soar, clouds gather,
When tigers stride, winds keen.

The empress wielded the sun’s power,
Her kin held sway in the palace’s depths.
Alas for Huan Yanfan,
Who spared Wu Sansi’s life.

Xiuling’s hills rang with song and dance,
Yuyang’s drums and war drums stirred.
Could peace be scorned so lightly?
Yet blame fell on one Yang Guifei.

Sword Pavilion scraped the sky,
Tong Pass leaked tigers’ forms.
Both capitals fell to ruin,
The nine ancestral temples toppled.

Joy’s peak brings sorrow’s fall,
Favor’s bond breeds harm’s betrayal.
Matters left no recourse,
Who could compare such woe?

From then on, regional lords thrived,
No subjects showed their sovereign reverence.
Relying on merit, they shunned court,
Citing bandit suppression as pretext.

Each amassed mighty troops,
Who pitied the court’s decline?
They extorted the throne’s indulgence,
Watching the altars’ fate.

Seizing lands without royal decree,
Plundering to fatten themselves.
The imperial carriage fled in disarray,
Escorts faltered in disarray.

A tail too large knows no easy sway,
A whip too long cannot command.
Long-nurtured treachery brings certain woe,
Raising tigers courts lasting harm.

The nation’s steps grew ever more frail,
The lord’s heart filled with vain regret.
When fortune comes, flowers bloom bright,
When power wanes, leaves scatter wide.

Ten clans divided the Central Plains,
Five houses took turns on the thoroughfare.
Vainly bright stars still gleamed,
Yet dawn in the east had not broken.

Hardly had they returned to Changlu Garrison,
When they were driven to Huli Marsh.
Jiangxiao’s troops took what they sought,
Xuanwu’s fire burned in folly.

Surrender at Zhongdu was blameworthy,
Death at Lecheng was laughable.
Taiyuan’s court saw morning entrants,
Liu Zi heard evening wails.

Affairs bore layer upon layer of strife,
Human hearts shifted with each turn.
Cold ashes hid embers within,
Rotten bones still claimed turtle’s fame.

Deceit and cunning thrived in shadows,
Hardship and sorrow clung to the self.
To guard against harm, skin and flesh heal fast,
Yet saving the heart’s core comes too late.

Disaster strikes before one turns,
Words wound like gnawing at the navel.
To rise is to fall,
To near collapse is to be propped up.

Dizzied, all men revile,
In peace, all the world yearns.
Only by curing grave illness
Can one be called a good physician.

Long-neglected fields lie barren,
Treacherous paths are hard to tread.
Without a true lord’s emergence,
How can deeds be done?

House and state spun to their limits,
Lord and minister met but rarely.
Heaven lent its hand,
And our Song thus laid its foundation.

Wise plans adapted to each circumstance,
Heroes raised their heads in submission.
To this day, over a hundred years on,
People know not war or weapons.

Success and failure must bow to fate,
Rise and fall hinge on the times.
Heaven’s mechanisms shift without cease,
Master strategists vary their plays.

Past events are but faded traces,
Yet earlier records may serve as guides.
Compared to the game of weiqi,
They differ not by a hair’s breadth.

Waxing and waning follow heaven’s turn,
Yin and yang keep to the Dao’s bounds.
Fortune and misfortune shift with men,
Movement and stillness pivot on the hinge.

Those who rush will stumble first,
Late bloomers wither last.
Better to forget both harm and gain,
Than to suffer their exchange.

To catch fish, one needs a fish trap,
To snare rabbits, a snare.
Yet clinging to the catch,
One becomes a sheep in tiger’s guise.

The Dao is vast—hear Laozi’s words,
Talent is rare—hear Confucius’s.
To grasp form is to awaken,
How can one fear delusion in the game?

Black and white cannot be sullied,
Life and death are not to be sighed over.
Striking the mark like a shattered target,
The blade meets no resistance.

Do not marvel at others’ scorn,
Nor fear cold eyes’ scrutiny.
Having mastered wondrous use,
Why fret over many paths?

Following the Dao, the Dao is gained,
Aligning with heaven, heaven does not oppose.
Exhausting principle to fulfill nature,
Speaking freely to dispel words.

Gazing outward, one knows simplicity,
Listening to echoes, one discerns the rare.
Great broth needs no seasoning,
Dark wine cannot be diluted.

Supreme soldiers cannot be attacked,
Clever calendars cannot be calculated.
Wise words cannot be spoken,
Fleeting chariots cannot be pursued.

Brothers excel in love,
Father and son in kindness.
The realm may also be entrusted,
But this move must not be hoarded.

*To Guo Wen of Gong County, Written in the First Year of Huangyou*
Soft winds on Jade Creek bear drunken steeds,
Flowers bind Stone Grotto, song-boats sway.
Though near enough to see, immortal and mortal part,
I cannot join your third-month wanderings.

*On Presenting My New Residence to Palace Attendant Liu Junyu*
South of Lǚdao Lane, bamboo paths stretch long,
Green willows shade waters that part and flow.
All the worthy have bought scenes of serene heart,
I alone dwell here to nurture autumn’s resolve.

Compared to Chen’s gate, I’ve overstepped,
Yet beside Yan’s alley, I may still fret.
Though winds and rains rage without cause,
I trust they cannot overwhelm Shen Yin’s reclusion.

*Sent to Prefect Han Zihua of the Three Cities*
Luoyang has been a capital
For over two thousand years.
Each step treads through maps and records,
Each glance spans past and present.

Northwest, palaces stand tall,
Southeast, mountains and rivers lean.
The Yi and Luo rivers mirror men’s clarity,
Before the gates, Song and Shaoshi loom cold.

The finest waters and bamboos lie
South of Lǚdao Lane.
Below stands the hermit’s chamber,
A single path leads to the wicker gate.

Weeds hide his dwelling,
Pigweed seasons his meals.
He serves his parents above, his wife and children below,
Thick or thin, as fate allows.

Though others cannot bear such cares,
He himself does not change his ease.

I perused histories and grasped the rise and fall of states,
Delved into classics to find their root and source.
A guest then said to me: "Why do you alone refuse to grasp the moment?
The pure court now employs talent,
The sagely lord seeks worthies in earnest.
Dao and virtue, benevolence and righteousness—
These are no mere empty words.
Merit and achievement prize timeliness;
Why not seek a fine office?
Above, you would eat the Son of Heaven’s salary,
Below, succor the remnant of the common folk.
On thoroughfares, you would raise grand mansions,
Beyond the city walls, broad fertile fields.
Crimson gates ablaze with gold and purple,
Green towers thick with pipes and strings.
In your outer stables, fat and noble steeds stand arrayed,
In your rear chambers, delicate beauties gather.
Entering, you sit in a lofty hall;
Exiting, you ride in a splendid carriage.
How resplendent your cap and sword,
How composed your bearing and ease.
You would discourse on the affairs of the realm,
In a vast assembly, your words would kindle bright mist.
None would dare lift their gaze to you,
All would hold their breath, awaiting your countenance.
This is what is called a true man—
His ambition may be seen and known.
Why then must you afflict yourself,
Your form and visage like a withered eel?
You trace the deeds of the ancients,
Gleaning from the volumes they left behind,
Yet before a single ditch of water you linger,
Enamored of a few stalks of bamboo.
This is what is called a common fellow—
What lofty integrity is there to aspire to?"

I dared reply to my guest:
"Affairs are not so easily explained.
It is not that I delight in ragged hemp,
Nor that my mouth loathes rare delicacies.
How could I not know the gourd’s worth,
Yet firmly decline to grasp the whip?
I fear only the sight of gaping jaws,
Dare I forget the adornment of garden and hill?
Deepest depths hold layered waves,
Loftiest heights hold layered peaks.
Tread them as level ground—
This is no hardship for men.
Poverty and low station are what men find bitter,
Wealth and honor are what men find shifting.
To dwell in them as though they were nothing—
This is truly what men find difficult.
Advancing, I follow my own Dao;
Retreating, I nurture my own wholeness.
Since I do not yet know how it would be in another’s place,
How could I not remain steadfast?
I dare say your counsel
Holds nothing I might adopt.

O, how splendid the jade!
Born in the hills of Jing.
Though He the Carver may have known its worth,
The state of Chu did not concur.
The Way’s rise and fall, its bending and stretching,
Advance and retreat, its timing and precedence—
If one does not follow this principle,
When the jade is shattered, whose fault is it?
If the Dao remains unfulfilled,
Its mandate lies with Heaven.

Of late, I wandered through the Three Cities,
Lingering still in hesitation.
The renowned ministers of this age
Deigned to extend me special courtesy.
Some, in the clear night, discoursed on the Dao;
Others, by the rear pond, set boats adrift.
For several evenings, gatherings of literature and wine—
Boundless was the joy.
Beyond the cold of the tenth month,
Before the myriad leaves under hoarfrost,
I returned to my walled dwelling,
To sleep drunk beneath the bamboo window in the bright sun.

I raise my gaze in thanks to the gentleman’s understanding,
And at his behest, compose this piece.

*Matching Rhymes with Zhang Yuanbo, Bureau Director, at the End of the Year in Jiayou 1*
At forty-six, I am old,
Ashamed to lack talent.
The last days of the year just turn auspicious,
The new spring again stirs the ashes.
Not only have I forgotten profit and salary,
But I have also cast off the body’s form.
White hairs already exceed half,
Time hurries on of its own accord.

*Thanking Prefect Wang Mixue of Yanzhou for His Gift of Wine*
A grand prefecture brings new wine,
A dense small garden blooms with fine flowers.
On what day shall I drink beneath the crimson trees,
Startled to find it ill-suited to a rustic’s home?

*Encountering Spring in the Small Garden*
A hundred flowers hide in the small reclusive garden,
Each following red and purple to sprout new buds.
The Lord of the East lends the power of yang’s warmth—
No less than the rich and noble houses of dukes and marquises.

*Matching Rhymes with the Two Young Ministers Zhang on White Chrysanthemums*
Pale and pure, dawn congeals frost,
Fitting to follow autumn’s close.
Knowing myself to be pure and white,
Who pities my solitary fragrance?
Not that jade lacks luster,
But rather startled that snow bears scent.
White blossoms float in jade liquid,
One hue blends with the jeweled cups.

*On the Birth of My Son in Jiayou 2*
Now at forty-five years of age,
I have only just become a father.
Nurturing and teaching are truly my duty,
But whether you live long or die young, whether wise or foolish, rests with you.
If I live to seventy,
I will see you at twenty-five before my eyes.
I wish for you to become a great worthy—
I know not whether Heaven will consent.

*Four Idle Chants*
1.
All my life like an official,
I have followed my lot amid wind and waves.
What is lost knows no limit,
What is gained—how much can it be?
Since I have strayed from concerns of governing the world,
I may yet preserve Heaven’s harmony.
When there is wine in the cup,
I drink and sing again.

2.
At forty-five, I have just begun to know the path of fate.
Who would have thought Heaven would not cut me off,
That I would become a father only now?
I set aside zither and books,
Dare I hope for a grand household?
All things are thus—
Why trouble myself with needless worry and fear?

3.
Living in Luoyang for eight or nine years,
My heart has been troubled by only two or three things.
We meet, each with white hair,
Sitting together, much is said in pure discourse.
Human affairs have long been silently settled,
Worldly sentiments I have long known.
When wine is poured, do not press me—
Let me slowly reach the point of tipsy delight.

4.
I wish for the joy of a single gourd,
Yet I have not even two *qing* of fields.
My sincere devotion has not pierced the sun,
But white hairs already adorn my temples.
The clouds’ mood grows colder and more distant,
The pine’s heart grows older and firmer.
In recent years, I have grown ever more indolent,
Often recalling the old forests and springs.

*Matching Rhymes with Young Minister Zhang on Returning to Heyang*
Once I was a guest in the springtime of youth,
Today I return as a hoary old man.
Today and that day are already a lifetime apart—
How much rise and fall lies within?

*Eight Poems on Tall Bamboos*
1.
Over a hundred stalks of tall bamboo,
I know they were surely grown for me.
Suddenly, I find something gained,
Often I stroll idly around them.
I trust myself, yet perhaps I have not attained;
I know myself, yet perhaps I am not clear.
Secretly comparing myself to the ancients,
I cannot help but feel shame.

2.
Tall bamboos stand by the clear ditch,
The pavilion is small, yet also secluded.
Though the season belongs to summer,
The wind and dew already foretell autumn’s chill.
Moonlight emerges from the forest,
The sound of springs flows beneath the steps.
Who knows the feeling of this night?
Vast and distant, it cannot be gathered in.

3.
Tall bamboos are already lovely,
How much more so beneath the drooping willows?
The recluse has no carriages or caps of office,
Yet attaining this, he may well marvel.
Since I cannot stoop even an inch,
Why should I seal my sack in apology?
If Jia Yi had known this,
His bitter weeping would have ceased.

4.
Tall bamboos lean together in green,
Naturally emitting their lingering purity.
From time to time, a gentle breeze comes,
Ten thousand leaves sound as one.
The Dao, though sullied, attains the principle of leveling,
Things, though hollow, contain distant feeling.
Walking alongside my idle companion,
How clear and peaceful our thoughts.

5.
Tall bamboos like green banners,
Emerald willows like low-hanging canopies.

The recluse has his couch and carriage,
Day and night he faces them.
In the stillness of his dwelling, he feels his spirit open,
In the leisure of the scene, he delights in true communion.
Rather than lose my true nature,
How much better to follow what I love.
Tall bamboos mingle with towering parasols,
Startling the first signs of autumn’s season.
The evening cool is especially delightful,
Old scrolls are fit to unfurl.
Pavilions and ponds are wrapped in gentle breezes,
Gardens and groves linger in the late light.
If life holds such joy,
Why quibble over trifles?

Tall bamboos rise some ten feet high,
Still above the loftiest blossoms.
The brushwood gate stays shut by day,
Green and emerald face each other all the while.
Not only is the body at ease,
The heart, too, is level and vast.
To know the joy of idleness,
Is itself to rival lords and ministers.

Tall bamboos stay green through winter,
Only in the fourth month do they shed their leaves.
Sprouting like the stilling of weapons,
Shedding sheaths like the doffing of armor.
Their slender stillness is truly lovely,
Circling them, one loses count of the turns.
Alas, the common grasses and trees,
Waste their time beneath hoe and spade.

*End of the First Scroll of Yichuan’s Jirang Collection*
*Beginning of the Second Scroll of Yichuan’s Jirang Collection*
*Yichuan Shao Yong Yaofu*

**Drinking in Autumn at the Song Garden in Zhengzhou, Presented to Zhou Zhengshu, Registrar of Guancheng**

Two capitals stand four hundred li apart,
Between them lies a famed garden, the Song family’s domain.
Ancient trees pierce the sky like swords and halberds,
Long vines trail to earth like coiling dragons and serpents.
I arrive on a day late in autumn’s close,
You set the feast before me beneath twilight blossoms.
The rain has passed, the bamboo pavilion still damp as we part,
At dawn’s return, crows rise from the road home.

---

**Revisiting Gongcheng and Baiyuan’s Old Residence on the Double Ninth**

In my homeland, the festive day arrives,
Yet climbing high, I find only sorrow.
Mountains and rivers lie beyond a dream,
Wind and moon span a decade’s span.
White hairs now streak my temples,
Yellow chrysanthemums twine the old fence.
My countrymen must laugh at me—
A man who brocades his robes, yet fails as a son.

---

**Passing Through Shaan in the Third Year of Jiayou**

What radiance did my ancestor’s Dao possess?
The *Two Nans* divided, bestowing one region.
He aided Zhou as Grand Protector,
Enfeoffed in Shaan to assist King Cheng.
Years stretch distant, boundless,
Mountains and rivers grow faint, vast.
Though his descendant proves unworthy,
Still I recall the sweet pear tree.

---

**Inscribed on the Yellow River**

Who says its benefits outweigh its harms?
I say its turbid flow has never cleared.
From Kunlun in the west to the sea in the east,
How many voices cry out in grievance?

---

**Passing Through Tong Pass**

Barriers tighten in times of disorder,
Yet stand idle in peace.
Though mountains and rivers form natural defenses,
Can virtue and power ever be contested?
Failing to fathom the thousand-in-one truth,
Its "hundred-and-two" fame rings hollow.
Distant lands long since ceased to be foreign—
Why still rely on the crowing of roosters?

---

**Inscribed on Mount Hua**

Among the Five Peaks within the realm,
The state reveres their seasonal rites.
Mount Hua stands as one,
A mighty guardian of the western frontier.
The Tang called it the Golden Heavenly King,
Now it is sealed as the Compliant Sage Emperor.
Alas! Such a spirit,
Usurps the place of heaven and earth.

---

**Staying at the Huaqing Palace**

In the sixth year of Tianbao’s reign,
A palace was built by the warm springs.
The Radiant Emperor and his consort
Came here yearly thereafter.
Purple pavilions wrapped in cool winds,
Towering peaks beneath the bright moon.
Yet why do the twin stone urns
Still trickle fragrant streams?

---

**Ascending the Chaoyuan Pavilion**

Embroidered Ridge rises in layered peaks,
Nineteen winding turns climb steep and high.
After a light rain, all is mist,
Emerging faintly from the clouds.
The golden carriage’s past is distant,
The jade statue’s traces remain, broken.
Even now, by the Wei River’s banks,
The sight of Chang’an endures.

---

**Composed on the Road to Chang’an**

Why weep on the road to Chang’an?
The ancients traveled it, as do men today.
Unheeding of cold or heat, dawn or dusk,
Wheels and hooves clatter endlessly.
This land, too, knows hardship—
Rain turns to mud, wind to dust.
Mud and dust shift without number,
In great storms, none dare venture out.

---

**Inscribed at the Temple of the Marquis of Liu**

Toppling Xiang, enthroning Liu—easy as turning a hand,
Ending Qin, restoring Han—like resetting a board.
He rolled and unfurled the realm with a day’s planning,
Forged his heart’s source in grain-abstaining seclusion.
All Yellow Stone’s teachings were put to use,
Yet what need had he for Red Pine’s company?
For talent and achievement like his,
None through the ages can compare.

---

**Ten Poems Inscribed at the Temple of the Marquis of Huaiyin**

**I**
A single man’s rebellion deserved execution,
The slaughter of three clans seemed merciless.
The Han’s Dao then was half-hegemonic,
Xiao He’s kingly counsel lacked true honor.
To establish such great merit was no folly,
Yet craving a princedom reeked of greed.
Four hundred years of Han’s flame were secured,
Only to be slain once peace was won.

**II**
Having found a true lord in life,
Why feign kingship to accomplish deeds?
Who says misfortune began here?
One should not turn to resent the High Emperor.

**III**
For a time, Han Xin was a loyal hound,
For ages, Xiao He a hegemonic minister.
Both transgressed the bounds of proper conduct,
Yet their crimes fell short of such a man.

**IV**
Han Xin never rebelled against Liu,
Yet Xiao He’s suspicions of Han ran deep.
Had he heeded Kuai Tong’s words then,
The High Ancestor’s fame might have been undone.

**V**
Though endowed with talent and wisdom,
His survival and retreat were not sincere.
The Five Lakes’ misty waves remain,
Yet none follow Fan Li’s footsteps.

**VI**
Had Han Xin not existed, Xiang could not have been destroyed,
Without Xiao He, Han could not have been restrained.
Know this: the realm cannot be ruled single-handedly—
Without the High Ancestor, Xiao would have struggled.

**VII**
The Han’s foundation was laid by debating merit,
Five men of other surnames were enfeoffed as kings.
None matched the might of Huaiyin,
Who dared to root out the Qin’s resurgence.

**VIII**
Han Xin relied on merit, foresaw little,
The Han emperor owed him virtue yet clung to power.
To imprison and execute him was no great feat,
Yet such cruelty was needless.

**IX**
If glory is seized violently, so too is humiliation,
After much joy, sorrow must follow.
Had he relinquished his princedom after success,
He would have remained a lord for generations.

---

**Inscribed on the Prefectural Tower of Fengzhou**

Willows trail green sashes,
Their leaves stirred like flying canopies.
The perilous tower’s thoughts are endless,
All day I face it in idle repose.
Crows depart, the grove empties,
Clouds drift, the mountains remain unobstructed.
My feelings follow the paired swallows home,
My intent meets the lone goose’s flight.
The evening bugle sounds, now faint, now clear,
Layered cliffs shift between light and shadow.
The setting sun hangs in sparse crimson,
Distant waters ripple with gentle currents.
The frontier’s gaze is thick with misty peaks,
The capital lies as if beyond the heavens.
Why does my heart, long a stranger,
Lean eastward, clinging to the railing in anguish?

---

**From Fengzhou Back to the Qinchuan Post Station, Sent to Vice-Prefects Xue and Yao**

Ninety days of joyous gathering,
Now turned to a dream at a glance.
The bright moon and clear river—
Once more, the eastern pavilion is beyond sharing.

---

**To Zhang Yuanbo of the Western Terrace, Who Sent a Poem in the Snow**

Snowflakes in Luoyang, large as hands,
No fire in the brazier, no wine in the cup.
At dawn’s first light, a knock at the door—
A poem from the Western Terrace, delivered through the snow.

---

**Seeing Off Zhang, Registrar of Yishi**

A thousand *li* of official travel in this world,
Two layers of parting from parents at home.
Remember now: the roc’s flight begins here,
The road ahead is perilous—seek favor wisely.

---

**New Year’s Chant, Fifth Year of Jiayou**

Qu Yuan knew his errors with age,
Confucius read the *Yi* through the years.
Human feelings end here,
But heaven’s intent is not in vain.
Affairs weary me more each day,
Yet my longing for the mountains grows ever firmer.
Who shares this resolve?
To grow old together by the Yi River.

---

**Five Spring Excursions**

**I**
Plum blossoms on the Five Ridges welcome the year’s end,
In the third river valley, the first month brings cold plum’s praise.
Ten thousand *li* apart, yet a month ahead—
Now I know spring’s colors come from the south.

**From the Five Spring Excursions (continued)**

**II**
Whose masterful tune flows from the Qiang flute?
All day, its pure fragrance lingers in the winecup.
I imagine the wanderer far from home,
Standing here too, lost in lingering steps.

**III**
The spring hues of Luoyang stretch boundless,
And east of the city, they grow yet more splendid.
A gentle breeze sways a thousand willows,
Soft ripples stir half the river’s blossoms.
In misty clarity, kingfishers glide over level banks;
In warming sun, mandarin ducks descend on shallow sands.
Long have I not seen the sovereign’s western journey—
Now travelers only sigh at their temples’ silvered frost.

**IV**
The second month blooms in full radiance,
Yet the imperial banners have not come—spring drifts unmoored.
Beneath green willows, I wander through every fragrant path;
Amid red apricots, I return half-drunk with wine.
A few fallen petals lure butterflies in chase;
A slanting sunbeam hears the orioles’ song.
With clear cups and wine, my aged parents rejoice—
Still, I may don motley robes and play before their steps.

**V**
The finest festival under heaven is Cold Food Day,
And the world’s most famed gardens lie in Luoyang.
Jingu’s warmth blends with palace halls’ emerald,
Bronze Camels gleam beneath silken radiance.
By the bridge, willows trail their slender threads to earth;
Beyond the flowers, swings rise halfway up the walls.
White horses tread lightly on grass like shears—
For over a decade, I’ve roamed here in delight.

**VI**
In the third month, peonies burst into full bloom,
And drumbeats rise wherever pavilions stand.
Laughing girls in carriages exchange banter;
Below the towers, crowds drift by at leisure.
Rippling jade-green waves sway the curtains,
While Shangyang’s floral breath fills the wine vessels.
This is the climate the Western Capital favors—
Yet now, who mourns the barren trees’ vain allure?

---

**Awakening in the Bamboo Courtyard**
Awakening in the bamboo courtyard, I recline on the armrest,
As the languid summer day stretches long.
Orioles just lead their fledglings, teaching tender tongues;
Apricots hang heavy, donning their pale gold.
Raindrops break the quiet dream in fragments,
While wind stirs distant thoughts into flight.
A small channel plays with water in dense green shade—
Looking back, I realize days have passed once more.

---

**Six Autumn Excursions**

**I**
In the seventh month, lotuses blaze in full bloom,
And I linger near the southeastern garden.
Sometimes the wind brushes the pond’s heart,
Bearing boundless fragrance from the water’s surface.
Mist-painted streams run deep—I wander lost;
The evening lake at Dongting has not yet brought me home.
Then, a sudden shower whispers past—
And fresh coolness arrives with my wine.

**II**
Before autumn, the vast clarity stirs in secret,
And Luoyi now knows the season’s balance.
Courtyards turn cool—people rejoice together;
Gardens, washed by rain, grow even clearer.
Turning the boat on Yi River, the breeze drifts gently;
Reining the horse at Tianjin Bridge, the moon shines bright.
The sages’ thoughts divide like Gao and Kui—
Let poetry and wine celebrate this peaceful age.

**III**
The eighth month’s light is not yet bleak,
And pine pavilions, bamboo groves suit it best.
Just as day and night find their pause,
The balance of heat and chill arrives in due time.
The bright moon enters my heart as if with intent;
A kindly breeze meets my face as if it knows me.
Idle folk sing and chant in contentment,
Unconcerned whether the court heeds their verse.

**IV**
My home lies south of the city by water and bamboo,
And autumn’s joys have never failed me.
I love the mild chill of this season,
While half-drunk, time slips by unnoticed.
Trusting my horse on the imperial avenue after light rain,
Leaning on the railing of a monk’s pavilion at dusk.
For ten years, I’ve chased every fair scene—
Now, I ask the wind to whiten my temples.

**V**
Though the ninth month’s splendor wanes,
The Central Plain’s beauty is not yet spent.
My eyes take in autumn’s hues for a thousand *li*;
My hands hold two or three branches of yellow blossoms.
Fine wine easily dissolves idle years,
But bronze mirrors need not reflect my aging face.
If I must make others believe,
Who would know the sincerity I’ve poured out?

**VI**
In frosty skies, endless thoughts arise—
I cannot bear the tower’s height, forcing my gaze.
Distant mountains fill my sight in vain;
The autumn palace stands empty and aloof.
Clouds stretch over far peaks, a thousand *xun* straight;
Sunset scatters crimson threads through the sky.
Countless sorrows cannot be spoken—
With whom can I open my heart to the west wind?

---

**An Autumn Day’s Occurrences**
Crows clamor in the daytime woods—
For whom do they toil so bitterly?
Crickets chirp through the nighttime courtyard—
For whom do they labor so ceaselessly?
Alas, how brief their lives,
Yet they never rest.
I ask: how long is this?
The two who forget speech and silence.

---

**On the Road to Mount Shang**
Ten lodgings bring me to Shangyan,
Though distant, the journey is not harsh.
East and west, I trace the Luo River,
Gazing at Qin’s mountains within and beyond.
Beyond the mist and rosy clouds, I am free—
Yet my heart remains with my parents.
The courtyard is not far—
I can set a date for my return.

---

**Matching Magistrate Zhang Zihou’s Early Plum Blossoms**
Only the Queen Mother of the West could craft such lightness,
Snipping the sky’s edge into scattered white clouds.
Endless pure fragrance and pristine beauty—
Even the fairest offerings at the winecup yield to you.

Plums shade the spring stream, water winds around the hills,
Plum blossoms dazzle as the brook murmurs.
In Nanzheng, the earth is warm, and they bloom early—
By the start of spring, they’ve already bloomed many times.

A thousand flowers vie in succession,
But if one speaks of lofty standards, only the plum remains.
I understand your intent in binding them—
You cherish their pure threads, letting them bloom first.

Frost upholds their noble bearing, lifting it high;
Wind carries their cold fragrance far and wide.
The prefect is moved, the traveler is touched—
Golden cups are drained in celebration.

---

**On the Road in Shang Mountains**
Fading embers, dim lamp, the night deepens,
As I silently ponder past affairs, wrapped in a cold quilt.
A frosty sky, a bright moon—though a thousand *li* apart,
It cannot compare to the inch of heart grieving for the times.

---

**Matching Prefect Song of Shang’s Early Plum Blossoms**
South of the mountains, the land is as warm as Lingnan,
And in the twelfth month, plum blossoms have already bloomed for days.
Unbending, they defy the common flowers’ ways,
Standing apart from the crowd, claiming spring’s first light.

From the horn, their notes drift, colder than bone;
From the flute, their melody enters the spirit.
A fair brow’s makeup fades, clinging to pale powder;
A painted beam’s warmth stirs light dust.
The magistrate cherishes their beauty, offering them to the prefect;
The prefect shares their fragrance even with rustics.
Holding a few branches, I inhale deeply—
How can I bear to let fine wine touch my lips?

---

**Matching a Friend’s Poem on Freeing the Heart**
Though one may not foresee the future,
Wealth and rank, fame and achievement—are they truly won by force?
To cleanse the heart, nothing serves like wine;
To roam in ease, nothing compares to poetry.
Here, amidst water, bamboo, clouds, and mountains,
How can I betray the seasons of wind, flowers, snow, and moon?
A man’s grand ambitions lie in action or retreat—
When, if not now, should I unknit my brows and smile?

---

**Matching Prefect of Shang’s Poem on Ascending the Tower to View Snow**

**Matching Prefect of Shang’s Poem on Ascending the Tower to View Snow**

At the western tower, the snow sharpens my gaze,
My body, step by step, seems encased in crystal.
A thousand flakes, ten thousand flakes, artfully adorn the earth,
Half-dancing, half-flying, they slant against the pillars.
Their form like jade shards—fragile yet scattered,
Their substance like willow catkins—even lighter still.
Who says the wanderer at the world’s edge is bound?
With wine before us, our hearts hold twofold feeling.

---

**Matching Prefect of Shang’s Poem on the Western Tower After Snow Clears**

The great snow first clears as the sun dips low,
Why spare the climb? I ascend the high tower again.
A few peaks stand like swords unsheathed,
A single stream winds in fresh threads of ice.
The Kunlun’s heights, transplanted, are all jade,
The Milky Way, fallen, turns to silver.
The reclusive man regrets his lack of fine lines—
Yet the scene has never failed to favor men.

---

**Matching Prefect of Shang’s Poem on Ascending the Tower as Snow Melts**

The last snow melts, the ice breaks open,
The wind and light gradually embrace the tower.
The traveler has not yet reached the sun’s edge,
But spring’s hues descend once more from heaven.
Moreover, the cup never lacks wine—
How can the ridge bear to be without plum blossoms?
Were it not for the prefect’s golden bond of friendship,
Who would deign to pour their heart out for this unworthy one?

---

**Matching Prefect of Shang’s Poem on Moonlit Snow After Clearing**

Snow fills the mountains, frost fills the courtyard,
A cold radiance, the moon grinds a single wheel of light.
The wanderer’s heart holds little joy in fleeting time,
Yet the night’s brilliance, vast and white, is all in vain.
Bamboo near the curtains scatters broken shadows,
Wind through the pavilions carries lingering clarity.
I regret having no fine verses to repay the splendid scene—
Alone and forlorn, even dreams refuse to come.

---

**Matching Prefect of Shang’s Poem on Ascending the Tower After Snow Clears**

A hundred-foot tower stands in the wake of light snow,
At dusk, the idle gaze grows piercingly clear.
Mountains stretch through evening mist, rising then falling,
Water parts the sparse woods, faint yet bright.
At the sky’s edge, sunset glows in a thousand strands,
The wind carries the last notes of a distant horn.
This moment, this scene—truly fit for a painting,
Yet I fear the painter’s brush lacks such precision.

---

**New Year’s Eve on the Road**

**New Year’s Eve on the Road**

Countless moments stretch to the coming year—
Pour the fragrant wine again, let it flow.
The Dipper’s handle points to *chǒu*, the dark waning,
The year’s arrow strikes man, and spring returns once more.
No need for worldly cares to obstruct this hour,
For my heart’s tangle has long turned to ash.
Fleeting fame lies beyond the floating clouds—
Swift as a breath, time presses on.

---

**Matching Prefect of Shang’s Poyn on the Auspicious Year of Jiayou (1061)**

A weary frame in travel meets the new year,
Moved by a lifetime’s hair, so quick to fade.
The cup drained, my breast remains desolate,
Joy spent, my spirit sinks into ennui.
Gazing toward immortals, wind and moon shine clear,
Green blinds frame the night, so long and slow.
Facing this lifeless form, mere clay and wood,
I cannot but feel my soul dissolve.

---

**Matching Wang, Attendant of the Palace Secretariat, on Ascending the Prefecture Tower to View the Mountains**

Four worthies once lingered here,
A thousand years on, men still blush with shame.
A man of renown finds the world hard to escape,
While one with nothing in his breast idles in the hills.
Affairs, seen through rise and fall of ages,
Reveal the Dao in ruler and minister’s advance and retreat.
If you harbor rare talent, rare use will find you—
If not, you must bear a lifetime’s leisure.

---

**Four Poems on the Temple of the Four White-Haired Ones**

1.
Mighty Qin lost control, blood flowed wide,
The realm sought a lord, yet none could be found.
Just when heroes locked horns in contest,
Who knew into whose hands the deer would fall?

2.
The true man of Bashang had already soared,
The four looked at one another, speechless.
Xu said, “Heaven’s mandate has its course—
Better to follow the tracks of Chao and Xu.”

3.
The Han emperor’s pride could not bend them,
Only the crown prince’s humble words brought them forth.
Though aged, they still achieved great deeds—
To this day, their lofty virtue shines like sun and stars.

4.
Tian Heng fled to the sea and won renown,
Shang came to Chang’an, strong for a hundred *li*.
Only when the four were made to perfect their integrity
Did one know Gaozu was a true king.

---

**Thanking Prefect of Shang, Song Langzhong, for Sending a Household Register for Mount Tianzhu, Following the Original Rhyme**

A talisman from Shang flew here, fresh and new,
Fulfilling a bond beyond this life’s measure.
Most joyous, the purple lingzhi first in hand—
Tianzhu in the southwest stands neighbor to heaven.

My first intent was to serve at the emperor’s side,
But spring’s Dipper turned in the capital’s circuit.
Now to dwell beneath Tianzhu’s peak,
No fear of night travelers ahead.

I do not present life and death to the august throne,
But embrace mist and haze, learning reclusion.
Much thanks to Magistrate Shi for leaving the right seat vacant,
To welcome once more a mountain man to Tianzhu.

A cluster of mist and haze locks the drifting clouds,
Tianzhu’s lone height is fine for a true man’s perch.
From now on, I make plans for a western return—
Why seek further fords among men?

---

**Four Poems on Mount Tianzhu**

1.
Long have I roamed with the deer, achieving nothing,
Yet deep is the grace of Magistrate Shi, who enrolled me.
Through ten thousand ages, name and surname endure—
Bound to Tianzhu’s peaks, they too shall remain.

2.
No success, I’ve run with the herd of deer,
But your kindness runs deep, Magistrate, in granting me a register.
Through all time, name and fame persist—
Let them abide with Tianzhu’s towering summits.

---

**To Prefect of Shang, Song Langzhong**

Returning first to Luoyang, endless affairs await,
A man of leisure, my place seems at odds.
Now, at last, I feel ease and freedom,
Like the carefree days on Shang’s slopes.

---

**Awakening from a Nap in the Small Garden**

Beyond the gate, it seems a deep mountain,
Nature’s truth, truly, can be regained.
Beyond the rush of caps and carriages,
Sun and moon pass in their course.
The garden’s waters flow with life,
Grasses and trees idle in the windless air.
Spring birds break my secluded dream,
Chattering on the branches, soft and sweet.

---

**Three Poems on Wandering the Mountains**

1.
Long confined in the city’s gates, my heart grows weary,
The moment I step beyond, my eyes brighten.
I’ve seen all the sights of Dragon Gate on the Yi,
Listened to the last echoes of the Luo’s waters at Nüji.
At Mount Tai’s shrine, the red sun rises,
From Heaven’s Altar, white clouds arise.
This body is already pledged to true companions—
Not for trifles will I weigh gain or loss.

2.
Spring’s end is the perfect time for climbing,
Human feeling and heaven’s mood both harmonize.
Boating on the Yi as the wind turns at night,
Fishing at the stream’s gate as the moon rises.
My carefree spirit soars, poetry runs wild,
My ailing frame relies on wine to sustain.
Few are the days of this floating life—
How can I bear to furrow my brow between them?

3.
Joy is pursued, sorrow shunned—
A true man knows fate.
Minor deeds and fame are hard to secure,
But clouds and mountains bring me delight.
Fear not the distant mist and haze,
While strength remains, ride it.
Beyond enough, what joy is there?
Wealth and honor, once past, bring only grief.

---

**Two-Colored Peach Blossoms**

Rouge and powder both enhance their beauty,
A dazzling charm to overthrow city and state.
I suspect they are twin sisters from the Jewel Bud Realm,
Both willing at once to wed the spring wind.

---

**Composing Poems While Climbing Mountains and Facing Waters**

Mountains have towering peaks, waters have waves,
Neither sandals nor boats can contain them.
Not that wisdom and kindness lack joy in this,
But few who climb and face them shirk the toil.
Words of taste know only sweet meats and roasts,
Who speaks truly can recognize jade and jasper?
I lament my failure to exhaust human talent—
Ever resenting how highly men judge today.

---

**Two Poems Thanking Grand Chancellor Fu for Summoning Me to Office**

1.
Your summons, much thanks, was not forgotten,
Thinking my breast held something to offer.
If I advanced, how could I bear official duties?
At leisure, why take a name in vain?
The messenger said, “If you do not wish to serve,
You may come and be granted a title of ease.”
I wish to be like Chao and Xu, calling myself a subject,
Content to grow old in the age of Yao and Shun.

2.
My eyes are full of worthy men in court ranks,
A sick man has no way to bind the realm’s fate.
I wish to fulfill my plan to grow old in leisure,
But heaven’s intent remains unknown.
How many layers of carriages and caps repay the body’s worth?
Better to have clouds and mountains fill my gaze.
Fine scenes never fail to inspire verse,
But my bold heart has all dissolved away.
The swan and wild goose know the joys of rivers and lakes—
Why trouble with petty nets and snares?

---

**Responding to a Question on the Teachings of Names**

Since the world’s beginning, teachings have spread,
Among them, the bold are called true Confucians.
Cultivating the self has its Dao—those who awaken first,
What age has lacked men who grasped its mysteries?
Radiant as cinnabar and ink, the meaning of classics and history,
Bright as sun and moon, the sage’s path.
Though I, a humble student, have long immersed myself,
How dare I slander the world’s heroic talent?

---

**Seeing Off Academician Wang Bochu on His Mission to the Northern Capital**

A man’s resolve is set when the coffin closes,
His grand designs weigh heavily.
The road ahead cannot be without nostalgia,
But in office, how can he bear to forget his feelings?
In leisure, speech must be precise,
Planning ahead ensures peace.
Yet the road ahead cannot be without nostalgia,
And in office, how can he bear to forget his feelings?

Who says there is no supreme strategy for governing the frontier?
To strike at the enemy’s plans—no troops are needed.

*End of Volume Two of Yi Chuan’s "Striking the Earth"*
*Yi Chuan Shao Yong Yaofu*

**Congratulating a Retiring Official**

Common sentiment mostly delights in holding office,
Yet how could a man of understanding ever be bound?
Surrendering the seal was never for scant salary,
Nor was hanging up the cap for advanced age.
Through things’ nature, he grasped the principles of rise and fall,
And so awakened to Heaven’s power in acceptance and rejection.
Fitting to release his cares amid pure scenes—
In his homeland, fine forests and springs await.

**Early Autumn**

Summer departs, yet heat lingers still,
After rain, coolness at last arrives.
Before the bridge, waters have long flowed,
Beyond the sky, thunder yet startles.
A curved armrest hides in quietude,
The crossbar gate opens in leisure.
All bold ambitions are now spent—
Why trouble the heart further?

**Written in Passing**

Laughable, yet also pitiable—
What, after all, is human life?
All ten thousand affairs
Should be gathered into a single song.
Worldly affairs shift faster than a palm’s turn,
Years pass swifter than rushing waves.
In stillness, the true qi’s savor—
What is gained is beyond measure.

**Injured Foot**

Misfortune comes from the unexpected,
Yet the harm was never deep.
Self-betraying the principles of nurturing life,
It brought parents sorrowful concern.
Suddenly, walking became difficult,
By chance, climbing was hindered.
A month passed before I could go out—
Hard to forget the admonition of *Lezheng*.

**Leisurely Stroll**

The garden lies desolate,
I chant verses circling the marsh’s edge.
Wind startles after the first autumn sacrifice,
Leaves fall before the frost arrives.
Withered grass frames the slanting sun,
Evening clouds prop up the distant sky.
When shall I perceive the true image?
Only by entering wordlessness.

**Morning Rising**

Mountains high, waters deep—
No remedy for the present.
Earth holds the affairs of a moment,
Heaven opens the heart of eternity.
Light mist veils the morning pavilion,
Fine rain scatters through green woods.
Though this scene is plain,
Where in the world can it be found?

**Moonlit Night**

Rain clears, the wind is fine,
Autumn’s touch, the sky not yet cold.
I move my bed beneath the steps,
Watching the moon rise from the forest’s edge.
Wine is here to share,
But no guests to join the joy.
When later I meet a true friend,
This scene will be hard to seek again.

**Basin Pond**

Three or five small lotuses,
A basin holds little water.
Though not a vast marsh,
Still, tiny waves arise.
Roughly evoking rivers and lakes,
Yet no mandarin ducks or egrets pass.
The recluse’s delight is hard to suppress—
Often pacing, drunk and chanting.

**Two Poems on Roaming the Mountains**

The Luo River has many fine peaks,
The Yi River many fine bamboos.
Each has its season for wandering,
Though frequent, the eye never tires.
Seeking purity is no breach of integrity,
Exploring the secluded is no disgrace.
Deer do no harm to men—
The heart harms no deer.

The Two Peaks have many fine summits,
The Three Rivers many fine clouds.
Gazing without weariness,
Harmonious qi subtly nurtures the spirit.
If a single thought stirs restlessly,
Ten thousand affairs follow in turmoil.
People say the value of idleness is great—
Become a man of idleness.

**Composed on the Road to Longmen**

The principles of things and human nature are clear,
Why fret over one’s life?
Expansion and contraction lie in my calculations,
Use and rejection follow the times, without fixed names.
All around, clouds and mountains are joy,
Not a speck of glory or disgrace need startle.
The gates of nobles are said to be deep as the sea—
For thirty years, I’ve strolled past with arms swinging.

**On Fame and Gain**

Fame and gain, in the end, are no joy,
Wind and waves ultimately offer little peace.
Slightly near to good repute—take little,
Only when close to pure delight—seek more.
Good repute, though abundant, must bring trouble,
Pure delight, though ample, brings no sorrow.
Does the world know this truth?
Yet in endless succession, they follow the overturned tracks.

**Thirty Years’ Chant**

Thirty years span a generation,
Within them, much to laugh and sigh over.
Heaven’s giving and taking never cease,
Human right and wrong never end.
Fortunate mandarin ducks—heads turn white early,
The cuckoo’s cry—blood flows first.
Know this: talent is the harm,
Yet when talent is gone, one still frets.

**Responding to a Man’s Unrestrained Words**

Long unseen—how have you fared?
New poems emerge with laughter and words anew.
Through things’ principles, awakening deepens temperament’s calm,
After grasping Heaven’s heart, emotions grow distant.
You’ve fully attained Meng’s joy—boundless,
Yet to know Qu’s non-self—still in excess.
Mostly, empty fame is as you say—
This body willingly ages among woodcutters and fishermen.

**First Outing to the Luo River, Leaving Through Houzai Gate**

First stepping beyond the capital’s gate,
West and south, toward the Luo’s corner.
Mountains and rivers open distant thoughts,
Heaven and earth hang in both eyes.
Villages at dusk amid mulberries and elms,
Farmers’ fields of millet and sorghum in autumn.
The people have such joy—
Why wait for enfeoffment?

**Staying at Yanqiu Village**

Driving the carriage into the Luo River valley,
Dismounting to play by the flying spring.
Suddenly, the joy of clouds and mountains,
Utterly free of court and market clamor.
Not only does it delight the heart and will,
It naturally forgets the body’s words.
Asking friends in the dust—
Who has grasped it first?

**Staying at the West Temple of Shou’an**

Fine scenes truly move the heart,
Directly refreshing flesh and bone.
For the many fine vistas,
Especially glad to be near the capital.
Bamboo hues blend with mountain hues,
Pine sounds mingle with water sounds.
How could I refuse to love it all day?
I set my mat beside the empty pillars.

**Two Poems Crossing Yongji Bridge**

Behind the mountains, a brocade screen unfolds,
The river bends by Yongji’s turn.
Fields level as a palm,
Mulberries and oaks large as locust trees.
The slanting sun shoots through the rainbow’s departure,
Low clouds bring approaching rain.
Endlessly, I waste this pure scene—
Always ashamed of my lack of talent.

A stream, a creek—each a gate,
Creek gates where clouds gather again.
Rare birds warble in tall trees,
Secluded deer dart through wild brambles.
Rain trails across the flat land,
Rice paddies prop up distant villages.
The high city is half ruined—
Matters of rise and fall—best not discussed.

**Composed Upon Reaching Fuchang County**

How many love pure scenes?
Those who do must seek them far.
Only when near Han Peak,
Does one see the Luo River’s depth.
The county lies in the clouds and mountains’ embrace,
The people dwell in the heart of waters and bamboos.
Free of schemes, like idle things,
I feel even fewer know me.

**At Yan Hall**

Upon the river, several peaks green,
Amid the woods, a stream bright.
Wandering clouds have no fixed form,
Secluded birds bear no names.
Companions were not prearranged,
Return dates cannot be set.
The trifles of the human world—
Cease striving to be fierce.

**Viewing the Southern Mountains from the Upper Temple**

Peak upon peak,
Westward, they stretch to Liang and Yong’s expanse.

To view while traveling is not as good as to observe while seated.
Encompassing Tang and Han, engulfing Jin and Han,
Sunk and vanished—matters hard to inquire,
Only you remain, towering and rugged.

**At the County Magistrate’s Lotus Pond Study**

Before the magistrate’s small study,
The water is clear, the pond bears lotuses.
Not only to gaze at the blossoms,
But also to listen to the murmuring stream.
Just like by rivers and lakes,
Far from the dust and earth.
Where the ancients applied their minds,
Surely not in vain.

**The Shrine of Lady Ji**

To the southwest stands a lofty mountain,
Shrouded in the dim and distant.
Immortals cannot be seen,
Only endless clouds and mist.
The thousand-year-old shrine of Lady Ji,
Its gate by the banks of the Luo River.
Only the strains of *Nishang* remain,
Still passed down among the people.

**The Ruins of Lianchang Palace**

The Luo River comes from the southwest,
The Chang River from the northwest.
Where the two waters merge,
Palace walls bear remnant bricks.
Travelers vainly imagine,
All past affairs now mere traces.
Only Lady Ji’s mountain remains,
Facing the Three Villages Post.

**On the River, Recalling the Past**

Last autumn I roamed the source of the Luo,
This autumn I roam the Luo’s course.
Though the river’s waters are unfeeling,
The human heart grows still.
Eyes dazzled by a thousand mountains,
Each peak wreathed in mist.
When the mountains end, the mist does not,
Merging with the boundless sky.
Farmers busy tending crops,
Waterfowl idly seeking fish.
One toils for food, the other rests—
How different their ways.
Yet when events pass, joy and sorrow are seen,
When times change, expansion and contraction are known.
Looking back at these two,
Switched in place—what then?
Today’s mountains were once plains,
Today’s plains were once rivers.
If even mountains and rivers change so,
How much more should human affairs be trusted?
Fortunate to escape the red dust,
I ride the wind and waves, lashing the whip.
The land stretches far, rivers and plains vast,
A lone village by misty waters at ease.
Lightning flickers as dragons cross the shore,
Clouds scatter as rain shifts the mountains.
Farmers shoulder their hoes and leave,
Fishermen return with nets on their backs.
Only I am drenched,
Still on the road.

**Summer Drinking at Yan Hall**

Yan Hall opens to the heights,
Its eaves clinging to jagged cliffs.
Cool winds come through pine boughs,
Clear springs cascade through bamboo shade.
Fine fruits mingle red and green,
Choice wine flows shallow and deep.
Yet I recall the bustling markets,
Where stifling heat cannot be borne.

**Leisure at Yan Hall**

The net of heaven is wide, hard to escape,
The net of the world is dense, impossible to pass.
My heart long unmoved,
Freed from both nets.
Tall bamboos rinse in clear springs,
Towering pines welcome the pure wind.
Or again: the moon between pines, so free,
The wind beyond bamboos, so cool.
At this moment, meeting this scene,
It perfectly matches this heart.

**Composed on the River on the Day of Beginning of Autumn**

Wealth and honor are hard to love,
Poverty and cold easily bring sorrow.
Do not carry youthful airs
Into old age as shame.
Since there is extraordinary joy,
Beware of unexpected grief.
Who can guarantee the end from the beginning,
To remain forever a duke or marquis?

**Discourse on Bear Ear Mountain**

Long ago, Yu divided the Nine Provinces,
Guiding the Luo from Bear Ear.
Bear Ear has two peaks—
Which one is correct?
The eastern one lies near Chengzhou,
The western one is cut off by the Dan River.
The records speak of Shangluo—
This saying is right.

**Climbing Lady Ji’s Mountain**

I have seen many mountains,
But never encountered such marvels.
Vast cliffs like crouching tigers,
Perilous rocks like unfurled banners.
Clouds idle, now spreading, now rolling,
Rock forms shifting again and again.
Even with brush and ink, hard to depict—
On all sides, it is just so.

**Gazing South at Yi River from the River**

The mountain retains Yu’s chiseled gate,
The river holds Yao’s water marks.
The ancients are no longer seen,
Yet their traces may still remain.
Years and months easily wither,
Good and evil are hard to bury.
Do not pursue fame in recent times,
Striving for worldly esteem.

**The Cowherd Boy**

Following with hat and raincoat,
Never disturbing heaven’s harmony.
Warmth plays by the ruined city,
Cold nestles by ancient tombs.
A few notes from the ox-back flute,
A tune from the ridge’s song.
He seems without care,
Asking nothing of court affairs.

**Chanting in a Dream on the Road to Three Villages**

Speaking of dreams within a dream, still remembered,
Awake from the dream, the dream is yet distant.
Today’s favor and glory are empty joy,
Yesterday’s love and affection are hard to find.
Where water flows, how can there be no sound?
When flowers fade, how can there be color?
Passing this, meeting strangers on the road,
All as if never known before.

**Thirty-Six Autumn Thoughts**

**I**
The autumn night begins long,
Stars and constellations vie in brilliance.
The courtyard, washed by light rain,
Pillow and mat grow slightly cool.
Shining on things, no form escapes,
The empty mirror has its own light.
Shining on affairs, no feeling escapes,
The empty heart has its own constancy.

**II**
At the bright window, the sun’s glow fades,
The secluded courtyard, freshly washed by rain.
Red orchids quietly unfold,
Green bamboos idly lean together.
Honor and profit like floating clouds,
Feelings as light as water.
Not a man from beyond the heavens,
Yet thoughts arise from beyond the heavens.

**III**
The bright moon rises from the sea’s heart,
Cool winds stir at the sky’s edge.
Things reveal themselves,
The heart suddenly opens wide.
Understanding all the power of Zhou and Confucius,
Untying the knots of benevolence and righteousness.
Rites and laws were meant to guard against treachery,
Not set for us.

**IV**
Sparse rain drips on the tall parasol tree,
Gentle winds brush the tender willows.
This scene repeats year after year,
Yet people still grow old.
Worldly cares easily entangle,
Dusty hearts hard to shake off.
Floating life is already in a dream,
Forcing existence within it.

**V**
Clear rapids pattern mandarin ducks,
Cold pools embroider cormorants.
The vast sky, pure as water,
Does not hinder the autumn river’s green.
A man’s inch-long heart,
A warrior’s match for ten thousand foes.
Amid lotus-scented winds,
A lone boat—we will meet again.

**VI**
Yesterday I longed for cool broth,
Today I long to put away the fan.
Not only do people wield spears,
Heat and cold wage their own battles.
Profit and harm arise from feeling,
Likes and dislikes depend on perception.
To wish others to be good,
One must first be good oneself.

**VII**
Sweet persimmons, green as indigo,
Red peaches, fresh as blood.
I cannot bear to pluck them with my hands,
Much less bite them with my teeth.
Their color is already lovely,
Their taste even more exquisite.
Eating them, no fine words,
Alas for mouth and tongue.

**VIII**
The nation’s fate lies with the people,
The people’s fate lies with food.
Even if the sages were reborn,
This saying would not change.

Empty favors—how could they be esteemed?
They teach men to indulge and coddle.
Empty fame—how could it be exalted?
It teaches men to adorn and deceive.

The *Zhou Odes* speak of taking a wife,
The *Zhou Yi* speaks of the maiden’s return.
The Seventh Night’s worldly passions,
The pleading for skill in girls’ frivolous postures.
At dusk, clouds and rain pass—
People say the Herd-Boy and Weaver Girl meet.
Yet the thunderbolt’s trace vanishes without a mark,
How could the Herd-Boy and Weaver Girl be paired?

A pure breeze needs no companion—
It enters my hands of its own accord.
The bright moon needs no peer—
It enters my window of its own will.
When the heart is already at peace,
What outer thing could ever tempt?

Trivial matters—why discuss them?
I only fear the wine jar will run dry.
Green plantain leaves unfurl,
Blue reeds bow low.
Wind and rain howl through the sky,
The night deepens with the water-clock’s drip.

Those things are blameless in themselves,
Why should this feeling surprise?
Yet I think of the traveler on the road,
Still lingering at the edge of the horizon.
Faint mist veils the sparse woods,
A light wind sways the cold rain.
At dusk, people have all returned,
Only the flock of chickens still pecks at the millet.

This heart remains unmoved,
Yet this matter is most difficult to handle.
To sum it up in a single word—
I fear even that would waste breath.

In the eighth month, heat and chill balance,
The qi and flavor are naturally fine.
Facing the void, tall trees bow low,
From afar, the traveler appears small.
All traces of affairs are illusions,
With a heart devoid of intent, things are all resolved.
Why ask further about bitterness?
I wish only that you taste the smartweed yourself.

Yellow millet ripens in autumn,
Yellow chickens grow plump in autumn.
These things are easily obtained,
The ancients held them in high regard.
They can welcome guests and friends,
They can serve one’s parents’ chambers.
With coarse cloth, one can endure the year—
What else is worth pursuing?

Rice is born of heaven,
Yeast is crafted by man.
Ferment it and call it wine—
Drink it, and one may become drunk.
The rigid it makes supple,
The timid it makes resolute.
It skillfully wields the power of creation—
Its merit is no small thing.

Autumn’s hues deepen day by day,
The old heart grows ever more indolent.
Weary, I descend the steps to walk,
At leisure, I toy with books and scrolls.
Wide roads teem with wind and dust,
I hear it is hard to find a moment’s rest.
The gates of noble houses are already deep,
The imperial palace’s gates are even more distant.
The frontier geese have yet to arrive,
The swallows of the rafters have already departed.
Clouds and mountains stretch in a thousand layers—
Where shall we meet again?

The city gates of Jiye rise high,
The long pavilion roads wind far.
The land’s ways ruin men’s clothes—
Newly made, they soon turn old.
Beyond the intermittent cries of cicadas,
Sparse hawks descend ahead.
The years pass in vain,
Human affairs grow ever more desolate.

Washing the bamboo to preserve new shoots,
Turning the pages to find old writings.
Who knows the one who nurtures the heart
Would ever vie with the world for power?

The Mid-Autumn’s scenery is fine,
The Central Plains’ mist and waters are wondrous.
The season of the first chill,
The moment when men are half-drunk.
The couch awaits the bright moon’s sweep,
The lapel awaits the fine wind’s caress.
A single matter in the heart—
The world knows nothing of it.

A fine moon fills the high tower,
The high tower still in Mid-Autumn.
At midnight, cold dew descends,
A thousand miles of cold light flows.
Who will take up this mirror,
Wipe it clean, newly polished, and rest?
Illuminating one breaks the heart of ten thousand ages,
Whitening ten thousand ages’ heads.

Cold dew clings to withered grass,
A bleak wind shakes the evening woods.
Bird cries rise and fall,
The sky’s mood shifts from clear to overcast.
The season changes in an instant,
The heart holds a thousand ages’ longing.
Who says Ziji is dead?
The whole world lacks a knowing friend.

Wind-tossed willows scatter like a comb,
Frost-laden clouds thin as a broom’s sweep.
A high tower pierces the perilous void,
Low mist sways in the early cold.
At this moment, my mood is boundless—
How can I battle autumn’s aging?
I can only take up the wine bottle
And pour it out to the west wind.

Pond lotuses daily wither,
Hedge chrysanthemums daily flourish.
Among the ranks of things,
They contend with the seasons’ rhythm.
Flourishing and decay do not coincide,
The worthy and foolish cannot walk side by side.
How can one obtain the heart of pine and cassia,
Evergreen through all four seasons?

Men grow old as autumn grows older,
Mountains deepen, waters deepen.
Tall trees have already shed their leaves,
Wise birds sing in vain.
Sinews and bones are not as they once were,
Virtue betrays its original intent.
Fortunately, my remaining writings endure—
From time to time, they may still be sought.

In the ninth month, the qi suddenly turns stern,
Withered willows still harbor cicadas.
Beyond the frost, sparse bells fall silent,
The wind’s remnants carry clear sounds.
A thousand mountains confuse the distant gaze,
A lone osprey soars into the high heavens.
If one is not a transcendent being,
How dare one speak of perilous deeds?

Pears redden under heavy frost,
Pomegranates split after long rain.
These fruits are called treasures of the world,
Their flavors are truly astonishing.
The earth holds a hundred things in readiness,
Heaven hangs not a single word.
I fear I still have words,
Unable to match the workings of creation.

Only in the south are fine tangerines,
Only in the north are fine chestnuts.
Their husks may be somewhat alike,
Their flavors are surely not the same.
Do heaven and earth lack feeling?
All plants and trees bear fruit.
Things do not betray man—
Man betrays himself to things.

Butterflies flutter around cold chrysanthemums,
Crickets chirp on empty steps.
A dog lies at the gate,
All day, no guests arrive.
Clear waves flow in stillness,
White clouds pile in leisure.
How to bring forth heaven’s harmony?
From time to time, drink a cup of wine.

Red leaves battle the west wind,
Yellow blossoms laugh at the cold sun.
The Dao of heaven has its waxing and waning,
Human affairs have no fixed certainty.
Stillness prevails, leaving a lingering savor,
Dreams depart, revealing what was lost.
Profit and harm do not follow one another,
Right and wrong only then emerge.

On the Double Ninth, climbing high to gather,
Seeking the secluded to share refined joy.
Customs follow old traditions,
The weather offers a light chill.
White wine is drunk in unstrained drafts,
Yellow blossoms are viewed with dew still clinging.
The floating world’s affairs sink away—
Why should one value fleeting waves?

Mountains stretch into the evening haze,
Crows vanish beyond the lone wisp of smoke.
Fading chrysanthemums fear the frost’s crushing,
Hidden orchids dread the wind’s ruin.
Hardship and danger—people do not favor them,
Wealth and honor—people cherish them.
My heart is not my own—
How could love and hate be for sale?

Water grows cold, revealing the heart of the pool,
Trees shed their leaves, baring the mountain’s bones.
Only now do I believe heaven has no bounds—
Ten thousand miles do not separate things.
I shed my clothes and hang them on Fusang,
Reach my hand to explore the moon’s cave.
Not betraying the heart of benevolence and righteousness—
A mere fifty-one years.

Grass is green, dew soaks the clothes,
Grass withers, wind cuts the skin.
Things’ feelings do not change—
Men’s minds forcibly conjure doubt.

The fork stirs, Yang Zhu weeps;
The thread lengthens, Mozi grieves.
Knowing this—why so late?
In vain, tears stream down.

Beyond ten thousand miles of clear sky,
A slice of moon on the frost.
Tall pines stand evergreen,
While all other blooms fade and vanish.
Teeth daily grow weaker,
Hair daily falls away.
Offenses already mount high—
This heart cannot be trifled with.

Grass withers, mountains and rivers grow poor;
Trees shed, heaven and earth grow lean.
Dust fills the wind’s path,
Sunlight barely slips through cloud-gaps.
The past cannot be chased again,
The future may yet be saved.
Other matters I dare not speak—
To speak them would surely bring blame.

Wine I drink, not too much,
A few cups flush face and heart.
Before drunk, I cannot stop;
Once drunk, persuasion is vain.
Who says the ten thousand things are vast?
Do they extend beyond heaven and earth’s bounds?
Who says the ten thousand affairs are broad?
Do they stretch beyond the realm of human feeling?

**Poem for Prefect Zhang of Shanzhou at Stone Pillar Village**
You serve as Prefect of Shanzhou,
I am truly a descendant of Lord Kang.
Our first ancestor left a noble legacy—
I entrust you to seek what remains.
You sent me a poem,
Titled *Stone Pillar Village*.
When the stone pillar was first erected,
Its origins were lost in antiquity.
The *Fen Shaan Inscription* was carved there,
Yet only Tang scholars spoke of it.
Years have passed in great number,
Its beginning and end now lack complete text.
Hard to verify or correct,
How then to trace its source?
I lament my scant reading,
