My knowledge no greater than others’.
Beyond classics and histories,
I cannot dispel the world’s confusion.
If following Changqing’s *Gongyang*,
It should begin at Shaan.
If following your *Chen Biming*,
It should speak of Zhou’s completion.
Taking both together,
The meaning may yet be honored.
Dividing governance between east and west suburbs,
One may discuss it in terms of Shaan and Luo.
If this explanation nears the mark,
Perhaps it may ease the tangle.

The sweet pear’s shade still flourishes,
The stone pillar remains fresh and green.
The grand affairs of that time,
I could not witness firsthand.
The pure teachings of *Er Nan*,
The noble fragrance of those two lords—
A tale of beauty for a thousand years,
I may yet hear of it.

To abandon the classics and rely on commentaries,
Confucian scholars fundamentally reject.
I write this poem to clarify,
Hastening this humble reply to you.

**Free Expression**
Having attained the joy of stillness and emptiness,
I am no longer startled by favor or disgrace.
Mud and void—still attachments;
Equalizing things—ultimately a struggle.
Idly I pick up the brush,
From time to time writing my own name.
Who can truly harm their nature,
To conjure feelings beyond feeling?

*Volume Three of Yi Chuan’s "Striking the Earth Collection" concludes.*
*Volume Four of Yi Chuan’s "Striking the Earth Collection" begins.*

**Thanking Minister Wang Jun of the Bureau of Etiquette for the Completion of the New Residence in Tianjin**
*Seventh Year of Jiayou (1062)*

In the *renyin* year of Jiayou,
The new nest’s work was just □.
Still part of Daode Lane,
Yet nearer the imperial palace.
Railings face the towering Gate of Correct Deportment,
Verandas welcome the mighty Two Observatories.
Windows open to the echoes of Chan and Jian streams,
Terraces distant, gleaming with Yi and Song peaks.
Fine scenery is ever hard to come by,
Auspicious times are not easily met.
Lacking talent to aid the realm,
I am content to enjoy a year of abundance.
Water and bamboo cradle the heart,
Orioles and flowers dwell in deep pools.
Like Lao Lai, my joy knows no end,
The delight of quiet integrity is boundless.
Carefree, I keep company with true friends,
Planning to congratulate Your Excellency.
My red sincerity is but self-expression—
Not repayment for your profound kindness.

**New Spring Chant**
At fifty-two, my bones and sinews ail,
Yet this new spring I still share cups with friends.
Xunzi’s words on embodying form—ever rare,
Hui’s joy within—unmatched in merit.
Swallows come and go, vainly toiling,
Flowers bloom and fade, heedlessly hastening.
This heart does not sway for others’ joys or sorrows—
For twenty years, it has been as ash.

**A Guest’s Chant**
Between Yi and Song, a guest would speak no words,
Advancing or retreating—all awaits heaven’s will.
Loving quiet, I cannot forget streams and stones,
Finding joy in leisure—not to learn immortality.
No need to sigh over purple streets, hard for guests,
Just delight in the clean wind, which costs no coin.
Why quibble over bending a foot to gain a yard?
This heart is vast—it seeks no perfection.

**Encountering Spring in the Small Garden**
Content with pavilions and railings, I too play at beauty,
No harm in lingering by the wine stove.
Night eaves quietly admit the moon through flowers,
Daytime doors chill with mist beyond the bamboo.
Things, once understood, are all by chance—
Heaven’s decree to be idle is not in vain.
How much time fills the gourd’s sun and moon?
Twelve years of splendid scenery, wantonly seized.

**Late Spring Chant**
Spring arrives, the small garden teems with blossoms—
Who dwells in poverty, who in wealth and honor?
Beyond the gate, willow shade floats in emerald luster,
Before the steps, flower shadows glide in crimson light.
New swallows under the beams have yet to tune their tongues,
Returning geese at the sky’s edge have already taken flight.
I ask my heart’s source—what does it hold?
It answers: a fondness for carefree indolence.

**Cherishing the Fragrant and Luxuriant**
Weighing the world’s thousand affairs,
All lose to flowers’ shared delight.
Most of spring’s splendor lasts but a season,
Its full glory barely ten days.
I fear sorrow may bring discord,
And guard against wind and rain’s hardships.
Let not regret follow after—
Before the fire fades, one may still climb.

**Reply to a Gift**
My temples need not fret over turning frost,
For I have the solace of zither and books, and you.
Lacking great talent to serve a flourishing age,
I can but rest my head on the southern window.
I know my strength cannot be forced,
Yet still speak of mountains and clouds not yet surrendered.
Deep thanks to my old friend for such love—
Like a stranded fish, I am spared from the western river’s plight.

**Playing with the Brush**
At fifty-two, what more have I to fear in old age?
Affairs are valued when seen to the bottom,
Words are hard to speak to the end.
Above, there is a bright Son of Heaven,
Below, worthy lords and nobles.
Beyond a full belly and sound sleep,
I seek nothing more.

**Begging Wine from a Friend**
A hundred ailments plague this old body,
White-haired today, I shamefully drift along.
Though no purple edict summons me back to court,
Green mountains often enter my dreams.
Who is master of wind and moon filling the sky?
Are there no men in forests and springs across the land?
Market wine’s flavor is rarely pure—
I long betray my heart’s springtime.

**Reply to a Guest**
Countless acquaintances in the world,
Though many know me, few truly do.
Most gaze upon me for the Dao,
Yet know me shallowly through my poems.
Ask not of rise and fall in a hundred years—
Past and present all return to one game of chess.

**Riding a Steed**
One must seek a mount like the qilin or steed,
Yet how can such a steed appear in due time?

**A Word of Enlightenment**
A hundred schemes still prove clumsy,
But one word of confusion may yet dissolve.
The world holds no great matters,
Yet the land abounds with heroic talent.
I fear only that men are hard to find,
Not that the Dao remains unrevealed.
When envy’s heart is wholly cast aside,
What dust remains to trouble me?

**Thanks for the Gift of a Brush**
I cherish this gift of the study,
Gratefully received from afar.
The rabbit’s hair is firm and strong,
The bamboo shaft straight and long.
In stillness, I transcribe new poems,
In leisure, I copy old remedies.
Beyond such uses, it is enough
To nurture the blade’s keen edge.

**Composed on Affairs**
Heaven and earth hold eternal principles,
Sun and moon never hide their forms.
Beyond a full belly and sound sleep,
All else is but empty fame.
Though lacking the talents of Yi or Lü,
I remain a subject of Yao and Shun.
Why must one become a minister
To bring about great peace?

**Twin-Lotus Blossoms**
The Han court’s graceful sisters,
The divine maidens of Tiantai’s peaks—
All once knew the passions of their time,
Yet were banished to earth as auspicious lotuses.

**Reply to a Letter**
Confucius spoke of rectifying one’s nature,
Ziyou of embodying one’s form.
Who attains these two
Lives not in vain.
If friendships are forged through the Dao,
And feelings stirred by sincerity,
Then though three armies stand before you,
None can overpower you.

**In Response to a Letter**
Without rank, establishing deeds is hard;
Meeting the times, achieving merit is easy.
Seeking perfection invites ruin,
Pursuing greatness requires overlooking details.
To reject evil risks wounding kindness,
To tolerate evil risks harming righteousness.
Though the heart of a benevolent man beats,
His intent remains far from true benevolence.

**Reply to a Letter**
A minister’s yearly stipend
Would fund a poor scholar’s lifetime.
Worldly titles differ,
But the heavenly title is never lacking.
Without frost and snow,
How would we know the pine and cassia?
Though no office elevates me,
How can the Dao not ennoble me?

**Reminiscing with an Old Friend**
What ears and eyes have witnessed,
I recount from thirty springs.
Ten intercalary months have passed,
And people have changed once more.
The old families cling to their ways,
While the vermilion gates host new carriages.
All has always been by chance—
What can be called true merit?

**Idle Verses**
Casually I take up my brush,
Ever delighting in the spirit’s play.
Never without a scene to match,
Yet never bound by emotion.
Lines come drifting into being,
Poems form by chance.
The workings of heaven are hard to describe—
Yet one point stands clear.

**Idle Sitting**
I once planned for the years ahead,
Yet today’s affairs elude my grasp.
Heaven’s mandate does not favor me,
So I find solace in mountains and clouds.
Through no merit of my own, I grow detached,
And so approach the realm of the profound.
I would mock the true master,
Who toils without knowing why.

**Strolling at Tianjin**
The Son of Heaven’s ancient domain,
Where lush vitality still lingers.
Gardens and woods lie near the water,
Palaces and towers stretch into autumn’s distance.
The waves recall summer’s heat,
The bridge’s rainbow fades not in the clear sky.
On days when the world knows no strife,
This place invites lingering.

**Secluded at Tianjin**
My home lies within Luoyang’s walls,
And here, too, at Tianjin’s edge.
The sun rises early here,
Spring comes easily to the low sky.
Time favors the state’s flourishing,
Scenery enriches the recluse.
I may now cast aside cap and sash,
And grow old in leisure here.

**The Sound of Tianjin’s Waters**
The Luo River flows near my hut,
Its murmurs reaching my pillow’s emptiness.
The rapids startle in late autumn,
The waves quicken at the fifth watch’s dawn.
Gentle winds trace their delicate paths,
Fierce rains swell their mighty currents.
For the recluse, this is joy enough—
Why wait for pipes and zithers?

**Sleepless**
The night deepens as I sit idly,
Yet sleep eludes me in the long hours.
Tossing, I cannot rest,
And so reflect on past affairs.
Pillow laid, my mind grows dull,
Blanket drawn, my thoughts remain bright.
In the moonlit western window,
A few leaves of the plantain sway.

**The Small Pavilion of Heavenly Palace**
In summer, I reach the Heavenly Palace,
Leaning on the railing, my gaze knows no bounds.
The ancients held lofty aspirations,
The Son of Heaven built a mighty capital.
Pavilions lie deep within clouds,
Mountains and rivers fade into evening mist.
Travelers come and go in vain—
Who shares this sentiment?

**Listening to the Qin**
The qin is best heard at night,
Awakening a different feeling.
Just as the mournful gibbons fall silent,
The parting phoenix begins its cry.
The green mountains stretch endlessly,
White hair need not cause alarm.
Only when one grasps the mind of sitting-in-forgetfulness
Does one know the heart of the distant past.

**Twenty-Six Reflections on Tianjin**

Light clouds, pale sun—dusk at Tianjin,
Fierce winds, sparse woods—autumn on the Luo.
Walking alone, chanting alone, none understand,
As gulls and egrets alight on the islets.

Favor and disgrace—matters now unseen,
Rise and fall—times gone, only sorrow remains.
Look upon Chang’an’s roads today—
Even at dusk, people still come and go.

The phoenix tower’s depths lock away clouds and mist,
A hundred years pass with each sealing.
Grieving for the sacrifices at Fenyin’s western altar,
The imperial banners betray the heavens of Shangyang.

Who drew the long river through Luoyang’s walls?
The imperial carriage’s eastward journey made light of this place.
The mighty waves chafe at Tianjin’s restraints,
Day and night roaring in furious tumult.

The sun sets in the west, waters flow east,
Time shifts through the passing years.
Distant mountains gather their dark hues on all sides—
Who do they grieve for all day long?

Busy men bear burdens—what difference between the two?
Coming and going always follow this path.
How much better to sit idly, untalented,
With clouds and waters swirling about my robes.

They say fishing discerns the floating and sinking,
But such discernment demands deep intent.
I am ashamed not to know the harm in desire,
Yet lightly stir my heart in hope of catching fish.

Since ancient times, abandoned capitals hold many empty spaces,
Towering trees teem with cawing crows at dusk.
Where crumbling walls and broken ramparts are tilled by men,
Half were once the homes of ministers and nobles.

The phoenix towers stand cold in autumn’s chill,
Beneath the bridge, long waves flow toward the sea.
For a thousand years, this was the old imperial market—
How many times have people passed through here?

Hooves and wheels clash without cease,
Departing as if in pursuit, arriving as if in contest.
I reckon their hearts hold no other concern—
If not for profit, then for fame.

Trees fade into autumn’s embrace,
Homes nestle amid the sound of waters.
A few rows of crows fly off at an angle,
A cluster of towers clings precariously to the void.

The clear waters stretch to the azure sky,
The level plains merge with distant mountains.
An old man with white hair, his heart at ease,
Leans idly on the railing, gazing at the Luo River.

Last year’s man who leaned on the bridge’s railing,
This year rides past its edge on horseback.
Bridge above and bridge below—countless crossings,
Yet now I recall only thirteen springs.

By the embankment, grasses grow lush and green,
On the paths, travelers come and go.
The clear waters strive for purity but never attain it,
The spring winds refuse to let the willow branches rest.

Though currents rush, the scene remains serene,
Though blossoms fall in profusion, the mind stays at ease.
Unlike men of the world who age in busyness,
Never once in their lives knowing an open smile.

By the stream, I sit idly, my eyes half-lidded,
Waves strike the long embankment as if to break it.
How many waterfowl, their plumage bright with color,
Again and again take flight, then return.

Fame and profit have always followed talent,
No need for travelers to guess at one another’s schemes.
How much time lies within the gourd’s embrace?
I stroll the Heavenly Ford, watching the ebb and flow.

The land tilts southeastward as a whole,
When will its waters ever find peace?
The Heavenly Ford lies where the currents race fastest—
Endless depths, all roaring with the same sound.

Three thousand *li* away, the frontier is called wild,
For a hundred years, the realm has been at peace.
Yet how can it compare to the carefree man of the Luo River,
Who needs no fief to know true honor?

Willows sway around the embankment in the soft wind,
Pavilions across the water stand veiled in fine rain.
After the Double Ninth, wine leaves one half-tipsy—
At this moment, the flavor is beyond compare.

Observing the world’s affairs from a place of stillness,
Refining one’s feelings in leisure’s embrace:
When all storms subside and only still waters remain,
What in this world cannot be made level?

Since Sui and Tang, nobles and ministers have been exalted,
In recent times, men chase fame and profit through turmoil.
I ask the waters beneath the Heavenly Ford Bridge:
What sound did their rushing make in those days?

Countless nobles and ministers of former dynasties—
Later ages remember only their names.
Yet these waters beneath the Heavenly Ford Bridge
Have flowed with the same sound from ancient times to now.

Not a wisp of cloud dares touch the bright moon,
The bridge spans a thousand *xun*, lying across the green waves.
I reckon no such scene exists in the human world—
How could one’s mood not soar when gazing at the moon on Mid-Autumn?

In the city of Jiaoru, with friends of shared virtue,
Beneath the Phoenix Towers, we gather for the Mid-Autumn festival.
The fragrant wine is drained, and the guests depart,
Yet the moonlight and ripples still vie in brilliance.

Only when life is understood can one speak of constancy,
Only when nature is known can one discuss great plans.
This long river flows day and night without cease—
For whom is it driven so relentlessly eastward?

**Cheng Ming Yin**
Confucius was born with knowledge, not through study,
Mencius had prior insight, yet still needed cultivation.
Sincerity and clarity are our family’s own affairs—
Why do men of today seek them elsewhere?

**Sheng Shui Yin**
Water excels at leveling but not at straightening,
The plumb line excels at straightening but not at leveling.
If water and plumb line could be made one,
What in the world would remain unclear?

**Xin Suan Yin**
Pungent and sour are not the central flavors,
How can *shang* and *zheng* be the proper tones?
The world cannot distinguish right from wrong—
Who then can serve as master to calm the heart?

**Ji Mo Yin**
When silence is called for, speech is but dust,
When speech is called for, silence is but grime.
If one neither speaks nor stays silent as the moment demands,
How can dust and grime ever touch the self?

**Idle Musings on Life**
Not a trace of heavenly truth is lost,
Yet a thousand measures of human emolument are hard to come by.
In this age of peace, I have no other joy—
Only wine, three or five cups from time to time.

Bamboo rain chills the air as it soaks one through,
By the southern window, I wake from sleep and gaze toward Xiaoxiang.
The thatched eaves drip without cease—
I recall the nights spent aboard boats long ago.

At first light, the moon emerges from the pines,
In the height of summer, the wind comes from the water’s surface.
I have more than most men already—
How much more when I can raise my cup again and again?

My mother in the hall is over eighty,
Before the steps, my children laugh and call to one another.
Fine foods are taken as they come, in plenty or in want—
Is there any joy in the world to match this?

For three days in a row, I rarely lack pure delight,
Though deep drinking never exceeds five parts.
When we meet, our hearts hold no other matter—
We do not debate rise and fall, only discuss literature.

Flowers and trees mark the seasons with their scenes,
A thousand volumes of classics are called my livelihood.
If anyone asks where I dwell in idleness,
It is the first house in the Morality Ward.

**Cooling in the Small Pavilion of Heavenly Palace**
From the small pavilion, I gaze out over Luoyang,
The river and sky’s expanse cradles the divine capital.
Winds come from the peak of Ten-Thousand-Year Mountain—
How many mists and clouds share this clarity?

The small pavilion has done me great service,
Its coolness unmatched in all of Luoyang.
Ashamed of my own insignificance and good fortune,
My sleeves and lapels forever hold the winds of a thousand *li*.

How rare it is to bear the pavilion’s cool breeze—
Its mood is that of the ancient sages.
In Luoyang lives a nameless guest
Who for twenty years has enjoyed this coolness.

**Idle Musings in a Stifling Summer Retreat**
Men find the Heavenly Ford distant,
They must come with purpose.
In leisure, I know the Way is at peace,
In stillness, I feel my spirit open.

I grasp the *Yi* while watching a game of chess,
Discuss poetry while toying with a wine cup.
The world’s affairs take a thousand forms—
None of them trouble my heart.

**Visiting Longmen**
The river and sky show little difference,
A solitary bird descends to the sunlit sands.
The road ahead cuts through the mountain’s shape,
The river bends where the ferry crossing slants.

Caves and cliffs stand empty by the thousands,
Two or three households make up the inns.
The clear scenery is fine in all four seasons—
How much more so when the capital is not far off?

**Returning to the Luo River**
To buy stone is to have clouds in abundance,
To buy a mountain is to follow the water.
Clouds can bring about a mind without intent,
Water can serve as a mirror to still the self.

Nature is made clear by a mind without intent,
Feelings are stilled by the mirror’s reflection.
Neither can be lost—
One emerges from the other.

**Watching Fish by the River**
The air is cold with autumn’s touch,
The river long, the fish at play.
Though they know how to avoid the net,
They still fear swallowing the hook by mistake.

They have given up hope of leaping the dragon gate,
No longer worry about striking their heads.
I think of the joy by the Hao River—
Zhuang Zhou’s carefree spirit alone understands.

*The Fourth Volume of Yi Chuan Ji Rang Concludes*
*The Fifth Volume of Yi Chuan Ji Rang Begins*

**Three Poems on Immediate Matters in the Rear Garden**
*Jia You Eighth Year*

In this age of peace, though old, what care have I?
I wander my home’s garden at leisure, free.
A few willows cast their shade, just now entwined,
A few birds sing softly, their voices soon stilled.

Bamboo crowds the old path, rising high and low,
Water fills the spring channels, flowing left and right.
If you ask what joy the master finds here—
He answers: no different from a marquis’ life.

Nature nurtures my indolence, and I know its way,
In Luoyang, I’ve claimed my share of water and cloud.
I hear no worldly storms, no treacherous waves,
Only the long days and months within my winecup.

A game of chess detains a wandering guest,
A few cups of fine wine face the tall bamboo.
When all worldly matters are seen through—
Even Yan Hui’s seated oblivion seems no more.

---

In recent years, my illness is called "poetic madness,"
Each bout of madness must be met with wine.
My heart delights in the Dao, careless of restraint,
My words flow freely, unburdened by thought.

Friends grow closer with time, our bonds endure,
Like clouds and water, our leisure runs deep.
Now I understand Yuanming’s profound intent—
This window, in his day, rivaled Fuxi’s age.

---

**Watching Chess and Chanting Long**

The courtyard is still, spring deep, the door ajar,
Between bamboo, I watch guests contend at chess.
They gather gods and ghosts within their minds,
Arrange rivers and mountains within their grasp.

The board forms dragons and snakes in battle array,
The game ends, geese and crows scatter in flight.
Like Xiang Yu burying Qin’s soldiers in slaughter,
Or Fu Jian’s defeat before Jin’s might.

On the board, spears and shields clash fiercely,
Before their eyes, ice and fire swiftly shift.
Life and death rest on two households’ fate,
Victory and defeat hinge on a single move.

No mercy is given where paths cross,
Facing others, one must feign refusal.
To suffer harm in heart is truly dreadful,
Yet even lips and teeth may yet be healed.

To wound with subtle schemes is called good strategy,
To act with cunning in secret is deemed wise.
Look now upon Chang’an’s roads—
Who here has not played such games?

---

**Two Poems Ascending Chongde Pavilion on an Autumn Day**

Countless heroes, their ambitions crushed—
No need for sighs upon this height.
Mountains and rivers endure after a thousand wars,
Swords and crowns lie scattered after a hundred years.

Some take glory and fame as their own duty,
Yet who knows if wealth and rank are man’s design?
Painting and poetry exhaust the world’s wonders,
But what meaning lingers when leaning on the railing?

---

A hundred years have been called an age of peace,
Yet still, in the beginning, there was unrest.
The capital retains the air of Han and Tang,
The palace halls still lack a hegemon’s form.

Beyond the mist, jagged peaks barely emerge,
After frost, half the red trees fade and fall.
Wine in the cup cannot bring drunkenness—
The west wind wakes me again and again.

---

**Returning Late After Drinking on an Autumn Day**

Water and bamboo, gardens in autumn—how fine!
How could I bear to pour the fragrant wine so soon?
The Double Ninth has passed, chrysanthemums just bloom,
My heart is full, yet I’ve not learned to age with time.

Still clouds, drooping willows,
The wind carries a light chill, hastening dusk.
Boundless joy cannot be reined—
My horse’s hooves tread slowly through the celestial streets.

---

**Sent to Prefect Zu of Shaan, Gentleman of the Palace Zu Zezhi**

Do you recall our meeting?
Back then, in the eastern seas.
A thousand miles apart since we parted,
Ten years have slipped by in an instant.

Our paths differ, our names differ,
Yet our hearts are one, our years the same.
I hope we may meet again in clarity,
To share poetry and wine without end.

---

**Lament for Zhang Yuanbo, Director of the Bureau of Operations**

In recent years, the old guard has fallen one by one,
Stirring sorrow deep within me.
Of those who hung their caps in Luoyang and retired—
Only you were as pure as a transcendent.

Once we debated who was elder, who younger,
Now we vie to see who follows whom.
I pour wine to you—you must know—
For you, I shed tears before the west wind.

---

**Lament for Zhang Shirou, Section Chief**

All his life, he longed to establish merit and fame,
Who said talent must contend with fate?
His final poem expressed refined intent,
His coffin closed, but our friendship remained.

The affairs of the world in his heart—how to unfold them?
The realm’s talents will not be judged again.
If his spirit knows, he may find solace—
His descendants are more than capable of restoring the family name.

---

**Matching the Rhymes of Pei, Vice Magistrate of Dengfeng, Sent in the Renzi Year**

The tale of the narrow lane and bamboo bowl is well known,
Yet who am I to feel shame at such simplicity?
Since wealth and rank must come by fate,
Why ask heaven about rise or fall?

Silence and stillness cultivate my wild nature,
No path to soar like the sages of the age.
Those who come and go through golden gates—
Should they laugh at this old man tending his stony fields?

---

**Why Do You Send Poems to the Three Wealthy Lords of the Three Cities?**

Why do you urge me to ponder so deeply,
To seek transcendence with these few words?
Your actions should be like a thousand-catty crossbow,
Your tempering should be like a hundred-fold refined gold.

Fishing in water, you wield the power of life and death,
Playing chess, you stir the heart of war.
A cup of fine wine may bring some relief—
In the grove, I pour myself a drink from time to time.

---

**Letter Written on Behalf of a Friend**

In my youth, I aspired to stand above the world,
Reading ancient texts, I laughed at Xie An.
Who would have thought this body would grow old and useless,
With no idle ground to linger?

At chess, I meet worthy opponents, moves worth making,
But the zither lacks a knowing ear—I will not play.
Not only am I unworthy to withdraw in silence—
The ancients long lamented the rarity of opportunity.

---

**Visiting Yao Fuzhou, Vice Director of the Bureau of Evaluations, at Moon Dyke’s Western Garden**

Longing could not be restrained,
So I came to visit you on West Street.
Crossing ditches, winding through vegetable plots,
Trees bow their heads to make way,
My staff changes hands as I climb.

Among friends, our joy is complete—
What greater happiness could there be?

---

**Matching Rhymes in Thanks to Three Gentlemen of Dengfeng—Liu, Li, and Pei—for Inviting Me to Tour the Mountains**

The gentlemen invite me to Mount Song,
Each composing new poems in turn.
I shake off the dust, not daring to lag behind,
Yet in seeking clouds and water, I fear I’ve fallen behind.

Sanyang Palace lies near clusters of dark stones,
Wansui Peak towers, veiled in purple mist.
So many scenic spots I’ve yet to visit—
I long to follow the immortals’ footsteps and climb together.

---

**Ascending the Summit of Mount Song**

The nine provinces stretch far like a chessboard,
Wansui Peak stands tall, gazing upon peace.
If one man can govern the four seas,
Why should the Central Plains know strife again?

I long fear the rise of treacherous heroes,
Yet wish to be a simple subject of Yao and Shun.
Fifty-three years have passed in idle obscurity—
Now, at last, I rejoice to see the spring plowing.

---

**Viewing Shaoshi Temple in Dengfeng County**

**The Primordial Mountain**

When heaven and earth first congealed,
This mountain already stood at its loftiest height.
Peaks cluster like banners and pennants,
Mighty stones arrayed like swords and halberds.
At sunrise, the cliffs blush crimson first;
After rain, the mist glows an even deeper jade.
Who knows if immortals dwell here,
Long concealed within these hidden traces?

---

**Written in the Mountains, Sent to the Magistrate of Dengfeng**

No sooner had I left the county than I planned to stay the night,
Yet once in the mountains, I could not bear to turn back.
If the magistrate’s hall were free of civic affairs,
I would bring my tea set and ascend the peaks.

---

**Sent to Longtu of Zhengzhou, Zu Zezhi, on My Return to Luoyang**

Kindness runs deep as marrow—such is a loving parent;
Loyalty weighs heavy as mountains—such are old friends.
Passing through Songyang, the old haunts of my wanderings,
White clouds enfold this body clad in vines and creepers.

---

**In Response to Longtu’s Gift**

My family’s duty is to the clouds and mountains—
Without clouds and mountains, my face knows no ease.
The urge to wander cannot be bound by days or limits,
Even in dreams, my soul does not descend to the human realm.
Mists and peaks promise boundless joy,
While courtly caps and carriages offer but fleeting leisure.
Though I often think of gathering with friends in Luoyang,
When have I ever found time to climb even a little?

---

**On Ornamented Verses**

When adorned verses are perfected, one is called a master,
Yet the craftsmanship seems that of a true artisan.
Beware the cold-eyed observer’s gaze—
For puppets have no curtains to hide behind.

---

**Self-Reflection, Three Poems**

In the arena of fame and profit, it’s hard to plant one’s feet;
On the path to forests and springs, I turned back early.
Had I not, this fifty-year-old, worn-out body
Would still be tossed in the ceaseless winds and waves.

The sky’s wind and moon are my official charge,
The earth’s clouds and mountains my true authority.
Only I dare open my unguarded mouth,
Speaking freely to others without restraint.

Ever regretful my nature was dull and I heard the Dao late,
Ever ashamed my wisdom was shallow and the times unsuited.
A human life spans thirty-six thousand days—
For twenty thousand of them, this body has been at ease.

---

**Casual Jottings**

Opinions pour forth from every quarter—
Where are the true Confucians among the gentry?
The teachings of names and rites have always had their masters;
Across the ten thousand *li* of the Central Plains, are there truly no men?

Kings, emperors, lords, and earls—though their eras differed,
Rites, music, and the classics renewed the Dao in their own time.
Gazing at the past, so much is visible today—
Yet who knows what is meant by "statecraft"?

---

**Letter in Place of Writing to Magistrate Chen Chengbo of Shangluo**

Half a year remains before your term ends,
Yet Shangshan is remote and poor, with few skilled physicians.
Grief and sorrow may stagnate the *qi* after loss—
Chen Chengbo mourns his wife—
While summer damp breeds illness all too easily.
Even sages and wise men cannot escape misfortune;
The worthy alone know how to navigate sorrow and honor.
These words are simple, but their principle is not—
May they offer some small aid to a hero’s wisdom.

---

**Mid-Autumn of the Dingwei Year of the Zhiping Era: Traveling the Two Rivers of Yi and Luo**

**Sixth Day: Evening Departure from the West Gate of Luoyang, Lodging at a Monastery to Honor My Parents, Listening to Daoist Zhang Play the Zither**

As dusk fell, I drove my carriage out of Shangyang,
Beginning my journey by lodging in a land of water and clouds.
Then hearing several strains of an immortal’s melody,
I at last believed that within a gourd, days and months stretch long.

---

**Seventh Night: Ascending the Luo River, Lodging at Yanzhuang**

The eighth month, Yanzhuang’s grain ripens under autumn skies,
For farming families, wealth lies in a bountiful year.
A basket of millet, a gourd of wine—
Who envies the lords who feast on ten thousand coins?

---

**Eighth Day: Crossing the Luo, Ascending South Mountain, Visiting the Spouting Jade Spring; Magistrates Zhang and Zhao of Shou’an County Join the Excursion**

Crossing the Luo to view the Spouting Jade Spring,
A thousand peaks, ten thousand peaks stretch into the distance.
Between them, a single stream, long as snow,
Plunges into the icy pool, its age forgotten.

---

**Ninth Day: Ascending Jinping Mountain in Shou’an County, Lodging in the Town**

A cluster of mist and haze rises steeply—
Arriving here, the heart naturally stills.
Above dwell immortals whose names are unknown,
As the cave gate idly opens to drifting white clouds.

Riding side by side, we traverse Die Stone Creek,
Where sheer cliffs encircle us, level with the clouds.
Even the cascading waters seem to urge us to stay—
How could we betray our vow to dwell here one day?

*(Die Stone Creek lies five or six* li *south of the county.)*

---

**Tenth Day: Westward Passage over Yongji Bridge (a Tang Dynasty Bridge)**

On the tenth day, traveling west over Yongji,
Light rain dampens my robes from time to time.
The mountain spirits, in their kindness, seem to understand—
Fearing that travelers might miss the verdant peaks.

---

**Two Poems on Passing Yiyang City**

Six states, petty and weak, served Qin,
Exhausted by their running, yet still unable to please.
How could they slaughter half the realm,
As if the lands east of the Pass had no men left?

In those days, Yiyang was called a secondary capital—
Yet how could the state of Han have been so small?
Zhang Liang could not vent his lingering resentment,
So at Bolangsha, he struck the wrong carriage.

---

**Eleventh Day: Meeting Rain in Fuchang County**

Clouds shift slowly over the peaks,
The sound of springs lingers among the bamboo.
At this moment, boundless meaning—
Only the green-feathered birds know.

---

**Matching Rhymes with the Magistrate and Deputy of Du’an, Who Sent a Poem**

Not once have I lashed my horse into the red dust,
For I possess no talent worthy of the age’s favor.
My youthful ambitions have all come to naught—
Yet fate draws me to dwell in the green mountains.

In the shade of emerald bamboo, I open my silk-bound books;
Amid piles of white clouds, I bow to cascading springs.
Jinping Mountain faces Nanxi Stream—
One day, I shall wander there with you, my friend.

---

**Twelfth Day: Accompanying Magistrate Wang of Fuchang, a Gentleman of the Imperial Secretariat, to Dragon Pool**

*(The pool lies in South Mountain, fifteen* li *from the county.)*

Two pools, cold and dark, lap at the black cliff’s base,
Several peaks rise high into the azure sky-road.
Visitors hold their breath, daring not speak,
Ever fearful that thunder might erupt beside them.

Sitting quietly by the water as dusk approaches,
I linger, unable to leave.
Turning my horse’s head for one last look,
Layers of clouds and mist already separate us.

---

**Thirteenth Day: Visiting Shangsi Monastery North of the County and Huangjian West of the County**

To leave the dusty world is to enter the true realm;
To fail to finish with the monastic life is to remain in the secular.
If not here, where shall I seek the grotto-heavens?
Where else shall I find hidden blossoms?

Alas for the Five Hegemons who fought over Zhou’s embers,
Laughable the Three Kingdoms who scavenged Han’s remnants.
How much better to sit idly, untalented,
While clouds and waters keep my robes at a distance.

---

**Fourteenth Day: Inscribed on the Eastern Pavilion of Fuchang County’s Hall**

Autumn enters the Luo River, the scene at its finest,
Light rain has just passed, the paths lie aslant.
Within the bamboo-and-water grotto hides the county hall,
While mist and haze enfold the homes of the people.

Frost-touched leaves, red amid a thousand layers,
Beyond the sky, a few wisps of sunset glow.
Shallow or deep, the creek shimmers clear and bright,
Peaks high or low stand jagged in jade.

Birds choose their trees and fly far,
Clouds drift without intent, their departure slow.

**On Worldly Glory**

Vast worldly merit and fame often clash,
Outstanding talent and deeds invite sighs.
The floating life of sun and moon must still be cherished,
Half-aged sinews and bones—boast no more.

Here by this cliff, a hut should be built,
To delight in my true joy, boundless and free.

---

**Fifteenth Day: Parting from Fuchang, with Some Feelings**

The Lianchang Palace lies ruined, the Chang River remains,
Events fade, times shift, words drift idly on.
Below stands a desolate shrine, hard to inquire of—
Ancient locust trees stand bare, bamboos pierce the sky.

---

**That Evening, Lodging Below Jinping Mountain**

The moon is ever lovely to behold,
Yet never so perfectly round as tonight.
I suspect the White Maiden eases some old regret,
Meeting me here before Jinping’s peaks.

---

**Sixteenth Day: Responding in Rhyme to the Magistrate of Fuchang’s Gift**

Dao and righteousness bring joy—how rare the bond,
As the ancients said, knowing others is hard.
Literature does not bind the men of court and market,
Honor and disgrace touch not the cloud-and-water self.

Words delve deeply into the patterns of things,
Speech leaves no trace, all is natural truth.
One day, when the Luoyang fellowship gathers,
Our records will add yet another neighbor.

---

**Seventeenth Day: At Jinping Mountain, Thanking Messrs. Zhang and Sun of the City for Their Gift of Tea**

Mountains like indigo waves, waters like dye,
A single line of thought resists all restraint.
Still bearing the tea shared by my two friends,
I brew it where mist and haze run deepest.

---

**Evening View at Shou’an County**

No need to sigh over life’s glory or disgrace,
Just listen all day to the murmuring stream.
A lone goose vanishes through the dusk’s haze,
Two egrets slice the wild mist on their return.

Fine trees stand like aged ranks below green cliffs,
Lovely peaks encircle the county in emerald.
They say those who slight fame and gain come here,
Stealing moments from time’s relentless flow.

---

**Eighteenth Day: Crossing the Sheep-Dragging Slope, Southward to the Tomb in Yi River**

A three-foot barrow, a hundred-foot hill,
Where a mother’s love for her child lies buried.
This love, even in death, knows no end—
At dusk, I linger and turn back again.

---

**Thoughts on the Cheng Family—Father, Sons, and Brothers—Sent to Them**

Year after year, the season nears mid-autumn,
Fine waters, fine mountains, radiant in play.
This time, my return is hastened for you,
No lingering longer by the Yi River.

The climate is as it was that day,
The mountains and streams seem as of old.
Alone I come, alone I go—
Who understands this feeling?

---

**Nineteenth Day: Returning to Luoyang, Passing Longmen**

To and from Yi River, I cross Dragon Mountain,
Each crossing leaves my mind at ease.
Do not say the scene fails to move the heart—
How can one bear the grip of profit and fame?

No need for external things to toy with the spirit,
Worldly ways are ever fickle, ever fond of the new.
Only the fine wind and moon before the steps remain—
Their pure light still belongs to the idle.

---

**Inscribed at Longmen**

Fused and formed, the years forgotten,
Sheer cliffs and green walls lock in misty sorrow.
Half the great creation is Xia’s kingly labor,
The Yi River’s flow cuts through the mighty mountain.

The Eight Rapids’ roar lingers in the ear,
A river’s scenery all gathers in the pavilion.
Travelers, do not stir your railing-longing—
Countless heroes have idly whitened their heads.

---

**Who wielded the long sword to slay the long flood-dragon?**
The dragon slain, the sword sheathed once more.
Claws and tail coil, congealing into Hua Peak,
Fangs and tusks fierce, knotting into Song’s heights.

Wounds scar two places on the sheer green cliffs,
Blood gushes eastward in surging waves.
Even this beast could not preserve its head and tail—
Beware the clamor of slanderous tongues.

---

**Viewing Yi River from the Stone Pavilion at Longmen**

For days I’ve wandered freely through mist and haze,
Leaning on the railing, gazing at the splendor.
A hundred-foot tower reaches the birds’ path,
A river of mist and water belongs to the monks.

Only when the heart is at ease is there true joy,
Only then does official glory lose its boast.
To roam such scenes on carefree days—
Know then that happiness knows no bounds.

---

**Twentieth Day: Returning to the City, Meeting Old Friends**

Year after year, I wander through mist and haze,
Yet who has ever called me greedy?
Returning, I meet old friends to speak of pure delight—
No need to guard against misfortune with triple-sealed lips.

---

**Twenty-Second Day: Evening Stroll at Tianjin Bridge; Next Day’s Poem**

Last night, an old man strolled to Tianjin,
Pausing often where the bridge stands.
The Luo River’s waters only hear the departing oars,
The west wind merely hastens the traveler’s steps.

Mountains and streams grow somber under cold rain,
Towers and terraces rise uneven, veiled in dusk’s clouds.
Who truly grasps this scene’s clarity?
When frost threatens, the wild geese arrive as guests.

---

**Twenty-Fifth Day: Responding in Rhyme to Wu Chuanzheng, Vice-Director of the Left Treasury**

The light and scenery of Shangyang are fine for reading,
Beyond appearances lies a level path.
A fair moon guides the return at Fragrant Grass Ford,
A swift wind flies past Dongting Lake.

Were it not for frequent journeys to the Red Waters,
How could there be daily joy in the Yellow Sprouts?
Do not say Tianjin Bridge holds no affairs—
Even in leisure, one must apply effort.

*(His poem said: "From now on, the scenes south of Tianjin Bridge / Shall not all belong to Shao Yaofu." Hence these lines.)*

---

**Twenty-Ninth Day: Responding in Rhyme to Lu Gangshu, Registrar of Luoyang**

A sudden shower, late rain’s rustling remains,
By Phoenix Tower, I chance to drive my cart.
Xie Shen’s jade fragment—who can hold it?
Le Guang’s blue sky—how fortunate it stays unobstructed.

Long parted through summer and autumn, what news?
By lamplight, what books do you read?
I regret having no silks to reward Master Cheng—
Vainly I fret and return to my humble hut.

---

*Yichuan Jirang Collection, Volume Five Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Collection, Volume Six*

**For Zhou Shiyan, Magistrate of Pu’an in Jianzhou, Written on Behalf of Shao Yaofu of Yichuan**

To serve as an official—why sigh for what use?
No ascent to heights but begins in humility.
The noble man’s bending and stretching is the Dao itself;
We Confucians prize advance and retreat as circumstance allows.

Even now, where is Pengze’s return?
Yet the eastern gate’s departure may still come in time.
I grieve that the scenes of Yi and Song remain boundless—
A single name, a fine place, yet I seek further emolument.

From Shu to the Three Wu, the distance spans ten thousand li.
Last year, I gazed north; this year, I drive west again.
Jian’ge scrapes the sky and sun;
Qin’s plains bar the imperial capital.

Facing the wind where we recall each other—
Could you drink a cup with me?

---

**Another Quatrain**

Just when elders should gather in fellowship,
The high autumn arrives, desolate and bare.
A handful of yellow blossoms, a flagon of wine—
A year has passed since my old friend went west.

---

**In Response to Secretary Zhao Chongde’s Gift**

People say official life endangers cap and crown;
I cherish my life, far from market and court.
My rustic face cannot bear rushing to the Wei gates;
My idle self is fit only to visit Yang and Liao.

Though my poems lack discipline, a thousand verses remain;
Though rich in clouds and mountains, I have but a gourd of wine.
I dread the carriage’s eastward departure—
From now on, how can I summon the wind and moon?

---

**In Response to Director Wang Buyi’s Gift**

For twenty years I have dwelt in Luoyang,
Watching the world’s affairs swirl like dust.
My form is no more than grass and trees, wild and untrammeled;
My heart resembles an ancient bell or tripod, empty yet resonant.

I have bathed in the benevolent breeze to my very marrow;
Now I marvel at the poetry’s brilliance, like jade and jewels.
Do not let Zhuang Zhou say names and realities are flawed—
It is simply that I lack talent and delight the crowd of monkeys.

---

**In Response to Professor Wei’s Gift**

In this pure age, literature hangs like sun and moon;
Though talentless, I am fortunate to enjoy abundant years.
I wander Mount Tai’s Greater and Lesser Chambers;
I gaze upon the Yi River and the Luo.

Of old, Mencius found enlightenment hard to speak of;
In this age, without Yanzi, who is deemed worthy?
Each time I read to the root of heaven,
I fear the masters will ask of the profoundest mysteries.

---

**In Response to Vice Minister Wu Chongqing’s Gift**

Non-being and being are the ancestral homeland,
Yet all things differ by a hair’s breadth.
Every person may reach it, though I have not;
No thing obstructs—who could hinder whom?

Lost, liver and spleen become Chu and Yue;
Gained, coarse greens become fine millet.
A single word, through the ages, is hard to fathom—
Its wondrous workings must be seen at Lüliang.

---

**In Response to Instructor Sun Chuanshi’s Gift**

South of Tianjin Bridge stands my humble hut,
Where you, my lord, deign to visit by carriage.
Since retiring, I have forgotten restraint;
From long idleness, my learning has grown hollow.

Better to inscribe my name in history’s annals—
Or should I bear plain books and face the clouds and mountains?
A single piece of sincere devotion is hardest to describe;
Perhaps I may yet resemble the Red and Void.

---

**In Rhyme for the Longevity Snow Gathering with Academician Chen Chengbo**

The garden’s flowers renew in a single night;
Jade Terrace’s twelve peaks turn to dust.
In the city, bamboo-leaf wine surges in price;
At the feast, willow catkins vie to learn spring.

The Liang Garden gathering draws all the inked guests—
Who recalls the divine beings of Guye?
My surplus grain need not fill a thousand granaries;
Even a foot’s depth of snow should not be spurned for its frequency.

---

**In Rhyme for the Garden Gathering at the Historiography Bureau, Composed on the Spot**

Bamboo reaches high, pines encircle the pavilion—
Who comes here feels their bones and hair grow pure.
Plum branches, laden with snow, barely crack open;
Water veins, linked to ice, murmur in their flow.

The year’s end in late winter stirs inescapable feeling;
Half-drunk, my sleeves cannot contain my emotion.
Who pities the late Chancellor’s empty name?
How can our Dao be put into practice?

---

**On Zhang Xian of Kuixia’s “Remembering the Past at Baidi City”**

No need to resent Cao’s rise in Xuchang—
Had it not been for Liang and Yi, none could contend for the throne.
The realm divided, wind and thunder raged;
Cutting across the southwest, his might was unmatched.

Travelers come and go, idly pointing;
Historians praise and blame in their reckless prose.
Later generations fail to grasp the meaning of rise and fall—
Look upon the old battleground in the river’s heart.

---

**Leisurely Verses, First Year of Xining (1068)**

Fortunate is the scholar who lives in a flourishing age,
Whose home, though tiled, lies in the central capital.
Fleeting fame and hollow profit are not mine;
Green waters and blue mountains—where are they not found?

To choose a fine site, seek the fairest scenes;
To choose companions, select those who suit you.
Joy in idleness is the idle man’s affair—
Yet it differs from stealing moments of leisure.

---

A six-foot frame, content in the present;
How can I bear to waste the four seasons’ fair days?
In the second and eighth months, the weather shifts between warmth and chill;
With three or five friends, we discuss the Dao and righteousness.

Measure your strength in cups and plates, set out simple fare;
Open your heart to laughter and speech, follow nature’s truth.
I urge you: in such clear and idle matters,
Though old, why grow weary of their frequency?

---

Do not let true qi aid sorrow and grief—
The indignant death of heroes only lengthens the age.
Though many horses leap on the paths,
Beyond the heavens, phoenixes soar.

Three thousand guests may polish their jade edges;
A hundred and two mountains may bear sword points.
All is but a spring dream passing—
What else is worth lamenting?

---

**Awakening from Sleep by the South Window, Gazing at the Spring Mountains**

Waking from sleep by the south window, I gaze at the spring mountains—
Amid the mountains, mist and sleet mingle with haze.
A thousand li cannot escape the clarity of these two eyes;
In a hundred years, I have not seen a single idle man.

Emotions are like falling catkins, without high or low;
The heart is like wandering silk, drifting to and fro.
I fear the hidden birds know this feeling—
Thus they come to the branches, murmuring softly.

---

**Who Has Entrusted Creation to the East Wind?**

Who has entrusted creation to the east wind?
Once given to the east wind, affairs know no end.
Late winter should prepare its plans;
New Year’s dawn must demand its achievements.

Willow tips, warmed, sway gently;
Plum buds, stealing spring, half-reveal their red.
If only the feelings of those times remained—
Light robes fluttering amid the carefree young.

---

**Peach and Plum Verses**

Peach and plum, stirred by the wind, fill the branches with blossoms;
Stirred by the wind, peach and plum scatter apart.
Sorrow and joy follow one another, never leaving my grasp;
Worry and delight both reside in my brows.

When prosperity reaches its peak, it must decline;
When adversity reaches its nadir, it must turn.
Let men of today cease to praise the ancients—
It is only because men of today were born too late.

---

**Heartbreaking Song**

I know not what iron was forged into this needle—
Once forged, it pierces only the heart.
I reckon the human heart is no more than an inch—
When piercing, it must pierce to the fullest depth.

---

**Lament for Two Younger Brothers Who Passed Without Illness**

The love between siblings runs deep, unforgettable;
Even cutting out my heart cannot compare to this grief.

**In Times of Urgent Need**

In times of urgent need, you once aided me—
Yet now, who stands beside me in this hour?
The radiant *di* flowers bloom without peer,
While geese in flight break rank, their formation torn.

From this night of moonlit winds and clarity,
I gaze in desolation at the eastern fence—my heart breaks.
My two brothers lie buried beneath its boughs;
Later, I found his poem from the Double Ninth:
*"Clothes white as the moon of yore,
Flowers yellow as the years before."*
I sought to ask the eastern fence of its tale,
But the fence’s tale had faded into mist—
A language like an omen.

Where now to seek the eastern fence, my heart undone?
From this day forth, its story sinks into silence.
Where once we walked abreast, all has passed away;
No sorrow now compares to this.

Tears have dried, yet grief knows no bound,
Though the Yellow Springs lie not so deep.
How many days must pass ere it fades—
Thirty-two years of harmony and kinship?

---

**Another Poem**

My elder brother named *Yong*, my younger *Mu*—
Brothers in harmony, yet was our bond enough?
In daily life, one always stayed behind,
Serving our parents, raising children as they wished.
Our kind father lived to seventy-nine,
Four young sons ever at his side.
Do you recall the days we played in bright robes,
While joy filled the hall, so full it could be cupped?

Two years ago, our father suddenly passed,
And this year, your life was cut short too.
Now a lone son attends our mother with four young ones,
Each day facing the ancestral altar, weeping face to face.
How many *li* of intestines must I have,
How many *hu* of tears must I shed?
All spent, all shed—what can I do?
The sunlight of past kindness—how can it be repaid?

---

**A Quatrain**

The bond of siblings runs deep,
The joy of *xun* and *chi* long.
To know where it may be forgotten,
See the weeds upon their graves.

---

**Hearing the Cuckoo, Thinking of My Deceased Brother**

I recall last year’s early summer,
When we listened together to the cuckoo’s cry.
This year, the cuckoo returns again—
It is still the same early summer as before.

Even birds know the depth of human longing:
One cry has not ceased before another begins in sorrow.
My heart, following this sound, is already broken—
My soul, chasing this bird, where does it fly?

---

**Inscription for My Deceased Brother Zan**

After my coming, before my going.
At the moment of departure,
There was no yielding between us.

---

**Evening Stroll South of the Southern Garden, Thinking of My Deceased Brother**

South of the southern garden, grass like a mat,
I walk at dusk, the wind clear, no dust in sight.
Unable to share this joy with you,
I wonder if the clouds above are drifting from heaven.

A fleeting piece of this world, come to be human,
Drifting to and fro without cause.
I pity myself—

---

**Self-Compassion**

Heaven covers all without partiality, ancient and modern the same,
Yet the deep bond of siblings is suddenly emptied.
Five or seven years of family hardship,
At sixty, my head winds still afflict me.

Common sense cannot escape worldly customs,
Private plans are hard to entrust to servants.
If only the immortal’s old raft still remained—
The Yi River’s clouds and waters bring boundless joy.

---

**Self-Admonition in the Year Wushen**

Though old, I still long to sing and beat the pot,
Hoping to preserve some measure of Heaven’s harmony.
*Ming Yi* advises concealment—let it end here;
*Wu Wang* warns of calamity—what can be done?

Time flies like an arrow from my head,
Human affairs pass before my eyes like hemp.
If I do not take charge of myself now,
The losses to come will be even greater.

---

**Written on Behalf of Zhao Chongdao, Envoy to Beihai, in the Second Year of Xining**

Since I ceased my recitations,
I have but sung one vast song to the wind.
Parting is easy, reunion hard—my longing knows no end;
Ascending heights, gazing afar—what thoughts arise?

A hundred years are fleeting, time does not return,
A thousand *li* of longing bring even more cares.
Today, this cup is truly the North Sea—
How many like you, so refined, have passed through?

---

**Matching Rhymes with Wang Buyi, Minister of Rites, in His Gift**

I do not let sorrow burden the splendor of things,
Time flies past my eyes swift as a chariot.
Finding joy in contentment, I know my place;
Seeking sufficiency for peace—when will it end?

After meals, I sometimes wander the orchid garden,
After sleep, with nothing to do, I visit the monks.
The wind and moon of Tianjin surpass other places—
I long to share tea with you there.

---

**The Virtuous One’s Song**

The virtuous are hard to find, their thoughts steadfast;
In peace, take care not to presume you are unharmed.
Vying to be first on the path—how treacherous the schemes;
Lingering behind in speech—how rich the flavor.

What tastes sharp often breeds illness,
What pleases the heart often brings misfortune.
Better to seek medicine before falling ill
Than to seek it after the disease has struck.

---

**Two Poems: Inviting Guests to Wine as the Early Plum Blossoms Bloom by the Eastern Pavilion**

**I**
I love the early plum more than the winter plum,
A few branches blooming first, lingering in the sun.
If all on the southern ridges had faded before the year’s end,
How could Luoyang boast its blossoms in the new year’s turn?

Fragrance follows the warm wind, emerging from the valley,
Beauty drifts upon the wine, floating in the cup.
Gather noble guests to grasp the Lord of Spring’s intent—
Do not waste this moment of spring’s arrival.

**II**
Spring’s warmth fills Luoyang,
Do not refuse joy to console this fleeting life.
Deeply cherish wise friends who bring smiles,
Treasure the plum blossoms shining bright before your eyes.

Moreover, the old mountain hermit is somewhat fond of such things—
How pitiful the fragrant wine, so full of feeling!
If not drunk before the cup now,
When else to drink to peace?

---

**Long Chant of the Pure Wind**

In the cosmos, the qi of harmony—
Pure and cool, beyond compare.
With the seasons, it dispels illness,
For the year, it brings abundant harvests.

Rising from the tips of green duckweed,
Coming from beside the emerald trees.
On a moonlit night, it meets me,
Then enters the land of old friends.

Dense leaves sway like layered curtains,
Lush flowers dance in bright adornment.
Two or three distant flute notes,
A thousand strands of drooping willows.

Silken zither notes drift softly,
Deep orchid fragrance lingers long.
Pavilions stand by distant waters,
Terraces near tall bamboo groves.

In midsummer, it drives away the heat,
After rain, it brings the evening cool.
Lightly parting green lotus leaves,
Gently penetrating thin robes.

Waves stir sleeves in playful flight,
Ripples rise in glistening cups.
Idle sorrows cannot cling,
Hidden thoughts take flight.

Swift as riding the heavenly steed,
Sobering as sweet sugarcane juice.
Before me, I wander Penglai,
At my seat, I drift the Xiao and Xiang.

Cannot be bought with gold,
Nor measured by the *dou*.
Relying wholly on virtue,
Stored without need of granaries.

No fear of monopolies,
No worry of bandits or thieves.
Making this my joy,
I have never known sorrow.

---

**Long Chant of the Weeping Willow**

Weeping willows come in two kinds—
Some long, some short in their droop.
Only this long kind,
Is most fitting for quietude.

**Long Chant of the Weeping Willow**

Deep in courtyards after spring’s retreat,
In pavilions when evening scenes unfold.
Too frail to bear the mist’s thick shroud,
Helpless before the lingering sun’s delay.
A drizzling rain has just passed,
A crisp, cool breeze now stirs.

By Zhangtai Street, east and west,
Pillars stand where avenues divide.
Rising eyes meet trees beyond the wall,
Brushing heads, branches cross the path.
Swaying like green silk ribbons,
Drifting as if clad in golden threads.
Tossed and scattered, sunlit catkins fly,
In warm winds, tender strands dance bright.

Threads tug at hearts, inch by inch they break,
Catkins drift into ten thousand homes.
Graceful as Wang Gong’s bearing,
Supple as Zhao Feiyan’s sway.
Slender and fair as young Zhang Xu,
Soft and delicate as Shen Hou’s frail frame.
Lush and trailing, green sweeps the earth,
Feathery and fine, jade fringes the pool.

Enhancing blossoms’ fiery glow,
Enticing grasses’ lush verdure.
Dense, they shroud the mountain lodge,
Sparse, they frame the tavern’s banner.
Given to others, all weep in turn,
Horses neigh as riders pause to gaze.
Shadows swallow the creaking cart,
Beneath their shade, wheels rumble home.

Mournful in the gathering dusk,
Faint in the slanting light.
Vexed by careless plucking,
Grieved by weighty partings.
Prematurely withered, green lines the road,
First to flourish where streams run near.
Beyond the tower, cicadas just begin their cry,
By the bridge, orioles sing again.

Hating how they block the distant view,
Loathing how they mimic painted brows.
Wandering strangers, countless in number,
Which long branch once bound whom?
Frost-withered, all grow gaunt,
Yet next year, they’ll sway anew.

---

**Long Chant of Falling Flowers**

With wine, I battle flowers’ lushness,
Flowers’ lushness makes the wine run deeper.
Flowers fade in ten days’ time,
Yet not a hundred jars of wine are drained.
Still gladdened by petals clinging to sleeves,
Still cherishing those fallen into cups.

Only butterflies are truly passionate,
Bees are but fickle lovers.
Diminished, the radiance on the wall,
Enhanced, the crimson along the path.
Scattered through the deep courtyard,
Adorning the quiet lattice.

Yet I fear they’ll follow the flowing stream,
Still dread they’ll wed the distant wind.
Water flows, ever winding,
Wind blows, now east, now west.
Tattered after late spring’s end,
Scattered in the fading light.

Though pavilions have their masters,
No trace remains of galloping steeds.
Swords depart with favored consorts,
Armies besiege Shi Chong’s halls.
At Mawei, lingering regrets,
At Jingu, all is haste.

Cao Zhi’s verses grow ever poignant,
King Xiang’s dream has reached its end.
Falsely they speak of seeking the Luo River’s shores,
Idly they tell of counting Wu Mountain’s peaks.
Swallows still voice their grievances,
Orioles fail to carry messages.

If moss-coins could buy them back,
Willow threads might mend their fate.
Gazing in vain at the true arbiter,
Mournful, I rail at nature’s craft.
Let them bloom in wild abandon,
Never granted even a moment’s grace.

My heart resists the sweeping broom,
Words fail, my plans are spent.
Exquisite fragrance consumes the marrow,
Peerless beauty slays the hero.
Boast all you like of turning heaven’s will,
Vaunt your world-conquering deeds.

Yet time, once gone, cannot return,
Events, once past, are never repeated.
Blooming and fading stand opposed,
Rise and fall share the same principle.
Those who grasp heaven’s workings but shallowly
Cannot escape this restless longing.

---

**Long Chant of Fragrant Grass**

Let fragrant grasses cease to grow,
Let fragrant cups no longer pour.
If grasses grow without end,
How could the cup ever pause?

After rain, in idle pavilions,
Deep in spring, in small courtyard courts.
At this hour, curtains hang half-raised,
In this moment, wine just sobers the mind.

Tender shoots spread thickly,
Soft green already takes shape.
They soften the last pale rays,
Summon the light chill of dawn.

Quietly gracing the flowered village,
Idly adorning the bamboo grove.
By the stream, gentle waters lap,
On the plain, spring plowing has not begun.

Let not perfumed carriages trample them,
Forbid fine steeds to tread here.
Lying upon them, endless thoughts arise,
Gazing long, emotions overwhelm.

From distant terraces, brows first knit,
From lofty towers, eyes suddenly brighten.
Low, the evening clouds stretch blue,
Faint, the distant mountains fade to green.

Emerald meets the mandarin ducks’ shore,
Lush connects the willows’ isle.
By riverside piers, night sails drop,
On sea islets, evening boats lie crosswise.

A single tune from the garrison’s horn,
A few notes from the herdboy’s flute.
On sandy banks, a pair of egrets descends,
At the ferry, startled geese scatter.

Lush and dense, they cover the march’s route,
Tender and thick, they line the envoy’s path.
Far and wide, they spread along the roads,
Encircling every short and long pavilion.

Fading, the tomb of Qin’s First Emperor,
Scattered, the city of Han’s emperors.
Desolate, the old Bronze Sparrow Tower,
Ruined, the former Golden Capital.

Mist veils the deeds of past dynasties,
Haze dims the names of later ages.
Withered, they still hide cunning hares,
Rotten, they yet breed fireflies.

Though mown, how can they ever be gone?
Scorched, they sprout anew.
Vainly they cloak the burial mounds,
Yet still they long for peace.

---

**Long Chant of Spring Waters**

Spring lingers where waters run clear,
Spring departs, and swimming ceases.
Clearness does not fall short of purity,
Yet spring surpasses autumn’s grace.

Purity found in sunlight’s warmth,
Clarity sought in the cold’s embrace.
A degree beyond mere clarity—
Thus purity is deemed supreme.

Thin ice begins to float,
Light rain just ceases.
Vast and boundless, new isles appear,
Glistening, old shores gleam.

Lotus and water-chestnut leaves still droop,
Cattails and reeds now sprout.
Water-shield and pondweed emerge,
Duckweed and water-milfoil not yet dense.

In warm sun, mandarin ducks bathe,
In misty clarity, kingfishers glide.
Waves calm, twin carp leap,
Wind still, gulls play in flocks.

In western Shu, outings vie in splendor,
In eastern Ou, purification rites are grand.
The Peach Blossom Spring is recognized anew,
The Han River’s girdle-pendants return.

Beneath terraces, waters flow smoothly,
Beside embankments, they wander endlessly.
Before railings, they barely ripple,
Beyond the sky, they stretch ever onward.

Overflowing, the heart grows troubled,
Murmuring, the mind turns deep.
Distant mountains cannot block their course,
Parting shores refuse to hold them back.

By the bridge in the second month,
At the wild ferry in the Three Wu lands.
As before, two oars lie crosswise,
Yet now they send a lone boat forth.

The painter lays down his brush,
The poet leans upon the tower.
Beyond the Yangtze’s flying catkins,
Only parting sorrows stir.

---

**Long Chant of Flowers and Moon**

In youth, I craved but two lines of verse,
All worldly joys seemed folly then.
Now I long to undo past regrets,
Yet how can my spirit match its former fire?

Each time flowers bloom and the moon waxes full,
What is this feeling that returns?
In such moments, without poetry or wine,
I would rather die than awaken.

When flowers meet the bright moon, their spirits soar,
When the moon sees rare blossoms, their radiance unfolds.
Man, flower, and moon become one,
And I feel this body roam the jeweled realm.

Yet I fear the moon may be veiled by clouds,
I dread the flowers may be torn by the wind.

If poetry and wine are not taken up again,
This moment will slip by, becoming but a fleeting guilt.
Fortunately, there remains a long poem to gather,
Fortunately, there remains a clear wine flask to hold.
When poem and wine flask are shared from time to time,
It is as if flowers and moon call to one another.
With flowers but no moon, the flowers age in sorrow,
With moon but no flowers, the moon resents its solitude.
The moon’s resentment can only be told through poetry,
The flowers’ sorrow relies entirely on wine to sustain.
When not a trace of moon’s resentment or flowers’ sorrow remains,
Only then does one know the skill of poetry and wine.
In such carefree leisure beneath the trees,
Who could fault this refined indulgence?

**Visiting the Shangqing Palace with Prefect Li and Academician Ge**
In Luoyang’s second month, spring sways and drifts,
Peach and plum bloom thick as marching screens.
High blossoms and low blossoms link in crimson,
While willows rise even above the highest flowers.
Leisurely accompanying the Prefect beyond the city gates,
We sought the true palace on Mang Hill.
No longer seeing the imperial procession’s western journey,
I stood in the wind all day, lost in melancholy.

**Begging for Bamboo Flute-Plants from the Estate of Minister Li Shaobao**
Wildly planted, idle flowers claim the earth,
Never deigning to linger with ease.
Yet how can one not love their frost-defying integrity—
Or the name still carved upon the flute?
When the phoenix arrives, they will bear fruit,
Where the dragon’s song is learned, can there be no sound?
This recluse begs for a few stalks to plant,
To gain honor from your noble house once more.

**Chant of Longing for the Mountains**
Behold the green mountains and white clouds,
Yet ponder the boundless merit they hold.
Who knows the joy they bring, and for what reason—
Is it merely to sate hunger with herbs?
A thousand clumsy poems fail to express my grievance,
A single cup of fine wine harbors spring anew.
How vast the days and months within the flask—
How many generations in the red dust grow old?

I fear the body may be idle while the heart is not,
Yet if the heart is idle, why dwell in clouded mountains?
If truly at ease in nature’s embrace,
Who would bury themselves in profit and strife?
My actions require no caution, no taboo,
My words need no artifice, no schemes.
Who could have imagined joy would reach such heights—
A heavenly steed leaves no trace, coming and going as it pleases.

**Chant of Resenting the Moon**
It is not that I begrudge gold,
But that Chang’e loves inconstancy.
Just before her ascent, she drips with dew,
No sooner full than the sky darkens.
I lean on the railing again and again,
Pouring fragrant wine, then pouring once more.
If only I could confine her in the women’s quarters,
Yet how deeply she has misled me, before and after.

**Chant of Sorrowing for Flowers**
Three thousand palace ladies in their robes,
Each hoping for favor, each lovely in her own way.
At first bloom, they seem delicate,
Half-opened, they become alluring and rich.
Before the heartwood reveals itself, fragrance already spreads,
As dew-laden powder falls, the soul melts away.
Facing such a fragrant cup, how many thoughts arise—
Yet soon wind and rain will ride roughshod over them.

**Matching Zhang Ziwang’s “Viewing Flowers in Luoyang”**
Creation has never failed mankind,
Myriad reds and purples reveal heaven’s truth.
The city teems with carriages and horses in vain chaos,
For not all who meet spring truly grasp it.

**Short Chant on Falling Flowers**
The garden’s peach and plum scatter in disarray,
Further torn by a wanton wind.
Ever sorrowful when they first fade,
Yet one must recall their unopened beauty.
Alas, how swiftly crimson splendor fades,
Unlike the slow change of green shade.
Ninety days of spring have all but gone—
How can I bear to furrow my brow before the cup?

**Short Chant on Fragrant Grass**
By flowered banks and waters, green as a mat,
Rising and falling through Han and Qin.
Claiming endless land across mountains and rivers,
How many through the ages have grieved for them?
Though severe frost kills, rain revives them,
Though wildfire burns, spring returns.
Yet why does this plant thrive so abundantly by the road?
Farmers forever weary of its encroachment.

**Short Chant on Weeping Willows**
By the stream, brushing the water, they sway,
Further tossed by the relentless wind.
At dusk, they cannot bear the thickening mist,
In deep spring, they chafe at the lingering sun.
Whose ethereal green silk scarf flutters?
Where do the golden-threaded robes dance?
Still fearing the parting lover’s heart is not yet broken,
The sky fills once more with scattered blossoms.

**Short Chant on Spring Waters**
Snow melts, ice thaws, filling the channels,
Kingfishers and mandarin ducks revel in autumn’s pride.
Ever resenting distant mountains that block the view,
Yet doubting if the branching stream can detain them.
Willows along the bank brush lightly,
New reeds by the shore sprout tenderly.
Endless misty waves fill the eyes—
The Three Wu regions send forth a lone boat.

**Short Chant on the Pure Breeze**
The pure breeze’s vigor has not yet fully waned,
Who says heaven’s heart has cast it aside?
Ever steadfast in fasting and solemnity while studying the *Yi*,
Ever ashamed of my carefree ways when composing verse.
The world’s fine scenes all pass before my eyes,
The age’s idle sorrows never touch my brow.
Born in an era of peace, untroubled by strife,
I grow old in these peaceful times.

**Late Spring Letter to Li Shenyan, Dragon Diagram Scholar**
Year after year, I dread the deepening spring,
For each time it deepens, my illness cannot bear it.
A small gathering leaves me wounded by wine,
The weather for tending flowers favors light shade.
The years change as they always have,
Yet the seasons cannot turn back an aging heart.
Only the front pavilion is fit for quiet sitting,
Where I face the wind and long for old friends.

**Early Summer Leisure Chant**
In the depths of green willows, orioles trill,
Their songs still able to delight in peace.
Is it not fortune for one to enjoy long years?
Does heaven not feel for the rare treasures it creates?
When peonies fade, purple cherries ripen,
When peonies bloom, the *ban* orchids flourish.
In the grove, a kind of idle wealth and honor—
Why should I yield to dukes and ministers?

**Letter in Reply to Judge Yao Fuzhou and Section Chief Zhou of the Kaifeng Prefecture**
The ways of the world often thwart our wishes,
To speak of advancement is to know nothing.
Better to ponder idle matters at leisure,
Than to debate endlessly on West Street.
Even with extraordinary talent to move the world,
Alas, my temples are already silvered.
The new moon on the horizon has always been slender,
Not for the sake of painting brows in the human realm.

Your letter says: *"I hope you take care of yourself, for I fear the woods will not long hold you."*
**The Yi River’s “Striking the Earth” Collection, Volume Six Concludes**
**The Yi River’s “Striking the Earth” Collection, Volume Seven**

**Written on Behalf of Shao Yong of Yichuan to Zhang Duhuan, Prefect of Haozhou**

Much ashamed, I too have found no kindred spirit—
Though the music of heaven is heard, it permits no pursuit.
Master Hui’s political acumen was naturally keen,
While Zhuangzi’s wanderings held profound intent.
In leisure, I briefly record the events of a spring;
In old age, my thousand-mile aspirations falter.
Among the friends of the Luo Society, when we meet,
Recite for me the *White-Haired Lament*.

**Three Responses to a Villager Declining Office**

Through life, I’ve done nothing to furrow brows—
The world should have no one who gnashes their teeth at me.
Why waste rain to hasten the fall of blossoms?
How could spring be needed to adorn old things?
Fortunate to live under Yao and Shun as true sovereigns,
I let the recluses Chao and You remain outer ministers.
A sick man of sixty should know his place—
No need for supervisory officials to press their appeals.

**Matching the Rhyme of Vice Director Wang Anzhi**

I fear my fellow villagers may not yet understand,
But once they know me deeply, what doubt remains?
In poverty, accepting salary is permissible;
In old age, taking office is hard to justify.
I have my forests and streams to settle my plain aspirations;
In death, my lack of talent will not trouble the imperial court.
If Xun and Yang had upheld our Confucian bounds,
They’d have spared themselves Han Yu’s critique of minor flaws.

**Matching the Rhyme of Liu Zhifang’s Gift**

The Creator’s craftsmanship holds profound intent—
Where I find joy is in the mountains and forests.
In youth, frequent illness kept me from seeking office;
In old age, lack of talent makes my heart unmoved.
By quiet waters, I walk amid flowers and moonlight;
In fragrant orchid breezes, I rest beneath pine shade.
At leisure by the window, I sleep at ease—
Wishing for enfeoffment or gold bestowed.

**Written on Behalf of Wang Shengzhi, Academician, Thanking Him for the Gift of Lai Stone Tea and Wine Vessels**

On Eastern Mountain lies a stone like jade,
Which artisans carve to hold wine.
You, sir, obtained it but cherished it unused,
And with great care sent it to this forest-dwelling elder.
This elder has always used clay cups—
Startled, he dares not lift it with his hands.
He sternly warns his children not to damage it,
Sealing it away to await a worthy friend.
Yet I know not when that worthy friend will return—
A man’s achievements remain unfulfilled.
The court first heeds the words of the concerned:
Today, none compare to the two barbarian foes.
The Han’s six commanderies were bounded by Liaoxi,
The Tang’s eight prefectures cut off beyond the mountains.
Beyond lie Gansha, Gan, and Liang—
The Central Plains have long been unable to reclaim them.
Why further bait them with gold and silk,
Burdening our people and hindering our arms?
Were it not for a bold strike from the court,
How could the frontier crush these decaying foes?
Drive them out past their stinking lands—
West through Yumen, north beyond the passes.
With the foxes beneath the city walls gone,
The wolves on the roads will scatter of themselves.
The sun blazes bright on the celestial thoroughfare,
While ominous vapors and shifting omens hide in dust.
Upon success, he refuses the highest rewards,
Returning his seal and gold greater than a peck.
Begging leave of the sovereign, he departs the capital—
His homeward saddle brushes the willows of Heavenly Street.
A thousand officials, like jade walls, block the road to detain him,
But he lifts his face, cracks his whip, and does not look back.
Villagers line the road to welcome the great prefect;
Drunk, he is borne by banners and gleaming brocade.
Alighting, he visits his ancestors’ graves and resumes governance,
Yet does not call at the yamen—only at the homes of kin and old friends.
Only then do I know this vessel has its time of use—
I shall offer it to you for long life.

**At Chongde Pavilion, Responding to Various Gentlemen on Silent Chan**

The vast sky races with the sun’s wheel—
Why trouble yourself to distinguish root from dust?
The roc’s ten-thousand-mile journey needs no carriage;
The crane’s three-thousand-year lifespan has its own spring.
Alchemy turns lead and tin to gold—yet it is false;
Painting a horse in ink seeks truth in vain.
Look upon this wind-whipped, freezing night—
Who stands firm at the gate?

**Leaning on the Railing of the Small Pavilion in the Celestial Palace**

This frail, six-foot frame, sick and weakened,
Fits the daily deepening of decline.
All the clarity I regret is in my heart—
Not a word can be entrusted before it’s too late.
Gold lies hidden in sand, yet one must sift to find it;
Jade is embedded in stone—how can one doubt?
This sorrow lingers in the autumn dusk’s west wind,
As I lean on every railing, unknown to others.

**Written on Behalf of Wu the Daoist of Yuntai Abbey, Huashan**

The central peak of Mount Hua soars five thousand *ren*,
Beneath it, the great Dao sees travelers come and go.
One glance back from horseback in those days—
A decade later, my dreaming soul still crosses the pass.
All my life, I’ve loved mountains, yet they never sate me;
From here, I’ve seen all the mountains under heaven.
To find another like Mount Hua is rare—
It lets a man while away his whole life in leisure.

**Written on Behalf of Zhang Wentong, Attaché in Chang’an**

No learning, no schemes—
My breast has long been empty.
My withered guts delight in wine;
My ailing eyes fear books.
By the Luo’s banks, wrapped in wind;
At Tianjin Bridge, after a light rain.
An old friend lies a thousand miles away—
Gazing toward him, what are my thoughts?

**Matching a Poem on Hearing Han Weigong’s Departure to Govern Yongxing, Passing Through Luoyang**

Aiding three reigns as Grand Minister,
His name endures through ages as a foundational merit.
He nurtured peaches and plums across the world;
He rode the winds and waves through half the seas.
In the tiger’s tent, the night’s chill steels his heart;
In the Phoenix Pool, warm waves leave his post still vacant.
The sovereign’s reign is in its prime, with Guo Ziyi at hand—
The lands of He and Huang are not worth attacking.

**Written on Behalf of Zhang Jingzhen, Salt Transport Commissioner of Baibo**

Autumn enters the mountains and rivers with majestic air—
I cannot bear this idle gazing in old age.
Our golden friendship weighs heavily on my endless thoughts;
The bonds of brotherhood stir endless feelings.
A half-finished game of chess passes the white day;
A single hairpin of gray hair tangles in the west wind.
Only you and your father have known me long—
Our pine and cassia hearts share the same hue.

**Written on Behalf of Zhang Taibo, Magistrate of Yinjiang**

I still recall that year sweeping our humble hut,
Brothers together at the start of our official careers.
A lifetime forgets the commonplace—
Yet a thousand miles still exchange letters as if face-to-face.
I know from afar that the winds and moon of Siming are fine;
The rivers and mountains are even more so in late autumn.
A single sail has not yet found time to fly free—
In vain, I see your father inquiring after me.

**First Signs**

To recognize the first signs is to be one of us—
Beware of lightly joining the world’s vulgar laughter.
Better to display clumsiness before the crowd
Than to jest in solitude.
A thousand strange flowers, in every form, yield to the eye;
One bright moon, a single wheel, always enters the heart.
With such moments, how could time be spent in vain?
I know the joy of being human.

**Western Pavilion in Late Autumn**

Around the railings, chrysanthemums bloom in unison;
Doors, windows, and pavilions are all fragrant.

**In High Spirits**

In high spirits, one cannot but sing and chant;
In times of joy, one again encounters abundance.
Deep autumn’s scenery is fittingly fine,
And in old age, sinews and bones remain roughly sound.
After drinking, why not ascend for a view?
Amid piled rosy clouds lingers the setting sun.

**Leisurely Stroll by the Heavenly Ford**

Within Luoyang’s walls, I roam freely east and west,
