Twenty years of indolence now fully spent.
Old friends abound, their kindness warm and deep,
While the capital’s grandeur exudes ease and grace.
The pond, level, resembles rivers and lakes;
The woods, still, evoke mountain valleys.
No need for deeds to shake the world—
The joy of quiet dwelling is boundless.

**In Reply to Two Gentlemen of Chang’an, Zhang and Qiang**

Your poems surpass treasures of linked cities,
Yet my talent falls short of Mi Heng’s.
I thought my plight like the steadfast boat would never meet favor,
But now, to my surprise, I am honored with splendid robes.
After rain on Min and E peaks, their sharpness is revealed;
After frost on winds and moon, their clarity appears.
Ancients spoke of few who could respond—
Was the "White Snow in Spring" an empty name?

**Letter in Reply to Prefect Zhang of Huainan**

Seeking fish by climbing trees is surely futile,
But seeking roasted fish by following fish may work.
Better to refuse the great goblet while drunk
Than to seek fine medicine after falling ill.
Do not plant rootless lingzhi—why bother?
Peaches of immortality bear fruit without difficulty.
Your kindness runs deep;
A mustard seed’s honor is still honor.

**Occasional Verse**

The consummate master refuses imitation,
Yet to act otherwise is to abandon the golden chariot.
To claim nothing is difficult under heaven
Is to deny true men exist in the world.
Heaven’s will aligns with the times for good measure;
Human nature finds peace in contentment.
Between heaven and man lies but a hair’s breadth—
Beyond it, all is as distant as the Five Lakes.

**Letter to a Friend**

Two decades have passed since we parted,
Years like arrows—only sighs remain.
Though long separated by hardship, east and west,
We exchanged earnest letters before and after.
Homeland’s mountains and rivers live now in dreams;
Old friends and kin lie half in ruins.
When shall we recount those days again,
Laughing into the west wind as we crack open wine jars?

**Wind Blows the Leaves (Third Year of Xining)**

The wind blows the leaves, not the roots—
Beware of bitterly pleading your roots’ case.
The Son of Heaven’s old capital is pleasant to dwell in;
The Sage’s lesser affairs are too trivial to discuss.
In the land’s long years, immortals are companions;
In the nest of peace, wealth and honor abide.
Having traversed ten thousand waters and a thousand mountains,
Returning, I recognize my true self.

**Leisurely Stroll**

I often recall sweeping my humble hut in those days,
Never letting the three paths grow wild.
I wished to be the world’s dragon-slayer,
But would I read unholy books?
Understanding fortune and misfortune, I knew when to advance or retreat;
Seeing heaven and earth, I discerned near and far.
Once I grasped the Way’s circular nature,
Not a trace of idle vexation remained in my breast.

**Seeking the Azure Sky in Wu and Yue**

You rush to Wu and Yue to find the azure sky,
Unknowing it lies before your eyes.
To see it with open eyes is still flawed;
To seek it with raised head is without cause.
Yan Yuan was in his "like a fool" days,
Mencius in his "unmoved" years.
How to find time to wander the jewel markets,
Loving others’ pearls and jades while fretting over coins?

**Buying Divination to Resolve Doubts**

Buying divination is buying doubt—
With deep illness, what medicine can cure?
Dreams within dreams, layer upon layer of delusion;
Forests on beds, error upon error.
Liezi riding the wind still depended on something;
The boaster chasing the sun surely grew weary.
Much toil, yet no achievement—
I’ve trodden all the world’s idle paths.

**Drinking Before Flowers**

They say beyond things lie misty clouds,
But are misty clouds beyond things worth boasting?
If you make comparisons your joy,
Then freedom from care is the immortal’s home.
A century’s fleeting light cannot be detained;
Ten days of fragrance depart unchecked.
To drink with flowers is no betrayal of wine;
To face flowers without wine is to wrong the flowers.

**Strolling in the Rear Garden After Spring’s End**

Green trees form a canopy in the sun;
Yellow orioles converse in pairs.
The small channel shimmers freshly;
New bamboo stands in uneven rows.
Leaning on my staff, I chant idly for long;
With a child in tow, my steps slow.
The fine breeze knows my intent,
Blowing toward me again and again.

**Letter to Wu Chuanzheng, Vice Magistrate**

Your steadfast heart is rare in this world;
The events of that day, this year, are hard to trace.
Snow and frost, though never mild, are not unbearable;
Pines and cassia never change their hues.
Scholars of Luoyi still trust me;
Only you know the winds and moon of Tianjin.
My dreaming soul, unaware of Luoyang’s distance,
Still visits you as in days of old.

**Gardens and Pools of Luoxia**

The gardens and pools of Luoxia need no gates;
No need to seek spring in other grotto-heavens.
Even unrestrained wanderers cannot match the idle guest;
Who asks the owner when entering everywhere?
The smaller pavilions’ railings frame flowers’ beauty;
The overgrown terraces reveal scenes’ truth.
Empty fame has misled endless affairs—
Not all empty fame reaches one’s person.

**Dream of Visiting the Eastern City to Call on Yang Yingzhi, Magistrate of Luoyang**

Last night, a clear dream took me east of the city,
Where streams divided, paths ran straight.
It was like riding a raft on the Milky Way,
But without Yan Zi to verify my tracks.

**Letter to Former Luoyang Recorder Lu Gangshu, Secretariat Drafter**

When your term in Luoyang ended, you shook your robes,
How could dust defile your distant journey?
Fortunately, the Way meets with clear days and months;
With bright eyes, you should see old rivers and lakes.
Only the worthy know when to act and when to stop;
True men can bend and stretch.
Why not return with the receding waters?
The three Wu regions remain as they were before.

**Reply to One Requesting Green Reed**

Among praiseworthy grasses,
Do not rank it with mugwort and wormwood.
Lush and spreading, it resembles bamboo;
Its verdant hue suits autumn best.
At first rustling in wind and rain,
It stirs thoughts of rivers and lakes.
Though it serves no use in the world,
It offers this brief respite.

**Carefree Chant**

The Way is fundamentally level,
Yet few are willing to walk it.
Gaining the heart brings no rich flavor;
Losing footing means deep pits.
Without penetrating heaven and earth,
How can one understand life and death?
In this matter,
One must be sincere and clear.

**Human Life**

People lament insufficiency,
But what more is there to seek beyond sufficiency?
Though my life is not yet sufficient,
It is also free of care.
Harmony with heaven is nurtured with wine;
True joy is drawn forth with poetry.
Do not doubt that time will hurry you—
It hastens men to early gray.

**Preserving Harmony**

**Entering Peaceful Abode at Night**

At night I enter my Peaceful Abode,
At dawn I rise to drink Great Harmony.
Probing the spirit, I know the Dao is at ease;
Cultivating simplicity, I gain much from heaven.
Sun and moon submit to their ceaseless sway,
Mountains and rivers endure their endless carving.
Those who seek to be such as this—
When all is said and done, who are they?

**What Moves Men Deeply**

What stirs the heart so deeply?
One searches yet finds no trace.
The Two Modes ever rest in hand,
The myriad transformations no longer weigh on the mind.
Jade lies hidden within stone,
Gold is often found in sand.
Though clear, it defies all reason—
One must sink into profound reflection.

**An Impromptu Chant**

A hair’s breadth apart,
Yet a thousand mountains, ten thousand peaks lie between.
Though one may forget sleep and food,
One will not abandon the workings of the world.
Not that I demand perfection of others,
But how hard it is to speak of this!
The heart of heaven is not far—
Yet once far, there is no return.

**Each Time I Visit My Eastern Neighbor**

Each time I visit my eastern neighbor,
My eastern neighbor grows ever more earnest.
Since I come, I am always filled with shame;
When we meet, we are as close as kin.
Food and drink follow our preferences,
Even the children obey of their own accord.
In my homeland there is such joy—
Why seek further benevolence?

**Each Time I Pass the Eastern Street**

Each time I pass the eastern street,
The eastern street resents my late arrival.
All we do is idle chatter,
Unmindful of how freely we open our hearts.
I possess a thousand joys,
Others harbor not a single doubt.
Half-drunk on joyful wine,
It is not yet late, yet I do not return.

**The Noble Man in Fellowship**

The noble man in fellowship
Is never without caution.
The petty man in fellowship
Is never without fault.
Only this true joy
Should be cherished and treasured.
In later years, when clouds and waters grow distant,
It may be hard to find again.

**Heaven’s Two Qi**

Heaven possesses two qi:
One yin, one yang.
From yin’s error come serpents and scorpions;
From yang’s harmony arise phoenixes.
If only serpents and scorpions would die,
They would no longer bring calamity to men.
If only phoenixes would be born,
They would forever bring good fortune to the state.

**No Guest Can Alter Heaven’s Will**

No guest can alter heaven’s will,
Yet some provide provisions for thieves.
At midday, brawls are often seen;
In the sixth month, frost descends.
Books lie unread,
Food goes untasted.
Meals barely fill a jar,
Books pile empty upon the bed.

**Love of Life, Fear of Death**

To hate death and love life—
Such is the constant nature of past and present.
If men’s hearts could give rise to affairs,
The world would know no war.
Even grasses and trees flourish in harmony;
How could mountains and rivers not be at peace?
Why then is there no joy in plow and cup,
No celebration of peace?

**Releasing the Small Fish**

Tiny and frail, not worth keeping—
To lose this is to lose a life.
I release you to rivers and lakes,
Where you may swim freely, far from pot and cauldron.
Beware the nets that lie deep,
Take care not to mistake the hook.
The world is full of those who cook—
Do not let yourself become their dish.

**Matching Rhymes for Minister Tian’s Gift**

Each day I stroll my garden,
Where the pure wind costs nothing.
Amid the city, I find rustic scenes,
Beneath the bamboo, I play with flowing springs.
Though I see no cause for sighing,
Why not nurture my boundless spirit?
Yet I am ashamed before the scholars of the world—
Their words on the Dao have not forgotten the fish-trap.

**Begging for Bamboo to Make a Flute**

The people of Luoyang love to plant flowers,
While I alone love to plant bamboo.
Though our loves differ,
Our hearts are equally content.
Flowers bloom red for but ten days,
Bamboo stays green through the years.
Both receive the grace of rain and dew,
Yet only bamboo escapes the shame of frost and snow.

**Matching Rhymes for Secretary Wang Buyi’s Invitation to Drink**

Through hardship, I recall Master Fuqiu,
Yet my homeland offers ample delight.
Before the wind, I am startled by white hair;
After the rain, I welcome the new autumn.
Though my official ambitions are faint,
My joy in climbing heights never fades.
The world’s vain worries
Never reach my heart.

**Again Matching Rhymes for Secretary Wang Buyi’s Gift**

The weather turns cool and fine—
Where is there a place not fit for wandering?
On the day the wild geese arrive as guests,
The hawk’s ambition stirs in autumn.
Forgetting form, we revel all night;
One misstep, and a life is lost.
How many on rivers and lakes
Have yet to reach their journey’s end?

**Matching Rhymes with the Three Secretaries Wang Visiting My Humble Abode**

To be content is to find beauty in certainty.
In Luoyang, poetry has its society,
And verses flow like divine speech from horseback.
White-haired, our friendship runs deep;
Yellow chrysanthemums mark the season’s renewal.
To be visited warms the heart;
To feel shame is to know the Dao is not pure.
Amid the desolate western wind,
With leisure, I am half an ancient man.

**A Letter in Place of Writing to Censor Lü Xianke of Nanyang, Guardian of the Imperial Counsel**

Two cycles of stars and frost have passed since we parted,
Our rise and fall unmarked by word or sound.
In the groves, laughter and talk must return to me;
The world’s peace and peril should rest with you.
Before the Son of Heaven, you often speak boldly;
On Hundred-Flower Isle, you move with ease.
I wonder, on nights of white moon and clear wind,
If you recall the old fisherman of Yi River.

**To Attendant Wu Chuanzheng**

How lonely the wind and moon of Tianjin Bridge!
Like me, do you remember them after autumn’s passing?
Each time I rely on the clear waves to send my voice,
I wonder if it ever reaches the Eastern Capital.

**To Former Registrar of Luoyang, Lu Gangshu, and Collator**

Having completed your term in Luoyang, you return to Wu and Kuaiji,
A man of grand ambition, your aspirations yet unfulfilled.
When you reach the finest landscapes,
Raise your cup without hesitation and gaze toward Tianjin.

**Matching Rhymes for Censor Zhang of Huainan**

The courtyard wutong leaves half-yellow,
The chrysanthemums by the fence just receive the frost.
As evening approaches, my mood turns heavy;
I take up my wine and gaze southwest.
I gaze for you but do not see you—
Only the wild geese flying south.
Just as I think to send a letter,
A few lines form of their own accord.

**Written the Day Before the Double Ninth**

Lately, illness has left me speechless,
Longing only to drowse in drunken sleep.
New wine arrives just in time for the Double Ninth,
Fine blossoms greet the season of Little Spring.
I know my fate is meager, looming overhead,
And wish no more affairs would come before my eyes.
Only the setting sun over Tianjin Bridge remains,
The sound of water still murmuring as of old.

**On the Double Ninth, Ascending the Stone Pavilion (Three Poems)**

**I**
Human feelings, once seen, are many;
Worldly ways, long known, are familiar.
After the fact, one gnaws with regret;
At the loss of things, one guards against hindrance.
Muddy water must be settled and strained,
Dusty clothes must be shaken and brushed.
If one must discuss the helmsman,
Who is truly skilled?

**II**
Events arise in a moment,
Once past, they cannot return.
At the time, they seem deeply lovable;
Afterward, they are unbearable to behold.
Summer departs—no need to speak of heat;
Winter comes—then one discusses the cold.
If men could grasp this principle,
Worries and troubles would find no purchase.

**III**
On this year’s Double Ninth,
Leaning on the railing, the air feels slow.
Though clouds and mist have grown faint,
The woods have not yet wholly withered.

**Where Heaven and Earth Open Their Embrace**

Where heaven and earth open their embrace,
Mountains and rivers delight the eye.
I lean on every railing in vain—
Who understands this feeling?

---

**Matching Rhyme in Reply to a Friend**

A million armored troops stir the frontier dust,
The court’s commission is no light trust.
Though the barbarian’s schemes run shallow,
A true statesman’s loyalty must be pure.
On the frontier, songs of sorrow rise in anger;
In the groves, drunken toasts turn to bitterness.
I long to offer a single word of good counsel,
Yet fear my friend may not know me truly.

---

**An Occasional Verse**

Wealth and rank breed arrogance in men,
And human feelings shift with time.
Virtue does not scorn others—
Over time, hearts turn ever more toward it.

Virtue follows constant principles;
Wealth and rank know no fixed term.
When frost comes, the weeds wither,
But the pines and cypresses grow lush in snow.

Most men flock to the powerful,
Yet when their fire fades, they depart.
The noble man fares well in hardship—
Through long restraint, his bonds grow firm.

When the blaze dies, its power does not return;
When affection is steadfast, men do not leave.
Strangers may seem like kin,
While kin may seem like strangers.

What matters is the closeness of heart and deed—
In closeness, good and evil part ways.
Is there truly nothing of worth in the world?
Surely there are men beneath heaven.

Having seen the stench of the foul,
One must think of jade and stone burned together.
How can the temper of the times endure,
Ever like spring in Luoyang?

---

**Untitled Verse**

Once I did not refine things,
And was misled by them.
Now I do not refine men,
Yet am met with their anger.

The misguidance of things can be explained,
But human wrath is hard to appeal.
I admit my faults to others,
And they, too, forgive me.

---

**No Wine to Drink**

Since the new laws took effect,
I’ve often suffered from an empty jar.
Whenever guests and friends arrive,
We idle the whole day together.

If I must beg from others,
Even close kin have none to spare.
If I must pawn my clothes to buy wine,
How can this last long?

---

**Reading Tao Yuanming’s “Returning Home”**

Return home! Let me follow my true nature,
Though affairs are past, my intent feels new.
When did I not live in such an age?
Yet how rare to meet a man like this.

As dusk nears, I sigh at the gathering gloom;
Facing the flourishing, I rejoice in the trees’ joy.
Pity that six hundred years later,
Another idle man follows in his dust.

---

*Yichuan Jirang Collection, Volume Seven Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Collection, Volume Eight*

**Visiting the Zhang Brothers of Nanyuan and Staying Overnight**

Mid-Autumn’s weather, fair as one could wish,
I came to Nanyuan to meet the reclusive Zhangs.
Too fond of drink to leave while dusk grew late,
I slept amid the mist and clouds of Water-Crystal Hall.

---

**Touring Longmen with Wang Anzhi, Vice Minister**

By nature fond of seeking secluded spots,
I visit Dragon Mountain four or five times a year.
Whether coming or going, I keep no count—
No worldly gain could make me linger there.

For days we lingered, watching the Yi’s flow,
Then divined a night on Incense Mountain’s stone tower.
A cool breeze stirred thoughts of distant things,
And misty rain played with the high autumn air.

---

**Returning to the City, Again in the Same Rhyme**

In high spirits, I explored Dragon Mountain’s depths,
As if wandering through a painted scene.
Regretting no fine wine to match the view,
Just when I wished to stay, I could not remain.

---

**Another Poem**

Early autumn’s light rain brings a gentle chill,
I lean on the eastern pavilion’s railing.
Who would have thought, amid such misty haze,
The pine study would seem a painted scroll?

*(The Pine Study, home of Anzhi’s younger brother, lies west of the water.)*

---

**Inscribed for Minister Zhang’s Hermitage**

Having served as the Three Dukes and lived long,
He ranks first in human blessings and virtue.
Yet his thatched hut still recalls his lord—
Right before the Ten-Thousand-Year Peak of Mount Song.

---

**A Letter on Behalf of Cheng Zhengshu**

Your venerable father governs west of Sword Gate,
His filial joy a model for the world.
The Tang ministers’ grandeur is still clear today,
While Shu’s people revel in past prosperity.

Where the Begonia Isle’s oars once paused,
By Golden Wild-Goose Bridge his horse once stood.
I know he frets over the realm’s affairs—
Who else but you would not forget your lord?

---

**Year’s End, Self-Admonition**

Once my ambitions soared like autumn’s might,
Now, recalling them, I blush with shame.
All forced schemes end in petty toil,
While the true Dao, unattained, drifts far away.

In stillness, one’s essence is hard to hide;
In old age, books gain deeper savor.
At ease, I converse with guests,
Yet piled memorials mislead lords and nobles.

Fortunate to be seen with favor by the wise,
I heed not my wife and children’s complaints of gray hair.
How dare I compare to Zheng Zhen of Valley Mouth?
Or scheme like the youths of Shouling?

Cauldron’s dragons and tigers, neglected in guard,
Chessboard’s rivers and mountains, abandoned in play.
Awakening from a nap by the sunny window,
A few soft bird calls just cease.

The finest woods and springs are bought with verse,
The fairest winds and moons repaid with wine.
Three hundred and sixty days fly like arrows—
How could I let my heart sink into idle sorrow?

---

**Joyful Song, Fourth Year of Xining (1071)**

At sixty-one, my body is not yet frail.
I’ve lived through two generations—
How could this be called an early end?
Moreover, I am still hale,
And free from care or vexation.
Why should I not rejoice?
Fine days are not few.

---

**To Li Jingzhen, Grand Master**

By the flowers, a quiet couch for idle rest,
Beneath the bamboo, a bright window for solitary thought.
What need for words to proclaim “the house is at peace”?
The green birds in the grove already know.

---

**Reflections**

Snake’s head and lizard’s tail are not the same—
Killing many is deemed a merit.
The four seasons’ winds and moons are infinitely fair—
Do not let idle matters trouble your heart.

---

**To Qin Boming, Minister of War in Bozhou**

The land of Three Rivers lies at the center’s sun,
Its qi nurtures rare blossoms to royal splendor.
A connoisseur of fine flowers is not far—
And fine flowers, ungrudging, offer full fragrance.

For years, worldly affairs have piled up,
Why frown before the blossoms?
A thousand schemes turn hair to white—
Better to leave the heart unburdened.

Amid boundless winds and moons, so full of feeling,
Let rich wine flush your face.
Yet men create their own doubts and barriers,
Weighing gains and losses, setting more traps.

Though poor, I sleep late without harm;
Others may fret, but I am at ease.
Tempering worldly passions brings moments of joy—
And fresh poems still number in the hundreds.

Where the heart returns is where the heart is absent,
Yet when the heart is absent, where can it be found?
If absence of heart meant absence of affairs,
Why then does gold form in water?

---

**Wine Holding Flower Shadows**

Wine brims with crimson, filled with flower shadows,
Poured into the harmony of heaven and earth within my breast.
I love best that singular savor—
Half-drunk, I am one with the Great Beginning.

---

**Another Farewell Poem**

Qin’s First Emperor once unified the Nine Provinces,
Yet the Nine Provinces soon passed to Liu.
Let others boast of their brilliance—
They merely toil in vain, their hair turning white.

---

**Thinking of an Old Friend**

Though the winecup is full,
An old friend a thousand *li* away—what can I do?
Willows trail by the pavilion, their branches slender,
Flowers near the eaves, their fragrance richer.

---

**Thanking Professor Wang Pingfu for Tea and a Poem on Flower-Viewing**

The Grand Academician knows flowers well,
And in their essence, finds it through tea.
After brewing amid ten thousand reds’ fragrance,
He sends the finest to the Celestial Ford’s first household.

---

**Viewing Flowers at Nanyuan**

On the third day of the third month, flowers in full bloom,
I climb the spring terrace with kin and old friends.
Usually sober, now I am drunk—
And even drunk, I can still raise a great cup.

Before the flowers, I drink; before the flowers, I grow drunk,
Drunk, I grasp the flower branches and sing.
Let the flowers not laugh at this white-haired man—
The white-haired man has seen many fine flowers.

---

**Admiring Peonies Alone**

Viewing flowers is easy; knowing them is hard.
Only the connoisseur leans on the railing.
In rain and dew’s work, one sees creation’s art,
In immortals’ ranks, their beauty is fixed.

Common blooms may be called fashionable,
But only the extraordinary are deemed transcendent.
One’s allotted share is not shallow—
It takes a lifetime of leisure to deserve them.

---

**Asking Spring**

In the third month, spring departs and cannot be detained,
Spring’s departure—how can its intent be entrusted?
All who speak of returning must return home—
Where, then, is spring’s home?

Where, after all, does spring return?
Countless spring grievances remain unspoken.
I would entrust the warbler to ask the reason,
But the cuckoo cries, “Better to go!”

Spring comes, sorrow departs—all for the flowers;
Spring goes, sorrow arrives—then clings to wine.
Long resenting sorrow’s weight and wine’s weakness,
Does spring, now ill, know the flowers’ plight?

---

**Self-Admonition in the Peaceful Nest**

Things, when well grasped, end in beauty;
Affairs, when cunningly schemed, lack justice.
Not stirring waves in the world,
One keeps ice and coals from the heart.

**Disasters and Blessings**

Disasters fall like autumn leaves before the frost,
Wealth and honor bloom red after the rain.
Creation’s design is clear—yet none perceive it:
How much merit lies in withering and flourishing?

**Exhortation Before Flowers**

Spring lingers when drinking before the flowers,
But when spring departs, the flowers fade.
To face the flowers and not drink wine
Is to let joy dwindle away.
Drink wine before the flowers,
And view them best when drunk.
To not drink wine before the flowers
Is to waste a year’s delight.

**After Writing *The Supreme Principles Governing the World***

Simplicity scatters, and the human Dao is established;
Laws begin with the Sovereign Fuxi.
Years and months shift and change,
Yet records are hard to verify in detail.
The Two Emperors initiated abdication,
The Three Kings rectified the social order.
The Five Hegemons relied on strategic terrain,
The Seven States vied for supremacy.
The Two Han soared like dragons and phoenixes,
The Three Kingdoms fled like tigers and wolves.
The Western Jin indulged in refinement,
While rebels flocked to the northern wastes.
The Eastern Jin cultivated purity,
Passing its fragrance to Song, Qi, and Liang.
By the time of Chen, it was not worth reckoning—
The southern lands became a scene of sorrow.
Later Wei exploited Jin’s weakness,
Sweeping away chaos to bring brief peace.
Yet before long after moving to Luoyang,
Rumors spread of eastern and western generals.
Northern Qi raised its flickering torch,
Later Zhou raced like a shooting star.
Sui achieved unification,
Paving the way for mighty Tang.
The Five Dynasties passed like inns,
The realm plunged into turmoil.
Had no true lord emerged,
How could the center have been secured?
A domain of ten thousand *li*,
Four thousand years of rise and fall.
Five hundred sovereigns took the throne,
Seventy states carved out frontiers.
Some unified the Six Directions,
Others controlled but a single region.
Some founded dynasties early or late,
Others reigned briefly or long.
Some rallied as their rule crumbled,
Others seized power in their prime.
Some disasters arose without cause,
Some fortunes turned to misfortune.
Some perils came from outer defenses,
Others erupted within palace walls.
Some ailments stemmed from allies,
Others festered in vital organs.
Laughter and talk sowed strife,
Feasts and banquets opened battlefields.
A single outburst of desire,
A clash of profit and harm.
Fierce struggles led to devouring,
Endless calamities ensued.
Mountains and rivers barely marked the land,
Tombs and mounds soon lay desolate.
Brambles were hard to clear,
Orchids and irises failed to flourish.
Dragons and snakes slithered across the plains,
Jade and stone mingled on Kunlun’s slopes.
Those who planned well were called Zhou or Confucius,
Those who harmonized were deemed Lao or Zhuang.
Yet how can one speak of one’s own afflictions?
How can all cares be forgotten?

**Gathering to Drink in the Dao**

What the crowd delights in
Is but clamor and dust.
What my friends delight in
Is pure fragrance alone.
Pure fragrance needs no music—
It dwells beside the distant past.
The distant past holds no other secret:
Harmonious *qi* ever swirls.
In our village, old friendships thrive,
With talent and culture abounding.
A single day’s visit brings joy,
As if the tenth month birthed spring.
Luoyang, ancient sacred land,
Where the Duke of Zhou once expounded.
The four seasons keep their proper course,
The four directions lie equidistant.
Each generation has its heroes,
Known for their many scholars.
Even its flowers and trees
Are unmatched in the world.
To encounter such a scene,
At such a moment,
And to laugh aloud—
How many in the world can do so?
Pure hearts pierce metal and stone,
Lively discourse startles ghosts and gods.
Heaven and earth become a single finger,
Wealth and rank like floating clouds.
In prosperous times, one eases relief efforts,
By daylight, one leisurely weaves plans.
Better to join the Wine Immortal,
And once more face this noble one.
Six hundred *li* of Shang and Yu,
Forty thousand catties of gold—
Cannot buy this joy,
So how can lesser things compare?

**Tipsy Revelry**

When the fence is breached and hats are askew,
The toad in the moon leaps to the sea’s edge.
When the small cart is overturned,
One visits the mountains and returns to the Celestial Ford.

**Thoughts on Chen Zhimò of Zhengzhou, Moved by His Passing Without Ever Meeting**

Exquisite things must be peerless,
Extraordinary people must be rare.
If creation brings them into being,
It is surely no easy feat.
One may hear of them across ages,
Or see them in one’s own time.
Chen was a man of our time—
Why did I only hear of him by ear?

**To the Four Zhang Brothers of Chengnan, Who Braved the Snow with Wine and Food**

A drought had lasted nearly through winter,
The river guardian’s prayers went unanswered.
Then, like geese in formation, you arrived,
And suddenly a foot of fine snow fell.
Wine flushed your faces red,
Guests’ hearts swelled with boundless joy.
By midnight, you left Tianjin,
And Tianjin stood stark and silent.

**Chant of the Great Cold**

Old snow has yet to melt,
New snow piles against the door.
Before the steps, a frozen silver bed,
From the eaves, icicles hang like stalactites.
The pale sun casts no glow,
The fierce wind howls in fury.
Each person has a tongue,
Yet words cannot be spoken.

**Matching Li Shenyan’s Poem on the Great Snow**

A thousand trees of jade flowers bloom overnight,
Blending with heaven and earth in dazzling white.
The pale moon goddess dances with slender waist,
While deep in the white jade hall, another melody begins.
The scholar by the jarred window clutches his books,
The armored soldier on the battlefield moves in silence.
The reclusive man, his bones thin, nears emaciation—
Fortunate to have, now and then, a cup of wine.

**The Small Cart**

Why not drink a thousand days’ wine in joy?
Why not cherish the four seasons’ flowers?
Wherever the small cart rolls, people rejoice,
And all of Luoyang seems like home.

**Matching Rhymes with Ren, Fiscal Commissioner of Zhe**

On the official path, I found a true friend—
How could I lightly betray Luoyang’s spring?
Though three thousand *li* separate river and lake,
Let not our hometown friends yearn too often.

**Matching Song, Capital Official, Requesting Plum Blossoms (Fifth Year of Xining)**

Though my small garden has four kinds of plum,
None bloom before the twelfth month like those south of the river.
I long resent the East Lord’s lack of charm—
He refuses to release spring before its time.

**Writing at the Eastern Pavilion as the Yellow and Red Plum Blossoms Bloom, Presented to a Friend**

Once a year, we see the twin plums,
But how many times can we see them bloom?
At sixty of a hundred-year lifespan,
Cease idle talk and raise your cup.

**Matching Ren, Ministry Official, Recalling Plum Blossoms**

I grieve that plum blossoms fade so soon,
Once withered, they are too haggard to behold.
Year after year, their pure fragrance deceives—
Better to plant a few idle bamboos.

**Weeping Willow**

Before the gate, the weeping willow sways,
Tossed by the east wind’s comings and goings.
It forgets that I am already old,
Mistaking itself for youth.

**Chant of the Utmost Spirit**

The utmost spirit is called human,
The utmost nobility is called lord.
In brightness, there are sun and moon,
In obscurity, there are ghosts and gods.

**Chant of Humans and Ghosts**

If one cannot serve humans,
How can one serve ghosts?
Though humans and ghosts differ,
Their principles are never distinct.

**Lifelong Exchange**

Throughout life, in dealings with others,
Never once have I tasted bitterness.
I have never wronged another,
Nor have others ever harmed me.

**Chant of Knowledge**

What the eye sees is called perception,
What the ear hears is called knowledge.
Yet how rare in this world
Are true knowledge and perception.

**Occasional Verses**

In wind, the forest has no still branches,
On wind, the pond has no calm waves.
If forest and pond are never still,
What then of birds and fish?

**Chant of Anticipating Misfortune**

Servants bully their masters,
Barbarians invade the Central Kingdom.
Since ancient times, injustice has been known,
Yet none can ever wholly eradicate it.

**Two Poems Sent to Wang Xuanhui of Sancheng**

Though my forest dwelling is humble,
Before the flowers, I drink often.
In this world, joy belongs to those without affairs—
Yet I fear it belongs only to the idle.

Dust rises on the road to your chamber,
While in my pot, flowers just bloom.
Why wait until my hair is fully white
Before composing my homecoming verse?

**Chant of Benevolence and Sagacity**

To exhaust the Dao is called sagacity,
To be like heaven is called benevolence.
Yet how is it that benevolence and sagacity
Dare not be matched in this world?

**Matching Xing Heshu, Academician, Upon Parting**

How vast the world’s road stretches eastward—
We meet without needing words to convey.
You compare yourself to Zhuge Liang,
While I am no Yellow Stone Elder.

In expounding the Dao, high or low, no matter great or small,
In speech, rise and fall have their beginnings and ends.
Talent and achievement must be cherished in comings and goings—
Beware of lightly following the ways of Western Jin.

**Chant of Striking the Earth**

They say there are immortals in grottoes,
But tales of grotto immortals may be false.
If one awaits the ninefold elixir,
Or seeks the vermilion crest for a thousand years—

In the Land of Longevity, a thousand trees of flowers,
In the Nest of Peace, joy overflows.
With joy, flowers, and wine,
I doubt not that I am a grotto immortal.

**Chant of Spring’s Departure**

Fine things are hard to come by,
Yet all vanish in a few days.
First tossed by wind,
Then ravaged by rain.

The drunkard awakens from wealth’s stupor,
The dream of immortality suddenly fades.
The wanderer, unaware of when to stop,
Still leans on the crimson balustrade.

**Flowers and Bamboo in the Southern Garden**

Flower paths and bamboo trails press close together,
Each day I walk them four or five times.
I let the flower paths encroach on the bamboo,
That the bamboo trails may face the blooming flowers.

Fragrance drifts far, clinging to sleeves,
Bamboo shadows layer upon the wine cups.
Who says the mountain elder lacks warmth?
Such reds and greens ever embrace.

**Two Responses to Wang Xuanhui**

Our Confucians have their own joy,
Yet few are willing to follow it.
To take Chan as joy
Is but to raise another layer of dust.

**Again**

The greatly enlightened are truly unobstructed,
Each person has their own home.
Even false flowers occupy the mind—
What then can be called true flowers?

**Azure Chant: Sent in Reply to Li Shenyan, Dragon Diagram Scholar of Caozhou**

The same hue, ever azure,
From ancient times to now, people gaze and cry out in sorrow.
Sun goes, moon comes, with little change,
Yang’s warmth and yin’s chill do not hinder each other.

After thunder’s swift crack, mountains and rivers split,
When sweet dew falls, grasses and trees grow fragrant.
In dark cliffs, ghosts and demons arise,
On clear plains, phoenixes appear.

A thousand flowers bloom in spring’s rain,
Ten thousand trees wither in a night’s frost.
This meaning is hard to fathom—
Only the worthy can enter its depths.

**Five Chants from the Forest**

The true artisan of creation allows no favoritism,
The clumsy schemer’s plans are ever slight.
After rising from my Nest of Peace,
Half-drunk on the rich brew of Great Harmony.

In the Land of Longevity, I go with basket in hand,
In the Land of Ever-Ripe Grain, I return with staff.
My body, like the age of peace, knows no trouble—
A few white whiskers let the wind blow as it will.

**Old Age**

The body of old age demands warmth,
In the Nest of Peace, spring is ever present.
All affairs leave the heart, free to recline or rise,
My four limbs obey me, stretching or bending.

Where garden flowers bloom thick, cool mats are spread,
When eaves snow flies, soft quilts are laid.
Who says the mountain elder is clumsy in use?
He too can nurture his own well-being.

**The Light and Pure**

There is a thing, light and pure, called Great Harmony,
Half-drunk, it best captures spring’s abundance.
Can the elixir of immortality truly change bones?
Can ascending to heaven in broad daylight be so?

When joy is full, I rise and dance,
At the point of forgetting words, I only sing.
Do not blame friends for my lack of restraint—
True joy assails the heart, and I cannot help it.

**Inviting Each Other**

We invite and urge each other to drink rosy clouds,
Hair tangled like autumn frost, temples streaked with blossoms.
What we recall are but past years,
Most of what we’ve experienced belongs to old officials.

Together we praise the grandson’s visit today,
Speaking again of old times.
Words are tender, full of feeling—
Let not the young laugh at our fussing.

**Born Without Tilling**

Born without ever tending fields,
Yet every year, every season, there is harvest.
With cups and plates as they come, all is joy,
Casual ponds and pavilions become excursions.

Wind, flowers, snow, moon—worth a thousand gold,
Water, bamboo, clouds, mountains—worth ten thousand households.
Awaiting the Yellow River’s clarity, how long can man live?
How much sorrow can the brows bear?

**Self-Admonition in the Nest of Peace**

I do not lash my whip toward the red dust,
Seeking only to reduce faults, yet still without means.
In vain, I recall the days of knowing my errors,
Mistakenly passing the years reading the *Yi* with Confucius.

When hair turns white, it’s hard to receive color,
When the heart attains thoroughness, what more is there to say?
The qi of utmost yang becomes jade—
Yet I fear the drilling and polishing are not yet firm enough.

**Two Poems Matching Junshi, Minister of the Flower Hermitage**

No need for cinnabar pillars or carved beams,
Layer upon layer, green shade naturally hangs.
Later generations, inheriting this natural intent,
Should not seek to add embellishment with planted shade.

Behind and before the hermitage, all is planted with flowers,
Blooming in turn, lovely in all four seasons.
The master’s affairs are simple, often at leisure—
He does not believe that the years can age him.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Volume Eight Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Volume Nine*

**At Sixty-Two, by Shao Yong of Yichuan**

At sixty-two, still hale and strong,
Death but returns me to the land of eternal ripeness.
Fair scenes and fine hours are not easily won—
What harm in sipping lightly and singing low?
Boundless years defy all constraint,
Frail flesh and bone must not be harmed.
How many heroes have wielded wit and skill—
Yet few have pondered deeply the ills they bring.

**Chant on the Game Beneath the Trees**

Even the idle have their duties,
And these four fill my days:
One, to greet dawn’s dew and watch the flowers;
One, to welcome evening’s breeze and gaze upon the willows;
One, to face the bright moon and chant poems;
One, to keep fine guests and pour the wine.
For twenty years I’ve lived thus—
Who among my peers could match this life?

**Matching Rhymes with Attendant Councillor Wu Chuanzheng’s Gift**

Fifty years spent poring over old books—
The world must think me dull and out of touch.
Yet I recall the weaver’s daughter, lost in time,
And grasp the wheelwright’s art of swift and slow.
My Daoist practice lacks refinement—this is my true flaw,
Though my livelihood is meager, I dare not call it vain.
In this age of harmony, I’ve received much—
How could my heart not rest in perfect ease?

**Poem for Academician Li of Yanfu Ward, Who Requested My Garden and Pond**

Nineteen times the banners of might were unfurled,
A family of meritorious generals through the generations.
In this peaceful age, you left glory behind to return home,
White-haired, passing the years in joy.
Cups brim with fragrant wine, afloat on spring waters,
New melodies rise from the depths of fair blossoms.
With such a garden, such a pond, such longevity—
Your descendants fill your eyes with boundless blessing.

**Chant on Gold and Jade**

Fine gold and fair jade are never paired by chance,
The finest things often lie buried deepest.
Robber Zhi wielded troops yet did no good,
While Confucius, landless, was met with suspicion.
If *zhongfu* demands trust to be true,
Why does *wuwang* bring disaster in its train?
Better to treat them as matters external—
The wellspring of the heart is joy, radiant and clear.

**Summer Day in the Southern Garden**

Summer trees, uncounted in their layers,
Cast deep shade where emerald leaves droop low.
A hundred birds call to one another—
Most are orioles, their voices bright.
**For Attendant Councillor Xie of the Southern Office, Who Sent a Vessel of Xi Yi’s Wine**

The peaks of Xian Zhang stand sheer, unyielding,
Since Xi Yi and Chen Tunan passed, none have matched them.
They poured with a hand raised high for worldly affairs,
Sipped with a tilt of the head for human feeling.
To paint a tiger and fail—yet the heart remains;
To mourn the qilin with no response—tears stream down.
But enlightenment comes without many words—
The first prize is to win this pure leisure.

**Matching Junshi, Minister of the Flower Hermitage, on Sitting Alone**

Sitting in stillness, nurturing the harmony of Heaven,
I have gained much from this practice.
Vast as a hall, yet dense with vines and creepers,
I forget the bearing of a noble minister,
And can welcome even rustic visitors.
Bound by the times’ joys and sorrows,
In the end, I know not what to say.

**Matching Rhymes with Capital Official Song’s Gift of a Palm-Leaf Whisk**

Luoyang has long been called a secondary capital,
Yet it has sheltered even one as unworthy as I.
Where countless mosquitoes hum like thunder,
How can a single whisk hope to sweep them all away?

**With Academician Wang Shengzhi, Admiring the Peonies of the Western Garden**

This flower is famed in Yangzhou,
But here by the Luo River, it is prized above all.
I know well its royal beauty can move all things,
And its heavenly fragrance binds men’s hearts in secret.
It crowns the host of blossoms, reigning through summer,
Holding its rare perfection without need of spring.
As the sun slants low, I mount my horse to leave—
Yet turn once more to lean on the crimson rail and gaze.

**Playfully Thanking the Wealthy Minister for His Gift of Banana and Bamboo Shoots (Three Poems)**

A renowned garden where not even crows may fly,
Yet now the Minister deigns to send for me.
Feasts from tripods have long been called wealth and honor—
Now he pairs them with flowers and bamboo shoots.

You send great shoots to startle me,
While my small garden yields none at all.
Though I’ve striven to temper my nature,
How can I remain unmoved today?

The work of responding to things lies beyond the world—
Who can force their way to such heights?
I am but a man who knows not his limits,
Seeking to heap molehills to match Mount Tai.

**In Reply to Li Xichun, Field Officer**

Though born in prosperous times, what can I do?
My nature and wit are too slight for great deeds.
My flawed disposition suits only the curling of the inchworm,
My meager talent dares not aspire to the flight of the roc.
Often I visit old friends, joy without end,
Or seek quiet places, returning late at dusk.
I love flowers best when half-open, touched by dew—
Yet how can I bear the dew that soaks my robe?

**Moss-Coin**

After each rain, it renews itself,
Not through frequent minting.
Though plentiful, it cannot be given to guests,
And if lost, no one suspects theft.
Covering the ground does not make one rich,
Filling the steps cannot relieve the poor.
To drown sorrow, one must have a cause—
To buy wine, there is no reason at all.
Scattered, it resembles counting rods;
Piled high, it seems a tangled rope.
Unable to aid one’s own urgent needs,
How can it spare help for kin?

**Chant on Sowing Grain**

Farmers sow grain in season,
Planting millet, not weeds.
Yet why, before the millet thrives,
Do the weeds flourish first?
If weeds are not uprooted,
The millet will never ripen.
Worse still, when both need water,
The weeds steal the nourishment meant for the grain.

**Matching Minister Junshi’s Gift**

I have never seen such clamor,
Even in the heart of the city, it resembles distant outskirts.
Though I lack a thousand-mile steed,
I have found a branch to nest upon.
The moon rises behind cloud-wreathed mountains,
The wind stirs the tips of pines and bamboos.
What have I truly gained?
Surely I need not shun the world’s mockery.

**Autumn Night**

Floating clouds disperse,
Stars and constellations gleam across the endless sky.
Green moss clings to crimson fruit,
Pure fragrance rises from white lotuses.
My body, still cool, wears linen,
My ears, now quiet, hear no cicadas.
Sitting long, all movement ceases—
The autumn sky is utterly still.

**On Revisiting My Garden in Autumn, I Take Up My Bamboo Staff and Don My Sable Robe—Both Gifts from Jingren—And, Seeing Them, Think of Him and Write This Poem**

The bamboo staff has long been my companion,
The sable robe, though spread anew, is still fresh.
Gradually it bears the snow of Min Mountain,
Brushing away the dust of the capital.
It steadies my steps on the drunken path home,
Fits my ailing body with ease.
Thanks to these treasures from my study,
At times I feel as if I see my old friend again.

**Clouds**

The clear sky is bluer than water—
How can a single cloud take flight?
Catching the sunlight, it becomes a vermilion phoenix;
Following the wind, it turns to white raiment.
Coming and going, it leaves no trace,
Hidden or revealed, it forgets all cunning.
Who can fathom Heaven’s principle?
In the end, where does it belong?

**Leisure**

In leisure, I observe all things—
Everywhere, there is freedom.
Fish are hooked for their greed,
Moths are scorched for rushing into flame.

**The Azure Wutong and the Phoenix**

The azure wutong starves the phoenix,
While white grains sate the wren.
Who is this rope-girt youth?
He sings as he gathers firewood.

**The Flowering Arbor’s Morning Glories**

The arbor blooms with morning glories—
At dawn they open, by sunrise they wither.
Though their flowers are fair, none linger to admire.
Gazing afar, clouds cling to the peaks;
Her makeup done, her kingfisher pins scatter.
A gauze pouch cradles the morning dew,
A jade parasol brushes the autumn mist.
Their longing is not like the sunflower’s,
Yet they fade before the hibiscus.
Fit only for a fleeting glance,
They waste the day in idle sleep.

**Matching "Autumn Night"**

Long I dreaded summer’s heat,
Now I welcome autumn’s night.
Sudden rain meets towering bamboo,
Cool winds sway the evening lotus.
How could withered sedge crickets
Outlast the failing willow’s cicadas?
If only I had a bird of the marsh,
Its clear cry would wash me clean.

**Matching "Sable Robe and Bamboo Staff" (Both Gifts from Fan Jingren)**

The noble man preserves all things,
Cherishing the old, not the new.
The staff grows from Shu’s rocky cliffs,
The sable runs through Yin Mountain’s dust.
It steadies treacherous paths,
Warms a frail and ailing frame.
Never set aside in motion or stillness,
It is as if I see the giver again.

**Matching "Clouds"**

The sky’s canopy stretches boundless,
A single cloud drifts alone.
It knows only to cling to stone,
Not to become heaven’s raiment.
Coming, it has no intent;
Going, it needs no device.
If it cannot bring timely rain,
Why return to the imperial realm?

**Matching "Leisure"**

Observe all things through the self—
Their principles are never distant.
Horses grow lean from too much riding,
Tortoises burn from scorching divination.
The parrot may speak, yet still be caged;
The wren eats its fill with ease.
The mist and waters of Yi and Luo are fine—
Would that I could share them with fisher and woodcutter.

**Matching "The Flowering Arbor’s Morning Glories"**

Leaves clamor thick as a cock’s crow,
Flowers bloom dense as kingfisher pins.
Dew glistens cold at dawn,
Mist veils the clear day’s haze.
Their blooms follow in ordered turns,
Yet open and close without sequence.
The master stands long in vain vigil,
Ever forsaking his morning rest.

**Two Quatrains to Old Friend Wei of the Three Cities**

Though aged, I am not yet feeble,
Chrysanthemums crowd my fence.
The weather turns crisp and fine,
Neighbors gather in good cheer.

The scene is fair, our bodies hale,
The sky clear, the road dry.
A light carriage on soft grass rides smooth,
Everywhere, pure delight.

**Autumn Day on the Stone Pavilion**

First clear skies, I lean on the monk’s pavilion rail,
The world grows bleak in the eighth month.
I test my strength on the uppermost floor,
Gazing from on high at the mortal realm.
Autumn deepens, the weather suits me well;
In old age, my heart loves only leisure.
I bid the distant mountains hold their hue—
Such feelings have long since faded.

**Matching Li Shenyan’s "Traveling Past Longmen"**

A thousand miles of autumn light fills my seat,
Our friendship rejoices in smiles of welcome.
The chrysanthemums await the Double Ninth,
As if saving their bloom for your cup of wine.

**Chant on Wind and Moon**

Cool winds are boundlessly pure,
Bright moons are boundlessly clear.
Purity and clarity never forsake me,
Ever fostering joy.
All day, threefold shame;
All day, threefold honor.
If honor and shame touch me not,
What cause have I for alarm?

**To Minister Fu**

The world strikes at joy and sorrow,
Who in this age can compare?
Three reigns as prime minister,
Four rivers as a man of leisure.
Illuminating all things through the ages,
He gathers back the one true essence.
I know not why,
Yet he treats me with such warmth.

**Chant on Wielding the Brush**

What man values is spirit,
Yet spirit, once possessed, is not pure.
Feigning truth from falsehood remains false,
Diligence to mask clumsiness still falls short.
Hunger sated brings sickness,
Seeking cure from sickness brings no true peace.
Many err in mistaking the sage,
But the sage never errs with the world.

**Inviting Sima Junshi to Stroll the Summer Garden**

The rain has cleared, the scene is fine,
Autumn deepens, the air not yet cold.
Might you join me in high spirits?
Let us linger awhile in the summer garden.

**Autumn Day After Rain, Gazing Idly**

Water gleams, clouds scatter, frost comes early,
Though late in the year, the yellow blooms are fair.
Though encircled by distant mountains on all sides,
A single slice of autumn light grows old.
Heaven creates all things without partiality,
Yet sages’ deeds baffle mortal understanding.
Common words need no boast—
Even mediocre talent suffices.

**Four Short Verses for Chen Jichang**

The eighth month is a small spring,
Small flowers bloom in profusion.
Evening brings a light rain,
Leaving the small carriage idle.

**Chant on Joy**

I ever delight in joy,
Joy that harms no righteousness.
Joy in heaven’s four seasons,
Joy in earth’s myriad things.
Joy in men’s fine conduct,
Joy in one’s own joyful deeds.
Beyond these joys,
I joy in the faintest intoxication.

**Chant Admonishing My Son**

Good and evil lie in where they dwell,
Dividing petty men from gentlemen.
To change course harms not one’s gentlemanly nature;
To persist in error is to become a petty man.
Good medicine cures when it benefits the ailment,
White jade is prized only when flawless.
To forge a fine vessel requires cutting and polishing—
How can faults not be made anew?

**On Hearing Shaohua Mountain’s Collapse**

Change leaves no trace in an instant,
Yet moving heaven and earth is no hard task.
If the giant spirit but stretched its hand,
Would Shaohua Mountain’s rise be all?
Six villages’ folk are swallowed,
Nine springs’ boulders overturned.
A woodcutter’s single word could be heeded—
Even in Yao and Shun’s time, it could be done.

**Chant on the Ancients**

Since ancient times, even great sages
Found matters difficult.
How much more for later men—
Could they so easily attain it?
Seeking it is endlessly hard,
Yet gaining it is effortless.
A thousand, ten thousand hearts,
But only one heart is right.

**Letter in Place of Writing to Old Friend Zu Longtu**

Thirty years of old friendship,
Meeting now with white-haired heads.
We once drank on the sea’s edge,
Roamed the Luo Society together.
Shedding the world’s storms,
We opened our hearts in pine and cassia autumn.
From now on, our brows
Should bear no idle sorrow.

**Chant on a Cold Night**

The sky adds a layer of cold,
I add a layer to my quilt.
I speak no words of the past,
Nor do what is excessive.
I blame myself strictly and thoroughly,
Demand little of others.
Only the great sage
Can stand in a place without fault.

**Chant on Knowing Fortune**

The cock’s duty is to herald dawn,
The dog’s duty is to guard.
Both serve their roles,
Yet all returns to serving their lord.

**On Hunger and Cold**

When I hunger, I too partake of food;
When I am cold, I too receive clothing.
Yet how is it I possess not a thread of merit
To repay the age?

**Trends**

Abandoning my divine tortoise,
I observe my own chewing jaws.
Turning from righteousness to profit,
Man lacks far-reaching thought.

**Gifts in Retirement**

Adorned in rustic gardens,
Silk bundles meager yet bound.
Having plotted the great,
The small lies within.

**Chant on the Unknowable**

The plow-ox sprouts crimson horns,
The aged oyster yields a luminous pearl.
Though men may wish not to use them,
Can mountains and rivers forsake them?

Affairs are fundamentally unknowable,
Things too are hard to constrain.
Once reduced to mere conjecture,
Righteousness loses its precision.

**Chant on Urgency**

At dawn’s extremity, one longs for rain;
In mortal illness, the heart seeks medicine.
In such moments for all,
No urging is needed to deepen resolve.

**Chant on Knowing Others**

Observe demeanor in times of urgency,
Reveal the liver and spleen in peril.
Deep hearts and composed faces may deceive in peace—
Beware of claiming easy knowledge.

**Chant on Speech**

A single word brings delight,
A thousand words stir greater wrath.
Though speech may unite,
It is speech that divides.

**Chant on Anticipating Misfortune**

Adornment nears empty vanity,
Yet empty vanity breeds deep misfortune.
To cure hunger, use food;
To relieve drought, require rain.

**Chant on a Human Lifetime**

A hundred million years lie ahead,
A hundred million years stretch behind.
Between them, a hundred years—
How much can one accomplish?

Even the lifespan of a tile,
Few reach a hundred years.
Why not rejoice,
Forcing oneself into sorrow?

**Thanks for the Gift of a Stone Zither**

Who took Heaven’s Pillar Peak,
And with a swift blade cleaved it in half?
Where springs once trickled, faint traces remain,
Where clouds embraced, their hue still lingers.

Neither the gates of power could move it,
Nor the halls of wealth turn it away.
What am I then,
To be offered as tribute to the Lord of the Underworld?

**Matching the Poem on First Snow, Tenth Month Twenty-Fourth Day, Presented to the Prime Minister and Elder**

On *renzi*, snow first arrives,
Sparse, then retreats to clear skies.
The world turns wholly white,
The forest beneath cannot bear its purity.

The cold scholar suffers dread,
The destitute tremble in fear.
With new laws restricting cups,
Why should they readily empty?

**Matching the Prime Minister and Elder**

Lofty towers have not yet celebrated,
Auspicious snow descends from cloud’s edge.
Though the earth is all white,
The heavens are not so cold.

A bountiful year may be awaited,
An inch of moisture hard to dry.
Let not the furrows be forgotten,
Trace the joy of *jirang* in the fields.

**At Tianjin Viewing Snow: A Letter in Place of Thanks to Scholar Jiang for Returning a Poetry Scroll**

The clear Luo flows to the heavens,
Cold clouds skim the earth in flight.
One stands upon the bridge,
Poetry returns through the snow.

**Viewing Snow in the Abode of Peace and Joy**

Layered clouds, drifting snow—
Within the Abode of Peace and Joy, gazing outward.
First marveling at the jade trees of the rear garden,
Then astonished by the shimmering Jasper Pool on level ground.

Before the Cold Food Festival, pear blossoms fade,
Before spring winds, willow fluff takes flight.
Wine leaves me half-drunk, the curtain half-raised—
Let not this feeling be known to outsiders.

**A World of Jade**

All before my eyes is jade,
The poor household thus grows rich.
Permitted to admire, not to sell,
Fitting for sorrow, not for ease.

After fine brew induces warmth,
Layered robes sustain the heat.
Would I dare, in this mortal realm,
To weigh gain and loss by the slightest measure?

**Responding to Minister Fu Han’s New Year’s Four Quatrains, Xining Sixth Year**

Four poems for the New Year,
Celebrating seventy years.
Consider the precedents of Tang—
Even the Duke of Jin was not permitted to retire.

**Matching Junshi and Duangming**

Cultivating the Dao in tranquil ease,
Frosted hair grows as it will.
Free from official blame,
Fortunate to escape worldly suspicion.

A thatched door suffices for contentment,
A broth of greens is never sated.
One peculiar joy—
Basking in the sun before the eaves.

**Four Joys of the Abode of Peace and Joy**

In the Abode of Peace and Joy, a joyful man,
At leisure, four treasures gladly keep him company.
One volume of poetry, carefree, gathers moon and flowers,
One tome of scripture, stern, astonishes ghosts and spirits.

One stick of incense, pure, uplifts the cosmos,
One cup of wine, fine, immerses in heaven’s truth.
Rejoicing in this great peace—how much more?
Only wishing the lord’s life span ten thousand springs.

**A Volume of Poetry in the Abode of Peace and Joy**

A volume of poetry in the Abode of Peace and Joy,
Singing to myself, chanting to myself, I find contentment.
Refining water and stone in idle endeavor,
Discerning wind and blossoms in quiet authority.

Thoughts take flight, suddenly mounting a thousand-mile steed,
Inspiration arises, first ascending the nine-tiered heavens.
When the mood strikes, altering a word or two,
Drunk, I chant five or seven verses.

Now fearing my heart communes with the moon beyond snow,
Now suspecting my body is an immortal in a cave.
The Silver River surges, churning waves in clear skies,
Jade trees gnarled, birthing purple mist.

All things with feeling may be depicted,
All bones free of illness cannot be purged.
Entrusted with themes, I am aided by spirits,
My lines, mistakenly, are spread by others.

Would I yield to noble houses’ constant music,
Or feel shame before wealthy chambers’ excess wealth?
If tallying such faults, how boundless they would be—
Why then do censors alone remain silent?

**A Tome in the Abode of Peace and Joy**

A tome in the Abode of Peace and Joy,
Titled *The August Ultimate*—what does it intend?
*Spring and Autumn*, rites and music, can they be abandoned?
Father and son, lord and minister—can they be abolished?

Vast, the era after Fuxi and Xuanyuan’s creation,
Majestic, the harmony of Yao and Shun’s beginning.
Fiery, Tang and Wu beyond war,
Turbulent, Huan and Wen beyond swords and bows.

Sun, moon, and stars shine brightly,
Kings, emperors, and lords’ voices resound grandly.
Thousands of rulers emerge with regulations,
Hundreds of millions of years form models.

Long governance breeds fear of overbearing power,
Deep troubles still call for the expulsion of evil.
Talents fit to command an age arise in their time,
Wisdom to aid the age is never absent.

The past is all subject to idle commentary,
The future must await another’s support.
Who is the master of creation,
To birth so many extraordinary men?

**A Stick of Incense in the Abode of Peace and Joy**

A stick of incense in the Abode of Peace and Joy,
Burned at dawn with no ordinary intent.
Disasters like these spare men from flattery,
Fortunes sought would outmeasure heaven.

Unlike the black and yellow robes beseeching temple statues,
Nor like children and girls perfuming their garments.
*Sincerity* arises—why trouble with prayer?
*Unwarranted* calamity—hard to dispel.

The empty chamber, cool and white,
The numinous terrace, bright and still, glows anew.

**Gazing at the Wind, Warding Off Bandits**

Gazing at the wind, warding off bandits—heart still drunk,
Facing the scene, like Yan Yuan, he sits in perfect forgetfulness.
In the Red Waters lies a pearl, cradling creation’s essence,
Within the Muddy Pellet, no thing bars the azure vault.

Born a man, still strong in body,
Meeting a flourishing age, the year abundant.
Sun and moon shine down—achievements vast,
Sovereign and minister shelter—how enduring their merit?

Not merely hearing the Dao has brought one to this,
Gold and jade—whose halls are not filled to overflowing?

**A Cup of Wine in the Abode of Peace and Joy**

A cup of wine in the Abode of Peace and Joy,
Not only to nurture qi, but to follow the true.
Sipped again and again, lightly drunk,
Patting one’s breast—all within is spring.

No different from sovereign and minister at first meeting,
Nor unlike heaven and earth at the dawn of boundless expanse.
The intoxicated savor—hard to name,
The brewing craft—impossible to describe.

Pouring holds depths, governed by harmonious principle,
Drinking knows no measure, entrusted with great schemes.
Beneath the Phoenix Tower, a carefree wanderer,
Within the walls of Jiaoru, a man at ease.

From the high pavilion, the view—flowers like brocade,
Where the small carriage rolls—grass like a carpet.
Unfurling and rolling up, the hand of rise and fall through ages,
Emerging and entering, a body of a thousand layers of clouds and waters.

After rain, quietly observing the mountain’s intent,
Before the wind, idly watching the moon’s spirit.
Such endeavors—weighed and distinguished,
Even ancient heroes, I fear, have not heard of them.

**On Chancellor Xie Fu’s Presentation of a New Scroll of Poetry**

A thoroughfare chosen, half planted with pines and bamboos,
An elder statesman resigns honor in a flourishing age.
Planting fine flowers to contemplate the nature of things,
Each draft of pure wine stirs the celestial truth.

The clear court will match the deeds of former years,
The jade grotto immortal—today’s body.
More still—twenty new poems,
Each word a rival to the radiance of spring.

Literature is the realm’s shared vessel,
Poetry, within literature, is no less refined.
Only by entering its nature does one know its true savor,
Only by attaining the divine does one see its wondrous craft.

Leisurely observing the wax and wane of years,
Silently reflecting the presence and absence of heaven and earth.
Its diction surpasses *Li Sao* in warmth and grace,
Though *Li Sao* lacks such ease and breadth.

**Delighting in Playful Verses in the Abode of Peace and Joy**

In the Abode of Peace and Joy, how fine to play the rogue,
To play the rogue—age must take its turn.
In bitter cold and scorching heat, doors stay shut,
When mild warmth and early cool arrive, one ventures out.

Wind and moon urge brush and ink,
Orioles and flowers draw one near wine jars.
Ask why one can live thus—
Only because talent nurtures the untalented.

**Matching Vice-Director Junshi’s Ascent of the Stone Pavilion**

Though the plain stretches far,
Who knows each thing renewed?
A perilous tower, a hundred feet high,
Holds ten thousand springs apart.

**Matching Vice-Director Junshi’s Wine-Ode**

Luoyang’s flowers and trees bloom across the city,
Further gifting the eastern capital’s paired flasks.
Thus do idle men turn wild,
Yet how can the red sun not sink in the west?

**Chanting Before Flowers**

Spring abides in flowers’ rivalry for beauty,
Spring departs—flowers wither in its wake.
Fine flowers cannot be detained,
Nor can fine guests easily meet.

Wine is drunk before flowers,
Flowers are best viewed with wine in hand.
Why pour another full cup?
To exhaust this moment’s joy.

**To Li Xichun, Agricultural Commissioner in Chengdu, in Rhyme**

I think of you, yet you do not return,
You linger in your Sichuan post.
Though white-haired and weary,
A pure heart should find its own ease.

Flower season—hard to meet,
Silkworm market—easy to rejoice.
Do not sigh at retirement’s delay,
Life’s toil is not yet done.

**Letter in Place of Li Zundu, Imperial Commissioner in Guangxin**

A thousand *li* beyond Jibei,
Han and Tang marked the frontier’s edge.
Yet how is it today’s land
No longer bears its old mountains and rivers?

The tiger’s tent—warriors revere it,
The carved bow—passed to heirs.
When the day comes to inscribe merit,
Let none fall short of later Yanran.

**Self-Matching "Playful Verses"**

In the Abode of Peace and Joy, how fine to play the rogue,
Knowing full well I lack surpassing talent.
Old age brings many ills, yet I take no medicine,
Youth’s bold heart has long turned to ash.

Courtyard grass, though cut, never fully vanishes,
Railing flowers, though tended, struggle to bloom.
A light breeze stirs half-drunken wine—
This joy comes straight from beyond the heavens.

*Yichuan’s "Striking the Earth" Collection, Volume Nine Concludes*
*Yichuan’s "Striking the Earth" Collection, Volume Ten*

**Thirteen Poems on Encountering Spring in Old Age**

**I**
Old age meets spring—do not suspect its guile,
For in these years, youth’s return I revere.
Men’s hearts grow cold, wine fails to cheer,
The sky stays chill, the flowers refuse to appear.
Beyond the dyke, wind bends the willows aslant,
Beneath the wall, rain nurtures moss half-grown.
Last year’s waves flowed eastward, gone for good—
Why then does fresh green rise anew?

**II**
Old age meets spring—spring now in full array,
Its splendor bright before the Smoke-Prohibition Day.
Just chilled, then warmed, the season tends the flowers,
Rain’s voice departs, the sky clears for wine’s hours.
To pour or pause, the cups must know their lord,
To plant the peach and plum—who holds the sway?
Pure talk already stirs the heart to joy,
Yet wild verse I take for strings to play.

**III**
Old age meets spring—rain just now cleared,
And clearer still, the approach of Pure Bright near.
The sky hangs low o’er palace halls, the days grow long,
The garden warm, but orioles still hold their song.
From lofty towers, the flowers seem brocade,
Where grass is thick as matting, carriages glide.
How generous the Lord of Spring’s decree—
Once more the world enjoys long peace.

**IV**
Old age meets spring—do not weary of its stay,
Living now by Heaven’s Ford, how fine the day.
With water and bamboo as life’s design,
I must become the master of wind and bloom.
The old estate’s debris I clear away,
New beds I tend with care, planting orchid’s perfume.
I know not if beyond this quiet land
Another place could hold this frame of mine.

**V**
Old age meets spring—now first I know its grace,
Yet fear its beauty belongs to idle hands alone.
Who grasps the Lord of Blue’s great artistry?
How dare I vie with youth’s unruly throng?
Wine from young brew I taste for its pure tone,
Flowers in dawn’s dew I watch for their bright spirit’s song.
All rare things are hard to entrust—
Yet Nature never fails the one who belongs.

**VI**
Old age meets spring—spring’s moods abound,
Who dyes the waves? Who twists the willow’s crown?
By pond and pavilion, love knows no end,
Yet how to match the thriving grass and tree?
Raise the cups to share the heart’s intent,
But guard against wind and rain that mar serenity.
Amid a thousand reds and myriad greens,
Is there another idle soul like me?

**VII**
Old age meets spring—do not disdain its stay,
Lest spring, in stinginess, bring worldly strife.
Of four seasons, only spring is fair,
In all the year, scarce ten days free from care.
No need to beg the moon for wine’s delight,
But spare the flowers from rain’s cruel blight.
Yet when a thousand petals drift away,
How much gold could buy such joy as this?

**VIII**
Old age meets spring—do not tire of its return,
For how many springs remain to see?
Press the winecup, urge the guest to stay,
Lest laughing branches mock my folly.
The world’s ways cannot bear the new or old,
Things’ truths are hard to tell from falsehood’s guise.
Who says the phoenix and simurgh speak too much?
This grievance runs too deep for words to rise.

**IX**
Old age meets spring—my zest is not yet spent,
I beg kind Spring to linger, to relent.
If good deeds fear the flowers’ fading hue,
Then with deep feeling, hold the candle high and roam.
In wine, the merit of a warhorse’s idle sweat,
In verse, the crime of quiet elegance.
The Lord of Spring cannot endure our jest—
He scolds the branches, never ceasing spite.

**X**
Old age meets spring—do not regret the wild,
For wildness checked, the spirit’s fire grows mild.
The garden, just when beauty turns too bright,
Is struck by wind and rain, by idle spite.
Flowers half-open—hasten to admire,
Wine just ripe—taste it first, take no delay.
Most fine things Heaven guards with jealous care,
Not by our whim are they bestowed this way.

**XI**
Old age meets spring—spring cannot bear my weight,
This burden comes from age’s slow advance.
One body’s decline—what is there to regret?
But spring’s departure fills the eyes with want.
When the red sun sinks, the wind grows fierce,
Where fallen blooms drift, the waters run deep.
The wandering oriole does not grasp spring’s end,
Still from the branches it sings its sweet refrain.

**XII**
Old age meets spring—I see through spring at last,
No need to grieve, no need to mourn its past.
Body and heart have their own place of rest,
How can mere grass and trees beguile the wise?
Today’s glory is tomorrow’s withered state,
This year’s freshness was last year’s new.
In this world, joy and sorrow ever chase—
How much wine could ever calm your mind?

**XIII**
To Minister Sima Junshi of Chongde, Long Awaited but Not Arrived

Your household swallows from the rafters last year,
This year past the festival, they’ve not returned.
I beg forgiveness for delaying you so long—
Your carriage comes at last through flowers deep.

Heaven bestowed on you eight measures of talent,
A rustic must turn back midway to meet.
One word from the immortal I cannot forget—
Beyond the flowers, your carriage still unseen.

**Two Parting Quatrains**

Beyond the tower, flowers block the narrow way,
Hard to forget your virtue, my longing stays.
I’d ask the peach and plum to offer thanks,
But peach and plum are mute—what can they say?

On the high pavilion, admiring flowers,
I bear the guilt of breaking my word.
If verse could ransom fault,
What time could I not keep you company?

**Ascending the Stone Pavilion on a Spring Day**

Throughout Luoyang, the homes of ministers and lords,
They plant peach and plum as livelihood.
Each year in the second month, from on high,
No homes are seen—only the flowers abound.

**At Sixty-Three**

Now sixty-three years old,
Teeth and hair may fade, but not my will.
Ashamed to waste my spirit in vain play,
How could I squander talent on reckless display?
Dread the sound of scraping bone,
Weary of schemes that test the blade’s keen edge.
The sage’s words I hold in reverence:
“To fall is simply to gain advantage.”
*(Master Chen Xiyi once spoke these words.)*

**On Reflecting on Affairs**

The ancients we do not see,
Yet we may observe their hearts.
Their hearts held nothing else,
But deep regard for righteousness.
The people of today we do not see in heart,
Only in face may we perceive.
Their faces hold nothing else,
But shallow regard for gain.
To regard righteousness is to benefit others,
To regard gain is to harm the people.
Benefiting others or harming the people,
In the end, it turns back on oneself.
If by luck one escapes,
The calamity still falls upon descendants.

**Occasional Writing**

Heaven births the myriad things,
Each fulfilling its own way.
Only humans are most wise,
Able to encompass all.

**On Noble and Base Natures**

Orchids and irises, fragrant and pure;
Unicorns and phoenixes, rare and divine.
Such are the people—
Few who are not virtuous.
Wolfsbane and wild aconite, venomous and foul;
Owls, turtledoves, and vipers, cruel and dire.
Such are the people—
Few who are not corrupt.
Virtue seeks only peace,
Corruption seeks only ruin.
Day and night without cease,
They take their turns before us.
Heaven covers all without partiality,
Earth bears all without favor.
Only when both can nurture,
Do we know their boundless grace.

**Occasional Verse**

Frogs and crickets scurry through mud,
Phoenixes soar beyond the clouds.
Though the distance between mud and sky is vast,
How can they ever know one another?

**Ode to the Harmonizing Decoction**

Two flavors blend within the jar,
A single sip brings wondrous effect.
Shared in leisure, no cause for care,
Drunk alone—yet who does so for sorrow?
A mere touch awakens the true master’s call,
Half-tipsy, one roams with Fuxi’s spirit.
All praise the joy of drunkenness,
But who would cease before the cup runs dry?

**Washing the Bamboo**

Through frost and autumn, pine and cypress stand,
Yet thickets grow unchecked, their shadows dense.
All useless branches I strip away,
Leaving only those that reach the sky.
Like the echo of a dragon’s song,
Or the phoenix’s return—work complete.
Not for their use alone,
But for their noble bearing, unmatched in the world.

**Ode to Heaven’s Will**

Heaven’s will is nothing but nature,
Beyond nature, no heaven remains.
Who fears the dark when no deceit is sown?
Seek the source to sever all greed.
Before the labor, words may still be spoken,
But at the harvest—what more is there to say?
The sage’s work is hard to follow,
For the priceless pearl lies deep in the abyss.

**A Playful Letter to Brother Longtu**

Brother, born in the same year,
Twenty-seven days my elder.
No grudge bends my brow,
Yet joy makes us clap our hands.
Our bond deepens day by day,
Our shared Dao and righteousness endure.
Only one thing is lacking—
A scribe to inscribe in cinnabar:
Eight hundred taels of gold.
*(You chose me to write the epitaph for your parent’s burial.)*

**Raising the Cup**

I raise my cup to my sons,
Now sixty-three years old.
Though my station is not lofty,
My discourse on the Dao leaves no shame.
Worldly glory fades,
Yet the joy of the grove still tempts me.
If you ask what tempts me—
It is this: to escape the world’s suspicion.

**Facing the Flowers, Seventh Year of Xining**

Flower branches gleam in the winecup,
I raise the cup to toast the blooms.
Wine runs out, but money buys more,
Withered flowers—no medicine revives.
Let no man drink to excess too soon,
Nor let the flowers scatter too fast.
Drink slowly before the blossoms,
For how long can spring endure?

**Four Ways**

The Way of Heaven waxes and wanes,
The Way of Earth rises and falls.
The Way of Man knows rise and decline,
The Way of Things flourishes and fades.
Rise and decline do not share the same age,
Flourishing and fading do not share the same time.
Yet why, when men face these,
Do they know such joy and sorrow?

**Forest Retreat**

A marvel hidden in the woods,
Unknown to the vulgar crowd.
Now a full moon’s radiant night,
Now a branch of blossoms unfurled.
The wine is not yet poured,
The guests must not depart.
Fortunate are we few in the world,
To have even this much.

**Spring Overcast**

Day after day, spring is overcast,
The gloom of spring ever deepens.
Though I have the strength to tend the flowers,
How could I lack the heart to love the moon?
Only when the moon is full can it be seen,
Only when the flowers bloom can they be praised.
Yet why do flowers and moon
Show so little mercy to man’s longing?
Fair flowers are hard to gaze upon for long,
Bright moons are hard to watch for long.
Flowers last but five or seven days,
The moon is full but twelve times a year.
Even at its fullest, it wanes,
Even in bloom, it withers.
Wind and rain are common things,
Why then does the human heart not rest?

**Toasting the Flowers**

I raise my cup to the flower branches,
Let the flowers understand this too:
Flowers do not bloom for ten days,
Man has but a hundred years.
In this fleeting, soul-stirring moment,
Who would think of the haze of wine?
Without poetry to sever the bond,
How could one unravel a lifetime’s confusion?

**Rising Late**

Half-remembered, half-forgotten dreams upon waking,
Seeming sorrow, yet no sorrow—only weariness.
Wrapped in robes, lying sideways, unwilling to rise,
Beyond the curtain, fallen petals swirl in chaos.

**Reflections on Affairs**

The noble and the petty stand opposed,
Wisdom above, folly below—unshaken truth.
Wolfsbane’s roots do not entwine with orchids,
Yet why do they share this heaven and earth?

**Three Delusions**

To age without rest is the first delusion,
To be at ease yet joyless is the second,
To be idle yet impure is the third.
These three delusions destroy the self.

**Four Joys**

First joy: long life as one’s domain,
Second joy: a bountiful year as one’s kingdom,
Third joy: leisure as one’s virtue,
Fourth joy: peace and health as one’s power.

**What Is It Like?**

Stand as a true man,
Act not as a petty one.
All in life is fated from the start—
Why waste effort in vain calculations?

**Questioning Spring**

Since ancient times, flowers must be paired with orioles,
Orioles and flowers were meant to appear together.
Why then do the flowers fade before the orioles arrive,
Yet still bear the name of "oriole and flower"?
I would ask spring, but spring does not answer,
What secret love does it hold for bees and butterflies?
The wandering oriole refuses to bear spring’s betrayal,
Singing a thousand cries, then ten thousand more.

**From the Tower to a Friend**

A guest often scorns the spring on level ground,
Yet spring cannot but be as it is.
None are as content in a humble lane as I,
Yet once I ascend this tower, I think of you.
All I see are verdant groves and clouds,
Half the tiled roofs veiled in blossoms, fresh and new.
To lean on the railing, the heart must be free—
Who in this world is truly free to lean?

**On Loss**

The more one loses, the less one gains,
Leaving only sighs in between.
A man’s glory or decline is but the way of things,
Flowers bloom and fade—why chase them?
We meet by chance, then part again,
Now sharing joy, now sharing sorrow.
If one sees through it all, nothing remains—
Why trouble oneself with distinctions?

**Placing Flowers**

Flower branches reflect in the winecup,
Within the winecup, fine flower branches.
My body has known two eras of peace,
My eyes have seen four dynasties at their height.
Moreover, my limbs remain strong,
And the season is at its most fragrant.
Wine holds the flowers’ crimson glow,
How can I bear not to drink to intoxication before them?

**Ode to Leisure**

To dwell in leisure, one must dwell in Luoyang,
For nowhere else in the world compares.
A land of culture, gathering the wise,
A city of mountains and rivers, ancient imperial seat.

**By Rare Blooms, Holding Fragrant Wine**

By rare blooms, I hold fragrant wine,
On softest grass, I move my little cart.
Only Yaofu fails his old friends—
Yet none of his friends fail Yaofu.

---

**Matching Rhymes with Academician Zhang Zijian**

Eight years of reunion, yet grievance remains,
How can parting wine be poured again?
Though my plain speech may seem like scattered pearls,
I know the pure and harmonious sound of jade.
To live in this world, one should be a man of worth,
To admire flowers, one must be in Luoyang.
I know the true men of past and present—
The craft of creation is no easy thing.

---

**Responding to the Twelve Poems Presented by Editor Huan of Gongcheng**

To capture likeness in painting is no easy match,
Though painting may depict form, it cannot grasp the heart.
Reading your ten poems three times over,
I find it surpasses revisiting my hometown once more.
The old capital stirs new longing,
