The homeland appears as of old, towering and green.
Of fame and worldly affairs, ask not—
Only two streams of clear tears to wipe away.

---

**Ode to the Joy of Things**

Sun, moon, and stars—heaven’s light,
Ears, eyes, mouth, nose—man’s spirit.
Kings, emperors, lords, and hegemons arise from sound,
Heaven’s intent is not far from human feeling.
Birds, beasts, grasses, trees—each kind differs,
Scholars, farmers, artisans, merchants—each rank forms.
How to ensure years of plenty and lasting peace,
That joy may be shared with all things in their glory?

---

**Ode to Welcoming Spring**

Spring arrives, and I seek it through poetry,
Spring departs, and I pursue it with wine.
Wine is drunk for spring’s coming and going,
Poems are written for flowers’ blooming and fading.
Flowers bloom and fade, poems are written again and again,
Spring comes and goes, wine is poured repeatedly.
My feelings run deep, not for the young in years—
How can one bear the surging harmony within?

---

**Ode to Late Spring**

Sentimental Pan Yu envies the willow catkins,
Drifting in and out of a thousand, ten thousand homes.
Youthful vigor and ambition are all lost,
Old age knows nothing of new affairs.
My poems may have faults, but others forgive,
While my wine’s merits I alone proclaim.
Yet one lingering sorrow remains—
Most of my old friends are now beyond the horizon.

---

**Two Poems Matching Rhymes with Minister Wang Zhongmei on His Retirement**

**I**
Seventy years in this world awaited,
Like Master Zhang, I compose my return to the fields.
To know the times and act accordingly is wisdom indeed,
Who would say nurturing one’s will is not right?
Moreover, there is joy in forests and streams,
No longer bound by official duties.
Recently, I drove my little cart to call,
Only to find you napping on a spring afternoon.

**II**
Since ancient times, talent soars when inspired,
Why does my lord alone know the times?
A lifetime of aspirations never bent,
In this flourishing age, hanging up your cap is fitting.
Willows sway gently in the breeze,
Flowers, pressed by spring, smile as the sun lingers.
The clamor of attendants shakes the earth as they pass,
Yet no one asks who it is.

---

**Matching Rhymes with Attendant Wang of Beijing on His Visit and Gift of Poems**

Your carriage came from Beijing to admire spring,
Geng Jun’s heart had already raced ahead.
Now frost falls heavily—what can be done?
This year’s flowers bloom in vain, all sound.
You honor me with fine verses and a visiting card,
Surely there are no clumsy lines to convey my name.
Heaven’s talent and beauty should be cherished—
I know you were not born in vain for this age.

---

**Ode to Joy**

Five joys in this life,
Five delights in dwelling in Luoyang.
Most take them lightly as commonplace,
Yet I hold them as no small matter.
My talent is unremarkable,
My knowledge leaves no record.
But my heart is at peace—
How could I ever exhaust these?

**The Five Joys:**
First, to be born in the Central Kingdom,
Second, to be born a man,
Third, to be born a scholar,
Fourth, to witness an era of great peace,
Fifth, to hear of the Dao and righteousness.

**The Five Delights:**
First, many good people,
Second, many fine deeds,
Third, many beautiful things,
Fourth, many lovely scenes,
Fifth, many grand principles.

---

**Ode to Joyousness**

Joyous, ever joyous,
Joy and delight, ever more joy.
Noble men are my friends,
Fine scenes are mine to behold.
Fine wine is mine to drink,
Fine food is mine to savor.
This body, born and grown old,
Lives entirely in an age of peace.

---

**Ode to the Heavenly Dao**

The Heavenly Dao is not hard to know,
Human nature is not easy to fathom.
Though one hears words,
One must also observe deeds.
Leaning on the desk—great effort,
Wielding the halberd—base endeavor.
The *Spring and Autumn* relies on seizing the moment,
A little cart is all one needs.

---

**Ode to a Single Chamber**

A single chamber can hold a life,
All four seasons bring eternal spring.
Never lacking fine wine,
Never without worthy guests.
Within the grotto, the flower admirer is Junshi—
His dwelling has a grotto.
At the edge of the sky, the moon-floating man is Junzhi—
His dwelling has a tower.
When we meet, there should be words,
Laughing at my excessive caution.

---

**Matching Rhymes with Junshi, Minister of the Eastern Capital, on His Solitary Stroll by the Luo River**

Caps and carriages crowd the nine thoroughfares,
Voices clamor and jostle for precedence.
Only you take none of this as a matter,
At last, I believe there are true men in this world.
The wind turns, the river’s voice grows faint,
The setting sun, pale, is veiled by clouds.
A pair of white egrets appears beyond the mist,
About to land on the sand, they take flight again.

---

**A Solitary Stroll by Tianjin Bridge After Rain**

Luoyang’s palaces are veiled in clear mist,
Since Tang and Han, their histories are recorded.
How many rises and falls have vanished—
Only the old mountains and rivers remain on all sides.

---

**Spring’s Colors**

Last year, spring departed and would not stay,
This year, where has spring’s color come from?
Luoyang is everywhere a Peach Blossom Spring,
My little cart slowly turns toward East Street.

---

**Ode to Great Peace**

In days of great peace under heaven,
In times of ease for all people,
When flowers bloom in profusion,
How can one keep from smiling?

---

**Four Poems Left at the Foot of Jinping Mountain During the Cold Food Festival**

**I**
The riverbank teems with peach and plum blossoms,
I cannot bear to see them ravaged by the wind.
How can I let this spring slip away?
I linger on the heights, reluctant to leave.

**II**
Cold Food Festival’s wind and mist at Jinping’s foot,
From on high, I raise my cup—what joy!
The riverbank’s peach and plum blossoms are radiant,
I cannot bear to see them shattered by the wind.

**III**
Boundless peach and plum blossoms await Qingming,
Only after a year do they fully bloom.
Do they not recall the Creator’s labor?
Why does the wild wind cruelly torment them?

**IV**
Half of spring’s flowers bloom in a hundred thousand ways,
Lately, the east wind has cruelly ravaged them.
Pitiful are the peach and plum, so gentle in nature—
Blown away, they utter not a word.

---

**Two Years of Visits to Jinping Mountain, Unable to Meet Magistrate Zheng—Sent to Him in Verse**

**Year after year, the myriad blossoms bloom in splendor,**
Beneath Jinping Mountain, spring’s rewards arrive.
Two years now, I’ve failed to follow your immortal footsteps—
Has the cave’s transcendent one not yet returned?

---

**Before the Eastern Pavilion, a single peony blooms in unmatched glory,**
Twenty-four branches, each a beauty knitting her brows.
Of all the world’s spring, Luoyang claims ten parts—
Yet the Shao family alone holds seven or eight.

**A single peony blooms in wondrous rarity,**
Twenty-four branches encircled by graceful maidens.
None in Luoyang City know this marvel—
The Shao family alone claims the season’s finest hour.

---

**Flower Season Hindered by Rain, Unable to Go Out**

In Luoyang, spring reaches its midpoint in the third month,
Swing ropes still hang, catkins drift on the breeze.
My small carriage stays idle in the mud of spring,
Amid scattered petals, orioles sing in flight.

---

**Chanting in the Abode of Peace**

In the Abode of Peace, duties are fulfilled as they ought—
Beyond this, what more could one seek?
I welcome all scholars under heaven,
And wander every garden in Luoyang.

What I must do, I do with utmost sincerity;
In observing others, I never strain to see their efforts.
Yet recalling those carefree springtime words—
They lift me to a hundred-foot tower.

**Sima Junshi once wrote:**
*“Only now do I know—*
*On level ground,*
*One cannot exhaust the sight of youthful spring.”*

In the Abode of Peace, nothing stirs—
Save a single scroll of Fuxi’s writings.
When weary, I rest my head, no need for sleep;
After meals, I take my staff and wander where I will.

I prepare to steep tea with dew-gathered water,
And guard against mixing herbs to cultivate perch.
Were it not for the sages who dispelled my ignorance,
I might have become a shallow man of this world.

In the Abode of Peace, I pore over old texts,
Old texts that record and link anew.
Three thousand days beneath lamp and candlelight,
Twenty years among waters and flowers.

Mountains and rivers have their masters, hard to claim;
Yet the wind and moon, free for all, I seize at will.
Let none ask of my idle chanting—
This art is not passed down in the world.

In the Abode of Peace, I am a lord of ten thousand households,
How could I bear to waste these fine days and fair scenes?
I’ve already grasped spring in its fullness—
Now, in old age, I would let my heart roam free.

When morning dew is heavy, flowers fill the balustrade;
When warm wine floats, cups brim with drink.
The Sage’s affairs are but trifles—
They spare both effort and worry.

In the Abode of Peace, spring dreams fade,
No worldly dust to clutter the heart.
A light breeze brushes past my seat;
Faint music drifts from beyond the heavens.

As the sun shifts, I lean on my staff and sandals;
Where flower shadows mingle, I linger by the wine jar.
Nowhere in the world compares to my homeland—
And how much finer, for my homeland teems with talent.

In the Abode of Peace, spring does not wane,
The mountain elder comes and goes in his small carriage.
By the water’s edge, green willows line the shore;
Beyond the flowers, fragrant grass carpets the dyke.

Bright-eyed, I watch the scenes of the third month;
Vigorous, I’ve lived through four reigns.
By the Phoenix Tower, near the Heavenly Ford,
I face the wind, tipsy, and return home.

In the Abode of Peace, the third month draws near,
Only in old age do I learn to cherish fragrant blossoms.
I know this one delight is mine to claim—
Who would leave even a single gold coin unspent?

Fine wine I drink until slightly drunk;
Fair flowers I admire at half their bloom.
Such feelings defy description—
I fear no one in the world understands.

In the Abode of Peace, as spring wanes,
I shut my door, too indolent to sit, few guests come.
A gentle rain clears between the bamboo;
Emerald birds flit above the flowers.

I record the finest scenes in verse,
And sustain joy with wine.
Beyond this, all else in life—
I turn my head and feign ignorance.

In the Abode of Peace, I am far from poor,
For within, there is a couch to hold me.
Confucian winds transformed, reaching the Dao,
Harmony fills the four seasons, ever like spring.

Sun and moon shine bright, the sovereign’s light;
Words become trust, trust arises from men.
Only man and the sun stand close—
Beyond this, what truth remains?

In the Abode of Peace, though unsettled,
A brief course of medicine restores me.
In ordinary times, free of illness, I take no herbs—
Even in misfortune, I await heaven’s will.

When reason dims, I must seek discourse;
When emotions reach their end, what more is there to say?
All other vain and idle thoughts—
I may as well exchange them for a day’s sleep.

In the Abode of Peace, spring is about to depart,
Though loath to compose a poem to send it off.
Though spring ages and is hard to reclaim,
Early summer soon arrives to take its place.

Drink wine, but do not let it lead to drunkenness;
Admire flowers, but take care not to let them scatter.
If one understands such things,
How could idle sorrows ever furrow the brow?

**He also wrote:**
In the Abode of Peace, the third month draws near,
Only in old age do I learn to cherish fragrant blossoms.
Beware drunkenness, lest it breed illness;
Fear the scattering of flowers, for they will soon take flight.

Drink wine, but do not let it lead to drunkenness;
Admire flowers, but take care not to let them scatter.
Scattered flowers and drunkenness—bitter tastes,
Bringing not joy, but sorrow.

Born a man in an auspicious time,
In the Abode of Peace, a life of ease and wealth.
With grand strokes, I write poems boasting of vigor;
With a small cup, I savor light, pure wine.

Mountains and rivers, clear after rain;
Grasses and trees, radiant in spring’s embrace.
The Creator’s craftsmanship, so exquisite—
All should be entrusted to the carefree.

In the Abode of Peace, though unbound,
Unbound, I never stray from the Confucian path.
Light wine I sip from a small cup;
Bold poems I write in large characters.

Flowers and trees, radiant after spring rain;
Mountains and rivers, clear in late autumn’s wake.
How vast the meaning in idleness—
Unashamed to be a true man in this world.

---

**On Eating Pears**

I urge you, do not favor the golden-flowered pear,
But rather, love the red-fading pear.
Though both share a hue like rouge,
Their tastes differ greatly.

The red-fading pear is sweet as maltose;
The golden-flowered pear makes you frown.
Such deceptions abound—
Before eating, you must discern them well.

---

**Matching Rhymes in Reply to Vice Director Wang Anzhi**

Peaks rise like screens on all sides,
How can I bear to see them piled with drifting clouds?
We’ve already shared countless flower-viewings—
Let us now arrange a few mountain excursions.

Though not yet old, autumn’s light inspires my brush;
At the first chill, wine fills my cup.
A light breeze is already delightful—
How much finer, if it comes from the depths of water lilies and lotuses.

**End of Volume Ten of *Yichuan’s Songs from the Beaten Earth* / Beginning of Volume Eleven of *Yichuan’s Songs from the Beaten Earth* / By Shao Yong of Yichuan, styled Yaofu**

---

**Viewing Flowers on the Third Day of the Third Month, Thinking of Friends**

On the third day, viewing flowers—how lush their intent!
This year, just as it was in years past.
Where now are those who once shared the sight?
Raising my cup, I still face the distant wind.

---

**Teasing Prefect Wu**

In recent years, men slight fine flowers,
Unknowing how the Lord of Spring resents their scorn.
He bids their price rise a hundredfold—
And buys back all the spring of Luoyang.

One plant bears twenty-four branches,
Each branch a beauty to overthrow a city.
Yet fearing the wild wind’s chill bite,
He has them all inscribed in verse at their prime.

At my home grows one peony, named "Hue-Enhancing Red,"
Which blooms in twenty-four branches.

---

**Oriole’s Song**

To rise to the high bough was ever its fate,
Though it left the valley not long ago.
Its tongue is but tender bamboo,
Its robe newly woven of gold.

For the flowers, it speaks of their brilliance;
For the willows, it tells of their grace.
At dusk beyond the willows, it still trills;
At dawn before the flowers, it sings again.

Its song swells when rain has passed;
Its trills fade as spring departs.
Let no traveler hear it—
The traveler’s path is already lost.

---

**On Appreciating Flowers**

Those who do not truly appreciate flowers
Love only their outward form.
Those who do appreciate them
Love only their inner wonder.

The form lies in their hues,
Hues men may imitate.
The wonder lies in their spirit,
A spirit no man can create.

---

**On Drinking Wine**

Those who do not drink well
Delight only in drinking much.
Those who do drink well
Delight only in drinking harmoniously.

To drink much is to grow sodden;
Sodden, the body falls ill.
To drink harmoniously is to grow mellow;
Mellow, the face glows bright.

---

**On Fewer Concerns**

Fewer cares, fewer dreams;
Fewer words, fewer faults.
The curtain hangs—one knows the day is long;
The willows stand still—one feels the breeze is light.

One sees only flowers bloom and fade,
Hears no gossip of right or wrong.
Why seek a grotto abode?
The years will pass slowly enough.

---

**A Spring’s Span**

A spring holds ninety days—
Half are lost to wind and rain.
The flowers are fair, yet no one sees;
The heart runs wild, yet cannot be restrained.

---

**The World’s Song**

The world is ever in tumult—
Who in it is free of cares?
How fortunate am I alone,
Reclining to watch Luoyang’s spring!

---

**Spring Waters**

Spring waters run clear into waves,
Yet waves can do nothing.
They cannot dye other things,
Only the lightest silks.

---

**Spring Rain**

Spring rain, fine as silk threads,
Drizzling down in gentle drops.
How can one downpour
Make all things flourish so?

---

**What a Pity**

What a pity—this flourishing spring,
Gone in a moment, never to be found again.
Before my eyes, gardens stretch without end,
Yet all I see are orioles singing, no men in sight.

---

**Pinning Flowers**

Pinning flowers, I feign youth;
Pouring wine, I pretend carelessness.
The flowers are fair, the wine is fine, the mood finer still—
Yet tomorrow, my illness will only deepen.

---

**Spring Departs**

As spring departs, do not lament its lateness;
Summer arrives—rejoice in its newness.
Though lingering fragrance remains,
It cannot compare to the shade of green.

---

**Matching Rhymes in Farewell to Prefect Li Junxi, Drafter of the Dragon Diagram**

The affectionate Prefect bids spring farewell,
Just as the flowers burst into fullest bloom.
Since ancient times, heroes have prized gratitude—
Fair blossoms, do not scatter too soon.

---

**Three Poems in Reply to Li Xi Chun, Agricultural Commissioner**

Last year, you sent me poems from afar—
Even then, I sighed at how few friends remained.
Now, barely half of those survive—
How long can we delay our joyful gatherings?

By bamboo groves and water’s edge, the heart feels light;
Beneath the moon, before the wind, the mood is rich.
In Luoyang’s gatherings, days without cares—
If not for our few, who else remains?

The sun and moon in my breast now brighten, now darken;
Wind and clouds beneath my brush now gather, now part.
Though old and without achievement, I am thus—
What would I do if I ever succeeded?

---

**On Encountering Spring in Old Age**

In old age, meeting spring—do not doubt it:
*Commentary: When the patterns of things unfold, when human feelings are laid bare.*
Though no form is visible,
Only the heart can know.

In old age, one learns to cherish the fragrant and fair.
*Commentary: The prime of the year, the height of the three spring months.*
The flowers are worthy of love—
How much more so the finest blooms?

I know this one moment of delight is fated.
*Commentary: When all plants vie in beauty, when rare flowers stand apart.*
The Lord of Spring’s deep intent
Is also to be known by men.

Who would let a thousand gold pieces go to waste?
*Commentary: When the sun hastens, when gold nears its end.*
Extravagant hearts spend all,
Only then do brows unfurrow.

Let fine wine be drunk to mild intoxication,
*Commentary: When the jar’s head foams, when the cup’s surface blooms.*
When guests come to call—
Even in winter, it is called "the time of Fuxi."

Let the fair flowers open halfway,
*Commentary: When the wind is light, as if smiling; when the dew is heavy, as if weeping.*
Only at the point of smile and tear
Does their rich fragrance cling to one’s robes.

Such meaning is hard to name—
*Commentary: When yin and yang first stir, when heaven and earth are not yet divided.*
Words cannot reach it,
Nor brush and ink depict it.

I fear no one in the world understands.
*Commentary: When wine reaches mellow drunkenness, when flowers are in fullest bloom.*
Mellow drunkenness leads to sodden stupor;
Fullest bloom leads to scattering petals.

---

**Matching Rhymes to Minister Xie Yanguo’s "Returning Drunk in Wind and Rain at Night’s Depth"**

In the Hall of the Dao, idle talk fills the hours,
Yet beyond the dust, cups and goblets do not fly in vain.
As I mount the small carriage, the streets are already still—
Drunk, I return with the wind and rain in the night’s depth.

---

**Matching Rhymes to Minister Xie Junshi’s "Only Flowers and Plants Mark Winter and Spring"**

At times, I ask myself—
Am I not a man from before Fuxi’s time?
The days pass, the months come, yet I do not record them—
Only flowers and plants mark winter and spring.

---

**Matching Rhymes to Minister Xie Kuang’s "Lesser Light Now Aligns with the Stars"**

A single word in poetry, its meaning still unclear—
The Lesser Light now aligns with the stars.
I, a man of leisure, have long been without proof—
Why open the back gate for more men of leisure?

---

**Matching Rhymes to Vice Director An Zhi’s "Only Now Knowing Peace Is the Ladder to the Dao"**

My dwelling’s name is "Peace and Joy"—how fitting the laughter!
Yet arm pain and head-wind follow one after another.
Just when I see peace, I find peace and joy—
Only now knowing peace is the ladder to the Dao.

**Matching Rhymes to Minister Kai Shu’s "To Gain the Most, Seek Nothing"**

A guest asks of worldly affairs—what are they like?
Yaofu replies: "I know nothing of others.
Dwelling beneath the forest, walking beneath the forest—
To gain the most, seek nothing."

---

**Matching Rhymes to Censor Bo Chun’s "The Master Was No Trickster"**

Great undertakings demand men of talent,
While the work of governance requires great ministers.
At ease in the "Nest of Peace and Joy," carefree in repose—
How could one know the master was no trickster?

---

**Self-Response: "This Joy Comes Straight from Beyond the Heavens"**

Gained from suffering, it ends in suffering;
Born of sorrow, it returns to sorrow.
Since it is found neither in sorrow nor joy,
This joy comes straight from beyond the heavens.

---

**Three Poems Parting from Chancellor Xie Yanguo**

**I**
You match rhymes with the Elder of Han,
Comparing me to Confucius.
You cite his return to Lu,
Pointing me toward the western frontier—
Another time, the move to Luo.
Sun and stars share in great deeds,
While deer and elk remain humble.
Honored by your praise and favor,
How can I repay what I know?

**II**
Confucius, heaven-endowed, truly enlightened,
His work in creation brought to fulfillment.
You compare me to his return to Lu—
How could Yaofu’s talents bear such a weight?
Once I dashed off wild verses before your seat,
Yet before your seat, you remained a sage of the grotto-heaven.

**Untitled Poems**

**Parting from Chancellor Xie Junshi, Grand Academician**

Boundless wind and moon inspire my thoughts,
Who dares compare to such limpid purity?

**Parting from Chancellor Xie Junshi**
Cao Zhi’s eight measures of talent now urge me on.
Silken verses enfold me, orchid lines unfold.
Ailing limbs, a body worn—
Sweltering heat shimmers the towers.
Better to chant your poems,
And the cool breeze will rise of itself.

**Chanting in Large Script**
Poems composed, half-drunk and mellow,
Transcribed with a brush broad as a rafter.
When the heart is full, let the hand roam free;
When the spirit congeals, dip the tip once more.
Lu Yang’s sun-reversing feat was but slight,
Zong Que’s wind-riding ambition still fell short.
To write of peace too vast for words—
Let the world sing its praises as it will.

**Teaching My Son**
To master oneself is rare among thousands.
Though one knows, speak as if one does not;
The difference lies in action, not inaction.
Only the comprehensive is called talent’s bloom,
Only the outstanding, a treasure at the feast.
How far apart are good and evil—
Some by nature, some by diligence.

**Arm Pain**
First the head-wind afflicted this ailing frame,
Now arm pain—what more can come?
No hindrance to the cup, only to bowing;
Though hair goes unkempt, books remain unneglected.
No need to seek cures in medicine—
Laughter and talk will dispel it all.
All things age and fall ill;
Why should man alone escape?

**The World’s Ways**
Only by stealing leisure does one find it—
I was born into an unhurried life.
Time is finite, all return to age,
But boundless wind and moon console the face.
Beyond my seat, only water and bamboo;
Ascending heights, only clouds and mountains.
Drunk, I sleep beneath the blossoms—
Only when the blossoms fade does the dream end.

**Rhapsody on Untrammeled Writing**
Who says the phoenix nests on Cinnabar Peak?
Beneath my brush, its nine plumes appear.
A single line, when apt, transforms like a tiger;
A grand composition, as if by divine communion.
A thousand patterns, like Sichuan brocade in new designs,
Ten thousand trees, their branches warmed by spring.
The heavenly steed leaves no trace across the eight poles—
Only the wind and moon, struck like chimes.

**Two Poems on the Whirlwind**

**I**
How can there be peace if men are not at peace?
Where hearts are level, strife cannot arise.
I shun the schemes of chess,
But delight in the words of the lute.
In youth, my heart clung to imperial canons;
In age, my thoughts rest on sacrificial rites.
I know where true achievement lies—
Pines and cassia show their green in deepest winter.

**II**
Pines and cassia show their green in deepest winter,
While mugwort and reeds flourish in summer’s height.
Once time has passed, the rope cannot strike;
Before gain and loss, men must contend.
In doubt, move not;
In fortune, walk always.
Men’s hearts are not so far apart—
How can there be peace if men are not at peace?

**Two More Poems**

**I**
Lately, illness has assailed this failing frame—
Why seek no medicine?
Before the pavilion, dense leaves form a canopy;
Beneath the steps, yellow blossoms scatter like gold.
Watching bees gather honey,
Observing swallows build their nests—
Not that the world lacks kindred spirits,
But that Yaofu plays the lute poorly.

**II**
Yaofu plays the lute poorly,
Not that the world lacks kindred spirits.
Old age cannot do what youth does;
Youth does not know the heart of age.
To nurture the spirit, sit in stillness;
To harmonize the mind, delight in pure chant.
Why seek no medicine?
Lately, illness has assailed this failing frame.

**Head-Wind**
Lately, the head-wind is unbearable—
Yet talk and laughter, song remain unchecked.
Men’s talents differ little,
But how much do matters truly contend?
Eyes closed, all before me is darkness;
Heart open, beyond the heavens—nothing else.
If all were won by cunning and calculation,
Many a hero would excel at scheming.

**Answering a Guest**
Speakers always overstate—
The Way must be rolled up, then unfurled.
Good things in the world are not constant;
Where in the world are there no extraordinary talents?
Nearing old age, I roam freely with my staff;
When poems please me, I take up the brush.
Yet even joy may feel constrained—
So I write wild verses in bold script.

**Growing Old**
Old, without achievement, out of step with the times,
In middle age, nursing illness, I only chant poems.
Following my thoughts, I seek release—
How could I stir trouble with words?
The world’s idle sorrows are all the same;
What more can one do in this life?
Leaning on my staff, I walk at dusk by the Tianjin Bridge—
I tell the gulls: beware, do not fly.

At sixty-three, twenty-five years in Luoyang.
In the quiet woods, the city yields mountain views;
By the level pond, my seat overlooks river lands.
Admiring blossoms, I am ever vexed by cups and plates;
Loving the moon, I am often wounded by wind and dew.
Seeing the boundless goodness of peace—
What matter if this body grows old?

**Matching Rhymes with Wang Anzhi, Judicial Commissioner**

One good deed is already excellence—
How can it match your hundredfold cultivation?
Long ago, your kind mother’s love detained you;
For years, the official path held no allure.
Your talent ranks low, but your virtue stands alone;
Your post differs, yet the academy honors your white hair.
Too long has the Luoyang circle kept us apart—
So much joy, now to be set aside.

**Understanding Things**
One who understands things is called perceptive;
One who knows the times is called wise.
Wisdom, misapplied, becomes harm;
Perception, overdone, loses its purity.

**Few Joys**
Why strive for fame or profit?
Why, sir, do you have so few joys?
To value oneself by the Way is right—
To be entangled in the unreasonable is meaningless.
The tale of ascending to heaven by daylight may be false,
But who has ever been drunk exchanging gold marten for wine?
Who says cares weigh more than mountains?
They are but commonplace in this world.

**Rising from Illness**
Illness began a month ago through procrastination—
Who knew it would linger so painfully?
Morning and evening, food is hard to take;
This frail body defies description.
Yet I urge guests to raise their cups,
And take up my brush to write poems.
Only then do I know the heart commands the qi—
When the heart is glad, even deep illness dissolves.

**Half-Drunk**
Half-drunk, I mount the carriage,
The carriage rolls smoothly home.
A light breeze greets my face,
Green willows brush my head.

If the mind embraces three affairs,
And the heart commands nine banners—
Such idle wealth and honor,
I wager none could fathom.

**Half-Drunk in a Small Carriage**
Half-drunk, I ride in a small carriage,
A glory unmatched in this world.
A gentle breeze brushes my face,
Weeping willows lightly graze my head.

If the mind embraces three affairs,
And the heart commands ten thousand troops—
Such idle wealth and honor,
I wager none would contest.

**Gazing in the Mirror**
At dawn, I gaze in the mirror and see a hoary head,
Delighted to find myself suddenly an immortal old man.
Boldly, I dare to speak for all under heaven,
Clearly voicing the lofty Dao of this world.
Though the world itself is fundamentally without trouble,
Still, the world ever abounds with worthies.
I ask myself: what use is this body?
This body is fit only for old forests and springs.

**On Human Malice**
A human life spans a hundred years,
Yet how many hardships lie within?
Now at sixty-three,
I have passed more than half.
Though my bones grow gaunt, it is no sign of purity;
Though my hair turns white, it is no measure of years.
Even if I were to transform now, it would not be untimely—
How much more so when I remain roughly hale and hearty.

**On Old Age**
My body ages in an era of peace,
My body is idle, my heart even more so.
Neither noble nor base,
Neither hungry nor cold.
When guests arrive, I must pour wine;
No day passes without a smile.
The first and smoothest path—
Who will accompany me on my way?

**On the Ancient Zither**
Long have I kept books and chess by my side,
And though poor, I have long treasured this too.
Its pegs carved from green jade,
Its markers inlaid with gold.
Often pawned to entertain guests,
Rarely played to seek understanding.
Lately, my servants are wicked—
I must guard against the day they boil the crane.

**Seeking Trust**
At first, I sought others’ trust,
Some knew, some did not.
Then I sought to trust myself,
And many came to know me.
Now I seek no trust at all—
Who would still raise doubts?
With nothing that must be, nothing that must not,
Wherever I go, all is harmony.

**On Scorpions and Snakes**
Snake venom strikes from afar,
Scorpion venom brings death near.
Though snake and scorpion differ,
Their venom is no different.
The snake strikes with its head,
The scorpion wields its tail.
Alas, in this world between heaven and earth,
One fears both head and tail.

**On Freedom**
The heart is but an inch wide,
Yet why do the two hands cling so?
The body spans but a few feet,
Yet why do the two feet tread so narrowly?
Who does not drink wine?
Who does not read books?
Alas, in this world between heaven and earth,
Only Yaofu is truly free.

**On Peace of Mind**
When the heart is at peace, the body is at ease;
When the body is at ease, the room feels spacious.
When both heart and body are at peace,
What matter can disturb them?
Who says the body is small?
Its peace is like Mount Tai.
Who says the room is small?
Its spaciousness is like heaven and earth.

**On Poetry**
Why is it called poetry?
Poetry is the expression of one’s aspirations.
Once words form a composition,
They convey the matters of the heart.
Not only must the words be refined,
But the intent must be refined as well.
Refining words yields marvelous lines;
Refining intent yields lingering flavor.

**On Doing Good**
When one does good deeds,
Good deeds are right to perform.
Even metal and stone can be moved—
How could one deceive ghosts and spirits?
Act in accord with fate and virtue,
Speak from the depths of the liver and spleen.
Ask not of what lies beyond the self—
Whether others know or not.

**On the Moment**
When trouble comes again and again,
How can one act with true spontaneity?
Embrace the new with resolve,
Follow the good without delay.
Even thieves have their own Dao,
Yet some men lack humaneness.
Virtue has no fixed form—
Where one dwells in peace, there lies the path.

**Chance Reflections**
The sun is the essence of all images,
Man is the spirit of all things.
All images and all things
Are born of heaven.
Words gain trust through man,
The moon shines by the sun.
Through man and through the sun,
How could there not be great peace?

**On Sitting in Stillness**
Life is fated from birth,
All things are fixed from their origin.
Who says man is the most spiritually endowed,
Yet falls short of things’ true nature?
Some say yin harbors ghosts,
Which can bring a man to death.
If death has its cause,
What is there to seek beyond it?
Moreover, man’s fate
Is struck by heaven, not by others.
If yin ghosts possess power,
What can they do against heaven alone?

**On the Joy of Stillness**
Harmonious qi is even through the four seasons—
What time is not spring?
Transforming the joy of nothingness
Into a tangible form.
Quietly appraising things in poetry,
Leisurely expounding principles to the heart—
Though there be no drums or pipes,
This joy is unmatched in the world.

**On Being a True Man**
To be a true man,
One must complete four tasks.

**On Wealth, Beauty, Fame, and Power**
Wealth can make men greedy,
Beauty can make men lustful,
Fame can make men arrogant,
Power can make men reliant.
Once these four perils are wholly cast aside,
What need remains in the dust of the world?

**Longing for Rain**
In the height of summer, rain has long withheld,
And all beneath heaven are steeped in sorrow.
How might a single cloud arise
To bring salvation to the human realm?
Though long drought at last yields rain,
And joy briefly soothes the ache of want—
It may settle the dust,
But cannot save the withering grain.

**On Weighing Righteousness**
The petty man, by nature, lacks discernment,
Seeing only profit as his guide.
The noble man, by nature, does not deceive,
Yet when gain appears, he weighs it against righteousness.
To ponder righteousness is to disregard death,
To see profit may make one forget life.
These two arise from
The level and the uneven.

**On Gold and Silk**
Gold and silk are but one kind of thing,
Yet their use is never constant.
As gifts, they are called "offerings";
As ashes, they are called "remnants";
In trade, they are called "goods";
In hoarding, they are called "treasure";
As bribes, they are called "graft";
In theft, they are called "plunder."

**The Bandit Lord’s Lament**
The Bandit Lord eyes wealth and goods,
His mind fixed on nothing else.
When taking, he fears it is too little;
When caught, he regrets it is too much.
The Bandit Lord eyes wealth and goods,
His mind set only on accumulation.
If only his greed when taking
Were like his regret when caught!

**On Treating Things**
In dealing with things, nothing surpasses sincerity—
Sincerity alone moves all under heaven.
The feelings of things know no distance,
The Dao of heaven makes itself clear.
One must attend to principle and righteousness,
And never boast of one’s abilities.
The world’s idle adornments
Are but empty names in the end.

**On Yao and Shun**
The eyes of all under heaven serve as eyes,
And are called "illuminating the four directions."
The ears of all under heaven serve as ears,
And are called "penetrating the four quarters."
In former years, tassels and floss were bestowed,
Valued for their lack of partiality.
To act without acting, yet nothing is left undone—
Knowing this, one does not live in vain.

**On Airing Books**
Bookworms do little harm,
But men who worm through books do great harm.
Bookworms may be banished by airing,
But what of men who worm through books?

**On Rain’s Trespass**
Such endeavors are hard for others to follow,
This skill is not passed down through the world.
To know heaven by peering through the window is common,
But to know heaven without peering—that is true knowledge.

**Lamenting Drought**
Just when rain is needed, it does not fall,
And where disaster has struck, it strikes again.
How is it that the hundred grains near ruin,
While only mugwort and weeds cover the land?

**On Idleness**
All things under heaven are like a grindstone,
Caught in the mechanism of withering and flourishing, gain and loss.
There is but one thing without counterpart—
It is as when heaven and earth were yet undivided.

**The Gatekeeper Invites a Friend**
A gentle breeze stirs, soft and clear,
A small pavilion overlooks the thoroughfare.
I do not wish to enjoy it alone for long—
Can you come and share it with me?

**Recalling a Dream**
Though the household is poor, the heart is content,
Though the body is coarse, the affection is deep.
Opening one’s robe reveals gaunt bones,
Speaking reveals a nature unspoiled.

**On Writing in Large Characters**
A poem composed, written in bold script—
Who else knows such exhilaration?
Waves like silver mountains rise,
Masts towering over a hundred feet.
Delighting in small cups of wine,
Composing poems in large characters—
In all the world of men,
Who else knows such joy?

**Rejoicing in Oneself**
The common folk flourish, knowing your virtue,
The times are harmonious, witnessing the emperor’s merit.
I, an old man born of humble tile,
Live out my days in this age of peace.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Eleven Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Twelve*

**Dream Within a Dream**
In dreams, one often speaks of dreaming,
Who knows the thoughts upon waking?
Unknowing, is this present moment also a dream?
Then speak again of the time within dreams.

**Midday**
Midday is for *Shi He*,¹
Exchange is the common way.
They know not one another,
Why ponder further?

**Moonlight Upon the Wuyan Tree²**
The moon reaches the wutong tree,
The wind comes by the willow’s side.
The courtyard deep, the people still—
With whom to share this scene?

**Walking in Moonlight**
Through the forest’s clefts, the sky grows ever bluer,
After the wind, the moon shines brighter.
On days when the world knows no trouble,
One may wander here at will.

**An Occasional Verse**
In this world, affairs are hard to settle,
In this world, matters are hard to secure.
There is a man whose name I know not,
In stillness, I see him idly wield his whisk.

**In Reply to a Question**
Idle words beneath the trees—
Why ask further what they mean?
I know myself to lack all discipline,
How can this be called poetry?

**To Li Shenyan, Dragon Diagram Scholar of Caozhou³**
What once was said to be right,
Now proves to be wrong.
How know that today is right,
And not give rise to later doubt?

What once was said to be me,
Now proves to be you.
Not knowing who I am today,
Who will it be later on?

**A Clear Night’s Verse**
The moon reaches the heart of heaven,
The wind comes to the water’s surface.
The same pure flavor—
Few, I think, truly know it.

**Thinking of the Sage**
Never meeting the Sage in his time,
Never seeing the Sage’s face.
Yet the Sage’s words may be heard,
The Sage’s heart may be seen.

**The Noble Man**
The noble man upholds the great essence,
The petty man lacks constancy.
He seeks no perfection in others,
And fears only to receive too much kindness.

**Contentment**
Content with one’s lot, the body knows no shame,
Aware of the subtle, the heart is at ease.
Though dwelling in the world of men,
One is as if beyond it.

**Reflections on Affairs**
The four seas, the three rivers, the five lakes—
All are linked by boats, not by carts.
Countless travelers pass in peace—
Can this all be due to a single censer’s incense?

**Ascending the Stone Pavilion**
A single sentiment, hard to restrain,
I have long feared climbing high to gaze afar.
Today, leaning on the railing, unlike other days,
How many times have I nearly descended—yet linger still.

**Recalling the Past**
Recalling when I first wrote in large characters,
Learning from men who drank wine and composed poems.
Had not true friends urged me on like metal and stone,
Forty-five years would have passed in vain.

**Certainty**
Certainly, in this world, only good deeds,
Not by heaven or earth, but by one’s own heart.
Do not cease because the effect seems distant—
Strive further, and greater merit will come.

**The Vague and Indistinct**
Vague and indistinct, yin and yang first transform,
Misty and swirling, heaven and earth begin their revolutions.
Amidst them, a fleeting moment of fine scenery—
How to find the skill to put it into words?

**Thanking Junshi and Duanning for Their Poems**
They say Kunlun abounds in fine jade,
The world tells of the bright pearls of the deep sea.
Such tales may lack proof,
But what I keep at home is no empty claim.

**On Courage**
One who excels me in courage—
When it comes to benevolence, how could I yield to a teacher?
Courage must be tempered by benevolence,
Benevolence must rely on courage.

**None Like**
None are as close as father and son,
None as distant as the barbarian tribes.
Yet even barbarians may reach harmony,
While father and son may part in estrangement.

None are as kind as father and son,
None as righteous as lord and minister.
If even these two may fail,
What need to speak of the rest?

Lord and minister uphold righteousness,
Father and son uphold kindness.
If righteousness is lost, they become enemy states,
If kindness is lost, they become strangers on the road.

**Neighborhood**
Neighbors passing time in leisure—
A flourishing sign of great peace.
My village abounds in good men,
All the more unlike other lands.

A flourishing sign of great peace,
The world’s finest talents.
On days when the world knows no trouble,
All come to Luoyang.

**Thinking of a Friend**
My desire to see you never ceases,
Yet long parted, what can be done of this feeling?
Though clouds and mist lie but a short distance away,
We cannot often meet.

**Loyalty and Trust**
Loyalty and trust are most sincere toward others,
In ordinary times, it is not that ghosts and spirits are slighted.
Why must they be reserved only for rivers and lakes,
And practiced only when hardship strikes close?

**In Reply to Zhang Chun, Secretarial Aide**
I fear to recall the past in old age,
All the more when I see the sorrowful poems of my late father.
Yet there is a broken verse that brings some comfort—
Now, whose descendants are weaker than mine?

**In Reply to Master Yin Chuchu**
A land of joy long allows men to avoid strife,
How else to describe a heart so pure?
Yet how dare I aspire to follow in such lofty footsteps?
One may scale perilous heights without need of steps.

**In Reply to Wang Maozhi’s Gift of Wine and Sichuan Paper**
White sheep’s jade dust is truly a fine thing,
Just when my arm aches and my head pains me.
Wine brings half a tipsy inspiration,
Why call a doctor when seclusion suffices?

**To the Gentlemen of the Anling County Registry**
Beneath the brocade-screened mountains, a fine place to dwell,
Where flowers, moon, wind, and mist remain unchanged.
I hear that lately, those with long sleeves pass by,
Lingering before the woods on horseback for hours.

**Human Sentiments**
Ancient affairs compared to present ones—
People of today are but people of old.
Only if feelings are not yet fully shared,
Once shared, they naturally draw close.

**Why So?**
Why is the plum tart?
Why is salt salty?
Why is tea bitter?
Why is shepherd’s purse sweet?

**Heaven’s Hearing**
Heaven’s hearing is silent, without sound,
Where in the vast blue to seek it?
Neither high nor far—
It lies entirely in the human heart.

**White-Haired**
Though the five blessings⁴ are hard to attain in full,
The three misfortunes⁵ have not befallen me.
On days of great peace, without trouble,
I become a white-haired old man.

**Heaven’s Will**
Men can speak and thus perceive,
Heaven’s will is wordless—do not deceive it.
Do not say that silence means nothing happens—
This is hardly something the common crowd can know.

**Thanking the Magistrate of Shou’an for the Deed to Shenlin Mountain**
In the southwest stands a towering mountain,
Its jagged peaks encircling like Penglai.⁶
Within lies a place fit to dwell,
Where springs are sweet, trees lush, and dust does not rise.
How generous is the intent of you gentlemen,
Who swiftly agreed and granted it like thunder.
When the waves of Tianjin calm in the eighth month,
I may take a raft and visit it once.

**Matching Rhymes with Wang Anzhi, Junior Vice Minister, Who Teased That Anzhi Was Not Abandoning Yaofu**
Anzhi has not abandoned Yaofu,
Yet fears others may slander him deeply.
Kaishu once spoke and was found at fault,
Xi Chun later said he was innocent.
Our feelings remain as before,
And suddenly, cups and plates are brought out again.

---
¹ *Shi He* (噬嗑): A hexagram in the *Book of Changes* (Yijing), often associated with biting through obstacles or exchange.
² *Wutong* (梧桐): Chinese parasol tree, often symbolizing refinement or melancholy in poetry.
³ *Dragon Diagram Scholar* (龙图): A title for officials versed in the *Book of Changes* or cosmology.
⁴ *Five blessings* (五福): Longevity, wealth, health, virtue, and a peaceful death.
⁵ *Three misfortunes* (三殇): Early death of children, misfortune in middle age, or dying without heirs.
⁶ *Penglai* (蓬莱): A mythical island of immortals in Daoist lore.

Here begins the translation:

---

I now believe your heart remains steadfast through the cold years,
And Yaofu has not been abandoned by you.

**Chant of the Small Cart**
Since thrice I snapped the leather bindings of my books,
Twelve years have passed without reading.
The long days dissolve in the great *□* son’s chamber,
While from the small cart I gaze upon the azure sky.
Leisurely, I become master of waters, bamboos, clouds, and mountains;
Quietly, I gain sway over wind, flowers, snow, and moon.
In the space between bowing and rising, all is joy—
Let others say I resemble an immortal.

**Evening Stroll Chant**
An evening stroll on Shangyang Dyke,
Staff of *qiong* bamboo in hand.
Silently following the fragrant grasses,
Leisurely chasing the wild clouds home.
The moon emerges above the pine tips,
The wind arrives at the duckweed’s edge.
Such scenes within the grove—
How many truly know them?

**Plucking Flowers Chant**
Things exist for the myriad people,
Humans are the spirits of all things.
Humans cannot live without things,
Things flourish only through humans.
Male and female are born of Heaven,
Husband and wife are made by humans.
Beyond Heaven and humans,
All else is but empty name.

**In Reply to Gentleman Ren Kaishu and His Brother’s Visit**
Bamboo shadows flicker as the game of chess ends,
Idle thoughts drift to the Nest of Peace.
Long unseen in these quiet times,
Yet fortune brings you riding side by side.
Your favor cannot be matched by gold,
Nor your praise surpassed by embroidered robes.
Ever fond of poetry,
You’ve led me into this enchantment.

**Minor Spring Days**
The eighth month, a minor springtime,
Like a man in his vigorous youth.
To eke out life is indeed meaningful,
Yet to reverse aging remains beyond reach.
If one possesses the arts of immortals,
Even grasses and trees may bloom anew.
Who knows if in days of great peace,
The world may resemble Yao’s reign?

**Deep Autumn Chant**
All year round, no affairs trouble me,
Flowers bloom in all four seasons.
The small cart carries me forth in high spirits,
Wherever I arrive feels like home.

**Mid-Autumn Chant**
Mid-autumn’s light is fine,
All the more so with the moon full and round.
Most people cherish such moments,
Yet how can one bear solitude?
Clear skies and good company,
Fine wine and a body at ease—
If any of these four are lacking,
This joy cannot be complete.

**Chant While Strolling by the Moonlit Pond with Gentleman Cheng and His Son**
Grass soft, sand smooth, wind gently flowing,
Clouds light, sun pale, willows gently swaying.
Wild words unremembered—what was said?
Deep drinking never matched this cup.
The scene is fine, so we wander at leisure,
Friends’ joy—how could we not open our hearts?
We vow to savor such delightful moments,
Yet fear such delights may not soon return.

**Autumn Gazing Chant**
Grass color blends with cloud color,
Mountain light meets water light.
A towering pavilion, a hundred feet high,
A few lines of wild geese in flight.

**Leisure Chant**
In spring, I admire Luoyang’s flowers;
In autumn, I play beneath Tianjin’s moon.
In summer, I don the winds of Song Mountain;
In winter, I delight in Longshan’s snow.

**On Viewing Master Chen Xiyi’s Portrait and Calligraphy**
Never seen Xiyi’s true form,
Never seen Xiyi’s traces;
Only heard Xiyi’s name,
Yet Xiyi’s heart remains unknown.
Until I saw Xiyi’s traces,
And then beheld Xiyi’s true form.
Only then did I know that through past and present,
The world has always had such men.
Xiyi’s true form is worth beholding,
Xiyi’s ink may be passed down.
Xiyi’s heart—a single piece—
Cannot be spoken, yet cannot be unspoken.

**In Reply to Junshi, Minister Duanming, on His Visit to the Divine Forest of Du’an**
I have claimed a secluded mountain for my dwelling,
Far removed from the dust of profit and fame.
The four seasons determine my wanderings,
Unmoved even by summons to come and go.

**Apricot Blossoms Fragrance**
A guest speaks of affairs in some prefecture,
Schemes endless, boundless.
I, startled, remain silent—
Only greedy to inhale the apricot’s fragrance.

**Evening Stroll at Tianjin**
The *zhi*-canopied carriages long since sparse,
Evening clouds stretch empty to the north.
This thousand-year-old imperial city,
A single stretch of idle palaces and towers.
The imperial parks still extend,
The towering eaves remain jagged.
The bridge arches like a rainbow,
The dyke curves like the new moon.
The banks of the winding stream have deepened,
The Han and Tang eras have long since faded.
A lone figure sits in the high pavilion,
Idly witnessing the rise and fall of dynasties.

**Joyful Chant**
The sun departs, the moon arrives,
An end gives way to a new beginning.
Half its journey in the heavens,
Half beneath the earth.
In its illumination,
There is no sorrow, only joy.
Who am I?
Yet joy ceaselessly fills me.

**Self-Portrait Eulogy**
Pine and cassia in conduct,
Oriole and flower in literary talent.
The bearing of rivers and mountains,
The sentiments of wind and moon.
Borrowing your visage,
Assuming your form.
In the leisure of the Nine’s remainder,
I come and go at ease.
(The Nine refers to the Supreme Ultimate.)

**Extravagance Chant**
Extravagance must not reach its limit,
Luxury must not be exhausted.
To reach the limit is to invite disaster,
To exhaust it is to court calamity.

**Abundance Chant**
In the world’s ordinary state,
Harm outweighs benefit,
Chaos outweighs order,
Sorrow outweighs joy.
How can human life
Escape this?
How can my life
Be consumed by external matters?

**Fear and Love Chant**
Humans possess true nature,
And all things align.
Humans lack true nature,
And all things fall into ruin.
Failing in benevolence,
Wrong is taken for right.
Failing in authority,
Right is taken for wrong.
With benevolence and authority lost,
What do fear and love know?
Unknowing of fear and love,
What use are benevolence and authority?
Unrestrained joy and anger—
Few avoid this fate.
Men and women alike
Should take heed.

**Autumn Pavilion**
Leaning on the autumn pavilion’s railing,
The human heart—how still it feels.
Heaven and Earth in this year’s months,
Tang and Han in these ancient mountains and rivers.
The pale sun before the frost,
The sparse sky after the rain.
Even the finest painter’s brush
Cannot capture this sentiment.

**Floating Life Chant**
Life floats at dawn’s edge,
Grateful to add another year.
Movement may bring regret,
Seeking perfection may not bring wholeness.
Matters of the human heart,
Words of the nature of things.
Though the world is vast,
Who can say it is not so?

**Beyond Strength Chant**
To make little seem much,
To make nothing seem something.
Striving beyond one’s strength
Cannot last long.

**In Gratitude to Academician Fu Qinzhi for His Visit**
Nothing is longer than Heaven,
Nothing greater than Earth.
Even Heaven and Earth have limits,
How can the rest be worth calculating?
Worldly attitudes shift in an instant,
Human feelings stop at this.
Even in achieving great deeds,
One relies on mighty influence.
If fortune aligns,
Take care not to force matters.
Though stern as frost and snow,
Remain unchanging as pine and cassia.
Just as I lament our long separation,
I rejoice in your arrival by way of principle.
Twenty years apart,
Yet you still recall my haggard face.

**Snow Appreciation Chant**

**Snowflakes and Wine**

One flake, two flakes—snow drifts down,
Three cups, five cups—wine warms the brow.
Such scenes defy all description,
As if heaven and earth had just begun their weaving.

**In Reply to Fu Qinzhi**

Qinzhi said to me:
"To chant many poems is not as good as chanting few;
To chant few poems is not as good as chanting none."
I said to Qinzhi:
"Neither many nor few,
Neither none nor must—
Yet chant I do.
Orchids in the room cannot but fragrance spread,
Bronze and stone struck cannot but sound be made.
For evil, grieve—but not to excess;
For good, rejoice—but not to indulgence."

**Strolling by Moonlit Pond**

Following fragrant grasses, I wander far,
Lingering for love of clear ripples, I return late.
Walking alone, chanting alone, sitting alone,
In the season’s first coolness, ere winter’s chill.

**Chant on Confucius**

Confucius was born in Lu, before my time,
A thousand and five hundred years since the Sage’s light.
Who today can grasp this Dao?
In his day, men compared him to Heaven.
Kings and emperors ruled the Central Plains,
Fathers and sons, lords and ministers—power passed down for ages.
The River yields no chart; I accept this fate,
Yet the meaning of refining the Classics was never in vain.

**In Gratitude to Monk Yuanyi for His Gift of a Poetry Scroll**

Reading ten of your poems,
How they stir my thoughts!
Like medicine dispelling doubt’s sickness,
Like tea banishing sleep’s demons.
The moon hangs bright above the pines,
Mountains rise steep beyond the waters.
Tomorrow, if time allows,
I may cross Tianjin Bridge again.

**Self-Portrait (Two Poems)**

What is passed down is prized at the feast,
All because in those days I found a true teacher.
I know now that none indulge me like the old,
And I rejoice in a heart as free as spring.
If fortune favors, I’ll serve the realm’s affairs;
Retired, I’ll be a man of mist and waves.
Within my breast, one clear and steadfast point—
Unbetraying of high heaven, unbetraying of men.

A dragon of land and sea, claws sheathed,
A crane of boundless skies, feathers folded.
Hard to match the steed galloping a thousand *li* a day,
Easy to content the wren nesting on a single branch.
Best of all is kindred spirits in carefree ease,
Climbing high towers and terraces together.
We’ve roamed every fine place among clouds and hills—
Who else is as leisurely as I?

**In Reply to Accountant Du Xiaoyou, Vice Minister of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, for His Gift of Poems**

Good men abound in every quarter,
Yet I possess not a hair’s worth of goodness.
With intent I seek the empty Way,
Without merit I idly love the noble.
Strolling, I watch the still waters;
Sitting, I gaze at the returning clouds.
Old, I live in an age of peace,
Where the court esteems culture and learning.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Twelve Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Thirteen*

**For the Kindness of Various Gentlemen in Jointly Purchasing a Garden at Tianjin’s Humble Abode**

Deep gratitude to the gentlemen who bought this garden—
Within the city walls, a grove and spring now lie mine.
Seven thousand paces of level flowing water,
Over twenty households pooled their silver bright.
In Jiayou’s time, I leased a dwelling by divination’s sign;
In Xining’s reign, the deed was mine to claim alone.
Beneath Phoenix Tower, a newfound idle guest,
In Morality Lane, an old immortal roams.
From Luo’s banks, the morning breeze fills my sleeves;
From Song’s peaks, the moon’s glow spills through my hall.
By the fence, I wear lotus and water-chestnut askew;
In the willows’ shade, I shake the dust from talk so light.
The streets stretch past bronze camels, flowers ablaze,
The dike joins Golden Vale, where grasses spread like silk.
Though spring’s youth lingers, I may yet venture forth,
The sun rides high, yet still I lie abed.
The cave named "Long Life" should have its master true,
The nest called "Peaceful Joy"—who holds its sway?
Dare I open wide my eyes upon the world,
To see the heavens anew in this human fray?
All time I pour into the winecup’s gleam,
All creation I shift into verse’s frame.
I know this patch of earth is fine indeed—
Worthy of Yaofu’s brush, broad as a beam.

---

**Ten Poems on Viewing Plum Blossoms at Zhang Garden South of the City**
*Two Poems in Response to Prefect Li Zizhen of Changshui, Matching His Rhymes*

The east wind overnight split the plum branches wide,
Butterflies and bees dance on, unaware.
I’ve stuck them in my hair and steeped them in wine—
Let others say I’m out of step with the times.

Plucked and sniffed, then sniffed again,
I cherish this fleeting fragrance, pure and keen.
Thanks to the host’s deep kindness,
Though the blossoms fade, he bids me come again.

Cool fragrance, pale beauty—here they thrive,
No storm or gale has marred their prime.
As the sun dips low, our joy runs deep,
Yet how, once gone, a year slips by in time?

When the red sun sinks, our passion burns brighter still,
As jade mountains crumble, our spirits rise.
I choose the fullest branch to break and wear—
Not to crown my head, but to soothe this heart of mine.

After the plum terrace’s feast, what lingers in the mind?
Returning, I tuck plum blossoms in my carriage small.
Passersby on the road may well laugh and stare—
Yaofu’s romantic heart is no slight thing at all.

Steeped in wine, their scent grows fiercer yet,
Played on the flute, their notes ring clearer still.
This fragrance, this music—where they come from is fine,
Worthy of a moment’s deep and frozen thrill.

Spring comes early, plum blossoms blaze,
Though I’ve never been one to drink, I raise my cup.
All day on the high terrace south of the city,
As if I’d journeyed once more to Jiangnan’s shores.

Four kinds of plum—some yellow, some red,
Their hues differ, yet their fragrance is one.
Even from afar, I must return again,
To watch them drift on the wind, drift on the sun.

Though the Five Ridges boast many sights,
The Three Rivers, though few, demand another gaze.
By the terrace, thousands of trees stand,
Deep in the heart of this famed garden’s maze.
Fine things in this world are ever to be prized,
Rare talents in the realm are not lightly found.
When next we meet in some distant place,
We’ll know this gathering was rare and profound.

---

**In Reply to a Friend**

Who says the idle man holds no sway?
His sway lies only in the poem’s sway.
The four seasons’ scenes—snow, moon, wind, and flowers—
All are gathered in these recent lines.

When early spring came to Luoyang’s walls,
I viewed the plums and chanted plum-blossom verse.
Though the plum blooms could not be sent afar,
I sent plum poems to convey my thoughts dispersed.

---

**Matching Rhymes with Junshi, Minister of Protocol, for His Gift of Wine**

The spring wind scatters snow in wild disarray,
How can the woods below not feel forlorn?
The frost’s authority is stern, hard to defy—
A petty man’s gift is hard to repay in kind.

---

**For Secretary Shou’an, Who Sent a Silk Screen from Beneath Jinxia Mountain, Where My Lost Scissors Were Found**

Jiangxia could still mourn a fallen shoe,
Shaoyuan wept only for a lost hairpin.
A single pair of scissors—what’s the loss?
Yet they were long used, and bore my heart within.

---

**For Junshi, Minister of Protocol, for His Gift of Eight Kinds of Mountain Vegetables**

Eight kinds of mountain greens, all medicinal sprouts,
Each plucked from its peak, each bearing its name.
The mountain elder, startled by the frost-terrace’s gift,
Bade his mountain wife prepare them herself.

---

**For Junshi, Minister of Protocol, for His Gift of Peonies**

From what frost-terrace did this rare bloom arise?
Sent to the hermit by Tianjin’s stream.
My wife, startled, rushed to see—
She’s only ever pinned sunflowers, it would seem.

---

**For Prefect Wang Xuanhui for His Gift of Wine**

I’d just plucked a flower branch for a distant neighbor,
Fearing I’d lightly waste this spring’s embrace.
Then came the honor of your gift—two jars of wine,
And the man of Shaoshi Mountain is no longer poor in grace.

---

**Matching Rhymes with Junshi, Minister of Protocol, on Viewing Flowers in Luoyang**

Luoyang holds the finest harmony of qi,
Every blade of grass, each tree, invites the gaze.
Even drinking water brings boundless joy—
Brother, you often raise your cup in praise.

Luoyang’s flowers and trees are famed throughout the land,
Our wanderings surpass the scholars’ own.
Mindful of the East Lord’s charge,
How could we not linger where beauty’s shown?

Luoyang’s friends are all remarkable men,
Taking turns to visit gardens as if they were home.
I laugh at Meng Jiao, so unused to poverty,
Who boasted of seeing all Chang’an’s blooms in a day alone.

The southern garden is planted all in peach and plum,
When spring arrives, we hope the flowers bloom soon.
Thanks to the host’s deep kindness,
The guest from Tianjin does not come lightly to this boon.

---

**Matching Rhymes with Junshi, Minister of Protocol, for His Gift of Wine**

Most men’s hearts are easily made proud,
Two combs are not as fine as a single gourd.
I fear Meng Guang’s heart grows ever more extravagant—
From now on, she may scorn even purple lingzhi shoots.

---

**Chant of Late Spring**

In the woods, I often rise from sleep late,
Now even the carriages and horses grow rare.
Spring deepens, the days lengthen, the curtains hang low,
In the windless courtyard, flowers drift through the air.

---

**Matching Rhymes with Vice-Marshal Gong Zhang of Zhenrong Garrison**

Fifteen years ago, I first met you,
And from that meeting, our bond felt like kin.
Though in the woods there’s little to do,
My heart cannot help but long for old friends again.

Comparing all things to their very core,
Even red sincerity must yield to truth’s pure light.
Among the gatherings of Luoshe, none compare—
When will you come to choose a neighboring site?

---

**Inscription for the Nest of Peaceful Joy**

Nothing is more secure than a kingdom at peace,
Nothing more joyful than a year of harvest’s grace.
If the kingdom is not at peace, the year brings no yield,
How can this nest know health and ease?

---

**Chant of Sorrow and Regret**

I dwell in the city amidst mist and clouds,
A small hermit by Tianjin’s tide.
My work is the classics, my study profound,
My life is water and bamboo, side by side.

Regret that clouds veil the moon,
Sorrow that wind mars the flowers’ face.
Beyond these regrets, beyond this gloom,
What else in the world has time or space?

**Lament and Joy**

When Wu Qi first left Wei,
When Zhang Yi suddenly entered Qin.
The Western River long received kindness,
Southern Chu was deceived again and again.

**Chant of Good and Evil**

The noble person studies the Dao and attends to fundamentals,
The petty person, seeing profit, forgets even life.
Attending to fundamentals, they do not move against principle,
Seeing profit, they do not act without bribes.

**Chant of What Is Learned**

What people learn,
Is fundamentally to learn human affairs.
If human affairs are not cultivated,
What difference is there in learning nothing?

**The Noble Person’s Conduct**

What makes a noble person?
A noble person is surely one who cultivates themselves.
Knowing the noble person’s path,
One makes others wish to follow and wander with them.
They side with righteousness, not profit,
Remember kindness, not enmity.
They praise goodness, not fear evil,
Favor joy, not sorrow.

**Chant of Reflection**

Confucius reflected twice,
Zengzi thrice examined himself.
Who am I?
How dare I forget self-cultivation?

**Chant of the Soaring Kite**

Who says things have no knowledge?
Autumn departs, spring returns—never missing their time.
This year’s new fledglings have grown,
When they leave, how can they not feel deep attachment?

**The Two Grudges of Zou and Tian**

Zou and Tian bore grudges against each other,
The diviner’s words—how could they be trusted?
Profit and harm injure truth to this extent,
The Tian family departed, their resentment hard to pacify.

**The Two Generals Sun and Pang**

Sun Bin lay in ambush, his strategy called masterful,
Pang Juan drilled into fire—how foolish!
All military strategists are deceitful like this,
Yet profit and harm remain unchanged today.

**A Single Word Moves a Person**

Not marrying as a woman,
Not taking office as a scholar,
A single word from a man of Qi,
And Tian Zi resigned.

**Chant of the Four Lords**

The age departed from the Three Kings,
Affairs fell to the Five Hegemons.
The Seven Warring States contended,
The Four Lords still vied in splendor.
Mengchang stood first,
Xinling second,
Pingyuan in the middle,
Chunshen last.

**Chunyu Kun’s Admonition on Wine**

Wine bestowed by the lord,
Drunk without tasting its flavor.
The law’s enforcers stand before you,
Fear and dread know no end.
At such a time,
One cup and you are drunk.

The clan fills the hall,
Filial and fraternal,
Honoring elders, respecting the young,
Old and young in proper order.
At such a time,
Two cups and you are drunk.

At the guests’ first seating,
Solemn and dignified,
Toasts and bows in hundreds,
Rising and descending with ritual.
At such a time,
Three cups and you are drunk.

Neighbors gather,
Like brothers,
In times of harmony and harvest,
Hearts full of joy.
At such a time,
Five cups and you are drunk.

Friends come and go,
Discussing the Dao, seeking righteousness,
Delighting in good things, gladdening the heart,
Climbing mountains, facing waters.
At such a time,
Eight cups and you are drunk.

Men and women sit together,
Cups and goblets uncounted,
Lamps flicker,
Caps and robes askew.
At such a time,
A full *dan* and you are drunk.

**The Great Fish of the Eastern Sea**

In the Eastern Sea there is a great fish,
No net or snare can come near.
Once cast ashore,
Even ants can trap it.

**The Clay and Wooden Effigy**

Clay and wooden effigies,
Take care not to laugh at each other.
When heaven sends great rain,
You can only mourn each other.

**Chant of Defending Against Slander**

Tian Dan’s merit covered the state,
Diao Bo’s words turned the lord’s heart.
Slander has existed since ancient times,
Yet it could still kill nine men.

**Chant of the Three Sovereigns**

In the age of the Three Sovereigns, all was bright and harmonious,
Even the nests of magpies and crows could be peered into from below.

At that time, the feeling was just the same—
Early spring’s morning air, the season’s prime.

**Chant of the Five Emperors**

In the age of the Five Emperors, all was like midday’s glow,
With radiant culture and rites in full flow.
No era before or since has matched such grace,
Beyond this, all else falls from that place.

**Chant of the Three Kings**

The age of the Three Kings was like autumn’s reign,
With power’s weight and lightness, each had its claim.
Deep valleys became hills, cliffs turned to dells,
Ever shifting, the earth itself never stills.

**Chant of the Five Hegemons**

The time of the Five Hegemons was like winter’s hold,
Though of the Three Dynasties, none shared its mold.
Even then, Guan and Yan wielded power’s sway,
Where father and son, lord and minister, still held sway.

**Chant of the Seven States**

The Seven States’ schemes were plain to see,
As Su and Zhang rose—yet was this just decree?
In those days, how did the world align?
Men of the marketplace became ministers divine.

**Sweeping the Land Chant**

In Guan and Yan’s time, some order remained,
But Su and Zhang’s rise left no name unstained.
From whence came the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors’ line?
Sweep the Central Plains clean and await peace divine.

**Heaven and Man Chant**

Though Fu Xi, Yao, and Shun may never return,
Tang, Wu, Huan, and Wen’s paths still may be learned.
Times differ, affairs change—yet both align,
Part by Heaven’s Dao, part by man’s design.

**Profit and Harm Chant**

Hound and hare both perish,
Clam and snipe locked in strife.
The old fisherman sits,
Reaping gain from their life.

**Timeliness Chant**

In its prime, the steed’s speed
Outpaces all in its prime.
Yet in decline, even nags
Leave it far behind in time.
When time and task align,
A word or laugh suffices to command.
But when time shifts and tasks depart,
Even death serves no demand.

**Two Teachings Chant**

To govern without changing customs,
To teach without altering the people—
Gan Long’s words may yet hold sway.
The common folk cling to habit,
Scholars drown in what they’ve heard.
Shang Yang’s way stands apart—
Not as others have averred.

**Words and Deeds Chant**

Words that never stray from *ren*,
Deeds that never stray from *yi*—
Heaven itself will bless such men,
All good fortune shall be nigh.
Words that turn from *ren* away,
Deeds that stray from *yi*’s way—
Heaven will not bless such play,
Who then can such men obey?
There was Lord Shang, who spurned *yi*,
Ravaged *ren* with cruel decree.
His laws became his own undoing,
His body torn by chariots’ shearing.
Now we know: to walk in *yi*,
To cultivate *ren* with care,
Is to be a man who gains
Years added to his span.

**Order and Chaos Chant**

When profit leads the way,
When brush and tongue hold sway,
Famine follows close behind,
Bandits rise like swarming bees.
When filial piety leads the way,
Sun and moon shine long each day,
Years bring peace, the land is blessed,
Life is long, the body rests.

**Great Peace Chant**

The old find their care,
The young find their support,
The toiling find their fare,
The dead find their grave’s report.

**Lord Shang Chant**

Lord Shang won his lord’s trust, wielding law’s might,
Zhao Liang spoke truth day and night.
When his decrees burned like fire’s glow,
How could he know his body would be torn asunder so?

**He Who Holds the World in Heart**

He who holds the world in heart,
Who resolves the affairs of men—
Such a one plays his part.

**Is It Only for the Present?**

Is it only for the present one seeks?
Seeking the past is no easier feat.

**First Emperor Chant**

Nine thousand days he swallowed the land,
Fifteen years he ruled all under heaven.
The blood of the pit had not yet dried when Gaozu came,
And the mounds of Mount Li were already desolate.

**On Delusion Chant**

To feign virtue with cunning schemes,
To gloss over faults with hidden guile—
Though human hearts may be obscure,
The Dao of Heaven remains ever clear.
When hands and feet are all exposed,
How can mere words be trusted still?

**Heaven and Earth Chant**

With nine, the host of dragons appears,
Bringing forth the ten thousand things.
With six, eternal constancy is blessed,
For the firmness of Qian brings advantage.
The four images with nine complete
Make thirty-six in all.
The four images with six complete
Make twenty-four in sum.
How can nine and six alone
Encompass all the world’s affairs?

**Primordial Epoch of the *Supreme Principles Governing the World* Chant**

Heaven and earth are like a chariot’s canopy and axle,
Covering and bearing—how high, how vast?
Sun and moon are like ants on a millstone,
Endlessly circling without cease.
The years above and below,
Their numbers are hard to fathom.
Yet take but one epoch—
Its principles may still be known.
Twelve myriad, nine thousand six hundred,
And three thousand years between,
Up to the traces of today,
Order and chaos, rise and fall,
All are recorded in the annals.
I can grasp them as one thread,
As if I had lived them myself.

**Responsive Dragon Chant**

The dragon, creature of yang,
Moves in accord with the seasons,
First to emerge among the ten thousand things,
Roaming the six voids.
It can dive, it can rise,
It can inhale, it can exhale,
It can grow vast, it can shrink small,
It can be, it can cease to be.

**Where Lies the Land of Immortals?**

Where lies the land of immortals?
The land of immortals is not apart from this chamber.
Before the eyes, no tedium,
Within the heart, a cool clarity.
In stillness, heaven and earth are vast,
In leisure, sun and moon stretch long.
If one can find contentment here,
It surpasses all other thoughts.

**In Gratitude to Attendant Officer Song for the Gift of a White Ox**

Its coat like frost and snow, its eyes like crimson,
Ears and horns square and even, over three feet tall.
Its form is strange, its mouth fit to plow the wilds,
Its nature tame, well-suited to draw a carriage in peace.
Where it grazes by the water, even dragons yield,
When led beneath the moon, hares may be driven.
From now on, in the paintings of Luoyang,
The artist’s brush will strive anew.

**Matching Rhymes with Vice Director Wang Anzhi’s Poem on the Six Elders, Hastily Composed**

Six elders, hoary-haired, their temples like frost,
Their hearts at ease, though age may seem like folly.
Together they choose gardens and pools—what harm in delight?
With cups and platters they rejoice, never too busy.
With staff and sandals they wander through a thousand years’ fate,
Their robes and headscarves thick with the fragrance of ten thousand flowers.
Our gatherings, so freely given, now number seven—
And with autumn’s approach, the air grows cool.

**Six Elders, Joined by You and Your Son**

Six elders, joined by you and your son,
In leisurely posts, far from reckless abandon.
Gatherings without care are easily joyful,
Duties toward others are hard to call busy.
Tender willows droop, greener still,
Dew-kissed peaches blush, warm and fragrant.
Drunk in spring, lying beneath the blossoms,
Just as the shade brings its own cool.

**Who Among the Six Elders Yields to Youth?**

Who among the six elders yields to youth’s domain?
Age need not shame them—they indulge their whims.
Together in stillness, they watch things move,
In leisure, they observe others’ haste.
The heart of heaven, the full moon, the toad stirs,
On the water’s surface, the breeze, the lotus fragrance.
Who would believe there is sorrow in this world?
New wine is brewed, the scene is cool.

**Six Men Gather in Prosperous Times**

Six men gather in prosperous times—
What reason have they not to let loose?
Gardens and groves cover the land, all theirs to share,
Wind and moon fill the sky, aiding their poetic toil.
Their writings pluck the finest blooms of Han and Tang,
Their elegance is steeped in the fragrance of Li and Du.
By the water’s edge, beneath the bamboo, in ease—
No worldly dust, only cool clarity.

**Six Guests Roam a Land of Drunkenness**

Six guests roam a land of drunkenness,
Far from the madness of common speech.
Together they rejoice in long-lasting peace,
Vying in song and verse, seeking the finest lines.
By the moonlit pond, fruits ripen in the grove,
In Golden Valley’s well, the tea’s aroma rises.
Beneath the thousand-year pine, dust is swept away,
Sleeves and lapels, though windless, feel cool.

**The Rustic, Now Seventh Among the Elders**

The rustic, now seventh among the elders,
Knows only rustic, unrefined abandon.
Each day he arranges his verses with care,
Each year he hurries to seize his leisure.
At summer’s end, he delights in new wine’s taste,
At spring’s beginning, he loves the early plum’s fragrance.
Why must he do so?
He cannot bear the chill in his heart.

**Singing Wildly in the Forest, Unfettered by Rhyme**

Singing wildly in the forest, unshackled by rhyme—
How could such singing be called wild if bound by form?
Where the small carriage rolls, orioles and flowers clamor,
When the great brush falls, gods and ghosts scurry.
The gate is barred, the market’s din far off,
The wall is opened, the window a jar—ivy and vines scent the air.
One thing in all the world is hard to find:
The cool breeze of the Luo River brushing the face.

**Matching Rhymes with Vice Director Zhang Jingzhi’s Gift of Three Study Treasures**

Three treasures of the study, all refined,
Yet in thanks, I fret no fine lines come.
I wish to praise this age of peace in song,
But alas, my talent does not match its splendor.

**Matching Rhymes with Vice Director Wang Anzhi’s Poem Thanking the Wealthy Minister**

Having seen much favor and disgrace, what is there to fear?
Leaving the dust behind, I rejoice in my own clarity.
In leisure, the qi of heaven and earth is vast,
In stillness, the light of time and space is pure.
My plain deeds and great plans leave no shame,
My full merit in heaven and earth is not in vain.
How fortunate this rustic, to meet such prosperous times—
A hundred years and more, the world at peace.

**Reed and Willow Chant Before the Peaceful Nest**

Before the Peaceful Nest, a small river bends,
New reeds and slender willows, green year after year.
Before my eyes, the light of time is good enough—
Who says life is ever lacking?

**Jar-Window Chant**

A broken jar, gladly discarded,
Yet when gathered, it serves a purpose.
When used, it must be fitted to the mouth,
When placed, it can hold a bed.
No need for carved windows or grand halls,
It can turn back the light of sun and moon.
Lying beneath it in peace,
One may compare to the sages of old.

**Two Sorrows of Life**

Life is ever burdened by two sorrows,
Sorrow in death, sorrow in living—hard to escape.
Some exhaust their strength chasing profit,
Others wear their hearts out seeking fame.
Many fear they will grasp it too late,
Yet before old age, their hair turns white.
What merit have I to stand above them?
Amidst it all, I alone raise my arms, free of care.

**Self-Portrait**

In all the world, none like me,
Ignorant, with no great skill.
In years and looks, like Li Wenshuang,
In bearing, like He Zhizhang.
Often sitting still, drinking tea,
Sometimes strolling, clad in Daoist robes.

Do not mock another’s path,
For peace and joy are my homeland.

**Mid-Autumn Moon**
Once a year comes Mid-Autumn night,
Nine times out of ten the sky is overcast.
To seek fullness, wait for midnight’s hour;
For brightness, watch until the moon stands high.
Where clouds part, the heart’s depth is revealed;
Sleepless, gazing, the mind grows ever keener.
We praise the ancients’ fine poetic lines,
Yet how can they match sharing this night across a thousand miles?

**Song of the Small Cart**
Spring warmth and autumn cool, the seasons fair,
Years of plenty, health unbroken, times serene.
Over grass soft as a mat, it rolls with ease,
Through blossoms bright as brocade, it moves at leisure.

**A Dream of Home**
In dreams I reached my native land,
A land unseen for twenty years.
Dimly, a new realm came in sight,
Vaguely, old mountains and rivers appeared.
My body lay beyond mist and clouds,
My home stood by the roadside.
Awake, the vision lingered still,
A fleeting moment—all was desolate.

**Evening Stroll by the Luo River Sands**
An evening stroll by the Luo River sands,
Countless stones upon the shore.
Green and yellow, long and short,
Large and small, square and round.
Though they number beyond reckoning,
Their true use is hard to find.
Where lies the fabled *langgan* jade?
Only sighs remain.

**Five Poems in Early Autumn, Harmonizing with Li Wenshi**

**I**
A single rain washes the palace eaves,
The Three Rivers’ scene grows clear.
Forest winds carry the breath of *hao*,
Leaves of trees send forth the sound of *shang*.
Suddenly, lotus buds appear;
Soon, chrysanthemums will bloom.
In these peaceful times, I grow old—
How can I repay this life of ease?

**II**
A narrow path, freshly rinsed by rain,
A secluded courtyard, thick with moss.
Before the wind, a mind at leisure,
Beneath the steps, quiet pacing.
I do not resign to aging bones,
Nor yield to the press of fleeting years.
