From time to time, I rest on fragrant grass,
Grateful for wine to share with friends.

**III**
The autumn of Wang Jin has arrived,
The scorching sun’s glow begins to fade.
Can one nurture the *zhi* like Mengzi?
In quiet dwelling, will I yield to Pan?
Between bamboo, wind rustles leaf by leaf,
Through pine crevices, the moon glows round.
The Luo She abounds with worthy friends—
With each, joy may be shared.

**IV**
By the pool, trailing willows sway,
By the rail, late flowers smile.
Faded lotus droops its tattered canopy,
Ancient cypress reveals its gnarled branches.
As the year wanes, I marvel at time’s ways,
In old age, I cherish the splendor of things.
The winds of Dongling have not ceased—
I recall the melons of my homeland.

**V**
The sun’s rays fade in thinning clouds,
The treetops’ leaves turn slowly gold.
How can one bear the changing hues?
Through to the end, frost will encroach.
Wine no longer brings intoxication,
The wind easily brings chill.
In old age, what does one desire?
Only peace and well-being, nothing more.

**What Does Yaofu Possess?**
What does Yaofu possess?
A single hue, in harmony with Heaven.
In summer, he dwells in the Cave of Longevity,
In winter, resides in the Nest of Peace.
Orioles and blossoms offer carefree delight,
Wind and moonlight aid his recitations.
I surmise that among human joys,
None surpass my own abundance.

**Longing for the Days When I First Learned to Speak**
Long have I recalled those fleeting words,
When at dawn I played before my parents.
Just learning to walk, I knelt at their knees;
Now aged, my temples are streaked with silver.
Once, at four or five years old,
Now, sixty years have passed.
Yet I still delight in my children’s play—
Joy and tears mingle together.

**In Reply to a Friend**
What is called a good place to dwell?
Not in strength or literary skill.
To act or rest lies within oneself,
Right or wrong is struck by others.
Counting the gains of sages past,
Surveying worldly affairs, truth is required.
Fortune and misfortune, regret and shame arise from action,
Resolute, steadfast, and reticent—these approach *ren*.
Change places, and all remains the same—why quarrel?
Silent yet trusted, no need for lengthy explanation.
Though dwelling among barbarians, one may still walk the Way,
No fear in one’s village, though bonds may not be close.

**Sitting Alone, Reciting**
Heaven’s decree is clear and insistent,
Yet many refuse to heed.
The four seasons each have their scenes,
The myriad things all bear feeling.
Fortune and misfortune are matters before our eyes,
Right and wrong are names left behind.
Who would waste their *qi* on idle strife,
Vainly contending with the world?

**Again**
Heaven’s intent is plain to see,
Yet many refuse to follow.
Orioles and blossoms, spring’s sudden warmth,
Wind and moonlight, rain’s first clearing.
Sitting in stillness, thoughts grow clear,
Reciting at leisure, the heart finds joy.
Who would waste their *qi* on idle strife,
Vainly contending with the world?

**Thoughts Not Yet Arisen in the Heart**
Thoughts not yet arisen in the heart,
Words not yet spoken from the mouth.
No spirit may peer within,
No person may find fault.
The noble one values vigilance in solitude,
Above, unashamed before the rafters’ hidden gaze.
Both gods and men reside in my heart—
The mouth must follow in their wake.

**Reciting in Self-Delight**
Within the city of Jiaoru,
Beneath the Phoenix Tower;
In courtyards of wind and moonlight,
On terraces of orioles and blossoms.
Times of harmony, years of plenty,
Strolling in leisure, sitting in stillness;
Friends well, body at ease,
Refined recitations, elegant discourse.

**Song of the Old Man**
A hoary old man,
Weary of all things.
Old objects, half-forgotten,
Old friends, rarely met.
Hidden flowers linger in mist,
Broken dreams scatter with the wind.
I only wish for the world—
Times of peace, years of plenty.

**Song of the Iron Ruyi**
This object is wrought of iron,
Never held in vain.
Ever beside the great *□* master,
Forever with the small cart’s child.
Striking, it shatters coral,
Tapping, it chips teeth.
Who would learn from such a lot?
Only to enter the lash’s reach.

**Reciting on Daoist Robes**
Daoist ritual employs this robe and kerchief,
Bowing only to stars, not to men.
Why must Yaofu bow?
How can one know men are not stars?

Daoist ritual employs this kerchief and robe,
Beyond the master, whom else would I bow to?
Why must Yaofu bow?
How can one know men are not my teachers?

In the attire of a Daoist—loose robes, dust whisk, and cap—
Casual visits among neighbors are the norm.
I hear that grotto heavens are much the same—
My homeland is no different from the immortals’ realm.

If the Dao resides only in the heart,
The work of creation may be sought within.
If one claims robes and kerchiefs make the Dao,
How dare Yaofu don such garments?

**Reciting on Four Matters**
Eyes, only when the time is right, then see;
Ears, only when the time is right, then hear;
Mouth, only when the time is right, then speak;
Body, only when the time is right, then act.
Ahead, no rich emoluments,
Behind, no mighty armies.
Only where *yi* resides—
What care for profit or fame?

**Reciting on a Chance Thought**
In my prime, I toiled and raced,
Accepting office as my lot.
What I gained was but a pittance,
What I lost was beyond measure.
Today passes like a morning,
Tomorrow slips by like an evening.
Inevitably, like a traveler on the road,
I am worn down by toil and labor.

**Reciting on Four Matters**
There are four gatherings one should not attend,
There are four times one should not venture forth.
Neither noble nor base,
Neither fixed nor certain.
Public assemblies, life celebrations, grand feasts, drinking parties—
Bitter cold, scorching heat, fierce winds, torrential rain.
In the village lanes, leisurely visits,
Body at ease, heart naturally content.
Thus for thirty years,
Fortunate to live in days of peace.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji*, Volume Thirteen concludes
*Yichuan Jirang Ji*, Volume Fourteen

**Shào Yōng of Yīchuān (Yáofū)**

**Occasional Writing**
Fine food—do not let it sate,
For sated, one cannot receive more.
Good people—do not let them tire,
For tired, they cannot endure long.
A lowly official bows, and others rejoice;
A high official bows, and others feel shame.
Official rank comes from without—
Why should the heart be thus?

Talent high yet fate meager,
Shame to dwell beneath others.
If not steadfast in poverty,
One does not know the Dao.

The virtuous and powerful,
Wherever they dwell,
Are like orchids and irises,
Inspiring love and admiration.
The wicked and cruel,
Wherever they dwell,
Are like tigers and wolves,
Inspiring fear and dread.

A wife too strong brings misfortune to her husband,
A servant too strong brings misfortune to his master,
A minister too strong brings misfortune to his lord.
A tail larger than the body,
Ice harder than frost—
If not discerned early,
The state falls, the family perishes.

**In Gratitude for the Gift of a Golden Sparrow Inkstone from Advisor Wáng Shèngzhī**

I have often heard of the Bronze Sparrow,
But never of the Golden Sparrow.
At first, I felt shame before the recluses,
For my knowledge of things was not vast.
From whence does the Golden Sparrow emerge?
It must come from the sacred peaks.
Severing the roots of white clouds,
Splitting the corners of green cliffs.
Once carved into a mighty inkstone,
It ascends to the imperial archives.
Water stored within reveals its warmth and luster,
Ink applied shows its depth and flow.
By the window, I delight as its mirror-like surface gleams;
Before my desk, I marvel as the moon seems to descend.
Such a generous gift—how can I repay?
I must offer uncut jade in return.
Why not keep it awhile?
For the great transformation requires measured use.
Why not keep it awhile?
For the worthy are chosen and promoted.
Why not keep it awhile?
For the treacherous are punished and diminished.
Why should it not be kept?
For the people’s sake, to bring them peace.
Then what am I?
I can only receive this gift with astonishment.
I must wield a brush as large as a rafter,
To avoid deep shame.

**Again, Using the Warm Air of a Sunny Window to Appreciate the Spring of Ink Blossoms—Thanking Advisor Wáng Shèngzhī for the Golden Sparrow Inkstone**

An inkstone named Golden Sparrow—unmatched in the world,
In repayment, I am shamed to lack treasures of the realm.
Yet the state’s scholar has poetry of refined elegance,
Where the warm air of a sunny window nurtures the spring of ink blossoms.

**Inscription on the Portrait of Fàn, the Loyal and Illustrious Duke**
Fàn and Shào dwelled in Luòyáng,
Híyí dwelled on Huà Mountain.
Chén and Shào were recluses,
While the Loyal and Illustrious Duke was a prominent official.
Shào came after Fàn,
Chén came before Fàn.
Three men bore similar appearances,
Two shared connected names.

**Reciting on Observing Things**
Time brings alternation,
Things wither and flourish.
People decline and prosper,
Affairs rise and fall.

**Reciting on Flowers**
Fine wine—could it not detain a guest to drink?
Beautiful flowers still know to bloom toward people.
Too tender-hearted to resist the flowers’ intent,
How can one leave the full cup before growing drunk?

**Reciting on Rightness and Profit**
Intent is not as good as rightness,
Rightness is not as good as profit.
Profit moves people,
Making them forget life and death.
Profit is not as good as rightness,
Rightness is not as good as intent.
Intent moves people,
Making them stir heaven and earth.

**Responding to Zhū Diànzhí’s Gift of a Long Rhyming Poem**
Your earnest gift in long verse,
Our village visits span many years.
The year’s affairs come full circle,
Spring’s blossoms follow in radiant succession.

**Trying the Brush**
The heart within the human frame is called the sun,
Able to cast its radiance upon affairs.
How is it that the bright sun illumines all eight directions,
Yet seems confined to the lofty terrace of the mind?
At home, peace and well-being—poverty is no harm;
Without illness, what does thinness matter?
In Luòchéng, I laugh and chant loudly,
Watching the world’s hands and feet scurry in haste.

**Trying the Inkstone**
Wealth and nobility may breed arrogance—few believe it,
Yet do they know how wealth and nobility depart?
Often, in quietude, time passes well,
But I fear idleness brings excessive thought.
The sun rises, and the sky is not dark;
When wind comes, how can water remain still?
In the world, there is much level ground—
Why must one take the shortcut?

**Asking About Seasoning the Cauldron**
I ask you about seasoning the cauldron—
Dare I inquire into the art of its craft?
Only salt and plum are hard to perfect,
But are there not ginger and cinnamon to add spice?
To blend the broth, one must seek beauty,
Yet how can the many mouths all be satisfied?
Beware of speaking lightly of the world’s affairs—
Yī and Zhōu were no ordinary men.

**Reading Ancient Poetry**
Leisurely reading the poems of the ancients,
I ponder their intent.
Though ancient and modern times differ,
Their intent remains unchanged.
Joy and anger, sorrow and delight,
Poverty and wealth, high and low—
Alas, what is this thing called emotion,
That moves people so?

**Bookworm**
Its form resembles a fish,
Its heart delights in gnawing books.
It dwells always in boxes and baskets,
Never in rivers or lakes.
It causes a thousand kinds of harm,
Yet no one speaks of cooking it.
Year after year, when summer peaks,
It molts and is renewed as before.

**Reciting in a Lean Year**
A lean year—my heart is not lean,
A poor household—my Dao is not poor.
Who knows that within heaven and earth,
There exists another fine universe?

**Ultimate Discourse**
Below lies the Yellow Springs, above the heavens,
Each person is granted a hundred years or so.
Yet knowing how many deaths are lived in vain,
It is as if one were never born at all.
To recognize a bright pearl, one must seek the vast sea;
To find fine jade, one must search the famous mountains.
First, one must exhaust all worldly affairs,
Then may one speak of transcending the world.

**Reciting on Seeking a Mirror**
A person without a mirror in flowing water
Should seek a mirror in still water.
Flowing water has no fixed form,
Still water has a fixed essence.
A person without a mirror in water
Should seek a mirror in others.
Water reflects a person’s appearance,
Others reflect a person’s spirit.

**Reciting on Studying Buddhism**
Fine food and rich robes are not easily endured,
Long days and seasons—what can one do with sorrow?
In youth, seeking fame, I turned to Confucius;
In old age, fearing death, I drew near to Śākyamuni.
Futile to sever ties—ties grow heavier;
Vain to seek relief from illness—illness multiplies.
The long river, a strip of silk in the distance,
Fortunately windless—yet waves arise.

**Reciting on Frost and Dew**
Heaven and earth possess moisture,
Which descends in abundance.

**On Frost and Dew (continued)**
Warm, it becomes heavy dew;
Cold, it becomes thick frost.
As dew, the myriad things rejoice;
As frost, the myriad things are harmed.
These two arise from the selfsame qi—
How manifest are grace and might!

**Reciting on Heaven’s Mandate**
What may be entrusted is fate;
What may be relied upon is Heaven.
Man acts not rashly—
Events do not arise by chance.

**On Nature and Disposition**
To cultivate form refines nature;
To follow traces refines disposition.
The worthy follow traces;
The sage embodies form.

**Reciting on Heart and Traces**
The sage comprehends the heart;
The worthy comprehends the traces.
Comprehending the heart knows no bounds;
Comprehending the traces knows no limit.

**Reciting on Observing Things**
No thing flourishes in twain;
No affair proceeds alone.
Glory and decay arise in turn;
Worthy and foolish advance together.

**Reciting on Thought and Care**
Before thought and care arise,
Even spirits and gods cannot know.
If not from me,
Then from whom else?

**In Reply to an Old Friend in Court**
In youth I cultivated literature,
And once beheld the splendor of the state.
Though I have not returned to mountain forests,
I have never forgotten the furrowed fields.
Does the deer in the granary lack ambition?
The phoenix and wild goose follow their own way.
Knowing affairs of today,
Great matters demand reflection.

**Reciting on Not Venturing Out in Winter**
Winter is not that I do not wish to go out—
Wishing to go out, I suffer the shortness of days.
Old in years, I fear long conversations;
In the cold, I dread returning late.
The mountain elder’s head is troubled by wind;
The affection of village friends runs deep.
If you must invite me,
Spring’s light is not far off.

**Observing Things**
Earth, through stillness, is square;
Heaven, through motion, is round.
Having rectified the forms of square and round,
One then discerns the power of motion and stillness.
Stillness prolonged must yield moisture;
Motion at its extreme thus ignites.
Moisture completes the water’s body;
Ignition fulfills fire’s function.
Water’s body is received by vessels;
Fire’s function is transmitted by fuel.
The body comes after Heaven and Earth;
The function arises before Heaven and Earth.

**Reciting on Family and State**
Depravity and uprightness differ in heart,
Yet family and state share the same body.
Depravity brings ruin;
Uprightness brings rise.

**Reciting on Depravity and Uprightness**
The worthy delight in uprightness;
The treacherous delight in depravity.
Delighting in depravity breeds contention;
Delighting in uprightness fosters harmony.

**Reciting on Righteousness and Profit**
The noble person esteems righteousness;
The petty person esteems profit.
Esteeming profit brings chaos;
Esteeming righteousness brings order.

**Reciting on Heaven’s Timing**
Man acts in affairs;
Heaven’s mandate is timing.
Timing, once lost, is hard to recover;
Affairs, once gone, are difficult to pursue.

**Reciting on Grace and Righteousness**
Deep grace makes one kin;
Weighty righteousness makes one lord.
When both grace and righteousness are attained,
Only then is one called human.

**Reciting on Leisurely Strolls**
What is called a true friend?
A true friend is not lightly found.
Without the ears of Music-Master Kuang,
How can there be the zither of Bo Ya?
Though the mulberry and elm cast their shadows,
How could I forget the heart of pine and cassia?
Walking alone on the moonlit embankment,
Each step is met with a lofty chant.

**Reciting at One’s Side**
The myriad transformations are complete within the body—
One must but nurture them deeply.
Why is it called a precious mirror?
Only because it is wrought of refined gold.
Wine, when scarce, is sipped like tea;
Poetry, when abundant, is chanted like history.
Yan Yuan found joy within the world’s bounds—
The world’s affairs are hard to bear.

**Reciting on Snow**
Fine wine and choice dishes with pipes and strings,
All night the cauldron boils in celebration of a bountiful year.
Deep within the lord’s gates, do they know?
A million displaced people lie beneath the open sky.

**Self-Composed on the New Year of My Sixty-Fifth Year, Xining 8**
My home lies within Luoyang’s walls,
Moreover by the banks of the Tianjin.
In my sixty-fifth year,
Amid the Song’s flourishing dawn.
The southern garden borders the thoroughfare;
The northern garden gazes upon twin towers.
Though dwelling in the capital,
It is as if I reside in a mountain stream.
Clear springs trace the channels;
Lush trees adorn the skies.
Cool winds descend through bamboo;
Bright moon appears amid pines.
Before me grow orchids and angelica;
Beneath my gaze, no ice nor embers.
At my seat, joy abounds;
In my breast, no strife.
Winter and summer, I do not venture out—
Heat and cold change in vain.
Glory and disgrace do not enter—
Wealth and honor parade in vain.
I loathe hearing of others’ faults;
I delight in speaking of others’ goodness.
Why hesitate to act?
Why take medicine when not dizzy?
Who says gold and stone are hard?
Their hearts, too, can break.
Who says spirits and gods are divine?
Their sincerity, too, can pierce through.

**Six-Word Reciting on a Small Carriage**
In days past, riding a carriage was common;
Now, seeing one ride, they rush in haste.
Since there are warnings from carriages past,
How can there be no caution in treading their tracks?
Before setting out, one must drink tea;
Before ascending, one must don traveling attire.
At the axle’s side, hang volumes of poetry;
By the shaft’s end, suspend a jar of wine.
The wheels turn slowly to pass fragrant grasses;
The canopy lowers to avoid drooping willows.
By the water’s edge, one must proceed with care;
Amid flowers, one must move with composure.
At dawn, one often traverses the path of shoes;
At dusk, one frequently passes the lane of peace.
In deep spring, one gazes upon radiant beauty;
In late autumn, one feasts sight on abundant harvests.
Before the Tower of Five Phoenixes, the moon’s glow;
Atop the Tianjin Bridge, the cool breeze.
In the Golden Valley Garden, flowing waters;
Beyond the Wei King’s Embankment, tall bamboos.
In stillness, time is most precious;
In leisure, the flavor lingers long.
All one passes is met with delight;
All one sees is free from attachment.
One may see farmers grasping their plows;
One may see silkworm maidens seeking mulberry.
One may see *miwu* covering the fields;
One may see caltrops filling the walls.
One may see thorns growing dense;
One may see orchids and angelica fragrant.
One may see chickens, pigs, and dogs at labor;
One may see eagles, ospreys, and phoenixes in flight.
The harmful are not seen to cause harm;
The good are surely not harmed.
The three peaks of Mount Hua stand towering;
The myriad acres of the Yellow Pool stretch vast.
If not to live as a man in vain,
Then do not squander leisure in Luoyang.
Having gained much of Heaven and Earth’s essence,
What harm if Old Man Yaofu departs?

**Reciting on Peace and Joy**
The Master of Peace and Joy,
Without fame or surname,
For thirty years has dwelt
By the Luo in Luoyang.
His heart holds the wind and moon;
His nature, rivers and lakes.
He takes flight at the sight of color,
Alighting only after soaring.
No lowly, no poor;
No rich, no noble.
No sending off, no welcoming;
No restraint, no taboo.
Never troubled by hardship;
Never drinking to drunkenness.
He gathers the world’s spring
Into his liver and lungs.
His pool nurtures his chants;
His jar-window invites sleep.
The small carriage delights his heart;
The great brush quickens his will.
Sometimes he dons a bamboo hat;
Sometimes he wears a half-sleeve.
Sometimes he sits amid the woods;
Sometimes he walks by the water’s edge.
He delights in seeing good people;
He delights in hearing good deeds.
He delights in speaking good words;
He delights in acting with good intent.
Hearing of others’ faults,
It is as if he bears thorns.

Hearing of others’ goodness,
It is as if he wears orchids and thoroughwort.

He neither flatters Chan masters
Nor flatters masters of esoteric arts.
Without stepping beyond his gate,
He directly reaches heaven and earth.
No army can overawe him;
No wealth of ten thousand bushels can entice him.
A joyful man of sixty-five years—
His jar-window invites his chants.

**Chant by the Jar-Window**

A guest knows nothing,
Only knowing to guard himself.
In guarding himself, there is nothing else—
Only seeking to avoid fault.
He has a few rooms,
A few acres of fields.
He uses a basin as his pool,
A jar as his window.
Walls higher than his shoulders,
A room larger than a peck measure.
A cloth quilt warms him beyond need,
A bowl of millet gruel fills him past hunger.
The qi he exhales from his chest
Fills the cosmos.
His brush falls upon the world,
Its radiance outshining jade and gem.
If one knows contentment,
Retreat becomes flourishing;
I never go out—
What retreat could there be?

His heart harbors no reckless thoughts,
His feet take no reckless steps.
He makes no reckless friendships,
Accepts no reckless things.
Fiercely debated,
He sweetly endures his humble state.
Loftily spoken,
None surpasses him.
The books of the sages never leave his hand;
The words of Yao and Shun never leave his lips.
Amidst harmony and heaven,
He delights in easy friendships.
He chants poems at ease,
Drinks joyful wine.
A hundred years of peace—
Is this not fortune?
Seventy years of health—
Is this not longevity?

**Chant by the Basin Pool**

A guest knows nothing,
Only knowing not to act.
In not acting, there is nothing else—
Only seeking not to deceive.
I have what others affirm;
Others have no fault of mine.
Thus he opens his jar-window,
And so he digs his basin pool.
Urban land is precious,
But rivers and lakes hold wondrous scenes.
Able to wander marshlands,
He need not step from his hall’s foundation.
Green grass beyond the curtain,
Yellow banks before the pavilion.
The moon sets within his pot,
Clouds fly within his mirror.
Since there are lotus and water-chestnuts,
How could there be no reeds?
Since there are tadpoles,
How could there be no dragons?
Sometimes clear and shallow,
Sometimes vast and boundless.
Sometimes limpid and pure,
Sometimes rippling with waves.
Wind stirs the duckweed,
Coolness rises in his sleeves.
Where is Lin Zong?
Where has Fan Li returned?
Dense snow falls softly,
Light ice spreads thinly.
Willows droop gently,
Fine rain drifts faintly.
Here one may find perfection,
Here one may forget schemes.
Here one may reflect things,
Here one may observe the seasons.
If not joyful in myself,
Who else could bring me joy?
On this day,
I meet Fuxi once more.

**Chant of the Small Carriage**

A guest knows nothing,
Only knowing he has a home.
With a home to return to,
His return is not distant.
His spirit terrace gleams with stillness,
Heaven and earth bloom with flowers.
He writes with a great brush,
Rides in a small carriage.
Born a man
In the Central Kingdom,
And dwelling in Luoyang—
What greater fortune could there be?
At the center of heaven and earth,
The true abode of emperors.
The legacy of Han and Tang,
Its grandeur naturally fine.
A realm of sages and worthies,
A deep pool of scholars.
A field of benevolence and righteousness,
Boundless in what one hears and sees.
Neighborhoods press close,
Friends and kin visit often.
Others suspect the sun’s chariot,
I say it is the stars’ array.
Sometimes he roams Golden Valley,
Sometimes he drifts on Moonlit Waves.
Sometimes he treads the Path of Shoes,
Sometimes he passes Bronze Camels.
Advancing and retreating like clouds and water,
Unfurling and rolling like mist and rosy clouds.
He extols the wind and moon,
Lifts and adorns orioles and flowers.
By nature he delights in wine,
Delights in a slight flush from drinking.
Before the flush comes,
His mouth already chants.
When chanting is not enough,
He breaks into a vast song.
When vast song is not enough—
There is nothing to be done.

**Chant of the Great Brush**

A guest knows nothing,
His nature too unadorned.
He neither envies nor seeks,
Neither clings nor insists.
His feet tread the root of heaven,
His hands probe the moon’s cave.
What his heart gains,
He pours entirely into his brush.
Thoughts distant, emotions fused,
Qi harmonious, spirit at ease.
Wine loosens him to a slight glow,
Silk spreads half a bolt.
Swift as the wind’s sudden gust,
Burgeoning as the clouds’ surge.
Fleeting as lightning’s flash,
Dense as the rain’s fall.
Now halting, now returning,
Now vanishing, now appearing.
It scours away dust and haze,
Clears the sky and reveals the sun.
Phoenixes soar,
Dragons and serpents coil.
Spring blossoms glow bright,
Autumn mountains stand towering.
Three thousand scholars in caps and robes
Bow to follow the Confucian way.
A million warriors in armor
Look up to heed military discipline.
Pines and cassia form a forest,
Orchids and irises fill the room.
Sichuan brocade unfurls,
Dawn’s rosy clouds brush past.
A pair of white jades,
A hundred measures of gold.
Wang Xizhi comes to seek,
Du Mu comes to beg.
Spiritual communion beyond the world,
Worldly affairs come to an end.
Observers are startled awake,
Those who collect it recover faster.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Fourteen*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Fifteen*
*Yichuan Shao Yong Yaofu*
*Observing the Changes, a Chant*

**A Chant on Observing the One**

A single thing comes with a single body,
Within that body lies another heaven and earth.
If you know all things are complete within me,
How could you set the Three Powers apart to root them?
Heaven from its center divides substance and function—
Or one might say, the workings of creation.
Humans weave their plans within the heart.
Heaven and humanity—how could there be two meanings?
Or one might say, all affairs.
The Dao does not move in vain; it rests in humanity.

---

**A Chant on Observing History**

Alas! The power of four dynasties’ kings
All enters this humble, ancient volume.
Some yielded, some contested—thirty thousand li,
Linked and transformed across two thousand years.
Who could continue the deeds of Tang and Yu?
The arts of Tang and Wu are lost to the world.
Times differ, and so do people—
How could Confucius not weep in sorrow?

---

**A Chant on Observing the *Book of Poetry***

To love one’s lord as in those days is rare,
Yet nothing captures human feeling like poetry.
Without the *Ya*, how could royal teachings be clear?
Only with the *Feng* can a state’s rise and fall be known.
No connoisseur compares to the Lord of Wu,
And even Confucius once polished its lines.
Three hundred and five poems—all the world’s affairs—
Who among later men would dare to criticize?

---

**Observing the *Spring and Autumn Annals***

The august royal house exists in name alone,
War never ceases beneath heaven.
States are destroyed, men are conquered—none lags behind,
Seeking alliances, exacting vengeance—never at peace.
The decrees of Jin and Qi burn like fire,
While the foundations of Wen and Wu chill like ice.
Only the sorrow for the unicorn remains a single heart,
Preserved for ten thousand years as if in cinnabar.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Three Sovereigns**

How vast the cosmos—it proclaims itself through me,
Beyond heaven and earth, what more is there to say?
At first, the Great Dao was no ordinary Way,
Just as there was a time before and after heaven.
Its methods were so subtle, no trace could be seen,
Its achievements so enduring, the years were unknown.
If the world were to rank meritorious deeds,
None could stand before them.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Five Emperors**

Willing to yield or advance the realm,
What words could describe their grace?
Where their robes hung, majesty flourished,
When jade and silk were offered, reverence shone.
Every thing followed the highest principle,
Each person willingly achieved great deeds.
How did they attain this?
Only by their brilliance, like the sun at noon.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Three Kings**

A vast expanse of the Central Plains—over ten thousand *li*—
Hardly a place for the weak to dwell.
Xia and Shang could still proclaim their calendars,
Yet Tang and Wu could not easily wield their weapons.
The Fire of Ze received its name and thus transformed,
But the Water of Heaven found no response—no need arose.
Those who take benevolence and righteousness as their heart
Would never become petty men of the world.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Five Hegemons**

Intent on honoring their names, their names suffered more,
As people rushed to obey, exhausted beyond endurance.
Lives were lived amid forests of swords and spears,
While justice and wealth were carried in their hearts.
Though they could sigh for the lamb and cherish ritual,
Alas, the phoenix never came to grace them.
Within the remaining years of Eastern Zhou’s Five Hegemons,
Only Confucius was heard to sigh.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Seven States**

At their end, they still schemed and maneuvered,
Deaf to words of benevolence and righteousness.
Who would have thought Qi could be broken yet Jimo preserved?
Or that victory at Changping meant burying Zhao’s men alive?
Seeing ghosts at dawn was no strange thing,
Killing in broad daylight caused no surprise.
Then came Su and Zhang, wielding their three-inch tongues,
Grasping throats so none could live together.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Qin Dynasty**

The seven states clashed with thunderous might,
Grinding against one another, never at rest.
Only when one lord’s heart was set
Did the four seas run red with blood.
Three thousand retainers soon became a dream,
A hundred and two mountains and rivers turned to autumn.
Boasting of enfeoffing marquises to guard the land—
Yet Zhao Gao was no marquis at all.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Two Han Dynasties**

The old battlegrounds of Qin’s broken mountains and rivers—
Who would have thought the people would farm mulberries again?
Nine thousand *li* of land opened to enfeoffment,
Over four hundred years bearing the title of emperor.
After ruin came Wang Mang and Dong Zhuo,
Yet none remembered the efforts of Gao and Guang.
With such splendor of culture in those days,
How could the city walls ever lie in ruins again?

---

**A Chant on Observing the Three Kingdoms**

Awe-inspiring, the tripod’s stand shook like thunder,
Yet its lofty traces are lost to time.
Drums and flutes in one region left emotions unfulfilled,
Bows and blades across ten thousand *li* strained their strength.
Debating war with Hengshi—was there no intent?
Watering horses at the Yellow River—was it all in vain?
Though one may speak of heaven’s timing, it is also human affairs—
Who could have known that beyond foresight, fine gold would be lost?

---

**A Chant on Observing the Western Jin**

Peace does not mean there is no worry,
To be at ease and forget danger is no good plan.
Ranking men’s talents relied on biting satire,
Judging the times required a taste for elegance.
A knife could not cut open the belly of Gonglü,
No wood could behead Liu Yuan.
The disaster lay in a single line at Evening Sun Pavilion,
While at the Eastern Gate, the waves sighed endlessly.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Sixteen Kingdoms**

Beneath the vast sky, the realm was called the imperial domain,
Where Great Yu once governed the aftermath of floods.
When clothes wore out, lice and nits multiplied,
When melons rotted, worms and maggots thrived.
Dragon robes were never meant for mad bandits,
Nor were the standards of court ever raised for rebellious Hu.
Confucius had words worth pondering—
In those days, they lacked a Guan Yiwu.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Northern and Southern Dynasties**

When the world was split between north and south,
Envoys never ceased their journeys.
Partial hegemons still upheld ancient laws,
A fragile peace bore traces of old stench.
Luoyang’s refined reputation praised Cui Hao,
The south’s extraordinary talent admired Xie An.
For two hundred and forty years, they rode side by side—
How could one casually call them barbarians?

---

**A Chant on Observing the Sui Dynasty**

From the start, the plans were flawed,
And those who followed only compounded the harm.
People were as insignificant as ants, coveting land,
Gold and silk as worthless as mud, pleasing concubines.
The campaign against Liaodong aimed for the wild frontiers,
The canal’s flow expressed weariness with the endless.
Thirty-six years—all swept away.
Had it not been so, the world would not have returned to Tang.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Tang Dynasty**

Heaven-born, divine, and martial, it settled the center,
For without it, the many rebels would not have been quelled.
Without the stern winds of the Zhenguan era,
How could the Kaiyuan age have known such glory?
Only from on high could the grandeur of mountains and rivers be seen,
Only in summer could the length of days and months be known.
For three hundred years, it unified the realm—
Though the deeds are past, the Dao shines ever brighter.

---

**A Chant on Observing the Five Dynasties**

Since the Tang’s fall and the collapse of imperial order,
The world’s people have suffered turmoil.
The altars of state hung on the whims of soldiers,
The court’s weight was decided by the clamor of regional lords.
Deep winter’s cold trees do not shed their leaves,
The faint stars before dawn still hold their light.

**A Chant on Observing the Five Dynasties (continued)**

Fifty-three years passed under five successive houses—
Only then did we know the land awaited a true king.

**A Chant on Witnessing Prosperity**

Amid the chaos of the Five Dynasties’ upheaval,
Suddenly the clouds parted, and heaven reappeared.
Grass and trees, bathed in fresh rain and dew for a hundred years,
Wheels and writs now traverse the old mountains and rivers for ten thousand *li*.
In ordinary lanes and alleys, officials still don their caps and sashes,
While gardens and pavilions, one after another, resound with pipes and strings.
Though the people grow old in this age of peace, spring remains young—
Orioles and blossoms do no harm, even as the sun climbs high and men sleep on.
We, who cultivate simplicity, rely on this enlightened age,
Fortunate beyond measure—how could we not know?
The realm’s finest talents now retreat into obscurity,
While in this world of fair scenes, brows unfurrow in delight.
Born only to know abundance,
Aged without ever enduring chaos or separation.
Five deeds surpass all previous dynasties—
Even under Yao, none could match them:
First: On the day of revolution, the markets did not alter their stalls;
Second: The conquest of the realm was achieved after ascending the throne;
Third: Not a single innocent life was taken;
Fourth: At a hundred years, only four reigns;
Fifth: A century without internal strife.

**A Chant on Rejoicing in Old Age**

How long can these silken temples last?
Why trouble with plucking each white hair?
Though I hold no office in this world,
I know well the affairs of men.
When spring warms and autumn cools,
That is my time to wander east and float west.
How many vast and fertile fields lie open—
Only now does this old mountain-dweller truly smile.

**A Chant on Paying Homage to Confucius**

Who, holding a scroll, does not read?
But how many truly grasp the nature of things?
His craft lies within the world’s speech,
Yet his subtlety transcends the carpenter’s line.
Refining being and non-being—heaven’s work;
Balancing order and chaos—the emperor’s task.
Great indeed is his intent in praising the *Yi* and compiling the Classics—
Surely none among the people since his time have matched it.

**Returning the Poetry Scroll of Beneficent Master Yi**

Monk’s staff and alms bowl have followed me, wrapped in a single cloth,
That cloth once brushed the dust of ten provinces.
His mind, long attuned to Buddha-nature, knows no hindrance,
His speech, though steeped in Confucian words, remains unforced.
The mountains and rivers of Wu and Yue are but yesterday’s affairs,
While the wind and moon of Yi and Song now belong to this body.
Strolling or sitting idly beneath pine shadows,
He must rely on sharp eyes to laugh at the world.

**A Chant on Heaven and Man**

To know all of human nature—how could heaven differ?
Yet not knowing is as distant as heaven from earth.
In youth, the qi is sharp but untried,
Unwilling to believe the world holds hardship.
To know both human nature and heaven’s will—
Speak of them as one, and where is the division?
To extend one’s own heart to others’ hearts,
What worry remains that the realm cannot be governed?

**A Chant on Spring at Brocade Screen Mountain**

Beneath Brocade Screen Mountain lies my home,
Where every year the festival of Cold Food arrives.
Early spring’s weather is already fine—
How much more so with a master so worthy for a hundred *li*?
Together we drink by the same stream,
Drunk, we sleep beneath ten thousand blossoms.
Tomorrow, the saddle turns eastward—
The scenery of Shangyang will be even more radiant.

**A Chant on Delighting in Spring**

Of the four seasons, I love spring best,
And in spring, I love the vernal equinox most.
Warmth gently nurtures all things,
No chill clings to men.
Fine flowers just unfurl their buds,
Exquisite wine is light and mellow.
A guest in this nest of peace and joy—
How could I not grow half-drunk?

**Four Chants on Observing Things**

The sun and moon shine no differently,
Yet day and night, the moon’s form changes.
Men and ghosts share the same emotions,
But life and death follow different principles.
If you cannot know life,
How can you know death?
If you cannot serve men,
How can you serve ghosts?

Orioles and cicadas differ in form—
Why trouble yourself with vain words?
When qi is abundant, strength overflows,
When sound fades, it is no different from silence.
Timing comes by one’s own effort,
Fate departs at another’s will.
Do not grieve over heart-wrenching matters—
Grief brings no benefit to the body.

The passions of past and present are one—
Who finds it hard to endure?
Discerning affairs is no easy task,
Knowing men is even harder.
Excessive doubt undermines trust,
Blind faith loosens vigilance.
Even Yao and Shun had their flaws—
How could they have lacked great villains?

What a man’s ears hear
Cannot compare to what his eyes witness.
Ears hear conflicting accounts,
Eyes see no deception.
To cast aside ears and eyes,
Relying solely on the tongue’s debate—
This may not achieve the world’s work,
But only invite the world’s laughter.

**A Chant on the Value of Spirit**

A man’s worth lies in his spirit,
Yet spirit often lacks purity.
To possess spirit and purity—
That is the first-rank man.
Without purity, there is no principle;
Right and wrong shift with anger and joy.
In anger, right becomes wrong;
In joy, wrong becomes right.
Anger estranges the good,
Joy aligns with the petty.
Few are the ruined states and lost families
Not born of this.

Take a wife for her gentleness,
Marry a husband for his talent and beauty.
How rare the upright woman
Who matches a true man!

**A Chant on Righteousness and Profit**

Nobler than hills and gardens,
Even meager silks are prized.
When righteousness comes first,
Profit follows naturally.
Abandon your sacred tortoise,
To gawk at my chewing jaws.
Having lost righteousness,
What can profit achieve?
To esteem righteousness is to yield—
The gentleman’s Way grows.
To esteem profit is to contend—
The petty man’s Way prevails.

**A Chant on the Small Carriage’s First Outing**

Beyond the world lie thirty-six grotto-heavens,
All seemingly arrayed within Luoyang.
On days when spring warms and autumn cools,
A small carriage can move but one palace a day.

**Composed at the Banquet of Prefect Wang Xuanhui**

The capital’s three-time host, master of the house,
Whose grand estate and famous gardens crown Luoyang.
Again, a new spring falls into his hands—
Ten thousand flowers’ fragrance illuminates the wine cups’ crimson.
Another spring has passed in a flurry,
Leaving only melancholy, drowned in wine’s depth.
All the radiant scenes have faded from view—
Only before the goblet do I recall old friends.

**A Chant in Late Spring, Replying to a Friend**

After a year apart, how do you fare?
Do not say that being neighbors leaves virtue unaccompanied.
Luoyang’s spring has already ended in a span of inches—
Do you still remember our past visits?

**A Chant on Hearing Music at Tianjin Bridge**

Famous gardens stand side by side in Luoyang’s spring,
Lanes and alleys are dust-free, silks and satins renewed.
Where is that green tower beyond the peach and plum?
The strains of music drift again to Tianjin Bridge.

**A Chant on Late Spring**

Willow catkins, full of intent, vainly imitate snow,
Elm pods, heartless, idly pile like coins.
Poverty and sorrow cannot bear spring’s betrayal—
Wine-sickness lingers, little changed from last year.

**Two Self-Inquiries**

Why have I been able to write poetry these past years?
Alas, the years age, and spring returns again.
The wandering oriole cannot bear the flowers’ scattering,
Crying until dusk, it still cries on.

**Why These Years of Poetry?**

Why these years of poetry?
Peach and plum stay silent, but the oriole has words.
It cries until dusk and still cries on—
Alas, the years age, and spring returns again.

**In Response to Commissioner Yu Gongda of Chengdu**

Two years ago, my carriage passed Tianjin,
Where we often raised our cups beneath the flowers.
Now in this brocade city, blossoms blaze—
Yet how could I recall Luoyang’s spring?

**Two Chants on Wu and Yue**

In the *yisi* year, Helü humbled Chu;
In the *wuchen* year, Goujian crushed Wu.
Had they seized the moment as of old,
Thirty-four years would have passed unknown.

Fu Chai, in the *dingwei* year, imprisoned Yue;
Goujian, in the *wuchen* year, destroyed Wu.
Twenty-two years of reversal—
Just as before, they struck at emptiness.

**A Chant on Duty**

The wren finds its nest in a single branch,
Not swayed by sweet words into careless pride.
Even with strength, crossing the North Sea is hard—
How could mere might move a goose feather?
I wish to express my gratitude clearly,
Not to squander favor in reckless haste.
Heaven’s blessings are many, yet fortune fades—
Spring flowers, though lush, still wither too soon.

**A Chant on Rise and Fall**

Sun, Chen, and Li—
Their downfalls were alike.
Though they had some culture,
Where was their heroic spirit?

Cao, Liu, and Sun—
Their rises were alike.
Though they had some talent,
Where was their world-changing destiny?

**A Chant on Civil and Martial Arts**

As a man of letters,
One must also be prepared for war.
The Way of civil and martial arts—
Both are my family’s affairs.

**A Chant on Good and Evil**

Gun had a son,
While Yao and Shun had none.
Why do blessings and calamities follow thus?

Yao and Shun had no heirs,
While Gun had descendants.
Why do fortune favor virtue and disaster strike excess?

**A Chant on Self-Examination**

Not being a perfect man,
I do not demand perfection in others.
But if I were a perfect man,
I would demand perfection in myself.

**A Chant on Well-Being**

Health without illness,
Fortune without disaster—
Even if the whole world enjoyed them,
They would not be enough.

**A Chant on Four Things**

Wealth, beauty, fame, and power—
The world clings to these.
Only when unmoved by the four
Can one truly see others.

**A Chant on Gratitude and Resentment**

In human nature,
Nothing weighs heavier than death.
Gratitude stirs the heart—
Even in death, there is joy.
Resentment binds the heart—
Even in death, it does not end.
So deep are gratitude and resentment,
They shape a person thus.

**Two Chants on Qin Chuan**

Back then, I rode through Qin Chuan,
And in a flash, twenty years have passed.
Hearing you speak of home,
I vaguely recall its old mist and haze.

Qin Chuan, land of Han emperors,
Now the eastern passes hold the capital.
How many sages and worthies fill the old histories—
Yet in the setting sun, only desolation remains.

**In Response to Prefect Wang Zhongxian of Jiang**

As a gentleman of Jiang, you bear the bronze tiger tally,
And your poems are no empty boast.
The land still bears the grasses and trees of Tang,
The mountains and rivers still stir the clouds and mist of Jin.
Your gardens and ponds overflow with verse,
The customs are pure, and lawsuits never reach your hall.
As prefect, you bring blessings to the people—
Your heart never fails the court.

**A Chant on Sun and Moon**

The moon shines, stars grow sparse;
The sun rises, the moon fades.
If there was a Shao Zhengmao,
Could there be no Confucius?

**A Chant on Flood and Drought**

Yao endured nine years of floods,
Tang seven years of drought.
Where now is the skill of their governance?

Nine years of floods,
Seven years of drought—
Had it not been for Yao and Tang,
Half the people would have perished.

**A Chant on Growing Old**

If I could turn back ten years,
I might still accomplish something.
But in this world,
The sun never returns to noon.
Now I am sixty-six,
My decline beyond compare.
My will outstrips my strength—
All know this well.

**A Chant on Human Affairs**

Nothing is harder to refine than affairs,
Nothing harder to know than people.
Human feelings shift with every gesture,
Yet affairs all reach the same end.

**A Chant on Difference**

The seeker does not obtain,
The refuser cannot let go.
How far apart are the two?

**A Chant on Greed and Righteousness**

To covet another’s evil—
No greater fault exists.
To covet another’s good—
This too is a sin.

**A Chant on the New Moon**

The new moon and the waning moon,
Their shapes are much the same.
Yet why does the human heart
Rejoice only at the first sight?

**In Response to Magistrate Li Shifu of Neixiang**

Though not like an immortal,
You escape heat and cold.
How could there be no streams and bamboos?
You never leave the forests and peaks.
The Dao differs from new learning,
Talent alone won’t move high officials.
Yet in times of peace and plenty,
There is still joy to spare.

The year is abundant, the times prosperous,
And your post lies in Nanyang.
Not neglecting the people’s affairs,
You wander among clouds and waters.
Spring offers peach and plum’s radiance,
The wind carries orchid and iris’s fragrance.
The prefect is both wise and capable—
No need for frantic haste.

**Tianchun Pavilion in Neixiang**

Neixiang has a garden called Tianchun,
Where in spring, peach and plum bloom like clouds.
The townsfolk bring wine beneath the canopies,
Singing and dancing in joy.
The magistrate remains at ease,
Claiming no ancient surpassed him.
A hundred kinds of red and purple peonies—
Glorious but fruitless, merely flamboyant.

**Jianyin Pavilion in Neixiang**

Your letter of *Jianyin* surprised me,
And at that moment, it stirred me to rise.

The people are pure, lawsuits rare as in mountain dwellings.
Beyond ledgers and cups, in leisure between official bonds,
Amidst talk and laughter, I know not—
Which neighboring county could compare?

**Li Shaoqing’s Invitation to Visit and Improvise**

The Luoyang spring departs, meeting immortals’ talent,
Spring gone, startled by summer’s sudden arrival.
A light rain passes, peonies just withered,
A gentle breeze stirs, herbaceous peonies newly bloomed.
Within the shade of green willows, cups and jars abound—
In health and prosperous times, how fine to let the heart roam free.

**Chant on Wine-Induced Illness**

Year after year at this time,
Wine-sickness seals the brushwood gate.
Early on, people feel much—
How bear spring’s return?
Flowers fade, butterflies flutter in disarray,
Daylengthens, cuckoos cry.
How might one regain those former days,
When the warm breeze first fanned so gently?

**Chant on Contention and Yielding**

Where yielding exists, can contention be absent?
Without continuity, how could renewal arise?
Contention and yielding arise from the heart,
Continuity and renewal emerge from traces.
Fuxi and Shennong yielded through the Dao,
Yao and Shun yielded through virtue.
Tang and Wu contended through merit,
Huan and Wen contended through force.

**Xie Wang’s Remembrance in Verse**

Before and behind the western study, half pines and bamboos,
Myriad cares settle, only then is truth seen.
Never did I expect that in this bright and limpid sky,
You could still recall old friends and kin.

**Matching Rhymes in Response to Ren Sifeng’s Gift of Verse**

The nobility of kings and marquises defies description,
Yet in paintings of Zhongshan, one glimpse suffices.
Not like my lord, who in sitting and walking,
Greedily admires Songshan, then loves Mount Tiantan more.
A hundred-foot tower pierces the perilous void,
Sky pale, clouds idle—beholding the emperor’s work.
Ascending one more level, the heart still unsatisfied,
Longing for you unseen, I see only towering Song.

Declining the banner, I came here to dwell in cloudy skies,
Hearing you’re well and climbing, how could I dread the toil?
On purple paths, affairs abound yet remain unseen—
My homeland encircled is none other than lofty Song.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Fifteen Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Sixteen*

**Yichuan Shao Yong Yaofu’s Poems in Response**

**In Reply to a Friend’s Poem**
Are my sinews and bones still as they were in years past?
Do my qi and blood remain as they were in days of old?
Yet I rejoice in one thing that has grown with time—
The skill in talk and laughter before the winecup.

**Chant on the Withering Cold**
Pines and cypresses, seen in winter’s grasp,
Alone reveal the year’s withering cold.
Their sound must be heard amid the wind’s embrace,
Their hue best viewed beneath the snow’s mantle.

**Matching Rhymes in Thanks for Ren Sifeng’s Gift of the "Carefree Pillow" Poem**
My lord bestows upon me a carefree pillow,
Fearing my carefree steps have not yet transcended.
Though body may wander carefree, it has not yet arrived—
I must join spirit and dream in carefree flight.

**Chant on Qi and Zheng**
Zichan never shrank from Zheng’s smallness,
Yan Ying never wished Qi’s decline.
Had these two worthies been born in their rightful place,
Their talents would have served as kings’ tutors.

**Poem Sent in Place of a Letter to Lü the Keeper of the Treasury**
The Zhou king’s eight steeds raced to the ends of the earth,
Yet how could they compare to the marvel of your four treasures?
Though Zheng and Luo’s winds and mists lie but a hand’s span away,
I regret I have no way to journey and behold them.

**Matching Rhymes with Vice Director Wang Anzhi After Rain**
The scorching toil of summer’s nine ten-day spans now past,
A single rain revives all things.
The frogs’ drums are not worth hearing,
The mosquitoes’ thunder not easily driven off.
Not only does it ease the year’s provisions,
But also settles the officials’ dues.
For a wanderer at leisure beneath the trees,
Why not delight in this moment?

**Matching Rhymes in Response to Minister He’s Gift**
I deemed myself unfit for the world’s affairs,
Passing winter and summer behind barred gates.
The path to blue clouds is smooth, yet I rise without merit,
Amid sparse green bamboo, I find my share of leisure.
Still, bright flowers reward my simple aspirations,
And fine wine brings a flush to my cheeks.
Lately, the signs of age weigh heavily upon me—
I fear such gifted talents are not easily matched.

**Chant on Purity and Harmony**
Pure but not harmonious—narrow and full of coarseness.
Harmonious but not pure—indolent and lacking in propriety.
To be both harmonious and pure,
Righteousness has no fixed form.
Move when the time is right to move,
Halt when the time is right to halt.

**Chant on Similarity and Difference**
Brilliance and swiftness risk fragmentation,
Weightiness and gravity risk stagnation.
Pursuits follow what one esteems,
Yet cannot escape similarity or difference.
What differs from oneself must be wrong,
What aligns with oneself must be right.
Right and wrong wage war over similarity and difference,
And one may pass a lifetime without knowing righteousness.

**Chant on the Moment**
In life, one seeks to please others;
In death, one seeks to please the spirits.
Pleasing others may bring wealth and honor,
Pleasing spirits may avert blame and calamity.
Though life and death follow different paths,
Are the principles of men and spirits truly distinct?
Alas, such overuse of the heart—
When will these vain intentions cease?

**Chant on Observing Things**
Ears sharp, eyes bright—a man’s body,
Endowed by the great potter’s hand, not born poor.
Only by delving into the moon’s cavern can one know things,
Without treading the heaven’s root, how can one recognize men?
When Qian meets Xun, observe the moon’s cavern;
Where earth meets thunder, behold the heaven’s root.
Roaming between heaven’s root and the moon’s cavern,
All thirty-six palaces bloom with spring.

The simple and honest lack keen insight,
The keenly insightful lack careful discernment.
How might one find both simplicity and keen insight,
To speak with them of the world’s affairs?

**Chant Over Wine**
With wine, I often raise a cup,
Now nearing seventy, what more do I seek?
Teeth failing, marriages and betrothals not yet settled,
Years of drought, fields yielding but a meager harvest.
Guests depart, and at times I fold my hands in leisure,
The sun high, with nothing to do, I quietly comb my hair.
Frost-touched locks do more than adorn poetic scenes—
They may yet serve as subjects for a painter’s brush.

**Chant on Autumn Thoughts**
A season of spring has barely passed,
When geese from beyond the clouds announce their return.
The turning of seasons comes before my eyes like this,
While the years slip from my head thus.
Though the ambitions of youth remain,
How can these sinews and bones not weaken?
Yet I rely on this inch of heart, ever glad—
Even the sages’ hardest truths I can comprehend.

**Matching Rhymes with Vice Director Wang Anzhi on an Autumn Outing**
Spring and summer have come and gone, fit for poetry,
Yet though fit for poetry, what use is it?
Often empty like Yanzi,
Yet far from matching Huizi in traversing things.
The mood of wind and moon is beyond bearing,
The sentiment of clouds and mountains cannot be contained.
A song, a chant—let us exchange verses,
Daring to match Anzhi and Jingzhi.

*Zhang Shaoqing Tuan, styled Jingzhi.*

**Matching Rhymes with Wang Anzhi on Attending the Autumn Gathering of Prefect Wang Xuanhui at the Luo Society**
From the inner chambers, guests and kin emerge,
Music played to new melodies, divinely inspired.
When red candles burn bright, sleeves of green flutter,
Where painted oars pause, green duckweed is claimed.
Once a visitor to the golden halls of early years,
Now one soon to depart the Phoenix Pool.
Your estate crowns the capital, called "Snail’s Retreat"—
How dare Shao Yaofu presume to be your western neighbor?

**Poem Expressing Shame for Failing the Three Appointments at Heyang, Heqing, and Jiyuan**
Autumn rains linger, the mud slick and deep,
Day after day, when will the skies clear?
Dear friends long for my arrival,
Yet my frail frame, averse to cold, cannot journey far.
Though the distance is not great,
Twice I set out, twice failed to fulfill the promise.
Two matters war within my breast,
Leaning on my desk, I sit in sorrow, finding no peace.

**Matching Rhymes with Vice Director Wang Anzhi’s Poem on an Autumn Engagement**
Rise and fall—why debate such things?
In the end, all matters even out.
A patch of fleabane and mugwort,
A body of clouds and waters for a thousand years.
The harvest comes at just the right time,
The cold and warmth of qi begin to balance.
From now on, let our meetings bring joy—
Gentlemen, do not weary of their frequency.

**Poem to My Eldest Son, Bowen, on His Failure in the Examinations**
What the Confucians esteem
Are conduct and righteousness, and literary composition.
Success or failure is never certain,
Flourishing or withering not easily measured.
Striving must be diligent,
Gain and loss are the common lot.
External things cannot be relied upon—
His words carry a profound flavor.

**Year’s End Poem to Myself**
The Way of Heaven has no eternal spring,
The Way of Earth no constant treasure.
Only those who receive the harmonious qi of the mean
Can become men of pure excellence.
Good fields yield abundant millet,
Poor soil suffices for brambles and thorns.
The ailanthus and mugwort,
Are fit only to serve as kindling.
Though born as the spirit of the myriad things,
One must possess their essence.
Without the spirit of the myriad things,
One is but a mere part of their kind.
To utter the rarest words,
One must harbor the rarest intent.
To manifest the fairest name,
One must perform the fairest deeds.
Aiding the times is a fair deed,
Enlightening the lord is a rare intent.
Alas, of these two,
I alone possess neither.

**Chant on a Gentleman’s Wine-Drinking**
Father kind, son filial,
Elder brother friendly, younger brother respectful.
Household well-provided, people content,
Times harmonious, years abundant.

**On Physical Well-Being**
Sinews and bones hale and hearty, neighbors and kin in frequent visit.
A gentleman drinks his wine—
His joy knows no bounds.

**Chant on Reading the Biography of Zhang Zifang**
First merit in founding the Han house,
How fine to begin well and end well!
One might suspect the later Red Pine Master
Was none other than the Yellow Stone Elder of old.
Use or retirement follows the times without fixed bounds,
Concealment or action rests with me—now blocked, now free.
The ancients are dead and gone, never to be seen again,
How grievous that few today keep such a spirit!

**Two Chants on Observing Things (Xining Ninth Year)**
The willow’s nature is most supple,
In one year it grows over a yard tall.
Though easily flourishing,
Alas, how easily withered!
All grains look up to the life-giving rain,
The driest stalks turn most lush.
How might this sweet moisture be had,
To briefly revive all living things?

**Five Chants on Order and Chaos**
Disorder outnumbers order,
Harm outnumbers benefit,
Sorrow outnumbers joy,
Ugliness outnumbers beauty.
One yin, one yang—
Why must it be thus?
The Central Plains, a stretch of idle fields,
Once bore the Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors.
The Three Sovereigns and Five Emperors had many descendants,
Some base, some poor, some rich, some noble.
Refining principle to the divine for practical use—
"Practical use" means entering and exiting the numinous.
The numinous has no fixed form, the *Changes* no fixed body,
Hidden in function, manifest in benevolence.

**Fire and Water**
Fire can overcome water,
Fire cannot overcome water—then the fire is extinguished.
Water can follow fire,
Water cannot follow fire—then the water stays cold.

**Husband and Wife**
A husband can restrain his wife,
A husband cannot restrain his wife—then the wife turns away.
A wife can follow her husband,
A wife cannot follow her husband—then the wife becomes perverse.

**Heaven and Earth**
Heaven can generate but cannot nurture,
Earth can nurture but cannot generate.
Fire can cook but cannot moisten,
Water can moisten but cannot cook.
Even heaven and earth lack perfect efficacy,
How could water and fire possess perfect power?
When the two attain their use and nourish each other,
They turn instead to oppress each other.

**Thirty-Year Chant**
Compared to thirty years ago,
Today is hardship.
Compared to thirty years hence,
Today is ease.
When order lasts long, people long for chaos,
When chaos lasts long, people long for order.
How might one obtain a thousand-year crane,
To ride away and wander the immortal mountains?

**Chant on Illness**
When the body is ill, one seeks medicine and physician;
If the medicine is not good, the body must suffer.
When the state is ill, one seeks men to heal it;
If the men are not good, the state must be imperiled.
When matters are not yet urgent, swiftly change course;
When matters have grown urgent, though one repent, it is hard to recover.

**Chant Before the Flowers**
This year’s flowers bloom as they did last year,
Today’s man opens them with yesterday’s heart.
Troubles are wholly absent—half a pinch,
Joys are ever present—a hundred cartloads.
Time has passed, but the mind has not,
Teeth and hair may wither, but the will has not.
When asked if Master Yao Fu has gone out,
I reply: "Just now from the grotto-heaven."

**Self-Description**
Warm in spring, cool in autumn, half-drunk,
Leisurely riding a slow carriage, a dust whisk in hand.
Though I lack great virtue to aid the living,
I at least adorn the scene of this era of peace.

**Chant on Departing Office**
When a gentleman departs his office,
The people are left with lingering blessings.
When a petty man departs his office,
The people are left with lingering misfortune.

**Chant with Staff in Hand**
Staff in hand, now in the southern garden, now in the northern,
Spring’s arrival brings especial comfort to declining years.
After the first clear weather following the Lantern Festival,
Just warming winds before the Cold Food Day.
The pond’s banks lightly adorned with tender grass,
The forest’s treetops faintly veiled in thin mist.
At this moment, how generous is the Lord of Spring—
In the midst of the formless, he fashions beauty.

**Chant of No Wishes**
I wish not for the court to appoint me an office,
Nor for the court to bestow upon me grain and silk.
I only wish the court would reduce corvée labor,
That the world might enjoy a little rest.

**Chant on Measuring One’s Strength**
Act according to your strength, and you will have no regrets;
Find joy where you may, and curb your striving.
You may seek a fine steed to ride,
But fear that all your life you will have no horse at all.

**Playful Reply to a Friend’s Chant**
Who is this Shao Yaofu?
Year after year, spring and autumn, he comes to call on you.
His carriage, in half a year, makes but one turn—
Not like a swift horse kicking up fragrant dust.

**Occasional Chant**
Gaoyao met Shun,
Yi Yin encountered Tang,
Wuding found Fu Yue,
King Wen gained Jiang Yuan,
Qi knew Guan Zhong,
Han recognized Zhang Liang,
Zhuge Liang opened Shu,
Xuandi inaugurated Tang.

**Chant on Observing Affairs**
A year’s affairs are diligent in spring,
A day’s affairs are diligent at dawn.
A life’s affairs are diligent in youth,
A single task’s affairs are diligent when new.

**Chant on Observing Things**
When profit is light, principle is weighty;
When profit is weighty, principle is light.
If profit cannot overcome principle,
Sincerity naturally abounds.
If principle cannot overcome profit,
Strife and anger naturally abound.

**Chant on Gold and Jade**
The sage emerges from among men,
The heart is cultivated through conduct.
Gold is found within the sand,
Jade is sought within the stone.

**Chant on Wind and Frost**
Grass bends before the wind,
Grass withers before the frost.
The pine sings before the wind,
The pine stands firm before the frost.
To bend before the wind, to wither before the frost—
How can one be said to exist or perish?

**Chant on High and Low**
From below, looking up—
Boundless wealth and honor.
From above, looking down—
Boundless baseness and poverty.
From the mind, observing things—
What thing can be one?
From things, observing the mind—
What mind is not equal?

**Chant on My Five-Roomed Hut**
Though my hut is small, it roughly shelters me,
At least sparing me the shame of renting another’s lodging.
Many in this world have no house to live in,
Begging warmth beneath another’s eaves.

**Returning from Viewing Apricot Blossoms by the Chan River**
East of the Chan River, apricot blossoms open,
Beyond the flowers, by Tianjin Bridge, I turn back at dusk.
I pluck an apricot blossom to wear in my hair,
To let others know I’ve come to see the flowers.

**Chant on Taking a Wife**
When a man takes a wife,
Let her have beauty, virtue, dignity, and bearing.
Should she bear a son,
Let him be a Gao Yao or a Kui.

**Chant on Good Deeds**
Good deeds are surely hard to force,
Worthy men must be sought with heart.
The floating life of sun and moon is not long—
Once time passes, a thousand regrets, ten thousand regrets.

**Chant of No Return**
Spring does not come again,
Flowers do not bloom again.
Man does not grow young again,
Time does not return again.

**Chant on the Hair-Cutting**
Who shears the hair to thank Lu Chen’s sinking?
The flesh and bones of the living cannot endure such encroachment.

**Chant of Turning the Sky**
In the human realm lies the power to turn the heavens,
Yet in the groves, many harbor hearts that grieve for the nation.
The sun passes its zenith, yet sorrow knows no end;
The moon nears its fullness, yet peril runs deep.
In the army, the scholar’s robes are my family’s way—
Where now to seek the Lord of Martial Might, Zhuge?
A heart that grieves for the nation does so out of love for the lord;
To love the lord, one must hold him dearer than oneself.
What the mouth preaches may not be true—
Only what the hands accomplish is real.
When vain thoughts stir, the mirror fails to reflect;
In the depths of worldly bias, no man is known.
To slander the world as utterly wicked is the gravest calumny—
How many heroes lie buried in the dust below?

**Six Attainments Chant**
The eye can discern,
The ear can hear,
The mouth can speak,
The hand can act,
The body can move,
The heart can let go.
When all six are mastered,
One’s virtue aligns with Heaven and Earth.
In eating and rising, in going and staying,
In speech and silence—
Not only is the mind at ease,
But effort is spared as well.

**Chant of Rise and Fall**
When power is at its height, one lifts their head in arrogance;
When qi wanes, they turn on their heel to sigh in despair.
To slander the world as full of worthies—
How could the world ever be so falsely maligned?

**Chant of Wealth and Nobility**
Great Shun shared the likes and dislikes of the people,
Thus following their desires to bring them peace.
To know that wealth and nobility are but ordinary things—
He who is made proud by wealth and nobility is no true man.

**Chant of No Delusion**
Let the ear hear no falsehood,
The eye gaze upon no vanity,
The mouth speak no idle words,
The heart harbor no reckless thoughts.
When these four are free of delusion,
They become the tools of sages and worthies.
Who am I, that I dare not aspire to emulate them?

**Chant of Good and Evil**
If one does not pursue goodness in others nor rid oneself of evil,
To claim knowledge of the Dao—is this not difficult indeed?

**Chant of a Spring Day in the Garden**
Spring’s warmth invites a stroll through the garden—nothing unusual,
Yet within this realm, it differs little from the land of immortals.
Daily among the bamboo, I keep company with true friends;
By the waterside, we often raise cups of wine.
After rain, birdsong flits through the trees;
Before the wind, the fragrance of flowers assails the senses.
Amid the groves, wealth and nobility bring the same joy—
Let them come freely, for there is no harm in it.

**Chant of Interpreting Words**
Man’s words are trust,
The sun and moon are brightness.

**On the Character "武"**
"武" is made by halting the spear—peace through strength;
"羹" is made with lamb’s flesh—harmony in the pot.

**Chant of Reflection on Affairs**
Orchids planted yet fail to flourish,
Thorns carved yet refuse to vanish.
Helpless before these two,
I linger as the year nears its close.

**Chant of Adversity and Success**
Adversity cannot bend me,
Success cannot make me lax.
To claim knowledge of the Dao—is this not difficult indeed?

**Chant of the Cosmos**
The cosmos rests within my hand,
All things dwell within my frame.
Vast and unceasing as if present,
How could their use ever weary?

**Chant of Prolonged Drought**
After long drought, we yearn for rain;
After long rain, we long for sun.
Such is the constant Way of Heaven,
Such is the nature of man.

**Chant of Cultivating Nature**
Cultivate nature, let it endure;
Focus the will, let it not divide.
What fear then remains
Of falling short of the ancients?

**Chant of Paths**
The path before me need not be narrow—
When paths grow narrow, no travelers pass.
When travelers cease, paths turn to waste,
And the world is mostly brambles and thorns.

**Chant of the Great Man**
The Way of Heaven is distant,
The way of man is near.
Fulfill human sentiment,
Align with Heaven’s principle.

**Chant of the Primordial (For Xing He-Shu)**
A single expanse of the primordial, called the Great Void,
In its quiescence, reveals true richness.
Who would flaunt the treasures within their breast?
The world’s finest talents suffer grave slander.
When principle flows smoothly, all words may be released;
Where righteousness finds rest, what place cannot be home?
Only after the cosmos settles in tranquility
Does one truly believe that heroes walk among men.

**Chant of Reflection on Affairs**
In doing good, measure your strength and portion;
Discerning opportunity lies in understanding human nature.
When human nature is exhausted, doubts become insurmountable;
When strength is measured, affairs naturally find balance.
Where principle flows, all before you is a path;
Where righteousness falters, thorns block the gate.
Who would heed these words?
Yet these words are clear—why do men not listen?

**Chant of the Great Song**
What is called discerning opportunity?
Only the divine can fathom the subtle.
Action and stillness rest entirely with me;
Use and rejection depend on the times.
Ever do I regret recognizing men too late,
Ever do I worry perceiving affairs too slowly.
Only when one becomes one with Heaven
Does one truly know the wisdom of Confucius.

**Chant of Fame and Gain**
Fame and gain never enter my breast,
Thus my breast’s qi surges freely.
Both love and hate are maladies—
When shall my spirit terrace find repose?

**Chant from On High**
Who transforms cruel poisons of a thousand kinds
Into a single stream of gracious light?
Mourning that the ancients are no more,
I lean on high and sigh deeply.

**Chant of Intent Fulfilled**
Intent is fulfilled in things,
Words are fulfilled in sincerity.
To feign emotion and dominate things—
This is not within my power.

**Two More Chants of the Great Song**
Sorrow and joy,
A hair’s breadth apart.
Order and chaos differ in form,
Yet mountains and rivers remain unchanged.
The sound of a flute drifts from afar;
Grasses and trees appear in the distance.
From some lofty tower,
A man leans on the railing as the sun sets.

Fine virtue is hard to find—
Wise one, it is best to desist.
Treading frost may yet be remedied;
Felling trees—what more is there to seek?
Beasts, when trapped, await another day;
Geese, when they fly, depart with autumn’s end.
When the people hunger, they must be fed—
Beyond feeding, all is but idle thought.

**Chant of Gentleness and Kindness**
The gentleman’s gentleness and kindness demand scrutiny;
The petty man’s deceit and falsity must also be known.
Startled by the world’s treacherous schemes,
I find the days and months within my gourd pass slowly.

**Chant of the Gentleman**
The gentleman aligns with righteousness,
The petty man with profit.
Aligned with righteousness, he daily rises;
Aligned with profit, he daily declines.
The gentleman values virtue,
The petty man values force.
Valuing virtue plants kindness;
Valuing force plants enmity.
The gentleman creates blessings,
The petty man wields power.
Creating blessings brings blessings;
Wielding power brings calamity.
The gentleman delights in goodness,
The petty man delights in evil.
Delighting in evil invites evil;
Delighting in goodness invites goodness.
The gentleman seeks praise,
The petty man seeks slander.
Seeking slander provokes anger;
Seeking praise brings joy.
The gentleman thinks of prosperity,
The petty man thinks of ruin.
Thinking of prosperity summons fortune;
Thinking of ruin summons misfortune.
The gentleman delights in giving,
The petty man delights in demanding.
Delighting in giving brings much joy;
Delighting in demanding brings much sorrow.
The gentleman delights in life,
The petty man delights in killing.
Delighting in life, the Way flourishes;
Delighting in killing, the Way perishes.

**Chant of the Primordial**
Before Heaven, do not oppose it;
After Heaven, follow its seasons.
Not opposing never falters;
Following seasons sometimes wearies.

**Chant of Refreshment**
Few are the flavors that refresh the palate;
Few are the deeds that refresh the heart;
Few are the words that refresh the mind;
Few are the acts that refresh the body.

**Chant of Utmost Sincerity**
Seeking little, thus one attains;
Departing not from learning, thus one is enlightened.
To keep the heart ever bright,
Nothing surpasses utmost sincerity.

**Chant of Recording Events**
Other mountains’ stones can polish jade—
Before the jade is fully wrought, age hastens me.
Bounded time slips away with each affair;
Unbounded decay follows man’s steps.
A thousand poems refine temperament and nature;
A cup of wine harmonizes sinew and bone.
Sixty-six years without a day of leisure—
Only now does my heart’s source begin to shine.

**In Reply to Scholar Ning’s Request for Poems**
Idle words beneath the pines—
Why need there be so many?
Three hundred poems or so
Are enough for chanting and recitation.

**Chant of Poetry and Wine**
The Sage’s words are hard to convey—
How could they last but a thousand or ten thousand years?
Only with a tranquil heart can one know the bright sun;
Only with clear eyes can one see the azure sky.
Ghosts and spirits’ forms are written in poetry;
Creation’s work is passed down through wine.
Without poetry and wine, these matters would not be conveyed—
And without poetry and wine, how could they be spoken?

**Chant of the White-Haired**
Who does not grow white-haired?
My whiteness comes not from sorrow.
Only because men are full of desires—
How can I be like them, untroubled?
Untroubled because I do not stir;
Full of desires because I seek much.
Aging is the common lot of men—
How could one not grow white-haired?

**Chant of the True Listener**
Only Confucius could speak of having no intent;
Only Mencius could speak of an unshaken heart.
Seek not white jade in the mountains—
Find yellow gold within yourself.
White jade in the mountains may sometimes be found;
Yellow gold within oneself is nowhere to be sought.
Who are we to dare claim understanding?
How can we know there are no true listeners in the world?

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Volume Sixteen Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Volume Seventeen*

**Chant of Humanity and Things**
Man broken must be mended,
Things broken must be repaired.
Yet mending and repairing, though many,
End only in exposure and ruin.
Man has man’s sentiments,
Things have things’ principles.
Though man and things differ in kind,
How can sentiments and principles be distinct?

**Chance Encounter Chant**
In the woods, with nothing to occupy the heart,
I nap through the day, rewarded with a cup of wine.
The lingering dream cannot be fully recalled—
Half drifts with wind and rain down the eastern street.

**Contemplating Things Chant**
With the first division of qi,
The Two Modes are already complete.
The round is Heaven,
The square is Earth.
Transformation gives rise to life,
Movement and stillness bring forth kinds.
Man stands between them,
Most numinous, most noble.

**Warring States Chant**
In the time of the Seven States, war was prized;
Might and cunning, deceit and force, all flourished together.
Lian Po and Bai Qi excelled in command,
Su Qin and Zhang Yi in stratagem.
At dawn a commoner, by dusk a lord—
Yesterday feasting from the tripod, today cooked within it.
Fan Sui, dismissed from office—what was his heart?
Cai Ze, entering Qin—on what did he rely?
The First Emperor roused his sleeves, and the world was pacified;
The Second begged to be a mere clod of earth, yet could not.
Three thousand retainers’ resentment was not yet spent,
When the hundred and two rivers of Qin gave way to Han.
Of all that remained, only empty names—
The fading sun, withered grass, the shapes of mountains and streams.
It all passed like a spring dream;
What more need be said of might and ferocity?

**Stirred by Events Chant**
A sword that cuts jade like mud is no idle boast—
Who knows that in this world there is Kunwu steel?
To speak well is not yet to be a true man;
Only he who acts rightly deserves the name of great man.
To grow old among woods and springs is my true wish,
Yet the people fill the ditches—what guilt is theirs?
Though it is not my affair, my heart aches;
I cherish this scroll of Fuxi’s ancient script.

**Five Further Chants**
All things possess their essence,
Man is the spirit of all things.
First examine the nature of affairs,
Then discuss the sentiments of men.
When qi is tranquil, the form is at ease and joyful;
When the heart is idle, the body is at great peace.
When the Lord of Yi and Qi governed the world,
They did not go beyond this principle of life.

Using medicine is like waging war—
When war is waged, can there be peace?
Seeking peace, peace is not attained;
Banishing illness, illness returns.
Decoctions do not fully restore,
While rich foods only provoke strife.
How then may one attain long life?
The auspicious sign of the Old Man Star.

All things turn on the Dao as their pivot,
Yet their origins differ in kind.
Though their natures are neither thick nor thin,
Their principles vary in refinement and coarseness.
Nothing compares to man in splendor—
Yet know that things, too, have their abundance.
Born in this age,
I am fortunate not to be a common man.

I have heard, but hearing is not like seeing;
I have seen, but seeing is not like experiencing.
Having applied the heart to experience,
Why trouble the mouth with words and deeds?
Revising poetry, one knows the transforming power of the brush;
Sobering from wine, one recognizes the harmony of the broth.
I reckon the affairs of men—
None escape this sentiment.

Before us lie a hundred million years,
After us, a hundred million ages.
Between them, the long-lived man
Scarcely reaches a hundred.
From his mouth come no good words,
In his conduct, no good deeds.
He merely possesses the form of man,
Yet lacks man’s true nobility.

**Treading the Dao Chant**
What age has lacked men to raise the radiance of virtue?
Today, the many worthies gather in the western capital.
The flourishing of culture and refinement in this time of peace
Surpasses even the full splendor of the Yuanhe era.

**Seeing Right Action Chant**
When one sees what is right, one must act;
When one does not see, then let it be.

**Measuring Strength Chant**
Act according to one’s strength; cease when strength is spent.

**Observing Things Chant**
Like the luan or phoenix, their bearing serene—
Those born thus are loyal or good.
Like the rat or sparrow, their bearing skittish—
Those born thus are wicked or cruel.

**Nobles Chant**
Nobles and great men, the world looks to them;
Careless habits spread, grave harm to their dignity.
This alone unfinished, how can they attend to that?
Not for lack of the Dao, but for the rise of greed again.
Topped with a scholar’s cap, their hearts hold Buddhist signs.
They sought peace of mind, yet stir their minds to noise.
Like raising a child—one should seek resemblance,
Yet teach not virtue, but theft instead.

**Self-Reflection Chant**
Old age comes with no achievement, teeth and hair decline,
