Near seventy years—what is there left to do?
At home, no illness, no need for medicine;
Now and then, moved by feeling, I still write poems.
Drawing water, I delight as fish gather near;
Plucking flowers, I rejoice as butterflies follow.
A lifetime of learning yields no other fruit—
Only this: my heart is free of turmoil.

**Observing Things Chant**
The painter depicts things over months and years;
The mirror reflects them instantly before us.
The mirror’s clarity may yet lack precision;
The craftsman’s hand may render unevenness.
Of all things level in the world, none compares to water.
It reflects the surface but not what lies within.
To see both surface and depth—only the sage.
Scrutinizing words and deeds, nothing escapes truth.
Fulfilling the nature of things, shedding one’s own feelings—
The virtuous must speak;
Those who speak may not act.

**Sleeplessness Chant**
Great shock brings no sleep, great sorrow no sleep,
Great grief no sleep, great illness no sleep,
Great joy no sleep—only great peace brings sleep.
Why no sleep? Entangled in cares.
Why sleep? Free of affairs.

**Two Zhegu Chants**
In the world, gold is prized above all—
Who says gold cannot be found?
Without idle words or refined intent,
What else can gladden a good heart?
Distant mountains on all sides offer pure freshness,
Hidden birds in a thousand songs send sweet sounds.
Though boundless spring has faded away,
Pray, listen to the zhegu’s cry.

Deep in emerald bamboo groves, the zhegu calls,
Its voice more stirring than the wine-pourer’s song.
North and south of the river, they ever follow,
After spring, before spring, they call of their own accord.
Exiled officials, hearts broken, startle from dreams;
Traveling soldiers, tears soak their collars.
Hearing it in sorrow, the bowels first sever—
Can such grief be endured?

**Primordial Chant**
If asked of the Primordial—there is not a single word;
Only in the Later Heaven does effort begin.
Boasting strength to move mountains and overshadow the age—
At this, can one force even a hair’s worth?

**Self-Joy Chant**
The qilin and phoenix have never left the wilds;
In times of peace, why fret and clamor?
Hearing of good deeds, my heart rejoices first;
Seeing rare books, my hands copy them myself.
Beneath bamboo, a tile’s worth of clear spring flows;
Atop pines, two rods of red sun rise.
Awake in my nest, I sit before it—
How could idle words dispel guests’ mockery?

**People’s Sentiments Chant**
When the people’s hearts are joyful, harmonious qi brings blessings.
When the people’s hearts are troubled, harsh qi brings calamity.
Blessings are like rain and dew—all under heaven prospers.
Calamity is like flood and drought—all under heaven suffers famine.

**Peony Chant**
The peony’s grace outshines all flowers,
And among them, some are kings.
Four hues transform into a hundred,
A hundred hues, a hundred fragrances.

**Letter in Verse Chant**
Gold must be smelted a hundred times to know its purity;
How can a water-mirror compare to a man’s discernment?
Not discarding old friends, one preserves them;
How dare one forget lifelong bonds?
Affairs of state demand practical words;
The Dao and virtue rest on sincerity alone.
Before the myriad grasses and trees,
Only the pine and cassia stay green through the cold.

**Minor Illness Chant**
When illness is slight, none suspect it;
When it deepens, medicine cannot cure.
Through diligence and humility, one ends well;
Through confusion and relapse, great loss follows.
Between self and things, no room for a hair’s breadth;
Between heaven and man, how can a thread intrude?
Knowing ancient joy is like present joy—
How many needless words are spared?

**Borrowed Poems**
Poems wild, books unconstrained—
In recent years, they’ve multiplied beyond count.
Most have scattered across the world,
Unreturned to the Nest of Peace.

**Effortless Chant**
In life, no forced verses,
No striving for depth in brush or ink.
The brush moves as the nature flows;
The poem forms to express the heart.
Poetry soars with the heart’s creativity,
The brush blooms like a garden of the mind.
What I delight in is my own delight—
Delight without excess.

**Myriad Things Chant**
All things are complete within oneself;
Heaven and earth do not fail man.
Time slips away like grass in the wind;
Affairs fall into routine.
Grieving that youth fades,
Startled by new white hairs.
If the heart holds nothing,
One becomes but dust—or flees like dust.

**Moon’s Hollow Chant**
The Moon’s Hollow and Heaven’s Root,
Between them, frequent comings and goings.
Wherever one dwells, all is ample;
Wherever one goes, all is at ease.
Each step finds its place;
The heart is full of spring.
Without poetry and wine,
How greatly one would be diminished!

**Great Image Chant**
The Great Image is empty at its core,
Its emptiness truly unchanging.
In action, it is the heart’s work;
In response, the mouth’s skill.
Relying on capacity, a thousand things exist;
With a single care, none remain.
Who knows this principle
Outweighs reading five cartloads of books.

**Hundred Ills Chant**
A hundred ills arise from feeling;
Light feeling, light illness.
Can one be without attachments?
Yet there is always something to rely on.
Autumn moon, a thousand mountains still;
Spring blossoms, ten thousand trees flourish.
If speaking of true work,
Human effort cannot contrive it.

**Small Cart Chant**
Spring’s warmth not yet oppressive,
Autumn’s chill not yet severe.
The small cart follows whim;
Wherever it goes, joy arises.

**Striking the Earth Chant**
Three thousand poems of *Striking the Earth*,
Twelve households of *Moving Nests*.
Taking joy as one’s work,
Cultivating the will as one’s livelihood.
Coming and going as one pleases,
Visiting in a small cart.
Who knows this joy
Needs not wait for splendor?

**Two Poems Left at the Northern Waters of Yang, Gentleman of the Bureau’s Garden and Pavilion**
Since ancient times, buying a dwelling has prized the sight of mountains—
How much does it cost to see mountains today?

Peaks of wonder encircle afar, parted by waters,
Towering trees loom low, lightly veiled in mist.
Travelers on the road suspect they pass through immortal grottoes,
Those leaning on railings fear they’ve become transcendent beings.

Ever dreading unseen entry into a painter’s hand,
To be rendered on silk and spread through the world.
In Luoyang, who does not buy a dwelling?
Yet who else buys one like yours?

A scene of radiance, no dust of this world,
Landscape through four seasons, a true painted scroll.
The rear garden’s blossoms, rare as those of Penglai,
The front pavilion’s peaks, fine as those of Penglai’s isles.

To grow old in such a place—
This too is the mark of a hero in the world.

**Chant at Autumn’s End**
Within days, autumn is spent,
A hundred thoughts cannot console its passing.
The garden’s beauty, though loved without end,
Yet grasses and trees yellow—what can feeling do?

Though aged, my limbs still strong,
From every high terrace, my gaze still far.
All day I raise my cup, yet not drunk,
Again I long to face the wind and sing boldly.

**Chant of the Unworthy**
The unworthy man,
His will set on wandering,
Body beneath the roof,
Heart above it.

The unworthy son,
His will set on vanity,
His own self not secure,
How can he secure his home?

**Chant of the Noble One**
The noble one’s departure,
Is as his arrival.
The petty man’s arrival,
Is as his departure.

Where there has been kindness,
Let there be no resentment.
Where there has been aversion,
Let there be no longing.

**Chant of the Petty Man**
The petty man has no constancy,
Abandoning root for branch,
Delighted when he gains,
Enraged when he loses.

**Chant of Grasping Hands**
Wealth and honor grasp hands,
Poverty and lowliness pull back.
Poverty and lowliness grasp hands,
Wealth and honor pull back.

Friendship of metal and stone,
Endures even unto death.
Friendship of the marketplace,
Hardly lasts long.

**Chant of the Great Changes**
Heaven and Earth take their places,
Adversity and prosperity reverse their forms.
Mountains and marshes share qi,
Increase and decrease reveal rightness.

Thunder and wind press close,
Constancy and benefit arise in intent.
Water and fire clash,
Completion and incompletion alternate.

The four images intersect,
Forming sixteen matters.
The eight trigrams stir one another,
Becoming sixty-four.

**Chant of Ceasing Chants**
Long have I wished to cease my chants,
Yet startled—my intent suddenly strange.
Seated, I know the nature of things,
Beyond words, I reach the celestial mechanism.

To gain a line is not so easy,
To complete a verse—how could I bear to discard it?
Who knows if in a thousand myriad ages,
A later age will lack its Confucius?

**Chant of Gold**
On the body, gold is carried,
No man sinks in his journey.
When sought, it may not appear,
When found, not through seeking.

Eloquence is not understanding,
Containment is the completion of the heart.
Only when one can play the stringless zither,
Can one then know the true friend.

**Chant of the Partridge**
Affairs take on a new form,
Yet newness soon turns old.
New and old are not beneficial things,
Principle does not follow man.

Do not fear the lateness of years,
Orioles and blossoms will herald spring again.
My remaining cup, fortunately unharmed,
Fitting to sing the partridge’s song often.

**Chant of Leisure**
In leisure, the flavor lingers long,
The long place is the land of immortals.
Abundant is the joy of forests and springs,
Pure, without the clamor of markets.

Freely roaming the mysteries of a thousand sages,
Drunk, embracing the fragrance of ten thousand flowers.
Do not make matters of heartbreak,
Heartbreak easily severs the bowels.

In leisure, the flavor is true,
The true place is the people of heaven.
Abundant is the joy of forests and springs,
Pure, without the dust of markets.

Freely roaming the mysteries of a thousand sages,
Drunk, embracing the spring of ten thousand flowers.
Do not make matters of heartbreak,
Heartbreak saddens and kills.

In leisure, the flavor is complete,
The complete place is the celestial immortal.
Abundant is the joy of forests and springs,
Pure, without the noise of markets.

Freely observing the mysteries of a thousand sages,
Drunk, embracing the beauty of ten thousand flowers.
Do not make matters of heartbreak,
Heartbreak is best turned away.

**Chant of the Vast Blue**
Every person bears the sky,
Do I bear it in vain?
One must know the principle of heaven and man,
Only then to know the power of creation.

Merit and fame return to the wine cup,
Vessels and deeds enter the poem.
I reckon the joy of leisure,
None compares to my wholeness.

**Chant of the Round**
Like a mirror, like a hook,
Revolving, autumn unremembered.
Hard to fathom the ways of heaven,
Easy to whiten the heads of the world.

In fullness, all rejoice,
In waning, all things grieve.
There is no life without breath,
Why wait for the ox of Wu to pant?

**Chant in Place of a Letter**
A year and more since we parted,
At year’s end, our thoughts turn to the beginning.
Again I trouble you with warmth,
To send afar my emptiness.

A hundred forms of decay and ruin,
Yet not a single care.
Idle chanting of forty words,
To briefly answer your letter.

**Chant of the Lost Poem**
Within my breast, wind and rain roar,
Beneath my brush, dragons and serpents move.
Before and after, they fall among men,
Over three thousand in number.

**Chant of Not Ceasing**
At sixty-six years,
Two things I do not cease:
With poetry, I offer to the true lord,
With wine, I toast the image-emperor.

My face has not yet flushed,
My heart first stirs harmonious qi.
Looking up and down between heaven and earth,
I know myself without shame.

**Chant of Ordering the World**
Fuxi, Shennong, Yao, Shun,
Tang, Wu, Huan, Wen;
August kings, emperors, lords, and chiefs,
Fathers and sons, rulers and ministers.

The Way of these four,
Its principle ended with Qin,
Descending to the two Han,
Then passing through the Three Kingdoms.

East and west in turmoil,
North and south in chaos.
Five Hu, ten clans,
The celestial order nearly burned.

Without Tang, there would be no salvation,
Without Song, no preservation.
For a thousand ages, ten thousand ages,
The Central Plains have had men.

**Chant of Knowing Men**
The noble one knows men through understanding,
The petty man knows men through self-interest.
Through understanding,
Those in accord with principle are deemed right, those at odds are deemed wrong.
Through self-interest,
Those in accord with oneself are deemed right, those at odds are deemed wrong.

**Chant of Words and Deeds**
To speak well is not difficult,
To act is the true challenge.
One must be a true man,
Only then can one be without shamelessness.

**Chant of Time**
Three hundred and sixty-six days,
Time passes before the eye like a rushing wheel.
Revolving without cease,
How can the four seasons long resemble spring?

**Chant of Raising the Cup**
Leisurely with guests and friends, raising the wine cup,
In the cup, flowers ever seem to bloom.
Pure discourse just leaves the mouth,
Harmonious qi already arises from the heart.

Intent beyond things is not gained from images,
Amidst sitting, spring does not return from heaven.
If this could be applied to the world,
What worry would the world not cast aside?

**Chant of Too Little Wine**

**Chant of This Thing (Wine)**
Lately scarce for the poor,
Yet now and then, a few pounds gained.
Like tea, it betrays the aged,
Like medicine, it fails old friends.
Before drinking, fear it will run dry,
Though poured, dare not drink too deep.
How to share it on Double Ninth,
With white-robed guests ever near?

**Quatrain on Watching Chess**
Unshaken by the urge to vie,
Yet black and white still grip the mind.
One path stands peerless,
Fully seen, yet none pursue.

Unshaken by the urge to vie,
Yet black and white still stir the heart.
One path lies smooth and sure,
Yet none tread—what a waste!

**Chant of Growing Old**
Grown old, no achievements, temples streaked with gray,
How many years remain to follow the heart’s ease?
How to find content beyond clouds and peaks,
And wander still in poetry and wine?
Large characters write poems to fulfill plain aims,
Small cups pour wine to flush the cheeks with red.
Spring thunder rouses creatures buried for a thousand years,
Beneath the brush, the azure dragon moves to and fro.

**Chant of Scattered Stones**
Tianjin’s shores are strewn with stones,
Amidst which I idly seek.
Pure jade is hard to find,
Jade-like stones are rare as well.
Some are dull and green,
Others gleam with phosphorescent white.
Yet none ring with a clear, ringing tone,
Nor bear the warmth and luster of true jade.

**Chant of Having Nothing**
No merit to my name,
Yet enemies I’ve made tenfold.
Nothing gained,
Yet losses know no bound.

No yielding in the least,
Yet strife I’ve stirred tenfold.
What’s lost is substance,
What’s gained is but an empty name.

**Chant of Admonishing a Son**
The rarest pearl is flawless,
The finest jade is without blemish.
If pearls and jade must be so,
How much more should a man strive to be without fault?
Failing to correct one’s errors,
Refusing to draw near the wise—
Though born into this world,
One cannot be called truly human.
Zhou and Kong are not enough to emulate,
Meng and Xiong not enough to follow.
One might as well abandon common fare,
Or flee to the wilds of the barbarians.
The Dao is not far from man,
For heaven and earth reside within the self.
Who seeks them beyond this,
Will find them nowhere else.

**Chant of Hu and Yue**
On the day Hu and Yue are of one heart,
Husband and wife turn against each other.
In this world, no matter great or small,
Gain and loss hang by a thread.

**Chant of Good Conduct**
To act well in times of trouble is hard,
Yet holding fast refines the self.
With nothing foreign in heart or mind,
Even barbarians grow gentle.

**Chant of a Hundred Years**
A hundred years slip by in haste,
A thousand miles stretch desolate and bleak.
How can one bear to follow the eastward-flowing waters,
Drifting without end, at the mercy of the tide?

**Chant of Year’s End**
Day by day, the days depart,
Year by year, the years accrue.
Though one may yet become a vessel great,
Alas, the temples have already turned to frost.
The will remains unchanged,
Yet wit and brightness fade.
Were it not for the heart’s attainment,
One might well aspire to be an immortal.

**Short Chant on Watching Chess**
Who says board games are mere leisure,
When gain and loss grind on without cease?
At the first advantage, look ahead,
Where strategy is needed, tarry not.
Two years to right the Three Supervisors’ crimes,
Seven days to occupy the Two Observatories.
When the world is at peace, with nothing amiss,
What more could one seek than reclining in Nanyang?

**Another Poem on Lending Out**
In the nest of peace and joy,
A myriad pipes of Nüwa’s panpipes resound.
Since lending it out in idleness,
Guests arrive, yet no delight remains.

**In Response to Wang Guifu’s Gift of "On Movement"**
Not just a thousand or ten thousand years,
The frost-defying pine and cassia stand alone.
Were it not for Yangzi’s learning of heaven and man,
How could Zhuangzi’s inner and outer chapters exist?
Already we’ve agreed to seek white stones by the moonlit pool,
And further to play with clear springs in Golden Valley.
Who says the clamor of shadows is too great?
Let the peaks rise and rise again.

**Chant in Reply to a Friend Urging Wine**
Who does not wish to be enfeoffed as a marquis?
Yet once enfeoffed, none are content to stop.
Great gains must guard against great losses,
Many worries stem from many desires.
How dare I compare myself to men of talent?
I know my measure is not that of a drinking sage.
How could I have forced myself in younger days?
Now, with age and snow upon my head, even less so.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Volume Seventeen Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Volume Eighteen*

**Yichuan Shao Yong Yaofu’s “Winter Solstice Chant”**
What is called the subtle moment?
The heavenly root, the principle most fine.
Here at the year’s final close,
There at the morrow yet to arrive.
This juncture is easily grasped,
Yet none refuse its fleeting sign.
Who knows this truth may guard their affairs,
But few can fathom its design.

**“Cup and Plate Chant”**
Beneath the trees, cups and plates lie still in quietude,
And long I wish this quietude would last as now it does.
Behold those who beat drums and strike bells—
Once their power wanes, friends are not easily called.

**“Joyful Chant”**
Praise the good, not the ill,
Remember kindness, not grudges.
Let each rejoice in themselves,
What need to lament few companions?

**“Virtuous Chant”**
Good as gold and jade,
Weighty as hills and mountains,
Dignified as phoenix and simurgh,
Fragrant as orchid and angelica.

**“Discourse Chant”**
If a matter is wrong, it stands apart.
If a matter is right, how could there be two ways?

**“Sincerity Chant”**
Though heaven does not speak, man may speak,
Yet when the heart may be deceived, heaven may be deceived.
Heaven and man are not far apart,
But only in the human heart do men not see.
The human heart precedes heaven, and heaven does not oppose it;
The human body follows heaven, serving its time.
Body and heart are not far apart,
But only in human sincerity do men not reflect.

**“Yaofu’s Chant”**
Yaofu chants: the world calls me dull,
Coming without season, going without measure.
Like mountains and rivers, my path knows no end;
Like streams and seas, my flow knows no cease.
Like orchid and angelica, my fragrance never fades;
Like the *Xiaoshao* music, my sound never breaks.
There are flowers, there is snow,
There is wind, there is moon.
Now gentle, now stern;
Now refined, now fervent.

**“Unexpected Chant”**
When matters arise beyond expectation,
Foresight cannot grasp them.
If not moved by recklessness,
Why indulge in needless sorrow?
Even in error there may be fortune,
And in darkness, unseen guidance.
Who understands this truth,
What worry remains under heaven?

**“Decisive Chant”**
Decide to resolve doubt—doubt must not linger.
To hesitate when one should decide
Is to invite chaos in return.

**“Dream of Worry Chant”**
The perfected have no dreams,
The sages have no worries.
Dreams come from overthinking,
Worries from overreaching.
If worries do not arise,
From where do dreams come?
Who understands this truth,
What more need they cultivate?

**“Human Sentiment Chant”**
To understand human sentiment,
Neither narrow nor broad.
All under heaven’s affairs
Are as clear as one’s palm.

**“Human Affairs Chant”**
Men must not act carelessly,
Matters must not be left to drift.
Drifting loses the moment,
Carelessness corrupts the man.
Men must not act rashly,
Matters prize attentiveness.
Rashness leans toward shallowness,
Attentiveness leans toward sincerity.

**“Teacher and Resource Chant”**
Not yet knowing the Dao’s meaning,
One seeks others as teachers.
Once knowing the Dao’s meaning,
Others come as resources.
Seeking teachers is not easy,
Being a resource is truly hard.
Pointing south to guide the Way,
Neither departing nor returning.
To feel shame at teaching others,
To feel joy at being taught—
Both are misplaced.
I alone am free of this.
To delight in teaching others,
How is this different from obstinacy?

**“Heaven and Man Chant”**
Heaven’s study is to cultivate the heart,
Man’s study is to cultivate the body.
When body is at peace and heart is joyful,
Then heaven and man are seen.
Heaven and man are not far apart,
Yet few know this—
Those who do are rare.
The body is governed by man,
The heart is governed by heaven.
If the heart is not joyful,
How can the body be at peace?

**“Yue Yi Chant”**
When Yue Yi served Yan,
His heart held a profound intent.
Breaking Qi’s seventy cities,
His blade met no resistance.
Why spare Jimo and Ju?
Yet his loyalty to Yan remained unbroken.
He wished for Yan to become king,
And for all under heaven to begin with Qi.
Was his will not yet fulfilled
When King Zhao suddenly died?
King Hui, ignorant of his worth,
Sent another to replace him.
Mighty Yan declined from then,
Never again to rise.
Since ancient times, lord and minister—
Their meeting is not easily found.
How precious are these thousand myriad years,
That move even heroes to tears.

**“The Perfect Ten Chant”**
What is called a perfect man?
One must possess perfect truth.
Not merely one who writes well,
Nor one who composes fine prose.
Not for fine brows and bright eyes,
Nor for new robes and fresh clothes.
To walk in the world of men,
One must first perfect the self.

What is called a perfect man?
One must accomplish perfect deeds.
If deeds are not perfect,
They remain incomplete.
Serving one’s father with whole heart,
Serving one’s elder brother with whole intent,
Serving one’s lord with whole loyalty,
Serving one’s teacher with whole duty.
A man’s life spans a hundred years,
How easily it passes!
Though but a fleeting moment,
How much transpires within!
To be called one who completes affairs,
One must first complete the self.
If the self is not yet complete,
How can one complete others?

**“Birthday Chant”**
*Cyclic signs: Xin-Hai, twelfth month, twenty-fifth day*
Year: Xin-Hai, month: Xin-Chou,
Day: Jia-Zi, hour: Jia-Xu.
Day and hour share Jia,
Year and month share Xin.
At this moment,
I was born into this world as man.

**“Admonition to My Sons Chant”**
The rooster can herald dawn,
The horse can take one’s place in travel,
The dog can guard and defend,
The ox can plow the fields.
Man, born between heaven and earth,
Spirit of all things—
Yet envying the worthy and resenting the able,
Better not to have been born at all.

**“The Constant Chant”**
Heaven and earth have constant principles,
Sun and moon have constant brightness.
The four seasons have constant order,
Ghosts and spirits have constant numinosity.
The sage has constant virtue,
The petty man has no constant disposition.

**“Yearning Chant”**
The world’s splendor fades from sight,
Before my eyes, only books command reverence.
Though a hundred thousand hardships still startle,
The years before thirty were the most bitter.
In youth, I knew only perilous paths,
In old age, I recognize the peace of ease.
Fortunate that my family’s legacy has heirs,
Enough to wander carefree with the white clouds.

**“Spring Chant”**
A piece of spring lies before my eyes,
Before my eyes, know this good spring.
Spring, autumn, winter, summer—free of burdens,
Snow, moon, wind, flowers—all flow as one.
To use true richness for one’s work,
Why fear other things harming its warmth and beauty?
How fortunate is my life—what abundance!
How could idle sorrows reach my ears?

**“Nearly There Chant”**
To demand saintliness of others
Is surely not yet complete.
To expect humanity from others
Leaves room for improvement.
Holding others to account is not hard,
But receiving reproach is not easy.
To be nearly there—
One still looks to Yanzi.

**“People and Things Chant”**
People flourish and must decline,
Things arise and must perish.

**“On Seeing Those Before Me”**
Having seen those who came before,
I now know what comes after.
If those before could rise,
How could what follows be abandoned?
Rise and fall begin with people,
Only then do we speak of heaven and earth.

**“Sighs of Amazement Chant”**
Yesterday, burning with power,
Today, caught in the net.
Such are the common affairs of men—
Why gasp in amazement, why sigh?

**“Left-Lapelled Robes Chant”**
Since ancient times, no supreme strategy has governed the frontier,
Only by virtue and righteousness does the Central Plain stand.
The royal army’s punitive campaign may be justified,
But how can one bear to speak of the Son of Heaven’s exile?
The two Jin dynasties fell into chaos and became overgrown grass,
The three lords endured humiliation, their names stricken from the records.
Who were Gong, Lü, Yan, and Guang?
Only now do I believe that reviving a nation begins with a single word.

**“Teaching and Exhortation Chant”**
To claim sageliness or benevolence—I dare not,
Yet empty words still suffice to console this fleeting life.
To clearly expound teaching and exhortation is the constant Way,
That descendants may forever uphold a good name.
Though today’s legacy remains obscure,
The blessings received in time to come will not be light.
If this intent flows throughout the world,
How could the world fail to know great peace?

**“The Unvirtuous Chant”**
Alas for the unvirtuous,
Endowed with cruel and violent power.
Not only do they ruin families,
But they can also ruin nations.

**“Many Affairs Chant”**
Many affairs invite worry,
Many doubts invite vexation,
Many gifts invite stinginess,
Many takings invite loss.

**“Conducting Oneself Chant”**
The noble person in conduct
Prefers others to wrong them rather than wrong others.
The petty person in affairs
Prefers to wrong others rather than be wronged by them.

**“Observing Nature Chant”**
People of a thousand myriad years,
Affairs of a thousand myriad years,
Passions of a thousand myriad years,
Principles of a thousand myriad years.
Only through learning can one
Sit and contemplate them all in clarity.

**“Observing Things Chant”**
Dwelling in darkness, observe the bright;
Dwelling in stillness, observe the moving;
Dwelling in simplicity, observe the complex;
Dwelling in lightness, observe the weighty.
What one dwells in is few,
What one observes is many.
Without dwelling, without observing,
What use are the many or the few?

**“In Response to Wu Chuanzheng, Vice Censor-in-Chief, Two Poems”**
*Also sent to Wang Shisan of Gaoyang*

Within Luoyang’s walls, a foolish old man,
For over a decade, I have not read books.
In old age, my feelings are hard to describe—
Pale mist and cold moon illuminate sparse pines.

Delighting in stillness, am I free of illness?
Loving leisure, my heart remains.
How could I compare to those self-content,
Who drift with the world’s ebb and flow?

**“Right and Wrong Chant”**
To call short right and long wrong,
To favor red and despise white.
A lifetime of trivial pursuits,
Never free from love and hate.
If love and hate do not depart,
How can right and wrong be settled?
Once love and hate are gone,
What use are right and wrong?

**“Purifying the Heart Chant”**
Many seek to purify the body,
Few seek to purify the heart.
Purifying the body washes away dust and grime,
Purifying the heart washes away depravity and excess.
Dust and grime are cleansed with water,
But depravity cannot be rinsed away.
To truly cleanse the heart’s filth,
One must pluck the stringless zither.

**“On Seeing Things Chant”**
Seeing things, I sing and chant,
Never needing deliberate intent.
Leisurely composing poems of bamboo baskets,
Quietly observing the world’s affairs.

**“Toiling the Fields Chant”**
In spring, plow and sow,
In summer, weed and hoe,
In autumn, harvest and prepare,
In winter, use and store.
When rain and dew are timely,
Shoots sprout and ears form.
But if flood or drought strike,
One still bears the blame.

**“Sixty-Six Years Chant”**
At sixty-six years,
Joyfully holding my wine cup.
In youth, no grand ambitions,
In old age, this open heart.
Often my heroic spirit stirs,
From time to time, fine verses come.
I have gathered three hundred poems,
And deem them equal to jade treasures.

**“Lenience and Severity Chant”**
Lenience makes the people lax,
Severity makes the people suffer.
Lenience and severity in balance,
The people find their own peace.

**“Minor Arts Chant”**
Though arts may be minor,
Their effects can strike deep.
If one does not delve into subtleties,
How can one attain the divine?

**“Gain and Loss Chant”**
People differ in worth,
Affairs vary in scale.
Gains are not easily won,
Losses come all too readily.

**“Fumigation and Study Chant”**
Between good and evil,
Fumigation still reveals traces.
Draw near to fragrance, and you will be fragrant;
Draw near to stench, and you will stink.

**“Love and Hate Chant”**
To hate death and love life,
To shun harm and seek benefit—
Such are the feelings of all under heaven,
No different in any.

**“Year’s End Chant”**
This feeling others do not know,
Yet I often sigh at the fading year.
Though I sigh at the fading year,
What can I do? It is hard to entrust.
This feeling others do not know,
Yet I often sigh at the long passage of time.
Though I sigh at the long passage,
What can I do? Others do not receive it.

**“Contentment Chant”**
Lightly gained, easily lost,
Much scheming, little achieved.
Virtue has no end to its benefits,
Goodness seeks no near fame.

**“Following Others Chant”**
To follow others and lose,
Is to treat their words as guests.
From now on,
How can I trust others again?

**“Poetry and Painting Chant”**
The painter’s brush excels at depicting things,
Skilled in wielding red and green.
Red and green enter subtle thought,
And ten thousand things reveal no hidden form.
Poetry and prose excel at depicting things,
Skilled in wielding sincere intent.
Sincere intent enters fine verses,
And ten thousand things reveal no hidden feelings.

Poetry is the will of man,
Speech is the voice of the heart.
Will is expressed through words,
Voice is shaped by rhythm.
To know many birds and beasts,
Is it only their feathers and quills?
To know many grasses and trees,
Is it only their branches and stems?
Without the *Feng*, *Ya*, and *Song*,
How could one know merit and fame?
Without the *Ze*, *Bi*, and *Xing*,
How could one know rise and fall?
Beholding the court’s grand affairs,
The majesty of the altars of soil and grain.
There were Tang and Wu’s foundations,
No You or Li’s songs of decline.
Knowing the difficulty of achievement,
How could one lose it through arrogance?
Banish great vermin and treachery,
Advance rare worthies and talents.
Gather the essence of yin and yang,
Seek the spirit of heaven and earth.
Draw from rivers and mountains’ purity,
Raise the glory of sun and moon.
Gather these as the people’s ultimate,
Inscribe them as the nation’s canon.
Broadcast them on metal and stone,
Perform them in the grand court.
Move the hearts of the people,
Inform the spirits and gods.
When people and spirits are both pleased,
This is what is called harmonious broth.
Since there were Yu Shun’s songs,
How could there be no Gao Yao’s responses?
Since there was Confucius’ editing,
How could there be no Ji Zha’s listening?
If one wishes to bring joy to all under heaven,
Without poetry, what else could suffice?
With the remnants of my learning,
Great peace may yet be achieved.

**“Poetry as History Chant”**
The historian’s brush excels at recording events,
Skilled in displaying its prose.
When prose prevails, truth is lost,
And all that remains are empty words.
Poetry as history excels at recording events,
Skilled in crafting their truth.
When truth prevails, ornament fades,
Not like the chaos of mere appearances.

The world is not one affair,
The world is not one man.
The world is not one thing,
The world is not one body.
In the eras of emperors and kings,
Their people endure as if ever-present.
For a hundred thousand myriad years,
Their deeds remain ever-new.
They can discern the myriad policies,
They can order the common people.
They can recount the ancestors,
They can instruct the descendants.
They can honor the Son of Heaven,
They can discipline the three armies.
They can advance remonstrance,
They can extol meritorious deeds.
They can transform customs,
They can deepen human relations.
They can beautify education,
They can harmonize the distant and the near.
They can rectify husband and wife,
They can clarify ruler and minister.
They can praise Heaven and Earth,
They can move ghosts and spirits.
How earnestly they admonish others,
How earnestly they instruct others.
How reluctantly they part from others,
How sincerely they bestow upon others.
No year lacks its fine festivals,
No month lacks its fine days.
No time lacks its fine scenes,
No day lacks its fine guests.
In the goblet there is fine wine,
At the seat there is no ominous vapor.
In the breast there are fine things,
In the heart there is no dust.
How can one bear not to wield the great brush,
Writing characters as large as cartwheels?
Over three thousand poems,
Spread as spring throughout the world.

**“Elaboration Chant”**
All affairs enter deep contemplation,
Their flavor grows ever deeper.
Though they once passed before the eyes,
They must be pondered in the heart.
Passing by, one has not fully seen;
Pondering, one is willing to seek thoroughly.
Only by thorough seeking can one see,
And only then is it true gold.
What is true gold?
True gold sinks into the bones.
Having endured forging and tempering,
It is worthy and needs no contemplation.
Once in hand, what need is there for eyes?
In conduct, how dare one be careless?
Carefree only after being at ease,
In the world there are those who understand.
What are those who understand?
Those who understand reside only in the heart.
The liver and spleen show no effect,
In bells and drums, one searches in vain.
If one can already disclose things,
What need is there to further play the zither?
The phoenix’s arrival is not for itself,
It is only to deeply move others.
What is the phoenix’s arrival?
The phoenix’s arrival bears no low intent.
With bodies all joyful,
Without things not harmonious.
If a state were like one thing,
The four directions would be like limbs.
How majestic were Yao and Shun,
How could they be named?

**“History and Painting Chant”**
The historian’s brush excels at recording events,
The painter’s brush excels at depicting things.
Depicting things and recording events,
Each excels in one.
Poetry as history excels at recording intent,
Poetry and painting excel at depicting emotion.
Depicting emotion and recording intent,
Both can be precise.
Depicting emotion does not depict things,
Recording intent does not record events.
Form and appearance emerge from creation,
Imagination completes Heaven and Earth.
Substance and function thus divide,
Even ghosts and spirits dare not differ.
What is poetry if not this?
It is history and painting combined.

**“Competition Chant”**
People have no love of competition,
Affairs have no excess of seeking.
Competition brings much humiliation,
Excessive seeking brings much sorrow.
Sorrow and humiliation arrive together,
And virtue does not wander.
Not only does it harm others,
It also becomes the body’s enemy.

**“Governing the Heart Chant”**
The heart is closer than the body,
The body is closer than others.
If one cannot govern the heart,
How can one govern the body?
If one cannot govern the body,
How can one govern others?

**“My Hut Chant”**
I too love my hut,
My hut resembles a rustic dwelling.
My nature follows the Dao, shared with the heavens,
My body is distant from the world.
Everywhere, fragrant grasses grow,
On my bed, disordered books pile.
Since there have been no affairs,
I no longer apply effort.

**“Human Spirit Chant”**
Heaven and Earth give birth to the myriad things,
Among them, humans are the most spiritually endowed.
Having the spirit of humanity,
One must also have human emotions.
Without human emotions,
One merely has the form of a human.

**“Passing By Chant”**
Do not be startled by things passing by in profusion,
As gains and losses grind against each other, never settling.
Though skills may be many, they have no real effect,
And when cleverness reaches its peak, it is but an empty name.
The warmth, coolness, cold, and heat of the four seasons,
The sweet, bitter, pungent, and sour of the myriad things.
Apart from this heart, all are external things,
Yet even this heart may not be fully awakened.

**“Disaster Chant”**
Disaster from without
Can still be dispelled.
Disaster from within,
How can it be withstood?
Between Heaven and humanity,
Examine both within and without.

**“Within and Without Chant”**
Eyes, ears, nose, and mouth,
Are humanity’s doors and windows.
Heart, gall, spleen, and kidneys,
Are humanity’s inner currents.
If one can guard within,
Without will not be received.
If within there is no guard,
How can without endure?

**“Fame and Profit Chant”**
Weighing heavily with fame
Reveals others’ emotions.
Enriching with profit
Reveals others’ intentions.
Emotions and intentions are within,
If within is weighty, without is light.
Fame and profit are without,
If within is base, without is noble.

**“Name and Reality Chant”**
Within, there is no reality,
Without, there is a name—
The petty person thus boasts.
Without, there is no name,
Within, there is reality—
What does the noble person lose?

**“Nature and Emotion Chant”**
The noble person follows nature,
The petty person follows emotion.
Following nature, one draws near,
Following emotion, one drifts far.

**“Exhortation Chant”**
People should not be negligent,
Affairs value earnestness.
Negligence approaches shallowness,
Earnestness approaches sincerity.

**“Doubt and Trust Chant”**
People should not be credulous,
Affairs should not be doubted excessively.
Credulity invites provocation,
Excessive doubt invites separation.

**“Order and Chaos Chant”**
Noble persons and petty persons
Often stand in equal measure.
Sometimes they halt, sometimes they act,
Now in order, now in chaos.

**“Timeliness Chant”**
The dragon does not leap in winter,
The firefly can fly at night.
Petty persons and noble persons,
All have their time.

**“Loyalty and Thickness Chant”**
The petty person is momentary,
The noble person endures.
Momentary, they incline toward perversity,
Enduring, they remain loyal and sincere.

**“Deep Winter Chant”**
The twelfth month draws to a close,
And one is startled that the year’s rhythm is exhausted.
Ice is stored in the northern lands,
The thaw has not yet come with the east wind.
In the dimness, one vainly seeks green,
On the flower branches, one forces a search for red.
Seeking spring, spring is not seen,
It is only within the breast.

**“Knowing Wrong Chant”**
All that came before today,
Knowing wrong, the heart can rely on.
Empty words are not worth speaking,
True conduct—what need for pride?
There is no medicine for aging,
But there is poetry to praise sagely brightness.
Though one may drink at times,
One need not imitate Liu Ling.

**“Winter Solstice Chant”**
At the winter solstice, midnight’s midpoint,
The heart of Heaven does not change or shift.
Where the first yang arises,
Before the myriad things are born.
The flavor of dark wine is just mild,
The sound of the great tone is truly rare.

If you doubt these words, ask Fuxi again.

**"White-Haired Chant"**
White-haired for many years now,
How much longer can these whiskers hang?
Yet I count myself fortunate still—
This decline is not yet complete.
Though my house lacks silver,
I have sons to carry on the line.
Dare I call poverty pure?
Few like me can claim the same.

**"On Poetry Chant"**
Poetry is the heart of man,
Without verse, the heart cannot be known.
When people harmonize, their minds are fully seen,
When Heaven grants, intent is linked.
Discussing things gives rise to fresh lines,
Judging texts refines elegant speech.
When inspiration comes, it is as if preordained,
No need for carving or chiseling.

**"Water and Cord Chant"**
Water can be level but not straight,
A cord can be straight but not level.
If only human nature were like water and cord,
The world would know no strife.

**"Punishment and Name Chant"**
The noble man seeks renown,
The petty man courts punishment.
Good and evil differ in kind,
Yet both arise from following desire.

**"Yin and Yang Chant"**
Yang moves as one, yin moves as two,
One governs Heaven, two govern Earth.
Heaven moves in sixes, Earth in fours;
Four governs form, six governs qi.

**"Human Affairs Chant"**
People come and go,
Affairs rise and fall;
Traces may be sparse or dense,
But people make no comparisons.
Master these four truths,
And you shall endure.

**"Inner and Outer Chant"**
Neat attire and dignified bearing—
This is outer cultivation.
Pure conduct and righteousness—
This is inner cultivation.
Cultivate both inner and outer,
And who would not seek you?
Disheveled dress and slovenly manner—
This is outer neglect.
Lacking righteousness in conduct—
This is inner neglect.
Neglect both inner and outer,
And who would not spurn you?

**"Rise and Fall Chant"**
Worthy descendants
Revive the family name.
Unworthy descendants
Ruin the family name.
Alas, descendants—
The root of rise and fall.

**"Life and Death Chant"**
To study immortality is to seek not to die,
To study Buddhism is to seek rebirth.
Rebirth or not dying—
Can man truly achieve either?
If man could truly achieve them,
Only then would it enter the realm of feeling.
How fortunate the recluse,
Unpitied by the world.
How lamentable the lord and minister,
Deeply deluded by the world.

**"Birthday Chant"**
Thirty-five thousand days—
This body is my allotment.
Once but a thing of the moment,
Now at last a man.
Long indebted to the power of yin and yang,
Ever owing the kindness of father and mother.
Raise a cup of birthday wine,
With children and grandchildren gathered in the grove.

**"Timely Matters Chant"**
When the time comes,
Its momentum may be seized.
When the time departs,
Its affairs arise anew.
What was done yesterday
Cannot be done today.
What is done today
Will surely change later.
Time prolonged breeds trouble,
Affairs prolonged breed corruption.
Corruption and trouble follow one another—
How can man find peace?

**"Ignorance Chant"**
Ignorant of yin and yang,
Ignorant of Heaven and Earth,
Ignorant of human nature,
Ignorant of the principles of things.
Presuming to teach others—
Would one not feel shame?

**"Water and Fire Chant"**
When water and fire are mastered,
Their union brings completion.
When water and fire are uncontrolled,
Burning and drowning come swiftly.
Not only water and fire—
All things are thus.
Knowing only how to use water and fire,
Not knowing the meaning of water and fire.

**"Central Plains Chant"**
The armies of the Central Plains
Take benevolence and righteousness as their guide.
Without benevolence and righteousness,
The four barbarians come to humiliate.
**"Joyful Drinking Chant"**
Yaofu loved to drink,
Drinking to preserve his true nature.
He did not like to become drunk,
Only to reach a gentle tipsiness.
What is the scene of gentle tipsiness?
The heart’s landscape like early spring.
What is the scene of early spring?
Heaven and Earth just beginning to stir.
Unknowing that the self is man,
Unknowing that man is the self.
Only knowing self and man,
Undivided from Heaven.

**"Feelings Chant"**
Human life has no fixed course,
Affairs take many forms.
In perilous doubt as a traveling official,
Read your family letters carefully.
Only after enduring hardship and danger
Can one truly appreciate peace and safety.
A man’s life’s work
Must be viewed when the pain has settled.

**"Action and Stillness Chant"**
When the time is to stop, stop;
When the time is to act, act.
Whether to act or stop,
Do not force it with human effort.

**"Times of Peace Chant"**
In times of peace, within gardens and pavilions,
In years of plenty, among villages and hamlets.
The flavor is hard to describe,
As wind, mist, grass, and trees all lie at ease.

**"Seeking Spring Chant"**
Grass color faintly green,
Flower tips vaguely red.
Hard to put into words—
Like intoxication in the heart.

**"Not Venturing Forth Chant"**
Winter and summer, rarely venturing out,
Only moving between southern and northern gardens.
If one encounters fine scenery,
One may yet reach the Three Heavens.
Westward two hundred paces to Heaven’s Street,
Northward three hundred paces to Heaven’s Ford,
Eastward four hundred paces to Heaven’s Palace.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji*, Scroll Eighteen concludes.
*Yichuan Jirang Ji*, Scroll Nineteen.

**Yichuan Shao Yong Yaofu**

The unvirtuous,
Endowed with impure qi,
Serpentine in disposition,
Wolfish in nature.

The truly virtuous,
Endowed with clear and luminous qi,
Golden and jadelike in nature,
Orchid and iris in disposition.

**"Different Chants"**
The noble person,
Unlike oneself,
Rejoices at goodness,
Delights in worthiness.

The petty person,
Unlike oneself,
Resents goodness,
Rages at worthiness.

**"Gain and Loss Chant"**
Time is hard to gain yet easy to lose,
Though the heart may regret, what can be retrieved?
Unaware that old age has already arrived,
Unaware that aspirations and wishes have gone astray.

**"Lament Chant"**
Lament! Time is hard to gain,
Grieve! The Dao remains untransmitted.
This year now draws to a close,
Tomorrow begins the new year.

**"Year’s End"**
Half a hundred, hair already gray,
Now even more silvery white.
Knowing myself as a scholar,
Others marvel at a divine immortal.
Wind and moon hard to forget—wine,
Clouds and mountains need no coin.
Sixty-six years now passed,
Tomorrow adds another year.

**"Inspired Writing Chant"**
*Xining tenth year*
Window bright, air warm and mild,
Wine fine, hand supple and soft.
Inspiration soars, emotions stirred,
Brush falls—spring blossoms burst forth.

**"Reflection on Shadows Chant"**
Nature resides within the body,
Shadows lie beyond the form.
Nature moves, the body follows,
Form moves, shadows gather.
If body and nature cease,
Where then are form and shadow?
Words beyond shadows—
What is there to marvel?

**"Joy and Sorrow Chant"**
Great joy and great sorrow,
Neither allows sleep.
If either could bring sleep,
What concern would not be resolved?

**"Insight Dawns Chant"**
When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
The body is wholly mine,
Growing lean, then plump again.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
To seal the Pass of Han,
A single pellet of clay suffices.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
Like wielding a treasured sword,
Cutting jade as if through mud.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
Thirst quenched by fine wine,
Illness cured by a skilled physician.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
A single word can
Resolve a century’s confusion.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
Within sentiment, the essence of things is clear,
Beyond principle, the workings of heaven revealed.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
Pitiable and contemptible,
Deserving scorn and ridicule.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments first break—
Unfit for exhortation,
Only for sighs.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are yet unbroken—
Only fit to wield the brush,
Not to hold power.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are fully broken—
Only fit for a first glance,
Not for prolonged stay.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
To know flowers in full bloom,
Leaves must first scatter.

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
With power to act,
What need for further cunning?

When the principles of things dawn,
When human sentiments are laid bare—
Dare one speak of worldly affairs?
Once in hand, what could be difficult?

**"Joyful Drinking Chant"**
All my life, I’ve loved to drink,
Loving light and mellow wine.
I dislike deep intoxication,
Only a gentle warmth suffices.
Harmonious as a second youth,
Gentle as early spring.
Yet this is hard to describe—
The Two Modes remain undivided.

**"Nobility and Baseness Chant"**
What strikes me,
I can act upon with strength.
What binds me to others,
Is hard to contend with force.
Noble as the lord of ten thousand chariots,
Yet never proud.
Base as a commoner,
Yet never oppressed.

**"Placement Chant"**
In times of uncertainty,
In difficult circumstances,
Amid peril and doubt,
Observe how others act.

**"Exertion Chant"**
Matters great or small,
People gain or lose.
Gain comes from care,
Loss from exertion.

**"Not Aging Chant"**
No one escapes aging,
Yet the sun may reach its zenith twice.
To not age is impossible,
But a second zenith may yet come.

**"Letter in Place of Writing to Zhen Zhang, Garrison Field Officer"**
Twenty springs have passed since we parted,
Suddenly your letter arrives—how earnest!
Are there not worthy men in all directions?
Yet a thousand *li* away, I still think of old friends.
Having seen much of the world, I know what to accept or reject,
Long familiar with human nature, I discern closeness and distance.
Now I grow old, content in decline,
Offering no aid to this bright age, I recline by the Luo’s banks.

**"Long and Short Chant"**
The noble person delights in sincerity,
The petty person delights in deception.
Sincerity endures through the years,
Deception fades with the days and months.

**"Delusion and Awakening Chant"**
The noble person corrects faults,
The petty person conceals wrongs.
Correcting faults leads to awakening,
Concealing wrongs leads to delusion.
Awakening brings fortune,
Delusion invites disaster.

**"Rectifying Nature Chant"**
Before birth,
One knows not how it is.
After birth,
One knows there is heaven.
Having come from heaven,
Nature is rectified in all things.
The noble person fulfills their form,
The petty person trifles with life.

**"Light and Darkness Chant"**
Light has sun and moon,
Darkness has ghosts and spirits.
Sun and moon illuminate things,
Ghosts and spirits depend on people.
Light is revealed through things,
Darkness is declared by people.
If people and things do not act,
How can light and darkness be distinguished?

**"No Shame Chant"**
Having witnessed events,
Having tempered things—
Between heaven and earth,
I move without shame.

**"Affairs Chant"**
Speech must be measured,
Principle values what is fitting.
What can be changed should be reconsidered thrice,
What must be done should be pondered again.

**"Self and Others Chant"**
Born after heaven and earth,
The heart exists before them.
Heaven and earth emerge from me—
What more is there to say?

**"Four Permissible Things Chant"**
What can be striven for: conduct,
What can be trusted: words,
What can be entrusted: fate,
What can be relied upon: heaven.

**"Four Impermissible Things Chant"**
Words must not be reckless,
Conduct must not falter,
Fate must not be neglected,
Heaven must not be defied.

**"Renting a House Chant"**
A new house brings joy to its dwellers,
A dilapidated one makes them wish to leave.
If the master keeps it well-maintained,
What time is not fit to live?

**"The Petty Person"**
The petty person has no shame,
Values profit, scorns death,
Fears no human punishment,
Cares not for others’ judgment.

**"Reflection"**
Their bones are bright,
Their spirit clear,
Their blessings meager,
Their fortune light.

**"Illness"**
The body is like a kingdom,
When ill, one must seek a physician.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji*, Scroll Nineteen concludes.

**"Recovery"**
When illness fades, medicine finds its rightful place,
Moderation and release bring proper harmony.

**"Second Month Chant"**
Beneath the trees, no other knowledge remains,
Only the appointed time of the second month.
Wine is tasted when newly brewed,
Flowers are admired at half-bloom.
There is only the joy of intoxication,
No sorrow of reckless abandon.
Who can preserve this scene,
And store it forever in heart and spleen?

**"Third Month Chant"**
All day through the city, spring’s departure is pursued,
Yet those who chase it know its numbered days.
Does the true Master ever fail to bring forth life?
Yet wanderers have no certainty to rely upon.
Pear blossoms, drenched in rain, dissolve into weeping,
Willow fluff, stirred by wind, refuses to settle.
A moment of clarity holds boundless good intent—
Alas, such intent is hard to bestow.

**"First-Rank Chant"**
To utter words of the first rank,
One must possess a mind of the first rank.
To become a person of the first rank,
One must perform deeds of the first rank.

**"All Things Chant"**
Success and failure must be left to fate,
Rise and decline each have their season.
Though petty men indulge in endless desire,
How could the true Master permit partiality?
This fleeting joy,
Soon turns to hollow sorrow.
Behold: when spring departs,
For whom do the wanderers roam?

**"Luoyang Spring Chant"**
No place in the four directions rivals Luoyang’s beauty,
No season in the year surpasses spring’s blossoms.
In beauty and bloom lies exquisite subtlety—
Yet who but the idle can receive such grace?
Luoyang’s people are accustomed to rare blossoms,
Peach and plum flowers scarcely count as flowers.
Only when the peonies burst into full glory
Does the entire city rejoice without end.

Peach and plum flowers bloom, yet none pause to gaze—
Their season is but a prelude to the peony’s hour.
When peonies bloom, wine’s price soars,
And midnight finds revelers still abroad.
Time refuses even a moment’s respite:
Ninety days of spring vanish once more.
How many fallen petals drift without refuge,
Half carried by streams, half swept by wind?

Spring departs, flowers fade, the days grow long,
Swallows chatter, orioles sing, each absorbed in their own haste.
Why do wanderers cease their comings and goings?
The green shade is no less rich than the red bloom.
Ten days, and the finest flowers are gone—
Pity the Green Emperor’s laborious craft.
Wanderers, do not despair of certainty:
The red bloom may not surpass the green.

Where, after all, does spring retreat?
Countless spring grievances remain unvoiced.
I would entrust the warbler to ask the reason,
But the cuckoo only cries, *"Better to leave!"*

The four seasons complete a year’s cycle,
Yet only spring reveals such splendid blossoms.
Ten thousand coins for wine are no extravagance—
Once the red bloom fades, will it ever return?

**"Self-Admonition"**
At sixty-seven years,
I live as a man of this world.
From a land of central uprightness,
My body contains all things.
Beyond heaven, no greater joy exists,
Within my breast, another spring abides.

**"Falling Flowers Chant"**
Ten thousand purples, a thousand reds—everywhere they fly,
A riverbank of peach and plum blossoms forms a careless path.
As fierce wind and torrential rain approach dusk,
The dancing pavilions and singing terraces grow sparse.
Floating on water, how can they find stability?
Scattered along the wayside, they have no refuge.
The warbler need not speak many words—
With each passing moment, spring has already gone.

**"Late Spring Chant"**
Flowers bloom—spring is at its finest,
Flowers fade—spring draws to a close.
Who would have thought the cuckoo,
Still able to cry, *"Return!"*

Spring arrives, butterflies flutter in chaos,
Spring departs, the cuckoo weeps.
How can it be as before,
When the warm breeze first stirred?

Birds do not comprehend human feeling,
They only know spring is ending.
Sometimes they call *"Pour the wine!"*
Sometimes they cry *"Go home!"*

**"Coin Chant"**
Called "spring and cloth," yet footless, they traverse the world.
Difficult to speak of their virtuous issuance,
They can open faces that never smile.
They repay the small debt of a life extended,
They relieve urgent needs like the great elixir.
Only the old men of Shang Mountain,
Cannot be bought with them—leisure.

**"Peony Chant"**
One hue, one fragrance—yet the fragrance is celestial,
The color unlike all others.
The true Master’s craftsmanship is exquisite—
Not something human intent can fathom.

**"In Memory of Academician Zhang Zihou of Fengxiang"**
Half the realm of Qin’s mountains and rivers,
Nurturing genius through the ages.
How many worthies have graced the past?
What era has lacked men to revive its pure wind?

**"Self-Conduct Chant"**
How does Yaofu conduct himself?
The entire city of Luoyang feels like home.
Having no virtue to set myself apart,
I follow things with utmost sincerity—nothing more.
Before my eyes, I see only the web of heaven’s offices,
Who notices the changing years above my head?
Not only does spring come and go—
Within my breast, the four seasons’ flowers always bloom.

**"Being Human Chant"**
To be human, one must join the human throng;
Without joining, one cannot fulfill humanity.
Great Shun differed not from others,
Even Emperor Yao’s kin followed proper order.
A heart at odds brings illness to the body,
Harmony in affairs brings spring to the four seas.
The heart governs the four limbs—it is the master,
Yet the limbs are even farther from the self.

**"Primordial Chant"**
Who undertakes the work of the Primordial?
How does one speak of it, and to whom?
If the Primordial can be spoken of,
What lies beyond ruler and subject, father and son?
The tricks before our eyes are plain to all,
The work within the heart is unknown to the world.
Heaven, earth, and self are but shifts in position—
This body differs not from Fu Xi’s.

**"Central Harmony Chant"**
Nature has no other secret—
One must recognize central harmony.
Few words in the heart,
Many affairs in the world.
Like timely rain ending a long drought,
Like medicine reviving one from deep illness.
If one thing remains unresolved,
How can one manage the ten thousand things?

**"Four Worthies Chant"**
Yanguo’s words unfold in rich detail,
Huishu’s words are concise and apt,
Junshi’s words flow with ease,
Bochun’s words are both measured and stirring.
The four worthies of Luoyang’s gaze,
Stood above all others.
In the Xining era of the Great Song,
They were the splendor of their time.

**"Old Age Chant"**
The passing years no longer startle my head,
Only my friends’ eyes grow clearer.
Bright moon often viewed from my seat,
Deep wine not poured for sorrow.
If my heart lacks foresight,
I remain but a youth in the world.
Whom shall I invite as companion?
A light boat drifting on rivers and lakes.

**"Heaven and Earth Chant"**
Between heaven and humanity, how can a needle’s point intrude?
Why seek distant truths when the highest principle is near?
When baleful flames rage, they burn all the fiercer,
Where grace flows, it soaks all the deeper.

**"Long March Chant"**
The long-marching soldier yearns for home,
The parched people long for rain like thirsting souls.
Fortune and misfortune turn as swiftly as a palm’s flip—
Could one lie awake at midnight without a sigh?

**"On the Highest Principle"**
Among the myriad things, humans are called most precious,
Yet sages rise above the people like stars apart.
A steadfast stone needs no doubt to endure the day,
But to know the subtle signs before harm—how few are so divine!
Without resolve and courage, good cannot be achieved;
With keen discernment, purity is still more prized.
Fortune and misfortune show their signs by gradual steps—
Not by heaven or earth, but by human hands alone.

**"Ode to Human Jade"**
Jade does not value itself—people value it;
Yet why should human worth yield to jade’s purity?
So flawless and fair, ever constant and true,
Its inner light and outer gleam both reveal its essence.
Though fame may first lie hidden in the stone,
Only human hands can carve it into treasure.
The ancients are gone and cannot be seen again,
Yet the words of the humble-robed sage were not amiss.

**"On Deceit"**
The deceitful distrust all others,
For the world runs on deceit.
If one trusts no one in the world,
Then no truth remains in speech.

**"Drinking Wine"**
Now and then, a sip of fine wine,
Nurturing heaven’s harmony in place of tea.
Without rain, the year turns to dire famine,
And the city’s burden holds no prosperous life.
Dwelling in fields, one must still care for the realm;
Entrusting affairs to kin, one must still tend the home.
If asked what progress age has brought,
I see only silver added to my temples in the mirror.

**"On the Joy of Things"**
Things have sound, color, scent, and taste;
Humans have ears, eyes, mouth, and nose.
All the world’s things reside within one body—
Look inward, and nothing is lacking.

**"Five Poems on Wang Anzhi’s Small Garden"**

**The Small Garden**
The small garden, newly repaired, stays close to home,
Rising to the hill’s crest, descending to the moat.
Luoyang’s land seems favored beyond compare,
And Tianjin’s visitors arrive without delay.
It may rival Penglai’s immortal gardens,
But let it not vie with Peach Blossom Spring’s praise.
I hear a pavilion here grows many herbs—
Yet still lacks the famed *zǐhèchē*.

**The Wild Pavilion**
A pavilion named "Wild," far from the dusty world,
Where Song and Shaos’ misty peaks send gentle winds.
Days and months, years and seasons—all belong to me;
Where else could one seek the realm within the gourd?

**The Humble Pavilion**
Xu You’s plan was not so profound—
Washing his ears was not as good as stilling his heart.
Here, one feels as if beyond the world,
Where no affairs intrude.

**The Medicinal Pavilion**
Mountain herbs, though many, few can name;
The master transplants them and marks each one.
If they can truly cure the world’s ills,
Why shun the red and purple blooms near the eaves?

**The Evening Glow Pavilion**
The high pavilion, newly built, brushes the clouds and mist—
How fine that it can hold the fading light!
Near the path where spring admirers come and go,
Who now lingers here among the wanderers?

**"Observing Things"**
Water brings rain, fire brings dew,
Earth brings mist, stone brings hail.
Water’s wind is cool, fire’s wind is hot,
Earth’s wind is mild, stone’s wind is sharp.
Water’s clouds are black, fire’s clouds are red,
Earth’s clouds are yellow, stone’s clouds are white.
Water’s thunder rumbles, fire’s thunder crackles,
Earth’s thunder rolls, stone’s thunder crashes.

**"Painting Sleep"**
Daytime slumber is no easy art,
Fitting even for the age of Fuxi.
With a heart free of care after a full meal,
One may stroll at leisure, wrapped in quiet.
Before noon, orioles trill among the courtyard boughs;
By dusk, few visitors tread the flower-lined path.
Behold the world’s many troubled souls—
Though their pillows and mats may be cool, they lack this peace.

**"On Advancing and Retreating"**
Advance and retreat—both paths are called guests;
Why trouble the seat with endless debate?
Lower your brows when seated, attending to all;
Swing your arms when walking, heedless of others.
Teeth and hair now decline, no longer youth’s days,
Yet in forests and streams, age brings eternal spring.
Does anyone know the one who acts without care?
For how could favor or disgrace ever reach such a person?

**"As a Guest"**
Suddenly I recall my days as a guest in southern Qin,
Gazing east from Luoyang, blocked by the Qin plains.
Clouds and mountains lie two thousand *li* away,
Nineteen years have passed since then.
Willow hues—are they not newly graceful?
River sounds—do they not still murmur as before?
If this frail body could return once more,
How could the feelings of old not fade into the distant past?

Suddenly I recall my days as a guest in eastern Wu,
Where my youthful heart first saw the land of water and clouds.
Island barbarians dwelt near the wild frontiers,
Tides brought merchants mingling with the sea trade.
Lying down, I watched the vast ocean encircle the earth;
Sitting, I saw the red sun rise from Fusang.
Vain births, vain deaths—how many there are!
Yet the name of "true man" is not easily borne.

Suddenly I recall my days as a guest in eastern Wu,
When my spirit soared, delighting in travel.
Climbing mountains, I never paused lightly,
And never easily ceased with leftover wine.
A thousand lotus stems perfumed the Chu plains,
A soft wind filled the sail past Yangzhou.
Looking back, it was like the dream of Handan—
In an instant, thirty autumns have passed.

Suddenly I recall my days as a guest in Taiyuan,
Where autumn passed in drinking, yet I did not return.
Distant mountains, nearby waters—all turned to sorrow,
High towers in slanting sunlight—all became grief.
Youth could not endure the sight of flowers,
And with too much feeling, tears only stained my robes.
Now, old in Luoyang’s walls,
I no longer bear such sorrow in my brows.

**"Nurturing Life"**
Grasp firmly like an infant,
Breathe strongly like a warrior.
Neither comes naturally—
Both require great effort.
The heart is the body’s master,
The will is the *qi*’s commander.
Sink the pearl into the deep abyss,
Nurture your own heaven and earth.

**"Illness"**
Yaofu was ill for three months,
Worrying the people of Luoyang.
Not just friends’ concern,
But as if for kin.
Recommending doctors with earnest hearts,
Seeking medicine with deep care.
How can I repay such kindness as before,
Visiting their doors to express my thanks?

**"Severe Illness"**
Fifty years of peace and joy,
Then suddenly struck by grave illness.
Still in the height of summer,
I lay abed for hundreds of days.
Acupuncture, moxibustion, and medicines—
A hundred treatments, yet none took effect.
I entrust my fate to heaven,
What loss is there in my heart?

**"Heaven and Humanity"**
Heaven gives this body, entrusted to human effort;
When human effort is exhausted, heaven’s decree arrives.
Heaven and humanity are not a hair’s breadth apart—
If even a hair’s breadth exists, they become two.

**"At Death’s Door"**
With fate, even in peril one does not die;
Without fate, even the best medicine is useless.
Only entrust one’s fate to heaven—
Beyond that, who can idly calculate?

**"Entrusting to Heaven"**
Heaven gives me life,
Heaven takes me in death.
I entrust all to heaven—
What is there that cannot be?

**"Obtaining the One"**
Heaven naturally obtains the One—there is no "after";
I obtain the One only after it comes from Heaven.
Only Heaven and the One are without duality—
How could I ever differ from Heaven?

**"Answering a Guest’s Inquiry About My Illness"**
The world values gold above all,
Yet I alone delight in poetry.
Life and death are one and the same,
While profit and harm idly pursue each other.
Decoctions and remedies are not without effect,
But my illness has already settled deep in the vital organs.
Still, I endure the burning moxa,
To comfort the hearts of friends.

**"On My Deathbed"**
Born in an age of peace,
Grown in an age of peace,
Aged in an age of peace,
To die in an age of peace.
A guest asks my age—
Sixty-seven years.
Looking up and down between heaven and earth,
I am vast and without shame.

*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Nineteen Concludes*
*Yichuan Jirang Ji, Scroll Twenty*

**Shaoyong’s "One Hundred Thirty-Five Closing Poems"**

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
I see the rise of sages and worthies in their time.
Sun, moon, and stars—Yao understood them;
Rivers and streams—Yu tamed them.
Kings and emperors were praised or censured,
Yet snow, moon, wind, and flowers remain unranked.
Not that the ancients lacked a perfect canon—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
I sit and watch the hours in my Peaceful Nest.
The primal qi revolves without the slightest pause,
The Two Modes shelter and cover without partiality.
The four seasons take turns as host,
While the hundred things renew and vie in marvels.
Having enjoyed so much domestic joy,
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
I am content in my Peaceful Nest.
My aspirations fulfilled, I need not seek fame,
For when a book is written, readers will come.
Forests and streams serve as heart’s delight,
Wind and moon suffice as hand’s resources.
How much broad and level land lies before me—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Half-drunk in my Peaceful Nest.
Inspired by moon and flowers, I chant verses,
Drafting letters and missives in their stead.
In the pond, a pair of fish leaps;
In the sky, how could there be no lone wild goose?
Countless friends reside in the south and north—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I find it lovable.
A precious mirror shapes the form but cannot touch the hair,
A phoenix knife meets the blade without leaving a thread.
If wind and dust do not invade the path,
How could dust ever settle on one’s robes?
To speak of sincerity and enlightenment is no easy task—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
I see the waxing and waning of all things in their time.
Heaven and earth’s full work must be revealed,
The court’s great beauty lies in its deeds.
If all remained silent, how could it be seen?
If none spoke, how could it be known?
Matters lie before us, yet people do not ponder—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I cannot sleep.
To savor orchid’s fragrance, one must have a master;
To expand the wind and elegance, for whom is it done?
Three thousand poems gather the bright moon,
Twenty-odd years twist my white beard.
Having fulfilled so many idle duties—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry reaches its end when words are forgotten.
Though words convey the essential mystery,
One must perceive the subtle in things.
Soup is known as bland only when unseasoned,
Music is recognized as rare only when soundless.
How much wind and flowers await revision—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Though old, my spirit is not yet spent.
I have claimed the leisure of water and bamboo,
And also possess the wealth of orioles and flowers.
The moon of the parasol tree shines into my bosom,
The wind from the willows blows upon my face.
With so much idle luxury surrounding me—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I stand by the Heavenly Ford.
The sound of water, intentional, endures through ages,
The color of mountains, indifferent, surrounds on all sides.
A lone goose vanishes into the distant mist,
A pair of egrets slants through the forbidden willows.
Scenery need not hinder my ease—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I linger again by the Heavenly Ford.
Lush orioles and flowers offer their beauty,
The wind and moon of Shangyang inspire novelty.
Phoenix towers and pavilions are seen amid clouds,
The gardens of virtue and Dao are glimpsed from my pillow.
I do not waste this age of peace with idle verses—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I grow old.
I observe the world through the lens of wind and elegance,
Yet through words, heaven’s secrets slip.
Few carriages and horses reach the grove,
Cups and goblets beyond the dust do not fly in vain.
A man of sixty-one years, free of cares—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I embody loyalty and forbearance.
The myriad things’ nature is seen in idleness,
The properties of all medicines are known in illness.
To be blind to success ensures failure,
To fail in knowing others invites deceit.
Family, state, and self are of one body—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I silently perceive.
At first, with intention, like father and son,
Yet in the end, without feeling, like barbarians.
Success and failure before one’s eyes remain unseen,
How can one know the peace or peril of the world?
Only now do I believe knowing others is difficult—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I recognize my fortune.
Before the sun rises, I pay homage to the Lord on High,
At the first breath of spring, I visit Fuxi.
Three cups, five cups—I urge myself to drink,
One game, two games—I play chess without contention.
What the music of *Shao* and *Hu* must be like—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I exhort myself.
Attaining the Dao depends entirely on studying with a teacher,
Transcending the dust requires inborn celestial endowment.
I fear only recognizing worthies too late,
I worry only about seeing goodness too slowly.
Thanks to friends who constantly instruct me—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
Poetry is when I am content.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
When most elated, I rise to dance,
At the stroke of my brush, words take flight.
The Peng soars ten thousand miles o’er the southern sea at first lift,
The crane returns to the distant shore after a thousand years’ flight.
Yet poetry is not merely this—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I sigh in wonder.
Fate is fixed, yet I refuse to believe in rigidity;
If there were no heaven, could falsehood truly deceive?
Talent is but a tool of cleverness,
Nobility bends to profit and harm.
Helpless, I watch spring age once more—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when my brush runs wild.
The azure sea’s gods hunt whales and leviathans,
The sunken land, devoid of water, hides dragons and serpents.
Mount Hua soars five thousand *ren* in towering splendor,
The Yellow River’s expanse stretches a hundred thousand *qing*.
All this I gather into my verses—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I come and go.
Early spring’s warmth lingers as the sun tarries,
Early autumn’s chill arrives with clouds soft and faint.
A gentle breeze stirs the drooping willows,
A fine rain passes over lush grass.
My light carriage lingers in the grove, unreturned—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I test my inkstone.
Jade, uncarved, still bears its raw discernment;
Gold, once refined, leaves no room for doubt.
Once, all mocked my careless stride—
Now, who does not shake their head in knowing?
Beyond the heavens, the phoenix flies where none may follow—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I try my brush.
Life and death are settled in their course,
And all else—honor, disgrace—may be known thereby.
At home, I act as one at home;
By the water’s edge, I speak as one by the water.
This spares both mind and effort—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I test my ink.
In a town of ten households, trust must exist;
Where three walk together, can there be no teacher?
Without counsel, plans grow crude and careless;
Too much thought breeds hesitation and shame.
Miss the moment, and it cannot be reclaimed—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I speak of the Dao.
Heaven’s ear, though high, hears but a whisper;
The hearts of men differ by no great measure.
In silence and scentlessness, all is complete—
Unenvious, undesiring, one attains it.
Even with ink and brush, it defies depiction—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I speak of things.
Things wax and wane with the seasons,
Men flourish and fade with the turning times.
The new moon at the horizon waits its time,
Fallen blossoms on the water—where to pursue?
All are but the common affairs of the world—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I speak of events.
If heaven could be ascended, it would need no urging;
If spirits held no proof, why would one pray?
Matters in the hall are easily arranged,
But those past cannot be altered before one’s eyes.
Power waxes and wanes—not in a single day—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I ascend the pavilion.
A thousand years of past affairs dissolve into mist,
A single ray of slanting sunlight scatters its glow.
The Yi and Luo rivers thread like slender strands,
Mounts Tai and Shao rise sharp as awls.
Contention has ever been but idle breath—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I recline by my desk.
Cherish every inch of time,
Boast not of the slightest talent.
Wine, poured for guests, is sipped from small cups;
Verses that astonish are writ in bold strokes.
In no realm do I fail to find contentment—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I chant of history.
Since ancient times, all has been but a thousand dreams,
The Central Plains reduced to a single chessboard.
The golden age of Yao and Shun fades in purple haze,
The wars of Tang and Wu wither in the grass.
Gazing on the past, much of the present is revealed—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I face the wine.
Though I lack a single good deed in this world,
My conduct has erred, known to all four directions.
In observing things, one must seek vitality;
Once formed, a pattern must find its expression.
Poetry is the destination of the heart’s intent—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I am middle-aged.
Though reclusion brings no wealth to my house,
Diligent humility has begotten sons to carry on.
My limbs, fortunately, remain hale,
And my words may still jest with ease.
Sixty-two years a man of no affairs—
I do not love to write poetry.

**"I Do Not Love to Write Poetry"**
I do not love to write poetry—
Poetry is when I laugh at myself.
Idle and carefree, yet never far from worldly matters,
My words now and then reveal heaven’s secrets.
The smallest deeds are hardest to establish,
The greatest achievements may come with ease.
Success and failure lie beyond all reckoning—
I do not love to write poetry.
